Patient Presentation: Musculoskeletal Difficulties And Muscular Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Musculoskeletal System
Discipline: Osteopathic Principle and Practice
A 45 year-old female presents to the office with right knee pain.  This pain has been intermittent for the last 3 months and has recently become more persistent.  The pain is sharp in nature and is worse with walking up and down stairs.  With the patient in the supine position you flex her knee up while monitoring at the joint line, the leg is then externally rotated and a genu valgus force is place while you bring the knee into extension.  During this procedure, the patient notes pain and you palpate a "click."  What do you suspect is the pathology causing this patient's knee pain?

1. Lateral Meniscal Injury
2. Medial Meniscal Injury
3. Medial Collateral Ligament Injury
4. The Unhappy Triad
5. Patellofemoral Syndrome

The correct answer is: B
This is the classic McMurray medial meniscus test.  The test is used to check for a medial meniscal injury.  A positive test is a "click" and/or pain.  A modification of this test called the Fouche test is used to test for lateral and posterior meniscal injury.  In this variation the leg is internally rotated and a genu varus force is placed on the knee.

Answer A: A positive Fouche test would be indicative of a lateral meniscal injury.


Answer C: To test for a medial collateral ligament injury you can perform a genu valgus test, where you provide a medial force on the lateral aspect of the knee to create strain on the medial collateral ligament. Laxity and pain would be a positive test in this case.


Answer D: Although a medial meniscus injury is a part of the "unhappy triad." There is not enough information in this question to make the diagnosis of a medial collateral ligament injury or an ACL injury.


Answer E: Patellofemoral syndrome often presents with similar symptoms as meniscal injury- pain walking up and down stairs. You would not have a positive McMurray's test in this syndrome. You would diagnose using the patella grind test.




Bottom Line: Meniscal injury is a common knee pathology.  It usually presents as knee pain, worse with stairs and "clicking" or "popping."  Sometimes the knee will lock.  One of the special test you can do to diagnose it is the McMurray medial meniscus test where the knee is bent, leg is externally rotated and the leg is brought into extension while provided a genu valgus force.

For more information, see:
Chila, A. Foundations of Osteopathic Medicine. 3rd Ed. Lippincott Williams and Wilkins; 2011. p. 610.
Hoppenfeld, S. Physical Examination of the Spine and Extremities. Appleton-Centure-Crost; Norwalk, CT; 1976. pp. 191-196.

Link: "Overview of neutropenic fever syndromes" on UpToDate.


Patient Presentation: Fever
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Health Promotion & Disease Prevention
Body System: Hematology/Oncology
Discipline: Emergency Medicine
A 65-year-old male presents to the emergency department with the complaint of fever. Past medical history reveals current chemotherapy treatment for prostate cancer. Physical examination reveals an emaciated elderly male with a temperature of 102.2°F. Laboratory analysis reveals:

hemoglobin   10.2 g/dL 
hematocrit  30.2%
WBC 3200/mm3,
neutrophil 243 cells/uL
platelet 5,000/mm3

Urinalysis and culture is obtained and is negative. Which of the following should be avoided in this patient?

1. chest x-ray
2. CT scan
3. digital rectal exam
4. sputum cultures
5. stool cultures

The correct answer is: C
The most likely diagnosis is neutropenic fever. Neutropenic fever is defined as a single oral temperature equal to or greater than 38.3ºC or a temperature equal to or greater than 38.0ºC for more than one hour in a patient with an absolute neutrophil count less than 500 cells/mm^3. It is a common diagnosis in cancer patients who are currently involved in a chemotherapy regimen, AIDS patients, and other immunosuppressed patients. The cause of the fever must be determined as soon as possible so treatment can be directed toward the cause. Blood, urine, sputum and/or wound cultures should be taken. A chest x-ray should be ordered for all patients with respiratory symptoms and a CT scan can be used to evaluate for abscesses or other occult infections. There is a specific treatment algorithm for treating neutropenic patients. First, patients are classified as low or high risk. Low-risk patients are those with solid tumors that are being treated on an outpatient basis with conventional chemotherapy agents, patients with minimal comorbidities and those who have had neutropenia for less than 7 days. Low-risk patients may be treated with oral ciprofloxacin and amoxicillin/clavulanate. High-risk patients are those patients with any of the following: Anticipated, prolonged (>7 days), and profound neutropenia (ANC < 100/uL) following cytotoxic chemotherapy, significant comorbidities including hypotension, pneumonia, new-onset abdominal pain or neurologic change, taking high-dose chemotherapy,and patients with hematologic malignancies. High risk patients first-line monotherapy must include an agent with antipseudomonal activity. Quinolones and aminoglycosides are not acceptable as monotherapy. Piperacillin-tazobactam 4.5g IV q 6h or Cefepime 2grams IV q 8h or Meropenem 1g IV q 8h or Imipenem-cilastatin 500mg IV q 6h. No single agent has shown superiority in the empiric treatment of febrile neutropenia. If any of these treatment regimens does not cause the fever to regress after 72 hours an anti-fungal agent should be added. A rectal exam should be avoided if the patient is thrombocytopenic because of increased bleeding risk. Any cause of bleeding in a thrombocytopenic patient may be difficult to stop but can be controlled with platelet infusions. In the presence of active bleeding, a platelet transfusion should occur in patients with platelet counts below 50,000.

Answers A & B & D & E: This test should be performed to rule out an organic etiology of the patient's fever.




Bottom Line: A rectal exam should be avoided in neutropenic patients if the patient is thrombocytopenic because of increased bleeding risk.

For more information, see:
"Overview of neutropenic fever syndromes" on UpToDate.
"Neutropenic Fever Empiric Therapy" on medscape

Link: "Pediatric Tetanus Clinical Presentation" on Medscape.


Patient Presentation: Pregnancy/Childbirth/Postpartum/Neonatal Assessment
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Nervous System
Discipline: Pediatrics
A 20-year-old G2P2 female presents to the office after the birth of her second child one week ago with the complaint that the patient has not been able to breast feed over the past 2 days. History reveals the newborn has become progressively rigid over the same time period. The mother had no regular prenatal care throughout the pregnancy and delivered the infant at home. The mother’s immunization history is unknown and the child has received no vaccinations to date. Vital signs of the newborn reveal a heart rate of 150/min, a temperature of 37.3°C (99.1°F), and a respiratory rate of 44/min. Physical examination reveals a rigid infant with increased tone and a fixed extended neck.
Question 1 of 2 in this set
The most likely diagnosis is

1. Enterovirus
2. Group B Streptococcus
3. Herpes simplex virus
4. Respiratory syncytial virus
5. Tetanus

The correct answer is: E
Neonatal tetanus usually results from tetanus spores infecting the umbilical stump of neonates of poorly immunized mothers. Neonatal tetanus is more likely to occur when sterile technique is not employed in the birthing field, which is more likely given her home delivery. This disease is rare in developed countries but accounts for an estimated 14% of neonatal deaths worldwide. Neonatal tetanus is preventable through maternal immunization and sterile technique during delivery.

Answers A & B & C & D: The above presentation is consistent with neonatal tetanus.




Bottom Line: Neonatal tetanus is a preventable disease that presents with a rigid infant in later stages of the disease. Infection usually occurs from an infected umbilical stump after delivery at home.

For more information, see:
"Pediatric Tetanus Clinical Presentation" on Medscape.

Patient Presentation: Pregnancy/Childbirth/Postpartum/Neonatal Assessment
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Scientific Understanding of Health & Disease Mechanisms
Body System: Gastrointestinal System
Discipline: Pediatrics
A 20-year-old G2P2 female presents to the office after the birth of her second child one week ago with the complaint that the patient has not been able to breast feed over the past 2 days. History reveals the newborn has become progressively rigid over the same time period. The mother had no regular prenatal care throughout the pregnancy and delivered the infant at home. The mother’s immunization history is unknown and the child has received no vaccinations to date. Vital signs of the newborn reveal a heart rate of 150/min, a temperature of 37.3°C (99.1°F), and a respiratory rate of 44/min. Physical examination reveals a rigid infant with increased tone and a fixed extended neck.
Question 2 of 2 in this set
Which of the following treatments impacts unbound toxin?

1. human tetanus immunoglobulin
2. intravenous immunoglobulin
3. metronidazole
4. penicillin G
5. trimethoprim-sulfamethoxazole

The correct answer is: A
Human tetanus immunoglobulin is the treatment of choice for neutralizing unbound tetanus toxin. Other principles in management of a patient with tetanus include halting toxin production, controlling muscle spasms, and management of dysautonomia. Halting toxin production can be achieved with antimicrobials. Muscle spasms may be controlled with sedatives and neuromuscular blocking agents. Dysautonomia can be managed with magnesium sulfate and/or beta blockade.

Answer B: Intravenous immunoglobulin has not been studied extensively for neutralizing unbound tetanus toxin. Human tetanus immunoglobulin is the treatment of choice for this purpose.


Answers C & D: Metronidazole and penicillin G can be used to halt tetanus toxin production, not to neutralize unbound toxin.


Answer E: Trimethoprim-sulfamethoxazole is not involved in the treatment of tetanus.




Bottom Line: Human tetanus immunoglobulin is the treatment of choice for neutralizing unbound tetanus toxin.

For more information, see:
"Pediatric Tetanus" on Medscape.

Link: "Clinical manifestations of adrenal insufficiency in adults" on UpToDate.


Patient Presentation: Skin, Nail & Hair Disorders
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Endocrine System
Discipline: Family Medicine
A 26-year-old female presents to the office with the complaint of skin discoloration. History reveals she has been feeling weak and fatigued with vague gastrointestinal symptoms over the past few weeks' duration. She has attributed these symptoms to the excess stress from her new job. The patient’s family history is significant for vitiligo. Vital signs reveal a blood pressure of 98/64 mmHg. Physical examination reveals the patient's palms, arms, and face are bronze in color, there is paravertebral hypertonicity from T11-L1 and you note a tender nodule about 2 inches superior to the umbilicus just left of midline. A random plasma glucose is found to be 40 g/dL. 

Question 1 of 2 in this set
The most likely diagnosis is

1. Addison disease
2. Conn syndrome
3. Cushing’s disease
4. hemolytic anemia
5. hemochromatosis

The correct answer is: A
This is a classic presentation of Addison’s disease (aka: primary adrenal insufficiency, hypocortisolism or hypocorticism) characterized by weight loss, muscle weakness, fatigue, and hypotension. Most people with primary Addison's disease also have hyperpigmentation of the skin, including areas not typically exposed to the sun. Sites of hyperpigmentation include areas of skin creases (e.g. of the hands), face, nipples, and buccal mucosa. You may see viscerosomatic changes from T11-L1 as well as Chapman points as seen in this case vignette.  The anterior chapman point is one inch lateral and two inches superior to the umbilicus, while the posterior point overlies the transverse process of T11.  The side of the chapman point (left or right) may be useful in helping you determine which suprarenal area is dysfunctioning.  The pathophysiology of hyperpigmentation in Addison’s disease is important and occurs due to the fact that melanocyte-stimulating hormone (MSH) and adrenocorticotropic hormone (ACTH) share the same precursor molecule. Therefore, an increase in ACTH production also increases MSH. Addison’s disease is often associated with other autoimmune comorbidities, such as vitiligo.

Answer B: Conn syndrome, also known as primary hyperaldosteronism is an adrenal disorder characterized by the overproduction of aldosterone. Patients almost always present with hypertension, unlike the above scenario, and may also complain of muscle weakness or cramping, in addition to headaches secondary to hypokalemia or increases in blood pressure.


Answer C: Cushing’s disease is an incorrect diagnosis in this case and would typically present with glucocorticoid excess, moon facies, buffalo hump, and abdominal striae.


Answer D: Hemolytic anemia can also present with hyperpigmentation of the skin but is unlikely in this scenario.


Answer E: Hemochromatosis is a hereditary disease characterized by excessive absorption of dietary iron resulting in a pathological increase in total body iron stores. Early symptoms include severe fatigue (75%), impotence (45%), and arthralgias (40%). Skin bronzing (70%) occurs late in the disease process and reflects a combination of iron deposition and melanin. The classic triad of cirrhosis, diabetes mellitus, and skin pigmentation should be noted.




Bottom Line: Addison’s disease (primary adrenal insufficiency) commonly presents with weight loss, muscle weakness, fatigue, hypotension, and hyperpigmentation of the skin.

COMBANK Insight : Remember that primary Addison’s disease is the only type that presents with bronzing of the skin. Both secondary and tertiary forms of the disease do not present with this feature.

For more information, see:
"Clinical manifestations of adrenal insufficiency in adults" on UpToDate.
"Addison Disease" on Medscape.


Last Updated: 10/27/2016

Link: "Clinical manifestations of adrenal insufficiency in adults" on UpToDate.


Patient Presentation: Skin, Nail & Hair Disorders
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Scientific Understanding of Health & Disease Mechanisms
Body System: Endocrine System
Discipline: Family Medicine
A 26-year-old female presents to the office with the complaint of skin discoloration. History reveals she has been feeling weak and fatigued with vague gastrointestinal symptoms over the past few weeks' duration. She has attributed these symptoms to the excess stress from her new job. The patient’s family history is significant for vitiligo. Vital signs reveal a blood pressure of 98/64 mmHg. Physical examination reveals the patient's palms, arms, and face are bronze in color, there is paravertebral hypertonicity from T11-L1 and you note a tender nodule about 2 inches superior to the umbilicus just left of midline. A random plasma glucose is found to be 40 g/dL. 

Question 2 of 2 in this set
An electrolyte panel would most likely reveal

1. hyperkalemia and hypercalcemia
2. hyperkalemia and hypernatremia
3. hyperkalemia and hyponatremia
4. hypokalemia and hypernatremia
5. hypocalcemia and hyponatremia

The correct answer is: C
The evaluation of patients with suspected Addison disease should always include an electrolyte panel to test for hyponatremia and hyperkalemia. Hyponatremia is the most common finding and occurs in 90% of patients. Hyperkalemia is also common can occur in up to 70% of cases. Hypercalcemia is uncommon but is still found in up to 10% of patients presenting with the disorder. Addison disease results in autoimmune destruction of the adrenal gland. The resulting low production of mineralocorticoids leads to low aldosterone. Subsequently, the patient becomes hyponatremic and hypotensive. Low cortisol also contributes to hypotension. The distal collecting duct of the kidney is responsible for sodium reabsorption . Since this process is diminished in these patients, hyperkalemia results.

Answers A & B & D & E: These answers are unlikely combinations of electrolyte abnormalities in patients with Addison disease. Please see explanation above for a description of the pathophysiology of Addison disease.




Bottom Line: The most common electrolyte abnormalities in patients with Addison’s disease are hyperkalemia and hyponatremia.

COMBANK Insight : In patients in acute adrenal crisis (secondary adrenal insufficiency), IV access should be established urgently, and an infusion of isotonic sodium chloride solution should be begun to restore volume deficit and correct hypotension. Some patients may require glucose supplementation.

For more information, see:
"Clinical manifestations of adrenal insufficiency in adults" on UpToDate.
"Addison Disease" on Medscape.

7 of 200

Link: Treatment of PCOS on UpToDate


Patient Presentation: Genitourinary Disorders/Issues
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Management
Body System: Reproductive System
Discipline: Obstetrics/Gynecology
A 24-year-old nulligravida female presents to the office with the complaint of difficulty becoming pregnant. History reveals irregular menstrual bleeding since the time of menarche. She has been trying to become pregnant for the last 14 months and her periods occur every 10-60 days. Physical examination reveals a well-appearing female in no distress. There is no tenderness to palpation in the abdomen and pelvis and speculum examination is unremarkable. Osteopathic examination is significant for hypertonicity and tenderness in the paravertebral musculature from T10-T11, representing a viscerosomatic reflex. Laboratory analysis is unremarkable. The most appropriate course of action is

1. clomiphene citrate
2. egg donation
3. in vitro fertilization
4. oral contraceptive pills
5. ovarian drilling

The correct answer is: A
Polycystic Ovarian Syndrome (PCOS) is the most common endocrine disorder in reproductive-age women.  Diagnosis requires two of the following three criteria: 1-Polycystic ovaries, 2-Oligo-/anovulation, 3-Clinical or biochemical evidence of hyperandrogenism.  On history, patients may complain of irregular menstrual cycles, hirsutism, acne, and infertility due to anovulation.  PCOS is also associated with hypertension, obesity, dyslipidemia, and diabetes. Weight loss (not listed here) should be a first-line treatment for overweight patients with PCOS as many women will resume ovulation after weight loss. Of these answer choices, clomiphene citrate is the first line treatment for ovulation abnormalities leading to infertility. Clomiphene citrate is a selective estrogen receptor modulator.  It reacts with estrogen receptors in the hypothalamus which results in increased pulsatile GnRH release and subsequent LH and FSH release. For women not pursuing pregnancy, oral contraceptives are the first-line pharmacological treatment as they will restore normal menses and control hyperandrogenism. 

Osteopathic Pearl: Viscerosomatic reflexes are a type of somatic dysfunction caused by changes in the autonomic nervous system. They can be acute or chronic, and are considered to be a somatic representation of visceral disease. When either branch of the autonomic nervous system (sympathetic or parasympathetic) over-stimulates a particular organ, a reflex reaction occurs. The soma at the same level of the organ's autonomic innervation takes on characteristics of somatic dysfunction (TART). T10-T11 is where the sympathetic viscerosomatic reflex for the ovary is located.

Answer B: Egg donation is indicated for infertility patients with diminished ovarian reserve or ovarian failure. Ovulatory dysfunction is not an indication for egg donation.


Answer C: In vitro fertilization is not first line therapy for ovulatory dysfunction. It can be utilized after failure of medical therapy.


Answer D: The case above presents a patient with complaints of infertility. Although oral contraceptive pills will regulate the patient’s menstrual cycles, they will also prevent pregnancy.


Answer E: Ovarian drilling is no longer commonly used for infertility treatment due to risk of adhesion formation and potential diminished ovarian reserve.




Bottom Line: Clomiphene citrate is the first-line treatment for ovulation abnormalities as the cause of infertility.

For more information, see:
Treatment of PCOS on UpToDate
Nye, Z. (2009). First aid for the COMLEX: An osteopathic manipulative medicine review (2nd ed.). New York: McGraw-Hill Medical.


Last Updated: 10/31/2016
8 of 200

Link: "Epiglottitis" on Medscape


Patient Presentation: Sensory Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Respiratory System
Discipline: Family Medicine
A 42-year-old male presents to the office with the complaint of a sore throat for three days' duration. History reveals it has progressively worsened since he woke up this morning and now it has become difficult to swallow. He admits to fever with chills and notes it is easier to breathe sitting up and leaning forward.  His voice is hoarse, however, he does not appear to be tachypneic or breathing with retractions. Physical examination reveals drooling, erythema of the posterior pharynx without exudates, and bilateral lymphadenopathy of the neck. You note paravertebral hypertonicity and warmth from T1-4 and a tender nodule near the right articular pillar of C2.  Neck radiographs are obtained as shown in the exhibit.
Question 1 of 3 in this set
The most likely diagnosis is

1. bronchiolitis
2. croup
3. epiglottitis
4. foreign-body aspiration
5. retropharyngeal abscess

The correct answer is: C
The patient has acute adult onset epiglottitis with airway obstruction. Epiglottitis is an acute inflammation in the supraglottic region of the oropharynx. In United States, epiglottitis is an uncommon disease due to the advent of the immunization against H. influenza. It is most commonly seen in the pediatric population although adult epiglottitis does occur, most frequently in men during the fifth decade of life. Presentation includes hoarse voice, fever, odynophagia, dysphagia, and preceding URI symptoms.

As with any other pathology in the head and neck we see TART changes from T1-4 reflective of viscerosomatic reflexes.  In this example we also see a posterior Chapman point near the articular pillar of C2 which corresponds to pathology/inflammation in the pharynx and epiglottis.

Answer A: Bronchiolitis is an acute, infectious, inflammatory disease of the upper and lower respiratory tract that may result in obstruction of the small
airways. Because bronchiolitis primarily affects young infants, clinical manifestations are initially subtle. Infants may become increasingly fussy and have difficulty feeding during the 2 to 5-day incubation period. A low-grade fever, usually less than 101.5°F, and increasing coryza and congestion usually follow the incubation period. In older children and adults, as opposed to infants, respiratory syncytial virus (RSV) infection is usually confined to the upper airway.


Answer B: Croup is a common, primarily pediatric viral respiratory tract illness. Croup manifests as hoarseness, a seal-like barking cough, and a variable degree of respiratory distress. Stridor is a common symptom in patients with croup. The acute onset of this abnormal sound alarms parents enough to prompt an urgent care or emergency department visit. Stridor is an audible harsh, high-pitched, musical sound produced by turbulent airflow through a partially obstructed upper airway. Croup usually begins with nonspecific respiratory symptoms, including rhinorrhea, sore throat, and cough. Fever is generally low-grade (38-39°C) but can exceed 40°C. Within 1-2 days, the characteristic signs of hoarseness, barking cough, and inspiratory stridor develop, often suddenly, along with a variable degree of respiratory distress.


Answer D: Foreign body aspiration can be a life-threatening emergency. An aspirated solid or semisolid object may lodge in the larynx or trachea. Respiratory distress, aphonia, cyanosis, loss of consciousness, and death occur in quick succession unless the object is dislodged. When the degree of obstruction is less severe or when the aspirated object descends beyond the carina, the presentation is less dramatic. Sudden onset of the classic triad (ie, coughing, wheezing, decreased breathing sounds) is frequently not observed. Presenting symptoms (other than cough) include fever, hemoptysis, dyspnea, and chest pain. A history of a choking episode is not always obtained or may have initially been ignored or misdiagnosed.


Answer E: Retropharyngeal abscess (RPA) produces the symptoms of sore throat, fever, neck stiffness, and stridor. Retropharyngeal abscess occurs much less commonly today than in the past because of the widespread use of antibiotics for suppurative upper respiratory infections.




Bottom Line: Acute epiglottitis may manifest with symptoms of stridor, hoarse, and a rapid clinical course.

For more information, see:
"Epiglottitis" on Medscape


Last Updated: 12/8/2016

Patient Presentation: Sensory Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Diagnostic Technologies
Body System: Respiratory System
Discipline: Family Medicine
A 42-year-old male presents to the office with the complaint of a sore throat for three days' duration. History reveals it has progressively worsened since he woke up this morning and now it has become difficult to swallow. He admits to fever with chills and notes it is easier to breathe sitting up and leaning forward.  His voice is hoarse, however, he does not appear to be tachypneic or breathing with retractions. Physical examination reveals drooling, erythema of the posterior pharynx without exudates, and bilateral lymphadenopathy of the neck. You note paravertebral hypertonicity and warmth from T1-4 and a tender nodule near the right articular pillar of C2.  Neck radiographs are obtained as shown in the exhibit.
Question 2 of 3 in this set
Which of the following radiographic signs is most closely associated with the diagnosis?

1. lead pipe sign
2. Sail sign
3. Steeple sign
4. string sign
5. Thumbprint sign

The correct answer is: E
The thumbprint sign is a finding on a lateral C-spine radiograph that suggests the diagnosis of epiglottitis. The sign is caused by a thickened free edge of the epiglottis, which causes it to appear more radiopaque than normal, resembling the distal thumb.

Answer A: As ulcerative colitis becomes chronic, the colon becomes a rigid foreshortened tube that lacks its usual haustral markings, leading to the lead pipe appearance observed on barium enema.


Answer B: Sail sign on chest radiograph may resemble the thymus gland in children.


Answer C: The Steeple sign is a sign on a frontal radiograph of tracheal narrowing and suggestive of the diagnosis of croup.


Answer D: In Crohn’s disease as inflammation penetrates the submucosa and muscularis layers, deep knife-like linear clefts form the basis of "cobblestoning" and fissure or fistula formation. This leads to a radiographic string sign that represents long areas of circumferential inflammation and fibrosis resulting in long segments of luminal narrowing.




Bottom Line: The thumbprint sign is a finding on a lateral C-spine radiograph that suggests the diagnosis of epiglottitis.

For more information, see:
"Epiglottitis" on Medscape.

Patient Presentation: Sensory Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Scientific Understanding of Health & Disease Mechanisms
Body System: Respiratory System
Discipline: Family Medicine
A 42-year-old male presents to the office with the complaint of a sore throat for three days' duration. History reveals it has progressively worsened since he woke up this morning and now it has become difficult to swallow. He admits to fever with chills and notes it is easier to breathe sitting up and leaning forward.  His voice is hoarse, however, he does not appear to be tachypneic or breathing with retractions. Physical examination reveals drooling, erythema of the posterior pharynx without exudates, and bilateral lymphadenopathy of the neck. You note paravertebral hypertonicity and warmth from T1-4 and a tender nodule near the right articular pillar of C2.  Neck radiographs are obtained as shown in the exhibit.
Question 3 of 3 in this set
The most likely pathogen responsible for this infection is

1. Haemophilus influenzae
2. Haemophilus parainfluenzae
3. Staphylococcus aureus
4. Streptococcus pneumoniae
5. Streptococcus pyogenes

The correct answer is: A
In adults, the most common organism that causes acute epiglottitis is Haemophilus influenzae followed by Parainfluenzae, Streptococcus pneumoniae, and Group A Streptococci. Noninfectious causes of epiglottitis include thermal causes (including those associated with crack cocaine and marijuana smoking, caustic insults, and foreign body ingestion. 

Answers B & C & D & E: The most common cause for epiglottitis in adults is Haemophilus influenzae.




Bottom Line: The most common organism that causes acute epiglottitis is Haemophilus influenzae. 

COMBANK Insight : Third-generation cephalosporins such as ceftriaxone are preferred as first-line agents because of increasing resistance to ampicillin. 

For more information, see:
"Epiglottitis" on Medscape.

Patient Presentation: Musculoskeletal Difficulties And Muscular Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Management
Body System: Musculoskeletal System
Discipline: Osteopathic Principle and Practice
A 33-year old male with a history of hypertension controlled with lisinopril and type 2 diabetes presents with chronic low back pain over the last 5 years. The current episode of low back pain has been ongoing over the last 3 days. He typically manages his low back pain with NSAIDs as needed, but would like to explore other treatment options today. He denies any lower extremity weakness, difficulty walking, or radiating pain. Vital signs are as follows: BP 135/85, HR 80, RR 14, T 99.1 F. On physical exam, the patient is an obese male who is pleasant and in no apparent distress. Patient has limited range of motion in thoracic and lumbar flexion. Fascial restrictions are noted in the thoracic and lumbar region. After treatment of the two regions with myofascial release, the physician notes that palpating the transverse processes at the level of T8 spinous process are rotated left, which become symmetric with flexion of the trunk. What is the most appropriate initial patient positioning in order to address his thoracic somatic dysfunction with post-isometric MET?

1. The patient’s trunk is extended, sidebent right, and rotated right at the level of T8 until the edge of the restrictive barriers are addressed at T8
2. The patient’s trunk is extended, sidebent right, and rotated right at the level of T9 until the edge of the restrictive barriers are addressed at T9
3. The patient’s trunk is flexed, sidebent left, and rotated left at the level of T8 until the edge of the restrictive barriers are addressed at T8
4. The patient’s trunk is flexed, sidebent left, and rotated left at the level of T9 until the edge of the restrictive barriers are addressed at T9
5. The patient’s trunk is extended, sidebent right, and rotated right at the level of T8 until the edge of the restrictive barriers are addressed at T8

The correct answer is: B
Muscle energy technique is a form of direct osteopathic manipulative treatment where the patient’s muscles are actively contracted from a precisely controlled position, in a specific direction, and against an executed physician counterforce. The majority of thoracic MET involves direct post-isometric relaxation. In this type of MET, the physician instructs the patient to isometrically contract the agonist muscle. Immediately following an isometric contraction, the neuromuscular apparatus is in a refractory state during which enhanced passive stretching may be performed. The physician may take up the myofascial slack during this relaxed refractory period.

Upper thoracic somatic dysfunctions (T1-T4) use the head and neck as a lever to induce motion at the segment; lower thoracic somatic dysfunctions (T5-T12) use the trunk as a lever. On physical exam, the patient is noted to have a T9 vertebra that is flexed, sidebent left, and rotated left. Note the guideline or rule of three’s in order to note the location of the spinous processes in relation to the transverse processes due to the fact that the thoracic spinous processes are long and angled inferiorly. Due to this configuration, the T9 transverse processes are located at the level of the T8 spinous process. 

With the patient seated, the physician monitors the T9-T10 interspace with one hand. With the other hand, the physician uses the patient’s trunk as a lever to induce extension, right sidebending, and right rotation until the edge of the restrictive barrier is reached at T9. The patient is instructed to flex the trunk and turn left while the physician provides an unyielding counterforce. The isometric contraction provided by the patient is maintained for 3 to 5 seconds and then the patient is to stop and relax. When the patient is relaxed, the physician positions the trunk in order to engage the edge of the new restrictive barriers in extension, right sidebending, and right rotation. The series of isometric contraction, relaxation, and re-positioning is repeated 3 to 5 times or until motion is improved at T9.

Answer A: The transverse process is located one segment above the corresponding spinous process. For example, the transverse processes of T9 are located at the level of T8.


Answer C: The transverse process is located one segment above the corresponding spinous process. For example, the transverse processes of T9 are located at the level of T8. Additionally, flexion, left sidebending, and left rotation are the positions of ease.


Answer D: Flexion, left sidebending, and left rotation are the positions of ease.




Bottom Line: The “rule of 3s” describes the relation of the palpable tips of the spinous processes to the thoracic vertebral bodies. The spinous processes of T7 to T9 are located a full vertebral body lower than their respective transverse process.

COMBANK Insight : The rule of 3s are as follows:

T1-T3: the transverse process is located at the level of the corresponding spinous process

T4-T6: the transverse process is located ½ of a segment above the corresponding spinous process (i.e. the transverse process of T4 is located halfway between T3 and T4)

T7-T9: the transverse process is located one segment above the corresponding spinous process (i.e. the transverse process of T8 is located at the level of T7)

T10: same rule as T7-T9*

T11: same rule as T4-T6*

T12: same rule as T1-T3

* - Some texts that state that the transverse processes of T10-T12 are at the same level as the spinous process

For more information, see:
Muscle Energy Techniques. In: Nicholas AS, Nicholas EA. Atlas of Osteopathic Techniques. 3rd ed. Philadelphia, PA: Lippincott Williams & Wilkins; 2015.
Normal Vertebral Motion. In: DeStefano, LA. Greenman’s Principles of Manual Medicine. Philadelphia, PA: Wolters Kluwer; 2017.
Thoracic Spine Technique. In: DeStefano, LA. Greenman’s Principles of Manual Medicine. Philadelphia, PA: Wolters Kluwer; 2017.
Savarese RG. OMT Review: A Comprehensive Review in Osteopathic Medicine. 2009:19, 136,138.

Patient Presentation: Digestive Difficulties
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Hepatobiliary System
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 45-year-old female presents to the office with a one week history of nausea and vomiting. Her medical history is significant for hypothyroidism diagnosed 5 years ago, for which she takes levothyroxine. She also has a history of osteoarthritis, for which she takes 2 tablets of acetaminophen daily. She smokes a half pack of cigarettes daily and drinks wine with dinner. She denies sick contacts or recent travel. Vital signs reveal a blood pressure of 136/67 mmHg, a heart rate of 72/min, a temperature of 38.6°C (101.4°F), and a respiratory rate of 12/min. Physical examination reveals scleral icterus and tenderness in the right upper quadrant. Serum laboratory results are shown below.

Aspartate aminotransferase  800 U/L  
Alanine aminotransferase 1100 U/L 
HepA IgM negative 
HBsAb positive 
HBcAb  negative 
HBsAg negative  

The most likely diagnosis is

1. acetaminophen overdose
2. alcoholic hepatitis
3. autoimmune hepatitis
4. hepatocellular carcinoma
5. viral hepatitis

The correct answer is: C
This patient presents with non-specific complaints of nausea and vomiting. Jaundice and elevated transaminases, as well as the rapid onset of the symptoms, suggest acute fulminant hepatitis. The main differential for acute fulminant hepatitis includes viral hepatitis, acetaminophen intoxication, Budd-Chiari syndrome, and autoimmune hepatitis.  Fulminant hepatitis is associated with other autoimmune diseases such as Hashimoto's thyroiditis and diabetes mellitus type I.

Answer A: While the patient does have a history of acetaminophen use, her acetaminophen dose is within normal limits which makes the diagnosis of acetaminophen toxicity less likely.


Answer B: Alcoholic hepatitis generally has a 2:1 aspartate aminotransferase (AST): alanine aminotransferase (ALT) ratio. This patient reports alcohol use but this laboratory result makes the diagnosis less likely.


Answer D: Hepatocellular carcinoma frequently arises in the background of cirrhosis and does not cause fulminant hepatitis with extremely elevated transaminase levels.


Answer E: Negative HepA IgM rules out viral hepatitis A infection. Positive HBsAb in combination with negative HBsAg and negative HBcAb indicate immunity from prior hepatitis B vaccination. In addition, she denies risk factors for viral hepatitis.




Bottom Line: Acute fulminant hepatitis is suggested by the acuity of onset and very elevated transaminases.

For more information, see:
"Autoimmune Hepatitis" on Medscape.

Link: "Shoulder Subacromial Injections" on Medscape


Patient Presentation: Musculoskeletal Difficulties And Muscular Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Management
Body System: Musculoskeletal System
Discipline: Family Medicine
A 56-year-old male presents to the sports medicine clinic with persistent left shoulder soreness. He was recently evaluated by his primary care physician and was diagnosed with subacromial impingement and rotator cuff tendinopathy. The patient was prescribed a non-steroidal anti-inflammatory for two weeks and he also has been attending physical therapy. These modalities have offered marginal improvement of his symptoms. Examination reveals discomfort with both the Neer's test and Hawkin's test. X-ray imaging of the shoulder is significant for calcifications at the insertion of the supraspinatus tendon at the greater tuberosity of the humerus. The decision is made to complete a subacromial injection into the patient's left shoulder. The correct approach is

1. insert the needle inferior to the posterolateral edge of the acromion and direct it medially with approximately 30° of cephalic angulation
2. insert the needle perpendicular to the junction of the sternum and medial clavicle
3. insert the needle into the bicipital groove, angled cephalically with the needle introduced almost parallel to the tendon sheath
4. insert the needle superior to the anterolateral edge of the acromion, angled perpendicularly to the junction of the acromion and distal clavicle
5. insert the slightly angled needle approximately 1 cm distal to the lateral epicondyle into the common extensor tendon

The correct answer is: A
The posterior approach is favored when injecting into the subacromial space. Using the acromion as a superficial landmark, the posterior and lateral margin should be palpated. Insertion of the needle, after obtaining consent and prepping the area, should occur at the inferior border of the acromion at the above mentioned margin. Angling the needle slightly lateral and in a cephalic direction will allow entry into the subacromial space. In review of the clinical presentation, patient's with subacromial impingement will commonly present with a positive Neer's test and Hawkin's test. Radiographic evidence of calcification in the rotator cuff, particularly the insertion of the supraspinatus on the humerus, supports the diagnosis of rotator cuff tendinopathy.

Answer B: This would be the correct technique for the sternoclavicular joint.


Answer C: This would be the correct technique for the biceps tendon.


Answer D: This would be the correct technique for the acromioclavicular joint.


Answer E: This would be the correct technique for lateral epicondylitis.




Bottom Line: Knowledge of the surface anatomy is crucial when completing a joint or bursa aspiration/injection.

"Shoulder Subacromial Injections" on Medscape

insert Link
Patient Presentation: Musculoskeletal Difficulties And Muscular Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Management
Body System: Musculoskeletal System
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 30-year-old pregnant female presents for evaluation of a tingling sensation and weakness involving both hands. She is currently in her thirty-second week of gestation and recalls these symptoms started about 2 weeks ago. She notes her symptoms are worse when she wakes up in the middle of the night. Physical exam reveals weakness of bilateral thenar muscles. The diagnostic test seen in the attached video reproduces her symptoms within less than one minute.

What is the most appropriate treatment at this time?

1. Observation
2. Corticosteroid injections
3. Non-steroidal anti-inflammatory drugs
4. Wrist splints
5. Surgery

The correct answer is: D
Carpal tunnel syndrome (CTS) results when the median nerve gets compressed in a confined space in the wrist known as the carpal tunnel. Pregnancy, obesity, wrist joint overuse, and rheumatoid arthritis are all risk factors for CTS. Median nerve compression results in paresthesias and weakness along the nerve’s distribution. The median nerve innervates the thenar muscles among other muscles in the hand and forearm. This explains the thenar weakness many patients suffer. The median nerve provides sensory innervation to the palmar thumb, index, middle, and half the ring finger along with their respective nail beds. The reverse Phalen test is seen in the attached video. This test requires the patient to hyperextend both wrists and hold for up to one minute. If this maneuver reproduces symptoms within 1 minute, it is considered positive and concerning for CTS. Initial treatment involves use of wrist splints, particularly at night when symptoms are often worse. Wrist splints are the preferred treatment method during pregnancy. Systemic treatments are not desirable during pregnancy given the CTS often resolves once
the patient is no longer pregnant.

Answer A: Offering this symptomatic patient, no treatment with observation only is not appropriate. There are non-invasive treatment options, such as wrist splints, that are amenable and safe to help relieve CTS during pregnancy.


Answer B: Corticosteroid injections are often used to temporarily relieve pain and inflammation associated with CTS. While this may decrease swelling in the carpal tunnel, corticosteroid injections are pregnancy category C and thus should not be used in this pregnant patient with safe alternative treatment options.


Answer C: Non-steroidal anti-inflammatory drugs (NSAIDs) are often used to temporarily relieve pain and inflammation associated with CTS. While this may decrease swelling in the carpal tunnel, NSAIDs overall are pregnancy category B and thus should not be used in this pregnant patient with non-systemic, safe treatment alternatives.


Answer E: Surgical release of the transverse carpal ligament may be utilized for patients with severe CTS that persist despite trying various non-surgical treatments. While this offers potentially definitive treatment by ligating the ligament putting pressure on the median nerve, it may result in further nerve damage, infection, and scarring. Surgery is not appropriate in this patient given she is pregnant at this time. During pregnancy women retain water which results in swelling. This swelling often resolves during the post-partum period and thus surgery may never be required by this patient.




Bottom Line: A positive reverse Phalen's test is concerning for carpal tunnel syndrome which is best initially treated non-invasively and thereafter with surgery if the symptoms persist.

For more information, see:
Common adult orthopedic conditions in the Musculoskeletal disorders section of Step up to USMLE Step 2. Page 196.

Patient Presentation: Disease Detection & Monitoring
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Diagnostic Technologies
Body System: Hematology/Oncology
Discipline: Obstetrics/Gynecology
A 23-year-old female presents to the office for her first prenatal visit. Past medical history reveals intravenous drug abuse and multiple sexual partners over the past year. She states she occasionally used condoms during intercourse and had a sexually transmitted infection once that was treated. An HIV screening test is recommended.
Question 1 of 2 in this set
The most appropriate initial HIV screening test is

1. enzyme linked immunoabsorbent assay
2. Northern blot
3. polymerase chain reaction
4. RNA viral load testing
5. Western blot

The correct answer is: A
The enzyme-linked immunoabsorbent assay (ELISA) is the most commonly used testing method for the initial detection of HIV. This assay has high sensitivity, making it ideal for screening by making false negatives extremely unlikely, but false positives a possibility. If a patient has a positive ELISA test, a confirmatory Western blot is warranted.

Answer B: The Northern blot is a technique used in molecular biology to study RNA gene expression and has a minimal role in the detection of HIV.


Answers C & D: Currently, PCR has no specified role in the screening of HIV, but can be used in conjunction with RNA viral load testing to monitor the therapeutic effectiveness of HIV treatment modalities in individual patients.


Answer E: Following a positive ELISA, Western blot is used as a confirmatory test.




Bottom Line: The most effective screening and confirmatory tests for the diagnosis of HIV are ELISA and Western blot, respectively.

COMBANK Insight : Acute symptoms of HIV infection can manifest as a flulike illness, consisting of fever, malaise, and a generalized rash. The asymptomatic phase is generally benign. Generalized lymphadenopathy is common and may be a presenting symptom.

For more information, see:
"HIV Disease" on Medscape.

Link: "HIV Disease" on Medscape.


Patient Presentation: Disease Detection & Monitoring
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Health Care Delivery Issues
Body System: Asymptomatic & General Symptoms
Discipline: Obstetrics/Gynecology
A 23-year-old female presents to the office for her first prenatal visit. Past medical history reveals intravenous drug abuse and multiple sexual partners over the past year. She states she occasionally used condoms during intercourse and had a sexually transmitted infection once that was treated. An HIV screening test is recommended.
Question 2 of 2 in this set
Despite explaining the indication, risks and benefits of HIV testing, the patient refuses her consent to the test. The most appropriate course of action at this time is

1. document her decision and abstain from performing the test without her consent
2. explain to her that if she does not consent to testing you will be unable to continue to treat her during her pregnancy
3. explain to her that she is obligated by law to have the testing done since she is pregnant
4. offer the patient free care if she agrees to have the testing performed
5. perform the test on a previously drawn blood sample without her knowing

The correct answer is: A
Despite being pregnant, the patient has a right to refuse HIV testing. Written consent must be specifically obtained for HIV testing to be conducted. After a thorough discussion takes place between the physician and patient regarding the importance of HIV screening, the patient may still wish to not have the test, as in the above scenario. At this point, it is necessary to document the decision and abstain from performing testing without her consent.

Answer B: Refusing to care for the patient if she does not have the test done is unethical. Patients have the right to refuse testing and it is the physician's duty to provide the best care for the patient within the patient's wishes.


Answer C: The patient is not obligated by law to have the test done, despite being pregnant.


Answer D: Offering the patient free care if she agrees to have the test performed is an exchange of services and is unethical practice.


Answer E: Performing the test on a previously drawn blood sample would violate the patient’s wishes.




Bottom Line: A pregnant woman has the same rights as a non-pregnant woman and can refuse care (which includes HIV testing and cesarean section) even if it can benefit or be potentially life-saving to the fetus.

COMBANK Insight : In all states, using drugs during pregnancy is not a crime. Physicians may not subject a woman to nonconsensual drug testing for the purpose of such criminal prosecution.

"HIV Disease" on Medscape.

17 of 200

Link: Dupuytren Contracture


Patient Presentation: Skin, Nail & Hair Disorders
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Musculoskeletal System
Discipline: Surgery
A 55-year-old male presents to the office with the complaint of a nodule in his right palm. Medical history reveals diabetes type two that is poorly controlled with insulin. He states that when he flexes his index finger it feels like it is catching on something. Occasionally his finger will even become stuck in a flexed position. He does not complain of pain, rather an uncomfortable feeling. Physical examination of the patient’s right hand shows a 1cm firm nodule at the MCP joint of the index finger. The most likely diagnosis is

1. ganglion cyst
2. wart
3. lipoma
4. trigger finger
5. Dupuytren’s contracture

The correct answer is: D
The most likely diagnosis in this patient is trigger finger. Trigger finger is a type of stenosing tenosynovitis. It commonly affects patients over the age of 45 and is more common in diabetics. It presents with a nodule usually located at the MCP joint of either the index or ring fingers. Patients complain of the finger “catching” and becoming locked in a flexed position. It is treated with conservative treatments such as stretching, splinting and ice followed by corticosteroid injections and finally surgery. A mismatch between the flexor tendon and the proximal pulley mechanism occurs in most cases of TF. Normally, the tendons of the finger flexors glide back and forth under a restraining pulley. Thickening of the flexor tendon sheath restricts the normal gliding mechanism. A nodule may develop on the tendon, causing the tendon to get stuck at the proximal edge of the A1 pulley when the patient is attempting to extend the digit, thereby causing difficulty. When more forceful attempts are made to extend the digit, by using increased force from the finger extensors or by applying an external force (for example, by exerting force on the finger with the other hand), the digit classically snaps open with significant pain at the distal palm and into the proximal aspect of the affected digit. Less commonly, the nodule is restricted distal to the A1 pulley, resulting in difficulty flexing the digit.

Answer A: A ganglion cyst is an out pocketing of synovial tissue. It can occur anywhere in the wrist and hand but is most common on the dorsal aspect of the wrist. It presents as a painless mass. Although ganglion cysts can be unilobulated, they are most often multilobulated, with septa made from connective tissue separating the lobes or cavities. Because there is no epithelial lining of the cyst wall, a ganglion cyst is not a true cyst and, because of this histologic observation, the theories of synovial herniation or synovial tumor formation are not supported and may be disputed. Hyaluronic acid predominates the mucopolysaccharides that make up the fluid within the cyst’s cavity, whereas collagen fibers and fibrocytes make up the wall lining. The development of these cysts is histologically observable beginning with swollen collagen fibers and fibrocytes, followed by a degeneration and liquefaction of these elements, a termination of degeneration, and, lastly, a proliferation of the connective tissue, resulting in a border that is dense in texture.



Answer B: Warts do not cause catching or locking of the digits because they are superficial to the flexor tendons.


Answer C: A lipoma is a benign fatty tumor. They are uncommon in the hand and would not cause catching or locking of the digits because they are superficial to the flexor tendons.


Answer E: Dupuytren’s contracture is also a common cause of locking of the digits, especially in diabetic patients. Dupuytren's disease is caused by a thickening of the palmar fascia and most commonly affects the fourth and fifth digits. Although the underlying etiology for the development of Dupuytren disease is uncertain, the basic pathophysiology involves fibroblast proliferation, and collagen deposition leading to contractures of the palmar fascia. Investigators have proposed several hypotheses for the pathogenesis of Dupuytren disease. Some theories about the etiology of the disease is that an individual with a genetic predisposition to develop Dupuytren disease experiences a second inciting event (ie, smoking, diabetes, trauma, alcoholism), resulting in microvascular ischemia. The localized ischemia causes two events to take place in the palmar fascia: (1) the conversion of adenosine triphosphate to hypoxanthine and (2) the conversion of xanthine dehydrogenase to xanthine oxidase. Xanthine oxidase acts as a catalyst for the oxidation of hypoxanthine to xanthine and uric acid; this conversion results in the production of free radicals. Free radicals result in fibroblast proliferation and the production of numerous cytokines. Interleukin 1 (IL-1) is the most abundant cytokine in Dupuytren disease and, via its receptor, upregulates the production of transforming growth factor beta (TGF-beta), fibroblast growth factor, epidermal-derived growth factor, and platelet-derived growth factor. This milieu of cytokines and growth factors results in the proliferation of fibroblasts and their differentiation into myofibroblasts (the primary cell type in Dupuytren disease). Excess type III collagen production occurs from an increased density of fibroblasts secondary to enhanced stimulation and diminished apoptosis, as well as an imbalance between the collagenases and their endogenous inhibitors. Dupuytren fascia exhibits an increased ratio of tissue inhibitors of metalloproteinases (TIMP) to matrix metalloproteinases (MMP).




Bottom Line: Trigger finger presents as a painless nodule near the MCP joint and can cause locking or catching of the digits. Conservative treatments are attempted before corticosteroid injections or surgery.

For more information, see:
Dupuytren Contracture
Trigger Finger
Ganglion Cyst

Link: Medscape "Bleomycin"


Patient Presentation: Respiratory Difficulties
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Management
Body System: Respiratory System
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 76-year-old male presents to the emergency department with the complaint of shortness of breath. Past medical history reveals chemotherapy treatment without radiation for treatment of squamous cell carcinoma. A chest radiograph is obtained and reveals bilateral reticular interstitial infiltrates as shown in the exhibit. The most likely pharmacological agent causing the patients symptoms is

1. azathioprine
2. bleomycin
3. busulfan
4. doxorubicin
5. fluorouracil

The correct answer is: B
The most likely diagnosis is pulmonary fibrosis, likely caused by bleomycin chemotherapy. Pulmonary fibrosis, which is classified as a restrictive lung disease, commonly presents with an insidious onset of dyspnea on exertion. Classic chest radiograph findings include the presence of bilateral reticular interstitial infiltrates. The chemotherapeutics noted to have an association with pulmonary fibrosis include bleomycin, busulfan, methotrexate, and cyclophosphamide. Other drugs, which can cause pulmonary fibrosis, include amiodarone, propranolol, nitrofurantoin, and sulfasalazine.

Answer A: Azathioprine is a cytotoxic medication that can be used as a treatment for pulmonary fibrosis.


Answer C: Busulfan also causes pulmonary toxicity but is used to treat the symptoms of chronic myelogenous leukemia.


Answer D: Doxorubicin is not associated with pulmonary fibrosis. It can cause cardiac toxicity.


Answer E: Side effects of fluorouracil (5-FU) include myelosuppression, mucositis, dermatitis, diarrhea, and cardiac toxicity.




Bottom Line: Bleomycin has a strong association with pulmonary fibrosis.

COMBANK Insight : It is necessary to know the classic side effects associated with common chemotherapeutic medications.

Medscape "Bleomycin"

"CDC: Vaccine and Immunization schedules."


Patient Presentation: Disease Detection & Monitoring
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Health Promotion & Disease Prevention
Body System: Asymptomatic & General Symptoms
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 32-year-old female with a past medical history significant for HIV and asthma presents to the office for a routine physical and prescription renewal. Vitals reveal a blood pressure of 124/78 mmHg, a heart rate of 62/min, and a respiratory rate of 12/min. Physical examination reveals a healthy appearing female in no acute distress. Laboratory analysis reveals a CD4+ count of 195. The most appropriate vaccination is

1. hepatitis A and B
2. measles, mumps, rubella
3. meningococcal
4. pneumococcal
5. varicella

The correct answer is: D
Live vaccines are contraindicated for immunocompromised patients and not recommended for pregnant patients. Pneumococcus is not live attenuated and is recommended in HIV/AIDS patients.  The pneumococcus vaccine is a conjugate (polysaccharide components) vaccine. The MMR vaccine may be used if CD4 > 200, but the general recommendation is to avoid all live vaccines. Killed vaccines include influenza, cholera, plague, polio, hepatitis A, and rabies. Toxoid vaccines include tetanus and diphtheria. Subunit vaccines include hepatitis B (surface proteins).

Answer A: Hepatitis A and B vaccines are routinely indicated in children. In adults, they are both indicated if there is a history of chronic liver disease, in men who have sex with men or multiple sexual partners, in household contacts with hepatitis A or B, and injection drug users. Hepatitis A vaccine is also indicated in travelers to countries with high endemicity. Hepatitis B vaccine is also indicated in those with end-stage renal disease on dialysis and in health care workers.


Answer B: The MMR vaccine is a live, attenuated vaccine. It may be used if CD4 > 200, however the general recommendation is to avoid live vaccines in immunocompromised patients. Also, the MMR vaccine series is generally completed in childhood.


Answer C: The meningococcal vaccine is a quadrivalent vaccine targeting serogroups A, C, W-135 and Y. It is routinely indicated at the age 11 visit. It is also indicated in adults with asplenia, terminal complement deficiency, military recruits, residents of college dormitories, and travelers to Mecca or Medina in Saudi Arabia.


Answer E: The varicella vaccine is another live, attenuated vaccine. It is routinely indicated in all children. There is also a higher-dose version indicated in all patients above age 60 to prevent herpes zoster.




Bottom Line: Pneumococcal vaccine is recommended for AIDS patients. Live attenuated vaccinations are contraindicated in immunocompromised compromised individuals and relatively contraindicated in pregnancy.

COMBANK Insight : Contraindications to vaccination are a more common topic than vaccination schedules. Mild illness and/or low-grade fever, current antibiotic therapy, and prematurity are not contraindications to vaccination. Prophylaxis in AIDS patients is another common topic that is tested. Pneumocystis pneumonia prophylaxis is TMP-SMX (or dapsone in sulfa allergy) starting at CD4 < 200. Toxoplasmosis prophylaxis is TMP-SMX (or dapsone in sulfa allergy) starting at CD4 < 100. Mycobacterium avium complex prophylaxis is azithromycin (or clarithromycin) starting at CD4 < 100 (although infection is more common with CD4 < 50). Additionally, if a PPD skin test is > 5mm induration, isoniazid is started as prophylaxis against tuberculosis.

For more information, see:

"CDC: Vaccine and Immunization schedules."

"Immunizations of High-Risk Populations" on Medscape

Link: Bacterial Pneumonia


Patient Presentation: Respiratory Difficulties
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Respiratory System
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 45-year-old male presents to the office with complaints of a persistent cough of two weeks' duration, chills, night sweats, and chest pain upon deep respiration. He says he has been "coughing up stuff" for the past week.  History reveals the patient does not smoke, but drinks 5-6 beers a night and more on the weekends, and has been married for 15 years.  He says he is having marriage problems with his wife because she thinks he drinks too much.  Physical examination reveals tactile fremitus and the upper lung sounds heard in the exhibit. 
Question 1 of 2 in this set
The most likely diagnosis is

1. left-sided heart failure
2. pneumonia
3. pneumothorax
4. lung cancer
5. pleural effusion

The correct answer is: B
This patient has symptoms consistent with pneumonia. Patients with pneumonia present with tachypnea, pleuritic chest pain, decreased breath sounds, rales, wheezing, and dullness to percussion. Labs show increased white blood cell count with left shift and a chest x-ray will commonly show lobar consolidation.

Answer A: Patients with left-sided heart failure commonly present with signs of pulmonary edema. These signs and symptoms include dyspnea, orthopnea, tachycardia, paroxysmal nocturnal dyspnea, and hypertension.


Answer C: Patients with a pneumothorax present with unilateral chest pain, decreased chest wall movement, decreased tactile fremitus, and increased resonance to percussion.


Answer D: Lung cancer is not the most likely diagnosis as smoking is the greatest risk factor for lung cancer and patients often present with dyspnea, hemoptysis, wheezing, hoarseness, and anorexia with resultant unintentional weight loss.


Answer E: While pleural effusions often presents with pleuritic chest pain as described in this case, these patients would also have diminished breath sounds on the affected side and a pleural friction rub on auscultation.




Bottom Line: Patients with pneumonia present with a history of chills, night sweats, tachypnea, pleuritic chest pain, decreased breath sounds, rales, wheezing, and dullness to percussion. They commonly have increased white blood cell counts with left shift.

COMBANK Insight : Audio/video questions are now present on Level 2. Audio exhibits will generally have heart, lung, or bowel sounds at various clickable listening posts. Usually one of the four available listening posts will have an abnormal sound. Video questions will display positive physical exam techniques such as the Rhomberg sign or Babinski sign.

Link: Klebsiella Infections on Medscape


Patient Presentation: Respiratory Difficulties
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Scientific Understanding of Health & Disease Mechanisms
Body System: Respiratory System
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 45-year-old male presents to the office with complaints of a persistent cough of two weeks' duration, chills, night sweats, and chest pain upon deep respiration. He says he has been "coughing up stuff" for the past week.  History reveals the patient does not smoke, but drinks 5-6 beers a night and more on the weekends, and has been married for 15 years.  He says he is having marriage problems with his wife because she thinks he drinks too much.  Physical examination reveals tactile fremitus and the upper lung sounds heard in the exhibit. 
Question 2 of 2 in this set
The most likely causative organism is

1. Klebsiella pneumoniae
2. Haemophilus influenzae
3. group B Streptococcus
4. Legionella pneumophila
5. Mycoplasma pneumoniae

The correct answer is: A
Klebsiella pneumoniae is usually an aspiration pneumonia commonly found in alcoholic and elderly patients. Patients with K. pneumoniae pneumonia commonly present with “currant-jelly sputum”, which is a dark-red, thick mucous. This community-acquired infection predominately occurs in the upper lung fields. K. pneumoniae is an encapsulated organism and grows very thick in culture because of the capsule. It is a gram-negative rod that will ferment lactose, but is indole-negative which differentiates K. pneumoniae from Escherichia coli. While K. pneumoniae is a common cause of pneumonia in alcoholic and elderly patients, Streptococcus pneumoniae is still the primary cause of pneumonia in this patient population. In addition to pneumonia, K. pneumoniae can also occasionally cause urinary tract infections.

Answer B: Haemophilus influenzae is commonly found in pneumonia patients with COPD, and due to the vaccine, will usually only present in unvaccinated patients. H. influenzae is a Gram-negative rod that will not grow on blood agar, but will grow on chocolate agar, due to the requirement for heme and NAD which are present in chocolate agar.


Answer C: Streptococcus agalactiae, Group B strep, is a common cause of typical pneumonia, and meningitis, in neonates. This organism can be found as a commensal of the vaginal tract and can infect the newborn during birth. S. agalactiae is a Gram-positive coccus that is beta-hemolytic on blood agar and resistant to bacitracin.


Answer D: Legionella pneumophila will usually present as an atypical pneumonia with a dry cough and difficulty breathing. Patients are usually immunocompromised, and include smokers, alcoholics, and the elderly. L. pneumophila is a Gram-negative rod that is transmitted via contaminated water droplets, and can be found in air-conditioners and grocery store food sprinklers. Diagnosis of L. pneumophila is by a urine antigen test followed by confirmation of growth on buffered charcoal yeast extract agar.


Answer E: Mycoplasma pneumoniae is commonly found in young adult patients and it is the most common cause of bacterial atypical pneumonia. Symptoms include a dry-hacking cough, interstitial infiltrates into the lungs, and possible pharyngitis. These symptoms are similar to Chlamydophila pneumoniae, and these two pathogens can be differentiated by the fact that M. pneumoniae will present with a positive cold agglutinin test.




Bottom Line: Klebsiella pneumoniae is a common cause of pneumonia in alcoholic or elderly patients and is associated with currant-jelly sputum.

For more information, see:
Klebsiella Infections on Medscape


Last Updated: 12/11/2016

insert Link
Patient Presentation: Disease Detection & Monitoring
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Diagnostic Technologies
Body System: Hepatobiliary System
Discipline: Internal Medicine

A 22-year-old female presents to the office for a follow-up after her recent diagnosis of Wilson’s disease.  She has no complaints and has no symptoms at this time. She was diagnosed after being screened when her younger brother was diagnosed. She has come to you to discuss treatment options. She has no significant past medical history and her only medication is oral contraception. Physical examination reveals no Kayser-Fleischer rings or hepatomegaly and neurologic and psychiatric exams are unremarkable.


Question 1 of 3 in this set
Which of the following sets of laboratory findings is most likely?

1. decreased ceruloplasmin and decreased urinary copper
2. decreased ceruloplasmin and increased urinary copper
3. increased ceruloplasmin and decreased urinary copper
4. increased ceruloplasmin and increased urinary copper
5. decreased ceruloplasmin and decreased urinary copper

The correct answer is: B
Wilson’s disease is an autosomal recessive disorder of copper excretion by the liver, leading to less copper in the circulation. Copper in the circulation binds to ceruloplasmin, so in Wilson’s disease patients will have decreased amounts of ceruloplasmin. Also, they will excrete more copper in the urine, as hepatic excretion is impaired.

Answer A: Urinary copper is increased in Wilson’s disease.


Answer C: Ceruloplasmin is decreased and urinary copper is increased in Wilson’s disease.


Answer D: Ceruloplasmin is decreased in Wilson’s disease.




Bottom Line: Ceruloplasmin is decreased and urinary copper is increased in Wilson’s disease.

Patient Presentation: Disease Detection & Monitoring
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Management
Body System: Hepatobiliary System
Discipline: Internal Medicine

A 22-year-old female presents to the office for a follow-up after her recent diagnosis of Wilson’s disease.  She has no complaints and has no symptoms at this time. She was diagnosed after being screened when her younger brother was diagnosed. She has come to you to discuss treatment options. She has no significant past medical history and her only medication is oral contraception. Physical examination reveals no Kayser-Fleischer rings or hepatomegaly and neurologic and psychiatric exams are unremarkable.


Question 2 of 3 in this set
The most appropriate treatment is

1. counseling on staying hydrated
2. dialysis
3. penicillamine
4. reassurance
5. ursodiol

The correct answer is: C
Wilson’s disease is treated with dietary changes and penicillamine and/or trientene, which work as copper chelators. Additionally, patients with Wilson’s should be given supplemental zinc because it decreases absorption of copper from the GI tract.

Answer A: Maintaining hydration helps prevent jaundice in patients with Gilbert’s syndrome.


Answer B: The main problem in Wilson’s disease is with copper excretion by the liver, not the kidneys, thus dialysis is not required unless copper accumulation in the kidneys leads to renal failure


Answer D: Reassurance is not appropriate in Wilson’s disease as copper accumulates in tissues, even when the patient is asymptomatic. Patients should be treated to prevent complications such as liver failure and psychiatric disturbances. Reassurance is used in Gilbert’s syndrome, as it is almost always a benign condition.


Answer E: Ursodiol is used to treat hyperbilirubinemia in primary biliary cirrhosis and primary sclerosing cholangitis.




Bottom Line: Copper chelators, such as penicillamine and trientene, are used to treat Wilson’s disease.

Patient Presentation: Disease Detection & Monitoring
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Health Promotion & Disease Prevention
Body System: Hepatobiliary System
Discipline: Internal Medicine

A 22-year-old female presents to the office for a follow-up after her recent diagnosis of Wilson’s disease.  She has no complaints and has no symptoms at this time. She was diagnosed after being screened when her younger brother was diagnosed. She has come to you to discuss treatment options. She has no significant past medical history and her only medication is oral contraception. Physical examination reveals no Kayser-Fleischer rings or hepatomegaly and neurologic and psychiatric exams are unremarkable.


Question 3 of 3 in this set
The most appropriate dietary advice to provide the patient is

1. avoid broad beans
2. avoid shell fish, cocoa, nuts and mushrooms
3. eat more liver and leafy vegetables
4. supplement vitamins A, D, E and K
5. supplement vitamin B1

The correct answer is: B
Patients with Wilson’s disease should be counseled to avoid foods high in copper. These include shellfish, chocolate, nuts, mushrooms, and organ meats.

Answer A: Avoidance of broad beans, like fava beans, is important for patients with glucose-6-phosphate dehydrogenase deficiency, which causes hemolytic anemia when patients take in oxidizing agents. Other things that should be avoided are drugs like sulfonamides, primaquine and tuberculosis medications.


Answer C: Liver and leafy vegetables are high in vitamin A. Vitamin A supplementation may be considered in patients as a preventative measure against retinal disorders as well as in patients with malabsorption syndromes. Vitamin A is also used as a treatment for measles and certain skin conditions (like acne).


Answer D: Supplementation of the fat-soluble vitamins (A, D, E and K) is important in cystic fibrosis patients and other patients with pancreatic insufficiency.


Answer E: Supplementing vitamin B1 (thiamine) is done in alcoholics before giving glucose to prevent Wernicke-Korsakoff syndrome which causes confusion, ataxia, and personality changes.




Bottom Line: Patients with Wilson’s disease should follow a low copper diet.

Link: Vulvar Cancer on UpToDate


Patient Presentation: Skin, Nail & Hair Disorders
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Genitourinary System
Discipline: Obstetrics/Gynecology
A 64-year-old female presents to the office with the complaint of a pruritic lesion on her left labia.  History reveals she is a smoker with a 40-pack year tobacco history.  Her past medical history is significant for HIV.  Upon inspection, the lesion is approximately 2 cm in diameter and warty in appearance. Inguinal lymph nodes are palpable on the left. The most likely diagnosis is

1. Bartholin's gland adenocarcinoma
2. melanoma
3. Paget disease
4. sarcoma
5. squamous cell carcinoma

The correct answer is: E
Vulvar cancer presents more commonly in post-menopausal women.  There are several histological types, including squamous cell carcinoma, melanoma, basal cell carcinoma, sarcoma, Paget disease, and Bartholin gland adenocarcinoma. Squamous cell carcinoma is the most common type, occurring in approximately 95% of cases.  Risks include cigarette smoking, HPV, vulvar intraepithelial neoplasm, cervical intraepithelial neoplasm, immunodeficiency, previous history of cervical cancer, and lichen sclerosus.  Patients usually present with a mass that causes pain or pruritus.  Any suspicious lesion should be biopsied. Early disease is treated with surgery.  Late disease is treated with chemo/radiation.

Answer A: Bartholin's gland adenocarcinoma is rare. The tumors are solid with deep infiltration. Any postmenopausal woman with inflammation of the Bartholin gland should be biopsied.


Answer B: Melanoma of the vulva is second most common. It usually presents as a pigmented lesion but amelanotic lesions have also been found.


Answer C: Paget disease of the vulva can also present with pruritis. The lesion appears similar to eczema. It is well demarcated and is usually multifocal. It is similar to Paget disease of the breast.


Answer D: Vulvar sarcomas usually present as a painless, enlarging mass. Histologic types include leiomyosarcoma, liposarcoma, rhabdomyosarcoma, and angiosarcoma. The prognosis is poor.




Bottom Line: Squamous cell carcinoma is the most common form of vulvar cancer.

Link: "Cauda Equina Syndrome and Conus Medullaris Syndrome" on Medscape.


Patient Presentation: Genitourinary Disorders/Issues
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Genitourinary System
Discipline: Emergency Medicine
A 62-year-old female presents to the emergency department with the complaint of urinary incontinence. History reveals she urinated on the floor while hurrying to the toilet and was unable to feel the toilet tissue on the right side of her perineum. The patient notes that she had been having difficulty passing urine for several days prior.  Physical exam reveals flattened lumbar lordosis, bilateral muscle spasms throughout the lumbar spine, bilateral sacral extension, and tenderness to palpation throughout the lumbosacral area.

Question 1 of 2 in this set
The most appropriate diagnosis is

1. disc bulge of lumbar vertebrae
2. urge incontinence
3. lumbar somatic dysfunction
4. conus medullaris syndrome
5. cauda equina syndrome

The correct answer is: E
Cauda equina syndrome (CES) is a rare but extremely important diagnosis to make due to its emergent need for neurosurgical intervention. The cauda equina is a horsetail like collection of terminal lumbar and sacral nerve roots inferior to the end of the spinal cord at L2. CES refers to the neurologic and urogenital symptoms that result from compression of the cauda equina nerve roots. Symptoms include bowel and bladder dysfunction, variable lower extremity motor and sensory nerve impairment, and perineal sensory loss known as “saddle anesthesia.” Bowel and bladder symptoms typically start as urinary retention and constipation followed by loss of sphincter control.

Answer A: A large lumbar disc herniation may result in CES if it is large enough to cause compression of the cauda equina nerve roots. In a disc bulge, however, the annulus fibrosus is intact and disc material is not extruded into the spinal canal making bladder changes and saddle anesthesia very unlikely.


Answer B: Urinary urge incontinence is not uncommon in women over the age of 60. However, it does not cause saddle anesthesia and is not commonly preceded by urinary retention.


Answer C: While this patient certainly does have lumbosacral somatic dysfunction, the more important and urgent diagnosis at this time, given the saddle anesthesia and urinary incontinence, is cauda equina syndrome. Additionally, although this patient may have underlying SD in the lumbar area, much of her dysfunction may stem from the primary diagnosis of Cauda equina syndrome.


Answer D: Conus medullaris syndrome (CMS) can present similarly to CES. The conus medullaris is the enlarged distal termination of the spinal cord superior to the cauda equina. CMS results when the conus medullaris is compressed. However, upper motor neuron signs such as spasticity and hyperactive reflexes are present in addition to lower motor neuron signs such as muscle flaccidity and loss of reflexes. CES presents solely with lower motor neuron signs. Symptoms of CMS tend to progress suddenly and presents bilaterally as opposed to CES which presents slowly and unilaterally.




Bottom Line: Cauda equina syndrome can be distinguished by the following symptoms; isolated LMN signs, perineal paresthesia, and bowel and bladder incontinence.

COMBANK Insight : Any surgical emergency is likely to be tested on your level 2 exam.

For more information, see:
"Cauda Equina Syndrome and Conus Medullaris Syndrome" on Medscape.
