Link: "Cauda Equina Syndrome and Conus Medullaris Syndrome" on Medscape.


Patient Presentation: Genitourinary Disorders/Issues
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Management
Body System: Genitourinary System
Discipline: Emergency Medicine
A 62-year-old female presents to the emergency department with the complaint of urinary incontinence. History reveals she urinated on the floor while hurrying to the toilet and was unable to feel the toilet tissue on the right side of her perineum. The patient notes that she had been having difficulty passing urine for several days prior.  Physical exam reveals flattened lumbar lordosis, bilateral muscle spasms throughout the lumbar spine, bilateral sacral extension, and tenderness to palpation throughout the lumbosacral area.

Question 2 of 2 in this set
The next appropriate step in management is

1. observation with NSAIDs and OMT
2. radiograph of the lumbar spine
3. immediate surgical consultation
4. MRI of the lumbar spine
5. lumbar puncture

The correct answer is: C
Cauda equina syndrome (CES) is an emergent diagnosis requiring immediate surgical consultation. Permanent paralysis and irreversible neurological damage may occur if this diagnosis is not addressed immediately.

Answer A: Conservative management such as observation of the patient with NSAIDs and osteopathic manipulation would be appropriate if the patient presented with low back pain without neurological compromise. This is not the situation in the above case. Immobilization should be a priority until neurosurgical evaluation is complete.


Answer B: Although the surgeon may order films of the lumbar spine, this decision should be made by the consultant. Furthermore, the most likely films would be an MRI rather than a radiograph.


Answer D: Although the surgeon may order imaging of the lumbar spine, the most important step is for the patient to be evaluated by a surgeon.


Answer E: Lumbar puncture would be contraindicated in this patient, it also would not give any diagnostic information.




Bottom Line: Cauda equina syndrome (CES) is an emergent diagnosis requiring immediate surgical consultation.

COMBANK Insight : Although in clinical practice we are often forced to utilize multiple modalities of treatment simultaneously, it is important that you think in sequence and choose your answer accordingly. For example, a patient who is hemodynamically unstable who presents to the Emergency Department with symptoms of cardiac tamponade would likely receive an EKG upon arrival even though pericardiocentesis is necessary. However, when asked the initial therapy for this case you must recognize that in theory (not actuality), pericardiocentesis would be the first-line treatment and would be the correct answer choice in this case.

Link: "Infectious Mononucleosis" on Medscape.


Patient Presentation: Skin, Nail & Hair Disorders
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Integumentary System
Discipline: Pediatrics
A 12-year-old male presents with a new onset diffuse, symmetric, salmon colored rash covering his body. Three days prior, he was seen by his primary care physician for fever, lymphadenopathy and exudative pharyngitis. He was treated with antibiotics but currently reports no improvement of these symptoms. The most likely etiology of the patients' rash is

1. allergic medication reaction
2. atopic dermatitis
3. mononucleosis with medication interaction
4. shingles
5. streptococcal pharyngitis

The correct answer is: C
The most likely cause of this patient's rash is from a medication interaction. This patient was seen by his primary care physician prior for exudative pharyngitis. Both Streptococcus pyogenes and Epstein-Barr virus can cause an exudative pharyngitis. The physician most likely treated the patient with amoxicillin for suspected Streptococcal pharyngitis. The patient’s symptoms are not improving and now have an appearance of a new onset rash. If a patient has mononucleosis and is treated with amoxicillin (a possible treatment for strep pharyngitis) a rash can result. This is seen in about 70% of patients. This is NOT an allergic reaction to medication. The mechanism is still unknown why mononucleosis patients have a rash with amoxicillin, but patients have been shown to be able to tolerate the medication at another time.

Answer A: Although, the mechanism is still unknown why mononucleosis patients have a rash with amoxicillin, this is not considered to be an allergic reaction. Patients have been shown to be able to tolerate the medication at other times.


Answer B: Atopic dermatitis presents most commonly as pruritic, red, scaly, and crusted lesions on the extensor surfaces and scalp.


Answer D: Shingles occurs as a reactivation of Varicella zoster virus. It is seen as vesicular, pruritic lesions that become pustular and later crusted lesions in a dermatomal distribution.


Answer E: Strep pharyngitis does present with exudative pharyngitis, fever, and lymphadenopathy. The patient has failed therapy and is not improving. Penicillins, including amoxicillin, are the drug of choice, but can fail on occasion due to local resistance patterns. It is possible that the patient failed therapy and had an allergic reaction to the medication, but more commonly, this scenario would occur in patients with mononucleosis with amoxicillin treatment reaction.




Bottom Line: Mononucleosis patient's that are incorrectly diagnosed with Streptococcal pharyngitis and treated with amoxicillin can subsequently present with a rash and no resolution of their symptoms.

For more information, see:
Ayala C, Spellberg B. Boards and Wards. 4th Ed. Philadelphia, PA; Lippincott Williams & Wilkins; 2010: 318.
"Infectious Mononucleosis" on Medscape.
"Infectious Mononucleosis in Adults and Adolescents" on UpToDate.

Link: Typhoid Fever" on Medscape.


Patient Presentation: Sensory Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Scientific Understanding of Health & Disease Mechanisms
Body System: Gastrointestinal System
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 40-year-old female presents with a five-day history of right upper quadrant pain that is waxing and waning in nature. She has experienced associated fevers, diarrhea, and a rash on the trunk. History reveals recent travel to Bangladesh two weeks prior to the onset of symptoms where she and her husband had been for a medical mission. Her husband also had three episodes of diarrhea but has since resolved spontaneously. Physical examination is significant for a fever of 102.0°F and blanching salmon-colored lesions on her back and chest. The most likely diagnosis is

1. Clonorchis sinensis
2. Entamoeba histolytica
3. Hepatitis A
4. Salmonella typhi
5. Shigella dysenteriae

The correct answer is: D
The patient is most likely suffering for an infection caused by Salmonella typhi. It is most common in the developing world and in areas of poor sanitation. It is transmitted by the fecal-oral route. Many of those infected have few, if any symptoms. However, some patients can develop a more severe illness characterized by spiking fevers, salmon-colored papules on the trunk, abdominal pain, and diarrhea. If untreated, patients may develop constipation due to inflammation of the Peyer’s patches, bradycardia, splenomegaly and changes in mental status.

Answer A: Clonorchis sinensis, also known as a Chinese liver fluke, is an infection found in Asia. It is acquired by ingesting infected fish. The flukes invade the biliary tree causing abdominal pain, jaundice and diarrhea. It has a long incubation period and can be present for years with no symptoms. Of note, it predisposes patients to cancers of the biliary tree.


Answer B: Entamoeba histolytica infection, also known as amebiasis, is also transmitted via the fecal-oral route and more common in areas with poor sanitation. It can cause cysts in the liver leading to right upper quadrant pain and diarrhea that has a more gradual onset than that caused by Salmonella typhi.


Answer C: Hepatitis A infection is also transmitted via the fecal-oral route and can present with right upper quadrant pain and diarrhea. Like Salmonella typhi it also is more common in the developing world. It presents with jaundice, and not salmon-colored papules.


Answer E: Shigella is another diarrheal illness that can cause abdominal pain and fevers. The incubation period for shigella is shorter than that for salmonella (1 to 2 days versus 1 to 2 weeks).




Bottom Line: Salmonella typhi infection presents with diarrhea, spiking fevers and salmon-colored papules on the trunk. It may lead to bradycardia, splenomegaly and changes in mental status.

For more information, see:
Typhoid Fever" on Medscape.

Patient Presentation: Fatigue & Weakness
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Scientific Understanding of Health & Disease Mechanisms
Body System: Head & Neck
Discipline: Osteopathic Principle and Practice
A 19-year-old female presents to the office with the complaint of fatigue. History reveals the patient was hit in the forehead with a softball 3 weeks ago and she has been fatigued and had difficulty concentrating in school. Osteopathic structural exam reveals a preference for external rotation of the paired cranial bones with flexion of the midline bones. 
Question 1 of 2 in this set
During this phase of craniosacral motion, the dura mater is pulled cephalad causing the sacrum to be pulled into

1. Counternutation
2. Nutation
3. Sacral flexion
4. Unilateral sacral extension
5. Unilateral sacral flexion

The correct answer is: A
The articular mobility of the cranium has two major phases which are flexion and extension. The primary motion occurs at the articulation between the sphenoid bone and the occipital bone, termed the sphenobasilar synchondrosis (SBS). When the flexion phase occurs, the SBS moves cephalad, causing the midline bones to fluidly move into flexion. The midline bones of the cranium consist of the sphenoid, occiput, ethmoid, and vomer bones. The paired bones of the cranium will move through an external rotation. The paired bones of the cranium are the frontal, temporal, and parietal bones. As the SBS moves cephalad, the dura mater which is attached to the foramen magnum, C2, C3, and S2 moves cephalad also. The attachment of the dura mater on the posterior superior portion of the S2 segment causes the sacrum to be pulled into sacral extension, also referred to as craniosacral counternutation. The terms nutation and counternutation were created in order to decrease confusion between the terms flexion and extension. During the craniosacral flexion phase, the sacral base moves posteriorly into sacral extension, also referred to as counternutation. During the craniosacral extension phase, the sacral base moves anteriorly into sacral flexion, also referred to as nutation.

Answer B: Nutation of the sacrum occurs during the craniosacral phase of extension. During craniosacral extension, the SBS moves caudad causing the midline bones to move into extension and the paired cranial bones to move into an internal rotation. Caudad motion of the SBS causes the dura mater to become more lax and the sacral base to move anteriorly into sacral flexion, also referred to as nutation.


Answer C: Sacral flexion is referred to as nutation when referring to a craniosacral motion. Nutation of the sacrum occurs during the craniosacral phase of extension, not flexion.


Answers D & E: Unilateral sacral extension and flexion are not physiological motions of the sacrum. Rather, it is a pathological strain pattern that can occur at the sacrum when there is a somatic dysfunction of the craniosacral motion.




Bottom Line: During the craniosacral flexion phase, the midline cranial bones move into flexion while the paired cranial bones move into an external rotation. A pull of the dura mater cephalad causes the sacrum to move into counternutation, which is when the sacral base moves posteriorly into sacral extension.

insert Link
Patient Presentation: Fatigue & Weakness
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Scientific Understanding of Health & Disease Mechanisms
Body System: Head & Neck
Discipline: Osteopathic Principle and Practice
A 19-year-old female presents to the office with the complaint of fatigue. History reveals the patient was hit in the forehead with a softball 3 weeks ago and she has been fatigued and had difficulty concentrating in school. Osteopathic structural exam reveals a preference for external rotation of the paired cranial bones with flexion of the midline bones. 
Question 2 of 2 in this set
Which of the following is true regarding this phase of craniosacral motion?

1. The AP diameter decreases and the transverse diameter decreases
2. The AP diameter decreases and the transverse diameter increases
3. The AP diameter decreases and the transverse diameter remains the same
4. The AP diameter increases and the transverse diameter decreases
5. The AP diameter increases and the transverse diameter increases

The correct answer is: B
The flexion and extension phases of craniosacral motion originate at the articulation between the sphenoid bone and the occipital bone, termed the sphenobasilar synchondrosis (SBS). When the flexion phase occurs, the SBS moves cephalad, causing the midline bones to fluidly move into flexion. The midline bones of the cranium consist of the sphenoid, occiput, ethmoid, and vomer bones. The paired bones of the cranium move into an external rotation in the flexion phase. Movement of the midline bones causes a decrease in the anterior-posterior diameter of the cranium while movement of the paired bones into external rotation cause an increase in the transverse diameter of the cranium.

Answer A: The AP diameter does decrease because of flexion of the midline bones of the cranium. However, the transverse diameter increases because of external rotation of the paired cranial bones.


Answer C: The AP diameter does decrease because of flexion of the midline cranial bones. However, the transverse diameter changes as well and does not remain the same.


Answer D: The AP diameter does not increase and the transverse diameter does not decrease. In fact, the opposite is true. Due to flexion and external rotation of the midline and paired cranial bones the AP diameter decreases while the transverse diameter increases.


Answer E: The AP diameter does not increase. It decreases because the SBS is pulled cephalad causing the midline bones to move into flexion. The transverse diameter does increase because the external rotation of the paired cranial bones leads to a widening of the cranium.




Bottom Line: During craniosacral flexion, the midline bones of the cranium move into flexion causing the AP diameter of the head to decrease. The paired cranial bones move into external rotation causing the transverse diameter of the head to increase.

32 of 200

Link: "Le Fort Fractures Imaging"


Patient Presentation: Trauma
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Head & Neck
Discipline: Surgery
A 16-year-old male presents to the emergency department by ambulance after being involved in a head-on motor vehicle collision. History reveals he was an unrestrained driver who lost control of the vehicle and hit a telephone pole at high-speed. The vehicle was not equipped with airbags. Physical examination demonstrates that his face appears long and flat.  Computed tomography scan reveals a fracture that extends posteriorly through the ethmoid bones and laterally through the orbit below the optic foramen and through the pterygomaxillary suture into the sphenopalatine fossa. This type of fracture is classified as

1. Le Fort I
2. Le Fort II
3. Le Fort III
4. Lisfranc I
5. Lisfranc II

The correct answer is: C
Le Fort fractures represent 10-20% of all facial fractures. The majority is caused by motor vehicle collisions but they can also occur due to falls or assault. The Le Fort system of classification divides fractures into 3 distinct patterns. Type I fractures involve separation of the palate from the maxilla, also known as a low septal fracture.   It results from force directed low on the maxillary alveolar rim with a downward direction and the fracture extends from the nasal septum to the lateral pyriform rims, travels horizontally above the teeth apices, crosses below the zygomaticomaxillary junction, and traverses the pterygomaxillary junction to interrupt the pterygoid plates.  Type II fractures results from a blow to the lower or mid maxilla and has a pyramidal shape with extension from the nasal bridge at or below the nasofrontal suture through the frontal process of the maxilla, inferolaterally through the lacrimal bones and inferior orbital floor and rim through or near the inferior orbital foramen, and inferiorly through the anterior wall of the maxillary sinus.  It then goes under the zygoma, across the pterygomaxillary fissure, and through the pterygoid plates.  Type III fractures involve complete craniofacial disjunction with extension posteriorly through the ethmoid bones and laterally through the orbit below the optic foramen and through the pterygomaxillary suture into the sphenopalatine fossa.   It results in a face that appears long and flat.  One of the limitations of the Le Fort classification system is that a combination of fractures is often encountered although it is still the most widely used classification system for facial fractures.

Answer A: A Le Fort I fracture involves separation of the palate from the maxilla, also known as a low septal fracture. It results from force directed low on the maxillary alveolar rim with a downward direction and the fracture extends from the nasal septum to the lateral pyriform rims, travels horizontally above the teeth apices, crosses below the zygomaticomaxillary junction, and traverses the pterygomaxillary junction to interrupt the pterygoid plates.


Answer B: A Le Fort II fracture results from a blow to the lower or mid maxilla and has a pyramidal shape with extension from the nasal bridge at or below the nasofrontal suture through the frontal process of the maxilla, inferolaterally through the lacrimal bones and inferior orbital floor and rim through or near the inferior orbital foramen, and inferiorly through the anterior wall of the maxillary sinus. It then goes under the zygoma, across the pterygomaxillary fissure, and through the pterygoid plates.


Answer C: Correct choice


Answers D & E: The Lisfranc joint is the articulation between the midfoot and the forefoot and involves all five tarsometatarsal joints. Fractures and dislocations at this joint are rare but can lead to chronic disability and pain if not properly diagnosed. There is no such thing as a Lisfranc II fracture.




Bottom Line: The Le Fort classification of facial fractures is divided into 3 fracture patterns: Type I the palate is separated from the maxilla, Type II the maxilla separates from the face and Type III involves craniofacial disjunction.

For more information, see:
"Le Fort Fractures Imaging"

Lubin, M. (1995). Medical management of the surgical patient (3rd ed.). Philadelphia: Lippincott.

Link: "Treatment of seasonal influenza in adults" on UpToDate.


Patient Presentation: Fever
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Management
Body System: Respiratory System
Discipline: Family Medicine
A 32-year-old male presents to the office with the complaints of fever, headache, and muscle pain of 3 days' duration. History reveals the symptoms began with a sore throat, and several co-workers have had similar symptoms over the past week. Past medical history is non-contributory. Vital signs reveal a blood pressure of 117/74 mmHg, a heart rate of 104/min, a respiratory rate of 14/min, an oxygen saturation of 96% on room air, and a temperature of 39.0°C (102.2°F). Physical examination reveals an ill-appearing, diaphoretic male with an erythematous pharynx, but no tonsillar exudates. The skin is warm to touch and lungs reveal minimal scattered rhonchi. Osteopathic examination is significant for several posterior Chapman reflex points; C1 posterior arch, T3 intertransverse space and T4 intertransverse space. The most appropriate treatment is

1. hospitalization
2. influenza vaccine
3. oseltamivir
4. oseltamivir and supportive care
5. supportive care

The correct answer is: E
This individual most likely has been infected by one of the strands of influenza. As no significant health history is present and the current symptoms do not demonstrate a patient with a severe infection, this patient can be treated at home with supportive care. This will include bed rest for about 3 days as well as increased fluid intake. It is important to emphasize to the patient hand washing and other measures to prevent the spread of infection to others. 

Osteopathic pearl: A Chapman point represents the somatic manifestation of a visceral dysfunction. Most posterior Chapman reflex points are located between the spinous and transverse processes; they have a rubbery texture when palpated. The posterior Chapman reflex points related to the upper and lower lung regions are located at the T3 and T4 intertransverse spaces, respectively. The posterior Chapman reflex point related to the pharynx is located at the C1 posterior arch.

Answer A: This patient is not demonstrating signs and symptoms of a severe or life-threatening infection, so there is no need for hospitalization. If the patient was hemodynamically unstable or had a chronic disease that was exacerbated by this infection, then monitoring in the hospital would be appropriate. Also those patients who are unable to care for themselves at home or are not tolerating oral intake (elderly, infants) may require hospitalization.


Answer B: The influenza vaccine is intended for the prevention of infection by certain strains of the influenza virus. Most vaccines now contain a trivalent dosage that includes two strands of influenza A (includes H1N1) and one strand of influenza B viruses. The vaccine becomes effective about 10-14 days after receiving it. During this acute infection, receiving the vaccine at this time would not be effective in treating or shortening the length of illness. The vaccine is specifically recommended for children greater than 6 months of age, the elderly, those with chronic disease, and health care workers.


Answers C & D: Oseltamivir is recommended for prophylaxis and also for the treatment of influenza. Prophylaxis is recommended within 48 hours after contact with an infected individual with a dose of 75 mg once daily. For treatment, this drug should be initiated within 48 hours of the onset of symptoms with a dosage of 75 mg twice daily for 5 days. While initiating treatment prior to 48 hours has shown benefit for outpatient treatment, those with severe infection requiring hospitalization should receive the medication even if it is greater than 48 hours from the beginning of symptoms. This patient is being treated as an outpatient, thus oseltamivir is not recommended as it has been longer than 48 hours that he has been having symptoms.




Bottom Line: Adults with influenza who are hemodynamically stable and able to tolerate oral intake may be treated with outpatient supportive care. Oseltamivir may be used in outpatients prior to 48 hours after the onset of symptoms.

For more information, see:
"Treatment of seasonal influenza in adults" on UpToDate.
Nye, Z. (2009). First aid for the COMLEX: An osteopathic manipulative medicine review (2nd ed.). New York: McGraw-Hill Medical.

Patient Presentation: Sensory Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Genitourinary System
Discipline: Osteopathic Principle and Practice
A 45-year-old female presents to the emergency department with the complaint of left flank pain. History reveals the pain began gradually over the last three days and is associated with urinary frequency and burning. Vital signs reveal a blood pressure of 117/82 mmHg, a heart rate of 72/min, a respiratory rate of 14/min, and a temperature of 38ºC (100.4ºF). Physical examination reveals left costovertebral and suprapubic tenderness. Where is a Chapman's point most likely located?

1. 1 inch superior and 1 inch lateral to the umbilicus
2. 2 inches superior and 1 inch lateral to the umbilicus
3. At the tip of the right 12th rib
4. Over the lateral thigh along the iliotibial band
5. Over the transverse process of T11

The correct answer is: A
Chapman’s points are smooth and discrete nodules approximately 2-3 mm in diameter that lie within the fascial tissue and along bone periosteum. They manifest as somatic findings of visceral dysfunction. This patient is most likely suffering from ascending urinary tract infection and hydronephrosis. Chapman’s reflexes for the kidneys are located anteriorly 1 inch superior and 1 inch lateral to the umbilicus, or posteriorly between the spinous and transverse processes of T12 and L1. The remaining locations represent Chapman’s points that would be little help to this patient, as they target different organ systems.

Answer B: Adrenal manifestations occur anteriorly, 2 inches superior and 1 inch lateral to the umbilicus.


Answer C: Anteriorly at the tip of the right 12th rib is a classic Chapman’s point for the appendix.


Answer D: Chapman’s points for the colon occur over the lateral thigh along the iliotibial band.


Answer E: Posteriorly, visceral dysfunction of the appendix can manifest over the transverse process of T11.




Bottom Line: Chapman’s reflexes for the kidneys are located anteriorly 1 inch superior and 1 inch lateral to the umbilicus.

Patient Presentation: Consciousness Alterations
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Management
Body System: Cardiovascular System
Discipline: Emergency Medicine
A 75-year-old male presents to the emergency department unresponsive. History reveals the patient collapsed while walking his dog. Physical examination reveals an unresponsive male without a pulse. The patient is intubated, CPR is in progress, and an ECG is obtained which confirms asystole in two leads. One dose of epinephrine is administered and CPR is continued. After 4 cycles of CPR, the patient remains in asystole. The most appropriate medication for this patient is

1. adenosine
2. amiodarone
3. atropine
4. epinephrine
5. magnesium

The correct answer is: D
This patient is in asystole, defined as absence of electrical activity on ECG. Management of asystole or pulseless electrical activity (PEA) involves 5 cycles of CPR and 1mg doses of epinephrine via IV or IO, repeated every 3-5 minutes. Vasopressin is also an alternative to the first or second dose of epinephrine. The ECG should then be re-evaluated and if a shockable rhythm is present the patient should be delivered a shock. Patients in asystole typically have poor outcomes and likely have underlying cardiac disease.

Answer A: Adenosine is used for supraventricular tachycardia. The 2010 American Heart Association (AHA) guidelines have included adenosine for stable, undifferentiated regular, monomorphic wide-complex tachycardia.


Answer B: Amiodarone is used for electrical shock-refractory ventricular tachycardia (VT) or ventricular fibrillation (VF).


Answer C: Atropine was previously part of the 2005 AHA guidelines, but was removed in the 2010 AHA guidelines for CPR and Emergency Cardiovascular Care (ECC).


Answer E: Magnesium is used in the management of Torsades de Pointes.




Bottom Line: Management of asystole or pulseless electrical activity is CPR and epinephrine.

Patient Presentation: Musculoskeletal Difficulties And Muscular Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Diagnostic Technologies
Body System: Musculoskeletal System
Discipline: Emergency Medicine
A 79-year-old female presents to the emergency department complaining of right hip pain after being found by her daughter laying on the bathroom floor. The patient states when she sat down to use the toilet, she felt a “pop” in her right hip and fell to the ground. The patient admits to a history of dementia, osteoarthritis, breast cancer, and kidney disease. Her prior surgeries include a left partial mastectomy six years prior, bilateral total knee replacements four years prior, and a right total hip replacement eight months ago. Her medications include furosemide 40 mg twice daily, aspirin 81mg daily, and acetaminophen 500mg as needed for pain. On exam, you note her right lower extremity is shortened and internally rotated with mild weakness with extension of her right big toe.  You note paravertebral muscle spasms from T11-L2.
Question 1 of 2 in this set
The most appropriate diagnostic test is

1. bone scan
2. CT scan without contrast
3. MRI
4. ultrasound
5. x-ray

The correct answer is: E
This patient has sustained a right posterior hip dislocation while positioning herself on the toilet seat. Posterior hip dislocations account for 90% of all hip dislocations, traumatic and non-traumatic. After undergoing total hip replacement, patients are at an increased risk for posterior hip dislocation since the posterior surgical approach violates the posterior ligaments, which subsequently become weakened. After surgery, patients are advised to refrain from positioning themselves with their operative hip flexed, internally rotated and adducted as this puts extreme stress on the posterior ligaments and leads to the prosthetic femoral head slipping out the back of the acetabular cup. This position is common when tying shoes, sitting on the toilet, or when getting in and out of the car. Once the hip dislocates, the femoral head sits posterior to the acetabulum, which forces the femur to rotate internally. This causes the affected extremity to appear shortened and internally rotated as in the above scenario. The hip may also be flexed, as this places the hip in a position of comfort. The most appropriate test to diagnose a dislocated prosthetic hip is an AP and lateral x-ray of the hip. As with any pathology or injury in the lower extremity, you may note TART changes from T11-L2 representing viscerosomatic reflexes from the lower extremity.

Answer A: A bone scan is a nuclear test used to find areas of increased bone turnover, such as in Paget’s disease of the bone, bone cancer, metastatic bone disease, osteomyelitis and occasionally fracture. It is not the best test to assess for a hip dislocation, which can clearly be seen on x-ray.


Answer B: A CT without contrast would be the next best step, but only after performing an x-ray. If there was any doubt as to the diagnosis based on the x-ray, a CT without contrast may be performed to better analyze the bony anatomy. However, prosthetic hip dislocations are usually easily seen on x-ray.


Answer C: An MRI is ideal for assessing soft tissues and the bone marrow and occasionally a fracture. However, while it can be used in patients with total joint replacements, it is not the most appropriate initial imaging study as it is expensive and time-consuming.


Answer D: Ultrasound of the hip is useful for diagnosing a fluid collection within a joint, such as in septic arthritis. It is not a useful diagnostic tool for this patient.




Bottom Line: When you see a patient with a total hip replacement who felt a “pop” then has a shortened and internally rotated lower extremity, think posterior hip dislocation. They need an x-ray and orthopedic surgery consult. This is considered an emergency and surgical intervention may be required.

COMBANK Insight : This patient has sustained a posterior hip dislocation as evidenced by the physical exam. A posterior hip dislocation will have a shortened extremity on the affected side and will be internally rotated. The danger with a posterior hip dislocation is that is usually it requires a lot of energy to dislocate the hip so the propensity for other injuries is high. There could be nerve and blood vessel injury with hip dislocations.

Since this patient has a hip replacement in the past, and her hip dislocated. You need to get an x-ray to confirm the dislocation. Many prosthetic hips that dislocate indicate a failure of the hardware and the over-whelming majority need to go the operating room to fix them.

For more information, see:
Miller MD, Thompson SR, Hart JA, et al. Review of Orthopaedics. Philadelphia, PA: Elseiver-Saunders; 2012: 377 Updated: September 2, 2012

Patient Presentation: Musculoskeletal Difficulties And Muscular Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Scientific Understanding of Health & Disease Mechanisms
Body System: Nervous System
Discipline: Emergency Medicine
A 79-year-old female presents to the emergency department complaining of right hip pain after being found by her daughter laying on the bathroom floor. The patient states when she sat down to use the toilet, she felt a “pop” in her right hip and fell to the ground. The patient admits to a history of dementia, osteoarthritis, breast cancer, and kidney disease. Her prior surgeries include a left partial mastectomy six years prior, bilateral total knee replacements four years prior, and a right total hip replacement eight months ago. Her medications include furosemide 40 mg twice daily, aspirin 81mg daily, and acetaminophen 500mg as needed for pain. On exam, you note her right lower extremity is shortened and internally rotated with mild weakness with extension of her right big toe.  You note paravertebral muscle spasms from T11-L2.
Question 2 of 2 in this set
The nerve responsible for the weakness in her right big toe is the

1. femoral nerve
2. obturator nerve
3. pudendal nerve
4. sciatic nerve
5. superior gluteal nerve

The correct answer is: D
In a patient with a posterior hip dislocation (prosthetic or native), the femoral head is pushed posterior to the acetabulum. This places pressure on the sciatic nerve as it courses out of the piriformis and down the posterior aspect of the thigh. Extension of the big toe comes from the extensor hallucis longus, which is innervated by the deep fibular nerve which branches off the common fibular nerve from the sciatic nerve. It is not uncommon to have distal parasthesias and weakness corresponding to the sciatic nerve distribution associated with posterior hip dislocations.

Answer A: The femoral nerve is anterior to the hip capsule. It is not under strain in a posterior hip dislocation. It is comprised of nerve roots L2-L4 and innervates the quadriceps, iliacus, sartorial, and pectineus muscles. Sensory distribution of the femoral nerve includes the anterior thigh and the medial leg.


Answer B: The obturator nerve also travels anterior to the hip capsule. It can be injured in an anterior hip dislocation, but not likely in a posterior hip dislocation. It innervates the adductor muscles of the thigh.


Answer C: The pudendal nerve exits superior to the hip joint. It is comprised of the sacral plexus and branches to multiple nerves that innervate the genital musculature.


Answer E: The superior gluteal nerve exits superior to the hip joint. It innervates the gluteus medius, and tensor fascia lata.




Bottom Line: The sciatic nerve is the only posterior structure at the level of the hip and is directly involved extension of the big toe via the extensor hallucis longus. Pathology corresponding to the distribution of the sciatic nerve can be seen in patients with a posterior hip dislocation.

COMBANK Insight : On COMLEX Level 2 anatomy questions are more commonly integrated into a clinical scenario. The distribution of large nerves may be tested. Remember the course and distribution of the sciatic nerve. It is comprised of L4-S3 nerve roots and courses through the greater sciatic foramen. It divides into the tibial and peroneal nerves.  The tibial nerve is responsible for innervation of the hamstrings (except the short head of the biceps femoris) and plantar flexor muscles of the foot. The tibial nerve's sensory distribution includes the low leg and plantar aspect of the foot. The peroneal nerve innervates the short head of the biceps femoris and dorsiflexor muscles of the foot. The peroneal nerve's sensory distribution includes the lower leg and dorsum of the foot.

For more information, see:
Miller MD, Thompson SR, Hart JA, et al. Review of Orthopaedics. Philadelphia, PA: Elseiver-Saunders; 2012: 189 Updated: September 2, 2012.

Patient Presentation: Masses & Edema
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Management
Body System: Endocrine System
Discipline: Surgery
A 50-year-old female presents for evaluation of an enlarged thyroid and chronic cough. An ultrasound of the thyroid is highly suggestive of a multinodular goiter and minimal tracheal deviation secondary to compression by the goiter. Pre-operative evaluation of the patient for a thyroidectomy reveals a history of gastric bypass surgery. The most important precaution for this patient is

1. admit patient to SICU for observation after surgery
2. careful identification and preservation of parathyroid tissue
3. careful resection of all thyroid tissue
4. delay restarting a regular diet after surgery
5. transfuse two units of blood pre-operatively

The correct answer is: B
Parathyroid tissue consists of four glands (two pairs) with each pair located close to upper and lower poles of the posterior thyroid lobe. Parathyroid tissue releases parathyroid hormone, which is controlled by ionized calcium by a negative feedback mechanism. Parathyroid glands are identified and preserved during thyroid surgery, with close monitoring of serum calcium. Calcium is primarily absorbed in the duodenum, which has the highest concentration of calcium transporters. Since ingested food bypasses the duodenum after a gastric bypass (GBP) surgery, total body calcium levels may further decrease, placing the patient at higher risk of osteoporosis. Acute hypocalcemia may present as perioral numbness/ tingling, muscle cramps or with seizures if severe. In patients with a possible underlying calcium deficiency, it is important to take extra care in preserving parathyroid tissue during thyroid surgery.

Answer A: The majority of patients following thyroid surgery are admitted for overnight observation with monitoring of serum calcium levels. Though pre-operative evaluation with CT of the neck showed some tracheal deviation, the patient does not show symptoms of respiratory distress and does not require monitoring in the SICU.


Answer C: Pre-operative evaluation of the nodules revealed a multinodular goiter, a benign condition. The main indication for the surgery is evidence of tracheal deviation and cough. Since there is no evidence of malignancy in the thyroid, there is no need for resection of the entire thyroid. Meticulous resection of the entire thyroid would only further increase the patient’s risk of possible injury to the recurrent laryngeal nerves.


Answer D: After GBP, patients feel full after ingesting only a small volume of food. Total food intake is greatly reduced and the patient must follow specific instructions for adequate nutrition. However, the above patient has just had neck surgery with only a past history of GBP surgery. Also, the patient does not complain of dysphagia or respiratory distress. The patient’s diet may be progressed as tolerated by the patient with no specific purpose for a delay.


Answer E: Iron is also normally absorbed in the duodenum and is frequently severely deficient in GBP patients, especially females. While daily iron supplementation is often required, pre-operative blood transfusion is unnecessary.




Bottom Line: Cautious preservation of parathyroid tissue during a thyroidectomy is especially indicated in a patient with history of gastric bypass surgery since calcium is mainly absorbed in the duodenum and such patients may already have calcium deficiency.

Patient Presentation: Musculoskeletal Difficulties And Muscular Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Musculoskeletal System
Discipline: Osteopathic Principle and Practice
A 85-year-old female presents to your office with right-sided rib pain.  She recently had an episode of bronchitis and feels that her coughing has made her symptoms worse.  She has tenderness on the ribs directly over where the pain is located.  In addition to your examination of her chest including auscultation of her lungs, you decide to evaluate her rib cage motion.  You note that upon inhalation the right side of her lower rib cage doesn't rise as much as the left side does.  The motion appears to be equal on exhalation.  What is the somatic dysfunction that you are palpating and where would you anticipate finding a tender point that may be treated using counterstrain?

1. Exhalation Somatic Dysfunction, tender point on the rib angle of right T10.
2. Inhalation somatic dysfunction, tender point on rib 10 at the anterior axillary line.
3. Exhalation somatic dysfunction, tender point on rib 10 at the anterior axillary line.
4. Inhalation somatic dysfunction, tender point on the angle of rib 10.
5. Exhalation restriction, no tender point would be expected.

The correct answer is: C
The findings that are being described in this question correlate with an exhalation somatic dysfunction.  When the patient inhales the right side appears to be restricted.  Since we always name somatic dysfunctions for the freedom, an inhalation restriction correlates to an exhalation somatic dysfunction.  Anterior tender points are associated with exhalation somatic dysfunctions (depressed ribs). These somatic dysfunctions are often caused by coughing, sneezing or overuse of upper extremity.  Anterior points are found on the corresponding rib at the anterior axillary line.  The lower points (AR9 - AR10) are associated with internal intercostal muscles.

Answer A: This question IS describing an exhalation somatic dysfunction, however exhalation somatic dysfunction typically correlate with anterior tender points. The tender point found at the angle of the rib corresponds to a posterior tender point and is usually associated with inhalation somatic dysfunctions.


Answer B: This question is describing an exhalation somatic dysfunction not an inhalation somatic dysfunction. The palpatory finding for an inhalation somatic dysfunction would be as follows: upon inhalation both the right and left lower ribs moved equally and on exhalation the right rib cage didn't descend as far, it felt "stuck up". The tender point in this answer would be the correct tender point for the case as it is describing an anterior tender point which correlates to an exhalation somatic dysfunction.


Answer D: This case is describing and exhalation somatic dysfunction and this would correlate to an anterior costal tender point.


Answer E: Exhalation restriction is the same diagnosis as an inhalation somatic dysfunction. This patient is reporting tenderness over her rib cage therefore a tender point would be a likely finding.




Bottom Line: Exhalation somatic dysfunctions correlate with anterior tender points and inhalation somatic dysfunction correlate with posterior tender points.  Anterior tender points for AR3 - AR10 are palpated on the anterior axillary line on the corresponding ribs.  Posterior tender points are palpated on the superior border of the corresponding rib at the rib angle.  We always name a somatic dysfunction for its freedom.  in this case the freedom of motion was in exhalation and the restriction was in inhalation, therefore the diagnosis is an exhalation somatic dysfunction which is a common finding in a patient who has had recent episodes of coughing.

For more information, see:
Nicholas A, Nicholas E. Atlas of Osteopathic Techniques, Third Edition. Philadelphia, PA: Wolters Kluwer; 2016: pp. 180-184.

Patient Presentation: Disease Detection & Monitoring
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Diagnostic Technologies
Body System: Musculoskeletal System
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 67-year-old female presents to the office for a follow-up after a thoracic compression fracture. History reveals she has had multiple fractures over the past year. A DEXA scan is performed to assess her bone density. Which of the following T-scores would support the diagnosis of osteopenia?

1. 2.5 to 3.5
2. 1.0 to 2.5
3. 0 to 1.0
4. -1.0 to -2.5
5. -2.5 to -3.5

The correct answer is: D
T-scores are used to assess bone density and are generated from DEXA scans. T-scores compare a patient's bone density to the optimal peak bone density for their gender and are reported as the number of standard deviations below the average. A T-score of greater than -1 is considered normal. A T-score of -1 to -2.5 is considered osteopenia and indicates the patient is at risk for developing osteoporosis. A T-score of less than -2.5 is diagnostic of osteoporosis

Answers A & B & C: This is a normal T-score. A T-score of -1 to -2.5 is considered osteopenia and indicates the patient is at risk for developing osteoporosis. A T-score of less than -2.5 is diagnostic of osteoporosis.


Answer E: A T-score of less than -2.5 is diagnostic of osteoporosis.




Bottom Line: A T-score of -1 to -2.5 is diagnostic of osteopenia, whereas a score of less than -2.5 is diagnostic of osteoporosis.

For more information, see:
"Screening for osteoporosis" on UpToDate.

Patient Presentation: General Concepts
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Health Promotion & Disease Prevention
Body System: Asymptomatic & General Symptoms
Discipline: Family Medicine
A study is being done on a new test for hypoparathyroidism. In a population of 1000 patients with and without the disease, 120 individuals have the disease and have tested positive with the new test, 552 individuals do not have the condition and have tested negative with the new test. 15% of individuals in the population being tested have hypoparathyroidism.
Question 1 of 4 in this set
What is the approximate chance that an individual who tests negative with the new test does not have hypoparathyroidism?

1. 30%
2. 55%
3. 65%
4. 80%
5. 95%

The correct answer is: E
This question is asking for the negative predictive value. The first step in answering this question properly is creating a 2 x 2 table. Using 1,000 as the sample size, 15% prevalence gives a total of 150 individuals who have the disease (1000 x 15% = 150). Since we know “True Positives” individuals account for 120, we can calculate “False Negatives” (150 – 120 = 30). We are also given “True Negatives” at 552, which allows us to calculate “False Positives” with relative ease. With our sample size of 1,000 and 150 individuals who have the disease, we know that 850 individuals are disease free (1,000 – 150 = 850). “False Positives” can be calculated as follows (850 – 552 = 298).

Negative Predictive Value (NPV) is the probability that a patient with a negative test does not have a disease.  Calculated: (D) / (C + D) = (552) / (30 + 552) = ~95% 

Answers A & B & C & D: The negative predictive value is 95%.




Bottom Line: NPV is the probability that a patient with a negative test does not have a disease. 

For more information, see:
"Glossary of common biostatistical and epidemiological terms" on UpToDate.


Last Updated: 12/9/2015

Patient Presentation: General Concepts
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Health Promotion & Disease Prevention
Body System: Asymptomatic & General Symptoms
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A study is being done on a new test for hypoparathyroidism. In a population of 1000 patients with and without the disease, 120 individuals have the disease and have tested positive with the new test, 552 individuals do not have the condition and have tested negative with the new test. 15% of individuals in the population being tested have hypoparathyroidism.

1. 30%
2. 55%
3. 65%
4. 80%
5. 95%

This question is asking for the sensitivity of the new test. The first step in answering this question properly is creating a 2 x 2 table. Using 1,000 as the sample size, 15% prevalence gives a total of 150 individuals who have the disease (1000 x 15% = 150). Since we know “True Positives” individuals account for 120, we can calculate “False Negatives” (150 – 120 = 30). Sensitivity is the probability that a screening test will be positive in patients with a disease.  Calculated: (A) / (A + C) = (120) / (120 +30) = ~80%

Answers A & B & C & E: The sensitivity is 80%.

Patient Presentation: Musculoskeletal Difficulties And Muscular Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Musculoskeletal System
Discipline: Osteopathic Principle and Practice
A patient presents with back pain after studying for his final exams. On structural exam you note T4 is more full on the right than the left side. When you ask him to bend forward the asymmetry becomes even more apparent. Which of these diagnoses would mirror Fryette’s second principle for a type II non-neutral dysfunction in this patient?

1. T4 extended, rotated right, sidebent right
2. T4 extended, rotated right, sidebent left
3. T4 extended, rotated left, sidebent right

4. T4 flexed, rotated right, sidebent right
5. T4 flexed, rotated right, sidebent left

The correct answer is: A
A type II non-neutral group dysfunction is named according to the superior segment over the inferior segment of involvement. Type II single segment dysfunctions are sidebent and rotated to the same side and will obtain symmetry through either flexion or extension. If the facet is fixed or locked open, flexion will gain symmetry of structure and extension and sidebending towards the fixated side will be diminished. Conversely if the facet is fixed or locked closed, it will have more symmetry in extension but flexion and sidebending to the opposite side will be diminished. Usually the somatic pain complaint will manifest on the side which the vertebral body is rotated towards.

Answer B: Fryette’s 2nd principle states that a single vertebral segment will be sidebent and rotated to the same side and will obtain symmetry through either extension or flexion. In this patient, his vertebra is rotated right more so when he flexes forward. His position of ease, therefore, is in an extended position. This cannot be the correct answer since in an extended dysfunction the segment must rotate and sidebend to the same side.


Answer C: This cannot be the correct answer since in an extended dysfunction the segment must rotate and sidebend to the same side.


Answer D: Fryette’s 2nd principle states that a single vertebral segment will be sidebent and rotated to the same side and will obtain symmetry through either extension or flexion. In this patient, his vertebra is rotated right more so when he flexes forward. His position of ease, therefore, is in an extended position, not in a flexed position.


Answer E: This cannot be the correct answer since in an extended dysfunction the segment must rotate and sidebend to the same side and this patient has an extended, not a flexed dysfunction.

Patient Presentation: Musculoskeletal Difficulties And Muscular Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Musculoskeletal System
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 31-year-old construction worker presents with right wrist pain. Patient reports presence of pain for greater than 6 months with occasional numbness in his hand. Physical examination is remarkable for decreased sensation along the thenar aspect of the hand and in the antecubital fossa. Biceps muscle strength testing of the right upper extremity is 4 out of 5. Osteopathic findings include right rib 1 ISD, T4FRRSR, Right rib 4 ESD, hypertonic trapezius and scalene muscles on the right, and a shallow supraclavicular space on the right.  Symptoms are reproduced by the maneuver as demonstrated in the attachment with a decrease in the radial pulse. 

The most likely diagnosis is

1. carpal tunnel syndrome
2. cubital tunnel syndrome
3. De Quervain tenosynovitis
4. Guyon's canal entrapment syndrome
5. thoracic outlet syndrome

The correct answer is: E
Thoracic outlet syndrome is a compression syndrome of the brachial plexus in the upper extremity causing pain, numbness, weakness and dysesthesia. Common causes of this syndrome include cervical ribs, muscular anomalies, injury to the upper extremity, or somatic dysfunction in the ribs, upper extremity, and/or upper thoracic spine as seen in this case. Repetitive activities such as reaching overhead, brushing hair, overhead lifting, prolonged typing, driving or speaking on the telephone can exacerbate the condition and cause pain. Symptoms include progressive unilateral atrophic weakness of the hand. Sensory deficits of the thenar aspect, as well as abnormalities along the T1 distribution, will be seen. The Adson's maneuver is a test utilized in the evaluation of thoracic outlet syndrome. A decrease in radial or ulnar pulse with the addition of the upper extremity is considered a positive test.

Answer A: Carpal tunnel syndrome is due to median nerve entrapment at the flexor retinaculum and patients are often unable to grip normally and cannot oppose the thumb and index finger normally. Carpal tunnel syndrome symptoms are typically felt in the thumb through 4th finger. Over time, patients can develop muscle atrophy of the thenar eminence. The most common presentation is numbness and tingling over the first three digits of the hand. Pain is usually worst at night. However, the Adson's maneuver is not specific for carpal tunnel syndrome. Also, muscle strength of the bicep should not be affected.


Answer B: Cubital tunnel syndrome is caused by ulnar nerve entrapment at the medial edge of the elbow. Cubital tunnel syndrome presents with similar symptoms of Guyon's canal syndrome. Symptoms include tingling and/or pain along the 4th and 5th digits of the corresponding hand. Altered sensation in these fingers may also be reported. Muscle wasting of the hypothenar eminence may be noted on physical examination. Although, cubital tunnel syndrome and Guyon's canal syndrome are both types of ulnar neuropathy, cubital tunnel syndrome is less likely in this patient. This patient denies pain in the elbow or history of trauma to the area. In addition, sensation on the hypothenar eminence is not affected.


Answer C: De Quervain tenosynovitis is a painful stenosing tenosynovitis involving the first extensor compartment of the wrist. Most cases are associated with overuse. Patients present with pain on thumb and wrist movement with focal tenderness over the radial styloid. The abductor pollicis longs and extensor pollicis brevis are typically involved. Plain films are non-diagnostic but may show soft-tissue swelling over the radial styloid. Focal abnormalities such as cortical erosion, sclerosis or periosteal reaction may be seen. De Quervain tenosynovitis is not the diagnosis in this patient. He presents with wrist pain and a positive Adson's maneuver.


Answer D: The ulnar nerve is the terminal branch of the brachial plexus. It is responsible for innervation to the muscles of the forearm and hand. It also is responsible for sensation to the 4th and 5th digits. Ulnar nerve entrapment at Guyon's canal may cause denervation and/or paralysis of the muscles. Anatomically, the ulnar nerve is entrapped as it passes through the tunnel formed by the pisiform and hamate bones and the pisohamate ligament at the level of the wrist. This produces atrophy of the hypothenar eminence and impairs both adduction and abduction of the 4th and 5th fingers. Muscle wasting of these digits may occur but is rare. Usually patients also complain of a “pins and needles” sensation of the 5th digit and the medial aspect of the palmar surface of the 4th digit. Physical examination will be evident for sensory loss along the ulnar distribution in the hand with preservation in the dorsal aspect of the hand. This patient complains of decreased sensation along the thenar eminence, therefore Guyon's canal entrapment syndrome is less likely syndrome.




Bottom Line: A positive Adson's test is suspicious of thoracic outlet syndrome.

For more information, see:
"Thoracic outlet syndrome" on Medscape.

insert Link
Patient Presentation: Musculoskeletal Difficulties And Muscular Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Musculoskeletal System
Discipline: Osteopathic Principle and Practice
A 49-year-old male presents to the office with the complaint of low back pain. History reveals the pain is achy and occurs after long periods of standing. Osteopathic structural examination reveals L2-L5 all have posterior transverse processes on the left and better motion is found when pressing anteriorly on the right transverse processes. These segments resist translation to the right. What is the most likely diagnosis?

1. Idiopathic scoliosis
2. Type I somatic dysfunction: L2-L5 neutral, rotated left, sidebent right
3. Type I somatic dysfunction: L2-L5 neutral, rotated right, sidebent left
4. Type II somatic dysfunction: L2-L5 flexed, rotated left, sidebent left
5. Type II somatic dysfunction: L2-L5 neutral, rotated left, sidebent right

The correct answer is: B
This patient's findings exhibit L2-L5 neutral, rotated left, and sidebent right, which is a type I somatic dysfunction. Fryette's principles indicate that in type I spinal somatic dysfunctions, rotation and sidebending occur in opposite directions and in the neutral plane of motion. Type I dysfunctions tend to be group dysfunctions, where type II are typically only 1 segment. Fryette's principles correspond to the thoracic and lumbar spine only.

Answer A: Idiopathic scoliosis is a 3-dimensional torsional deformity of the spine with a lateral curve of greater than 10º. While a type I or II somatic dysfunction may also be present, additional findings of lateral curvature are necessary on a radiograph and physical examination to make this diagnosis.


Answer C: The diagnosis of "type I somatic dysfunction: L2-L5 neutral, rotated right, sidebent left" is opposite from the rotation and sidebending findings in the case.


Answer D: In type II dysfunction, rotation and sidebending occur in the same direction and in either the flexion or extension planes. "Type II somatic dysfunction: L2-L5 flexed, rotated left, sidebent left" describes a type II diagnosis, which does not correspond to the case.


Answer E: "Type II somatic dysfunction: L2-L5 neutral, rotated left, sidebent right" indicates a type II dysfunction, however, the findings correspond to a type I dysfunction in the neutral plane with rotation and sidebending occurring in opposite directions.




Bottom Line: In type I somatic dysfunction, rotation and sidebending occur on opposite sides in the neutral plane of motion. Often, they affect a group of vertebrae.

COMBANK Insight : Make sure you are able to diagnose and name somatic dysfunctions.

insert Link
Patient Presentation: Skin, Nail & Hair Disorders
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Scientific Understanding of Health & Disease Mechanisms
Body System: Integumentary System
Discipline: Pediatrics
A 17-year-old female presents to the office for a health maintenance examination. Past medical history is significant for atopy. Physical examination reveals dermatographism. The immune system mechanism of this response is

1. IgE mediated
2. IgG mediated
3. immune complex mediated
4. Arthus reaction mediated
5. t-cell mediated

The correct answer is: A
Atopy is the inherited predisposition to asthma, allergies, and dermatitis. Dermatographism is seen in a small percentage of these patients. When the examiner writes a word on the skin of the patient it remains imprinted as erythematous wheals. It occurs when a patient has urticaria, otherwise known as hives. It is an IgE mediated reaction, involving mast cells and histamine release. This is also known as a Type I hypersensitivity reaction. Allergic reactions trigger IgE mediated hypersensitivity reactions and can present as erythematous wheals. These are pruritic, raised lesions that present transiently when exposed to an allergen. Treatment includes avoidance of allergens, antihistamines, steroids, and epinephrine for anaphylactic shock.

Answer B: Cytotoxic, or antibody mediated, reactions are known as Type II hypersensitivity. They involve IgM and IgG, which bind to a fixed antigen and cause lysis. Type II hypersensitivity examples include hemolytic anemia, ITP, Goodpasture's syndrome, bullous pemphigoid, Graves' disease, and myasthenia gravis.


Answer C: Immune complex deposition is a Type III hypersensitivity reaction that involves the release of neutrophil lysosomal enzymes. Type III hypersensitivity examples include SLE, RA, PAN, and post-streptococcal glomerulonephritis.


Answer D: Arthus reaction is also a Type III hypersensitivity reaction. It occurs in response to an intradermal injection of antigen. It is characterized by edema, necrosis, and activation of complement.


Answer E: T-cell mediated reactions are also known as Type IV hypersensitivity reactions. These reactions lead to the activation of macrophages. Type IV hypersensitivity examples include type 1 diabetes mellitus, multiple sclerosis, Guillain-Barré syndrome, Hashimoto's thyroiditis, PPD test, and contact dermatitis.




Bottom Line: Dermatographism is a physical examination sign. When the examiner writes a word on the skin of the patient it remains imprinted as erythematous wheals. It occurs when a patient has urticaria, otherwise known as hives. It is an IgE mediated reaction, involving mast cells and histamine release.

COMBANK Insight : It is important to remember the four different types of hypersensitivity reactions. They are as follows: Type I hypersensitivity (allergy): IgE mediated Type II hypersensitivity (cytotoxic): IgG or IgM mediated (antibody mediated) Type III hypersensitivity: immune complex deposition Type IV hypersensitivity: cell-mediated.

Link: "Acute renal failure" on Medscape.


Patient Presentation: Genitourinary Disorders/Issues
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Scientific Understanding of Health & Disease Mechanisms
Body System: Genitourinary System
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 67-year-old male presents to the office with the complaint of decreased urine output. History reveals the patient was discharged from the hospital earlier that day after being treated for a myocardial infarction with tenecteplase. Past medical history reveals hypertension, hyperlipidemia and depression. Vital signs reveal a blood pressure of 110/76 mmHg and a heart rate of 82/min. Physical examination reveals a soft, nontender abdomen and no suprapubic or costovertebral angle tenderness and a non-palpable bladder. Rectal examination reveals a mildly enlarged, non-tender prostate. Laboratory studies reveal:

Sodium  138 mEq/L
Potassium 4.7 mmol/L  
BUN 82 mg/dl
Creatinine    1.8 mg/dl

 Microscopic exam of the urine is significant for bland sediment and hyaline casts but no red blood cells.
 The most likely cause of his symptoms is

1. cholesterol emboli
2. decreased renal perfusion
3. hypertrophic prostate
4. rhabdomyolysis
5. tenecteplase

The correct answer is: B
The patient in the above scenario is most likely suffering from acute kidney injury secondary to a pre-renal cause. Pre-renal kidney failure is characterized by a BUN:creatinine ratio of greater than 20. There will also be a lack of granular casts or red or white blood cell casts in the urinary sediment as there is no direct insult to the renal cells. Causes of pre-renal failure include cardiac causes (such as an MI or CHF exacerbation) and volume loss (such as hemorrhage or dehydration).

Answer A: Cholesterol emboli may cause intrinsic acute kidney injury, characterized by a BUN:creatinine ratio of less than 20. Cholesterol emboli commonly present as petechiae in the distal extremities following thrombectomy and stent placement.


Answer C: In rare cases, a hypertrophic prostate can cause urinary retention and post-renal acute kidney injury. Post-renal causes of acute kidney injury lead to similar labs as pre-renal causes. Urinary retention presents as a painful, distended, palpable bladder.


Answer D: Rhabdomyolysis can cause intrinsic renal failure. It presents as weakness, myalgias and dark-colored urine.


Answer E: Tenecteplase is a thrombolytic agent that may cause hemorrhage, not acute kidney injury.




Bottom Line: Pre-renal causes of acute kidney injury are characterized by a BUN:creatinine ratio of greater than 20.

For more information, see:
"Acute renal failure" on Medscape.

50 of 200

Link: Medscape: Cervical Radiculopathy


Patient Presentation: Musculoskeletal Difficulties And Muscular Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Musculoskeletal System
Discipline: Osteopathic Principle and Practice
A patient presents with neck and arm discomfort of gradual onset. The patient's symptoms range from a dull ache, to a severe burning. The sensations radiate from the neck, into the right upper extremity. You suspect a radiculopathy secondary to stenosis of the intervertebral foramen. Which of the following tests is clinically useful in helping to confirm cervical radiculopathy?

1. Spurling Test
2. Lhermitte sign
3. Wallenberg Test
4. Adson Test
5. Wright Test

The correct answer is: A
Cervical radiculopathy is a dysfunction of a nerve root of the cervical spine. The seventh (C7; 60%) and sixth (C6; 25%) cervical nerve roots are the most commonly affected. Factors associated with increased risk include heavy manual labor requiring the lifting of more than 25 pounds, smoking, and driving or operating vibrating equipment. Other, less frequent causes include tumors of the spine, an expanding cervical synovial cyst, synovial chondromatosis in the cervical facet joint, giant cell arteritis of the cervical radicular vessels, and spinal infections. The typical patient with cervical radiculopathy presents with neck and arm discomfort of insidious onset. The discomfort can range from a dull ache to a severe burning pain. Typically, pain is referred to the medial border of the scapula, and the patient's chief complaint is shoulder pain. As the radiculopathy progresses, the pain radiates to the upper or lower arm and into the hand, along the sensory distribution of the nerve root that is involved.

The foraminal compression test, or Spurling test, is probably the best test for confirming the diagnosis of cervical radiculopathy. It is performed by positioning the patient with the neck extended and the head rotated, and then applying downward pressure on the head. The test is considered positive if pain radiates into the limb ipsilateral to the side to which the head is rotated. The Spurling test has been found to be very specific (93%), but not sensitive (30%), in diagnosing acute radiculopathy. Therefore, it is not useful as a screening test, but it is clinically useful in helping to confirm cervical radiculopathy.

Answer B: Lhermitte sign is an electric shock-like sensation radiating down the spine, and in some patients into the extremities, elicited by flexion of the neck. This sign has been found in patients with cervical cord involvement or cervical spondylosis, and also in patients with tumor and multiple sclerosis (MS); however, the Lhermitte sign should be negative in those with cervical radiculopathy. Manual distraction may reduce the neck and limb symptoms in patients with cervical radiculopathy.


Answer C: This is a test for vertebral artery insufficiency. In a supine position, the physician flexes the patient's neck. holding it for 10 seconds, then extends the neck, holding it for 10 seconds. This is repeated with rotation to the right and left, and in positions that the physician would attempt to mobilize the cervical spine. The test is positive if the patient has dizziness, lightheadedness, visual changes or nystagmus.


Answer D: This is a test for thoracic outlet syndrome. While monitoring the patient's pulse, the arm is extended at the elbow, the shoulder is extended, externally rotated and slightly abducted. The patient is instructed to take a deep breath and turn his/her head toward the ipsilateral arm. The test is positive if there is a decreased or absent radial pulse.


Answer E: This is a test for thoracic outlet syndrome. The neurovascular bundle can be compromised as it passes under the pectoralis minor muscle at the coracoid process. This test entails hyper-abducting the arm above the head with some extension. The test is positive with a severely decreased or absent radial pulse.




Bottom Line: The foraminal compression test, or Spurling test, is probably the best test for confirming the diagnosis of cervical radiculopathy.

For more information, see:
Medscape: Cervical Radiculopathy
Savarese, R. G., Capobianco, J. D., & Cox, J. J. (2003). OMT review: A comprehensive review in osteopathic medicine (3rd ed.). Place of publication not identified: Savarese.

Patient Presentation: Musculoskeletal Difficulties And Muscular Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Management
Body System: Musculoskeletal System
Discipline: Osteopathic Principle and Practice
A 68-year-old female presents with chronic neck pain.  She has had pain for more than 10 years and was recently evaluated by an orthopaedic physician who diagnosed her with spinal stenosis.  She denies having any radiation of pain, numbness, tingling or weakness.  Her pain is constant and ache.  You decide because of her symptoms and diagnosis to perform an indirect and passive technique.  Upon palpation of her cervical spine you note paraspinal muscle hypertonicity and tenderness along right articular pillar at C3 and just below the inion.  Which is the appropriate tender point to treat first?

1. The tender point that is the most tender to the patient.
2. The inion tender point as this corresponds to C1 which is the most proximal.
3. The tender point at C3 articular pillar as this is the most distal.
4. Treat the somatic dysfunction that you find at C3 and C1 with another method and then recheck the tender points.
5. Attempt to find a position that treats both at the same time.

The correct answer is: A
When using counterstrain to treat somatic dysfunctions, it is advised to identify the tender point that is the most tender first and treat.  Then reassess the other tender points.  Often the other tender points will resolve.  If you find multiple tender points in a row that are equally as tender you can elect to treat the middle tender point first, as well as proximal to distal.

Answer B: Although it is advised to treat the more proximal tender point first if several tender points lie in a row (i.e. posterior tender point for C3 and C4); or the middle if there are three or more tender points in a row (posterior tender points at C3-5). However, if one is far more tender you would elect to treat that tender point first then reassess and proceed accordingly.


Answer C: As stated in previous explanation, if multiple tender points of equal severity are identified in a row you would elect to treat the tender point either in the middle (if 3 or more) or more proximal first.


Answer D: Although this could be an option for treatment as tender points typically correspond with somatic dysfunction and can be alleviated by treating the associated somatic dysfunction, you have already stated in the question that you plan to treat the patient with counterstrain and further details regarding an associated somatic dysfunction is not provided in the case. Therefore you will be treating the corresponding somatic dysfunctions by treating the tender points.


Answer E: The typical position for the treatment of posterior C1 located just below the inion is marked flexion and the typical treatment for posterior C3 is flexion, side-bend towards and rotate away. Therefore it would be unlikely you would find a position that would treat the structures causing the somatic dysfunction at both levels simultaneously. In addition, your positioning is fine tuned to treat the exact level for which you have identified the tender point and somatic dysfunction. It would be best to treat the most tender point first and reassess the other tender point.




Bottom Line: When treating patients with counterstrain, if multiple tender points are identified in a given region, treat the most tender of the points first then reassess the other tender points.

For more information, see:
DiGiovanna E, Schiowitz S, Dowling D. An Osteopathic Approach to Diagnosis and Treatment, Third Edition. Philadelphia, PA: Lippincott Williams and Wilkins; 2005: pp. 147-149.

Nicholas A, Nicholas E. Atlas of Osteopathic Techniques, Third Edition. Philadelphia, PA: Wolters Kluwer; 2016: pp. 154, 162, 165.

Link: "Multiple Myeloma" on Medscape.


Patient Presentation: Fatigue & Weakness
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Hematology/Oncology
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 64-year-old female presents to the office with the complaints of fatigue, weakness, and a depressed mood for the last year. Review of systems reveals constipation and progressive back pain. Vital signs reveal a temperature of 37°C (98.6°F), a heart rate of 75/min, and an oxygen saturation of 97% on room air. Physical exam reveals conjunctival pallor, deep tendon reflexes +1/4 globally, and point tenderness with percussion over the L2 vertebra. Laboratory studies/values are shown in the table. The most likely diagnosis is

WBC  7x103/μL
Hgb 10.8 g/dL
Platelets 200x103/μL
Na 140 mEq/L
K 4.3 mEq/L
Cl 100 mEq/L
CO2 24 mEq/L
BUN 30 mg/dL
Cr 1.9 mg/dL
Ca 13.5 mg/dL                   
Total protein 9 g/dL
Albumin 3 g/dL
AST 25 U/L
ALT 28 U/L
Total bilirubin 1 mg/dL
Alkaline phosphatase        300 U/L

1. chronic lymphocytic leukemia
2. iron deficiency anemia
3. major depressive disorder
4. malnutrition
5. multiple myeloma

The correct answer is: E
Multiple myeloma is a hematologic malignancy characterized by plasma cell proliferation in the bone marrow. The excessive amounts of plasma cells interfere with production of other bone marrow cells, which can lead to leukopenia, anemia, and thrombocytopenia. In addition, the proliferating plasma cells release large amounts of antibodies into the blood. The clinical symptoms of multiple myeloma include back/bone pain, fatigue, weakness, weight loss, pathologic fractures, increased susceptibility to infections, lytic bone lesions and pathologic fractures, and renal impairment. Laboratory abnormalities include hypercalcemia, elevated alkaline phosphatase, and an elevated protein gap (total protein – albumin) which is normally 3-4 g/dL (5-6 g/dL in this patient). Multiple myeloma is typically a disease of the elderly with the median age 68-years-old and 70-years-old for men and women, respectively. 

The malignant cells of MM, plasma cells, and plasmacytoid lymphocytes are the most mature cells of B-lymphocytes. B-cell maturation is associated with a programmed rearrangement of DNA sequences in the process of encoding the structure of mature immunoglobulins. It is characterized by overproduction of monoclonal immunoglobulin G (IgG), immunoglobulin A (IgA), and/or light chains, which may be identified with serum protein electrophoresis (SPEP) or urine protein electrophoresis (UPEP). The role of cytokines in the pathogenesis of MM is an important area of research. Interleukin (IL)–6 is also an important factor promoting the in vitro growth of myeloma cells. Other cytokines are tumor necrosis factor and IL-1b.

Plasma-cell proliferation causes extensive skeletal destruction with osteolytic lesions, anemia, and hypercalcemia. Mechanisms for hypercalcemia include bony involvement and, possibly, humoral mechanisms. Isolated plasmacytomas (which affect 2-10% of patients) lead to hypercalcemia through production of the osteoclast-activating factor. Destruction of bone and its replacement by tumor may lead to pain, spinal cord compression, and pathologic fracture. The mechanism of spinal cord compression symptoms may be the development of an epidural mass with compression, a compression fracture of a vertebral body destroyed by multiple myeloma, or, rarely, an extradural mass. With pathologic fracture, bony involvement is typically lytic in nature. Bone marrow infiltration by plasma cells results in neutropenia, anemia, and thrombocytopenia. In terms of bleeding, M components may interact specifically with clotting factors, leading to defective aggregation. The most common mechanisms of renal injury in MM are direct tubular injury, amyloidosis, or involvement by plasmacytoma. Renal conditions that may be observed include hypercalcemic nephropathy, hyperuricemia due to renal infiltration of plasma cells resulting in myeloma, light-chain nephropathy, amyloidosis, and glomerulosclerosis.The nervous system may be involved as a result of radiculopathy and/or cord compression due to nerve compression and skeletal destruction (amyloid infiltration of nerves). General pathophysiologic processes include hyperviscosity syndrome. This syndrome is infrequent in MM and occurs with IgG1, IgG3, or IgA. MM may involve sludging in the capillaries, which results in purpura, retinal hemorrhage, papilledema, coronary ischemia, or central nervous system (CNS) symptoms (eg, confusion, vertigo, seizure). Cryoglobulinemia causes Raynaud phenomenon, thrombosis, and gangrene in the extremities.




Answer A: Chronic lymphocytic leukemia is more common in elderly adults; however, the chronic leukemias are marked by prominent leukocytosis.


Answer B: Iron deficiency anemia is a microcytic anemia caused associated with total body iron depletion. In the elderly, iron deficiency anemia should prompt an evaluation for colon cancer. The above patient has other findings in addition to the anemia making this a less likely answer.


Answer C: Major depressive disorder is characterized by two weeks or more of sadness, loss of interest, changes in sleep patterns, and decreased energy levels. Medical conditions should be ruled out before making psychiatric diagnoses.


Answer D: While malnutrition can be associated with anemia and fatigue, you would not expect to see renal impairment or hypercalcemia.




Bottom Line: Multiple myeloma should be suspected in elderly adults with anemia, fatigue, and hypercalcemia. Laboratory abnormalities include hypercalcemia, elevated alkaline phosphatase, and an elevated protein gap.

COMBANK Insight : The characteristic findings in multiple myeloma can be remembered by the mnemonic CRAB (C= hyperCalcemia, Renal insufficiency, Anemia, Bone pain, lytic lesions, and fractures).

For more information, see:
"Multiple Myeloma" on Medscape.
Multiple Myeloma
