Link: "Cocaine Toxicity in Emergency Medicine" on Medscape.


Patient Presentation: Sensory Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Management
Body System: Cardiovascular System
Discipline: Emergency Medicine
A 23-year-old male is brought to the emergency department in police custody complaining of chest pain. History reveals he was involved in an altercation at a bar. He admits to recent ethanol consumption and intranasal cocaine use. He is complaining of left-sided chest pain and a rapid heart rate. Vital signs include temperature 98.9ºF, blood pressure 165/90 mmHg, heart rate 132/min, respiratory rate 20/min and oxygen saturation 96% on room air. Physical examination reveals a diaphoretic male with dilated pupils, tachycardia and a two-centimeter laceration above the left eyebrow. Electrocardiogram performed in the emergency department shows sinus tachycardia without evidence of ischemia. The most appropriate next step in management is

1. aspirin
2. heparin
3. lorazepam
4. metoprolol
5. nitroglycerin

The correct answer is: C
This patient is presenting with cocaine-induced chest pain. Typical presentation includes diaphoresis, tachycardia, hypertension, mydriasis, agitation and flushed skin. Patients may develop chest pain even in the absence of coronary artery disease. This is due to coronary vasospasm, which causes angina. It is important to avoid the use of beta-receptor blocking agents in patients with cocaine-induced chest pain, as there is a risk of unopposed alpha-receptor stimulation, which can exacerbate hypertension and vasoconstriction.  Benzodiazepines are the first-line agents used in the treatment of cocaine-induced chest pain as they mitigate the sedative and anticonvulsive effects as well as aid in controlling hypertension and tachycardia associated with amphetamine toxicity.

Answer A: Although it would not be harmful to administer aspirin in this patient, its antiplatelet effects would not help to alleviate any of the symptoms caused by cocaine and is, therefore, not the best answer choice.


Answer B: In patients with acute myocardial infarction (AMI), heparin is routinely administered as an antithrombotic agent. Although not impossible, it is unlikely for a young otherwise healthy male to be having an AMI and more likely for his chest pain to be caused by vasospasm.


Answer D: Beta-receptor blocking agents, such as metoprolol, should be avoided in-patient with cocaine-induced chest pain as there is a theoretical risk of worsening hypertension and vasoconstriction.


Answer E: Nitroglycerin relaxes vascular smooth muscle and decreases blood pressure and may help to reduce cardiac pain and pulmonary edema but is generally not a first-line agent in cocaine-induced chest pain.




Bottom Line: Benzodiazepines are the first-line agent in patients with cocaine-induced chest pain.

For more information, see:
"Cocaine Toxicity in Emergency Medicine" on Medscape.

Link: Conduct disorder on Medscape


Patient Presentation: Cognitive Difficulties
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Psychiatry
Discipline: Psychiatry
A 13-year-old healthy male presents to his physician for a facial laceration. His mother is worried because he has been getting into trouble and recently stole his father's watch. His parents have tried multiple methods of punishment but none seem to work. Physical examination reveals a male with an aloof and distant personality.

The most likely diagnosis is

1. attention deficit/hyperactivity disorder
2. conduct disorder
3. intermittent explosive disorder
4. oppositional defiant disorder
5. schizoaffective disorder

The correct answer is: B
Conduct disorder is a mental health disorder that manifest in children and adolescents. Conduct disorder is characterized by oppositional and deviant behaviors. This individual has difficulty following rules. They exhibit antisocial behaviors such as fighting with peers, parents and authority figures. Criminality with stealing, vandalism and lying are common manifestations. Youngsters with conduct disorder often display cruelty to animals or people and/or start fires to cause intentional damage. Patients with conduct disorder often run away from home or stay out at night despite parental objections or consequences. These patients often have coexisting adjustment conditions such as learning disorders, mood disorders and/or substance abuse problems. Lastly, in conduct disorder this pattern of behavior has been present for at least 12 months and manifested before the age of 10 years. Conduct disorder usually appears in childhood as oppositional defiant behavior. Nearly one half of patients with oppositional defiant disorder develop conduct disorder by adolescence.

Answer A: Attention deficit/hyperactivity disorder (ADHD) is characterized by hyperactivity, behavior disinhibition, inattention and distractibility.


Answer C: A patient with intermittent explosive disorder presents with sudden, unpredictable outbursts that can be physical or verbal in nature. Patients with intermittent explosive disorder deny intent to harm people or animals.


Answer D: A patient with oppositional defiant disorder (ODD) presents with chronic argumentativeness and refusal to comply with adult requests. Although many features of conduct disorder overlap with those of ODD, patients with ODD do not display physical aggression and do not have problems with the law.


Answer E: Schizoaffective disorder is a mental disorder that combines features of both schizophrenia and a mood disorder. Clinical manifestations of schizoaffective disorder include hallucinations, delusions and disorganized thinking with elements of depression or mania. To diagnose schizoaffective disorder the patient must demonstrate psychosis for 2 weeks without evidence of a mood disorder. In addition, there must be a period in which the patient has features of both a mood disorder and schizophrenia. The symptoms of the mood disorder must be present for a majority if the time of the illness. This patient does not report periods of delusions or depressed mood, therefore schizoaffective disorder is not a likely diagnosis.




Bottom Line: Conduct disorder is characterized by aggressive behavior and a deliberate disregard for others.

For more information, see:
Conduct disorder on Medscape

Link: "Idiopathic Thrombocytopenic Purpura" on Medscape.


Patient Presentation: Bleeding
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Management
Body System: Hematology/Oncology
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 36-year-old male presents to the office with the complaints of easy bruising and bleeding from the nose and gums for the past two days' duration. History reveals the patient had an upper respiratory infection three weeks ago that resolved with supportive care. Physical examination reveals crusted blood and petechia on the gingiva and nares. Laboratory analysis reveals a leukocyte count of 8,000/mm3, a hemoglobin of 13 g/dL, a hematocrit of 35%, platelets 30,000/mm3. The most appropriate treatment is

1. aspirin
2. heparin
3. platelet transfusion
4. splenectomy
5. steroids

The correct answer is: E
The patient has immune thrombocytopenic purpura (ITP). ITP is a disease of peripheral platelet destruction in the absence of another identifiable cause, and is most often caused by circulating autoantibodies attacking host platelets.  Bone marrow production of platelets is typically normal or slightly increased in patients with ITP, as are the number of megakaryocytes identified if bone marrow biopsy is performed. Acute ITP typically follows an infection and lasts less than 2 months, whereas chronic ITP often lasts longer than 6 months and does not have an identifiable inciting factor. Patients can present with life threatening bleeding as a result of their thrombocytopenia, and in this case, the ABCs and acute treatment with transfusion is indicated. In a stable patient the treatment of ITP is corticosteroids and IVIg.

Answer A: Aspirin is an irreversible inhibitor of platelets and should not be used in patients with thrombocytopenia.


Answer B: Heparin should not be used in patients who are at risk of bleeding due to thrombocytopenia. Additionally, heparin may exacerbate ITP by causing heparin induced thrombocytopenia.


Answer C: Platelet transfusion is only given in cases of life-threatening and serious hemorrhagic episodes. This patient is currently stable and should undergo medical treatment with steroids and/or IVIg.


Answer D: Splenectomy is the treatment of choice in recurrent and refractory cases of ITP. This patient should undergo a trial of medical therapy before a splenectomy is considered.




Bottom Line: Medical treatment with corticosteroids and/or IVIG is appropriate in a stable patient with new ITP. Transfusion is reserved for unstable patients and surgery is reserved for patients with refractory cases.

COMBANK Insight : In many disease processes, it is important to know the treatment for patients who are 1) acutely ill, 2) stable, 3) have medically-refractory disease. Surgery is typically the last line therapy for these diseases.

For more information, see:
"Idiopathic Thrombocytopenic Purpura" on Medscape.

insert Link
Patient Presentation: Musculoskeletal Difficulties And Muscular Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Management
Body System: Musculoskeletal System
Discipline: Osteopathic Principle and Practice
A 20 year old college student comes in with right sided chest pain with every breath. He says it started after a vigorous weight lifting workout over the weekend. On exam it is noted that ribs 5-7 on the right move easily with exhalation but do not move superiorly with inhalation. In order to perform an HVLA technique to treat this somatic rib dysfunction the hands are placed in a similar way to thoracic HVLA. You stand on the side opposite the dysfunctional rib (in this case, the left side), flex and sidebend the head and torso left, and

1. Place your thenar eminence on the superior aspect of the 5th rib angle on the patient's back and thrust downward through the torso toward the fulcrum
2. Place your thenar eminence on the superior aspect of the 7th rib angle on the patient's back and thrust downward through the torso toward the fulcrum
3. Place your thenar eminence on the superior aspect of the 6th rib angle on the patient's back and thrust downward through the torso toward the fulcrum
4. Place your thenar eminence below the 5th rib angle on the patient's back, on the inferior aspect, and thrust upward through the torso toward the fulcrum
5. Place your thenar eminence below the 7th rib angle on the patient's back, on the inferior aspect, and thrust upward through the torso toward the fulcrum

The correct answer is: A
When preparing to treat rib somatic dysfunctions, it is important to first accurately diagnose the group of ribs that are restricted, followed by diagnosing the key rib.  Treatment should always be directed at the key rib.  Use the BITE mneumonic to diagnose the key rib. The Bottom rib in a group exhibiting an Inhalation somatic dysfunction is the key rib. The Top rib in a group exhibiting an Exhalation somatic dysfunction is the key rib.

In this case, Ribs 5-7 are stuck in exhalation (restricted moving into inhalation) so in order to move them superiorly in the front you would want to thrust downward on the posterior rib angle of rib 5.  Pump handle ribs 2-10 remain down instead of moving cephalad with inhalation and move on a transverse axis. Bucket handle ribs 7-10 do not move laterally with inhalation but move on an AP axis.

With a group of exhaled ribs you treat the uppermost rib of the group first so that the others may follow. With a group in an inhaled somatic dysfunction you treat the most inferior rib first.  The sequence of treating a rib dysfunction is to first treat any thoracic spine dysfunction, then the treat the ribs and the sternum after.




Answers B & C: When treating a group exhalation somatic dysfunction, the treatment needs to be directed at the key rib which is the upper most rib in the group, which in this case is Rib 5.


Answers D & E: The fulcrum (thenar eminence) is placed on the superior aspect of the key rib's rib angle, aiming downward when treating Exhalation Somatic Dysfunctions, not on the inferior aspect of the rib angle aiming upward as described in this answer choice.

Patient Presentation: Musculoskeletal Difficulties And Muscular Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Management
Body System: Musculoskeletal System
Discipline: Obstetrics/Gynecology
A 34-year-old G4P3 female presents to the office at 5-weeks gestation with the complaint of worsening joint pains. Past medical history reveals rheumatoid arthritis. The patient recently stopped taking her medication because of her pregnancy. Which of the following medications is the safest to administer during pregnancy?

1. Azathioprine
2. Methotrexate
3. Penicillamine
4. Phenytoin
5. Prednisone

The correct answer is: E
Treatment of autoimmune diseases is difficult during pregnancy due to the teratogenic nature of many commonly used medications. Rheumatoid arthritis symptoms typically improve during pregnancy and worsen after, however it is not unusual for patients to have a flare during pregnancy. During a flare-up of rheumatoid arthritis, low doses of prednisone can be administered with relative safety in pregnant patients.

Answer A: Azathioprine is teratogenic and its use should be avoided during pregnancy.


Answer B: Methotrexate is used to induce abortion of fetuses early on in pregnancy and should be avoided in this situation.


Answer C: Penicillamine is teratogenic and its use should be avoided during pregnancy.


Answer D: Phenytoin is teratogenic and its use should be avoided during pregnancy. It can lead to fetal hydantoin syndrome.




Bottom Line: Prednisone, at low doses, is safe to use in pregnant patients suffering from flare-ups of rheumatoid arthritis.

COMBANK Insight : Teratogenic medications can have drastic effects on a maturing fetus. Know which drugs should be avoided during pregnancy.

"Rheumatoid arthritis and pregnancy: beyond the basics" on UpToDate.

Link: "Wernicke's Encephalopathy" on UpToDate.


Patient Presentation: Substance Abuse
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Scientific Understanding of Health & Disease Mechanisms
Body System: Asymptomatic & General Symptoms
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 52-year-old male is brought to the emergency department by police after he was found behind a local restaurant vomiting. Upon presentation, the patient is combative, uncooperative, and smells of alcohol. Physical examination reveals signs of decreased sensation of the lower extremities. When gazing to the left, the right eye only minimally adducts while the left eye abducts with nystagmus. The patient is most likely suffering from a deficiency in

1. folate
2. vitamin B1
3. vitamin B6
4. vitamin B12
5. zinc

The correct answer is: B
The most likely diagnosis is Wernicke’s encephalopathy, which occurs secondary to a deficiency in Vitamin B1 (thiamine). The classic presentation of Wernicke’s encephalopathy includes presentation in an alcoholic with the triad of encephalopathy, ophthalmoplegia, and ataxia. Failure to initiate immediate therapy with thiamine can lead to coma and even death. Wernicke's may be precipitated by giving a patient IV fluids containing glucose that lacks B1.

Answer A: Folate deficiency may lead to the development of a megaloblastic anemia. However, folate deficiency does not cause neurologic symptoms.


Answer C: Deficiencies in Vitamin B6 (pyridoxine) can lead to convulsions, hyperirritability, and peripheral neuropathy. Look for patients recently exposed to isoniazid or oral contraception.


Answer D: Vitamin B12 deficiency leads to the gradual onset of symmetric paresthesias, bowel and bladder dysfunction, leg stiffness, and a stocking-glove sensory neuropathy. Look for this diagnosis in patients with pernicious anemia, vegetarian diets, and the individual's status-post gastric or ileal resection. Symptoms are caused by degeneration of the posterior columns within the spinal cord.


Answer E: Zinc deficiency typically leads to symptoms of alopecia and facial rash.




Bottom Line: Suspect thiamine deficiency in homeless alcoholics demonstrating symptoms consistent with the triad of encephalopathy, ophthalmoplegia, and ataxia.

COMBANK Insight : Wernicke’s encephalopathy can almost always be reversed with immediate administration of thiamine. Remember, however, to give thiamine prior to glucose always, as this condition can be elicited by high-dose glucose administration.

For more information, see:
"Wernicke's Encephalopathy" on UpToDate.

Patient Presentation: Trauma
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Management
Body System: Cardiovascular System
Discipline: Emergency Medicine
A 27-year-old G1P0 female at 36 weeks' gestation is brought to the emergency department by an ambulance after being involved in a motor vehicle accident. She is coherent and able to speak. Vital signs reveal a blood pressure of 98/58 mmHg, a heart rate of 90/min, a temperature of 99.0°F, and a respiratory rate of 20/min. Physical examination reveals a head laceration and multiple scrapes on her body. During the evaluation the most appropriate position to limit hypotension is

1. left lateral decubitus
2. reverse Trendelenburg
3. right lateral decubitus
4. supine
5. Trendelenburg

The correct answer is: A
When examining a pregnant trauma patient, be mindful of the hemodynamics in relation to the potential positional hypotension effect of supine positioning. Due to an enlarged uterus, positional hypotension is caused by aortocaval compression and may decrease cardiac output by upwards of 25% in the supine position. To avoid hemodynamic instability, position the patient in the left lateral decubitus, use a right hip wedge, or manual displacement of the uterus. Always be considerate and focus on the well-being of both the patient and the fetus.

Answers B & C & E: Pregnant patients should be placed in the left lateral decubitus position.


Answer D: Placing the patient in the supine position does not take in consideration of the potential hemodynamic instability that may be caused by a pregnant female in relation to positioning.




Bottom Line: To avoid compression of the inferior vena cava in a patient who is further along in her pregnancy, the left lateral decubitus position should be used.

COMBANK Insight : Trauma is the leading cause of maternal death, accounting for up to 46% of cases.

For more information, see:
"Pregnancy Trauma" on Medscape.

Patient Presentation: Sensory Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Management
Body System: Musculoskeletal System
Discipline: Family Medicine
A 72-year-old male presents to the office with the complaints of progressive right knee pain. The patient is 6 feet tall and weighs 215 lbs.  The patient reports the pain is worse after activity. The pain is worse at the end of the day and morning stiffness is minimal lasting only a few minutes. Physical examination reveals right knee crepitus and joint line tenderness without obvious effusion. The best initial step in management is

1. acetaminophen
2. weight loss and lifestyle modification
3. intraarticular glucocorticoids
4. oral non-steroidal anti-inflammatories
5. topical anti-inflammatories

The correct answer is: B
In osteoarthritis symptoms are typically progressive. Pain is the most common complaint, and is often worse with activity and relieved by rest. As the disease progresses patients may have pain with rest as well. Morning stiffness, if present, typically resolves within 30 minutes, unlike in inflammatory arthritis where morning stiffness persists for greater than 60 minutes. On examination, joint effusions, crepitus, and palpable osteophytic changes may be present.  The goal of treatment is to control the pain and swelling, minimize disability, and improve quality of life.  Treatment should be individualized, however it should generally start with non-pharmacological interventions first.  These may include exercise programs and counseling regarding healthy lifestyle habits and weight-loss.

Answer A: Acetaminophen is generally regarded as the first pharmacologic intervention of choice. It is used over non-steroidal anti-inflammatories initially because it causes fewer gastrointestinal issues and is generally more tolerable. Patients must be instructed on the hepatic complications that may arise from over exposure to acetaminophen and educated regarding the frequency with which it appears in common combination over the counter medications and cautioned about combining such medications.


Answer C: Intraarticular glucocorticoids are generally reserved for patients with persistent symptoms in one or a few joints despite the use of both nonpharmacologic interventions and oral or topical nonsteroidal anti-inflammatories, or in those in whom non-steroidal anti-inflammatories are contraindicated.


Answer D: Oral non-steroidal anti-inflammatories are frequently used in patients whose symptoms are inadequately controlled with acetaminophen or in whom there is obvious joint inflammation as acetaminophen is not an anti-inflammatory. They may be used in combination or in replacement of acetaminophen.


Answer E: Topical anti-inflammatories may be used in patients who cannot tolerate, or have a contraindication to, oral anti-inflammatories. They may also be used in those who have an increased risk for adverse effects of oral anti-inflammatories, such as those older than 75 years of age.




Bottom Line: Classic clinical criteria for diagnosis of OA of the knee includes knee pain plus three of the following six characteristics: Age greater than 50 years, morning stiffness lasting for < 30 minutes, crepitus with active range of motion of the knee bony tenderness, bony enlargement, and no palpable warmth.   Treatment should be individualized, but generally begin with non-pharmacologic interventions if appropriate and progress in a step-wise fashion.

For more information, see:

"Osteoarthritis" on Medscape

Patient Presentation: Sensory & CNS Difficulties
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Nervous System
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 53-year-old male presents to the office after he burned his left hand. He says that four days ago he was tending the grill at a family get together. He attempted multiple times with his left hand to see if the charcoal was ready by holding his hand over the briquettes. Though they were glowing, he could not appreciate the hot temperature. Upon further questioning, he also reports some neck pain that radiates down his arms and notes that the muscles in his hands are not as bulky as they once were. Physical examination reveals there is decreased muscle bulk bilaterally in his intrinsic hand muscles. He has decreased strength bilaterally in his distal upper extremities. He has loss of thermal sense and pinprick over his posterior neck and shoulders and down both arms. Deep tendon reflexes reveal the following bilaterally: biceps 1/1, triceps 0/0, brachioradialis 1/1, knee jerks 3/3, and ankle jerks 3/3. He has spastic tone in his legs and his right plantar response is extensor. Which of the following is the most likely diagnosis?

1. amyotrophic lateral sclerosis
2. Guillain-Barre syndrome
3. rhombencephalitis
4. syringomyelia
5. transverse myelitis

The correct answer is: D
This patient likely has syringomyelia. A central lesion in his cervical cord, such a syrinx, can explain all of his findings. He has a dissociative sensory loss and he has intact vibration and proprioception but loss of pain and temperature over his shoulders, arms and hands (a cape-like distribution). This is due to damage to the crossing fibers of the spinothalamic tract in the anterior white commissure. The posterior columns however, are spared. He has weakness, atrophy and areflexia (lower motor neuron signs) in his upper extremities due to the direct involvement of the anterior horn cells in the ventral horns. The upper motor neuron signs in his lower extremities (upgoing toe, spasticity, hyperreflexia) are attributable to the effect of the syrinx on the descending corticospinal tract. This patient will need an MRI of his cervical spine and evaluation by neurosurgery for possible drainage of the syrinx.

Answer A: ALS is a syndrome characterized by deterioration of anterior horn cells. Exam findings will show a mixture of upper and lower motor neuron findings as this patient had. ALS is a pure muscle disorder. This patient had sensory changes, which would not be expected in ALS as no sensory structures are affected. ALS, also, almost always begins asymmetrically while this patient had symmetric findings. ALS is a progressive, devastating disease. Due to effects on the respiratory and bulbar musculature, only about 4% of patients survive longer than 10 years.


Answer B: Guillian-Barre syndrome (GBS) is unlikely in this scenario. While GBS patients do typically have sensory findings, the more robust finding is a progressive ascending weakness. It is classically taught that the weakness begins in the legs and ascends upwards, but not every GBS case follows this pattern. Almost universally, GBS patients will have absent reflexes.


Answer C: Rhombencephalitis is an older term and usually used interchangeably with brainstem encephalitis (though anatomically the rhombencephalon is the pons, cerebellum and medulla, but not the midbrain). There are many causes of rhombencephalitis-infectious, autoimmune and paraneoplastic. Usually, when the term “rhombencephalitis” is used, it is referring to an infectious cause. The most common infectious cause is Listeria. This patient did not have any signs or symptoms that are referable to the brainstem such as cranial nerve deficits. Thus, rhombencephalitis is unlikely.


Answer E: Transverse myelitis is a demyelinating syndrome. It is unlikely in this case as demyelinating syndromes are more likely to affect young women. Transverse myelitis would also be expected to have a different presentation. Usually, there is full cord or hemi-cord involvement (Brown-Sequard Syndrome) at one or two spinal levels. The exam would more likely to show a loss of sensation at a distinct spinal level. The time course would also be expected to be slower as demyelinating lesions develop over days to weeks. Transverse myelitis patients will also describe Lhermitte’s sign, which is an electric sensation down the back with flexion of the neck.




Bottom Line: A central lesion in his cervical cord, such a syrinx, can explain a dissociative sensory loss with intact vibration and proprioception but loss of pain and temperature over the shoulders, arms and hands in a cape-like distribution.

COMBANK Insight : This case demonstrates the importance of neuroanatomy. With knowledge of the ascending and descending tracts of the spinal cord, his findings can be easily localized.

For more information, see:
Chapter 44. Diseases of the Spinal Cord. In: Ropper AH, Samuels MA. eds.Adams and Victor's Principles of Neurology, 9e. New York: McGraw-Hill; 2009.http://accessmedicine.mhmedical.com/content.aspx?bookid=354&Sectionid=40236358.

Patient Presentation: Sensory Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Diagnostic Technologies
Body System: Gastrointestinal System
Discipline: Surgery
A 72-year-old female presents to the emergency department with the complaint of sudden-onset abdominal pain. She is a long-term resident at a nearby nursing home. History reveals that the pain is diffuse in nature and associated with nausea and vomiting. Physical examination reveals a rigid abdomen and tenderness to palpation. You note paravertebral muscle spasms and increased warmth from T12-L2, and a tender nodule on the left thigh just anterior to the IT band.  A radiograph of the abdomen is obtained as shown in the exhibit.
Question 1 of 2 in this set
The most likely diagnosis is

1. intussusception
2. pneumoperitoneum
3. Sigmoid volvulus
4. stool impaction
5. viral gastroenteritis

The correct answer is: C
This patient is demonstrating classic symptoms of a sigmoid volvulus, which is confirmed by the plain film of her abdomen. Sigmoid volvulus is the most common type of volvulus of the large bowel and is responsible for nearly 10% of all intestinal obstructions. Plain films of the abdomen classically demonstrate a “double-loop” sign, which is seen in half of the patients with this diagnosis. Look for markedly distended sigmoid loops in the shape of a comma.

In this vignette we also see information about TART changes from T12-L2, representative of viscerosomatic reflexes from the sigmoid colon, and a tender nodule on the left proximal thigh, representative of a Chapman point for the sigmoid colon.  This information can help you determine the location of pathology in the abdomen but does not help you differentiate from pathology in the same region of the colon.

Answer A: Intussusception most commonly occurs in children between 5 and 10 months of age and occurs when the intestine prolapses, or “telescopes” into another section of the intestine. Symptoms include nausea, bilious vomiting and intermittent moderate to severe cramping of the abdomen. Children who are too young to speak may cry and draw their knees up to their chest. They may also present with bloody "currant jelly" stools.


Answer B: Pneumoperitoneum is caused by perforation of the GI tract, most commonly due to a bleeding ulcer or another perforated viscous. Diagnosis is usually made by history and upright abdominal films, which show air-fluid levels.


Answer D: Fecal impaction occurs when a large mass of dry stool lodges in the rectum causing chronic constipation and does not demonstrate x-ray findings consistent with what is seen in the above scenario.


Answer E: Viral gastroenteritis is a common diagnosis and is usually self-limiting. Diarrhea is the most common presenting symptom, although nausea and vomiting may also be noted. The radiographic findings in this patient's case do not indicate gastroenteritis.




Bottom Line: Sigmoid volvulus is responsible for nearly 10% of all intestinal obstructions and presents with abdominal pain, distension, and absolute constipation. Plain films of the abdomen reveal dilated loops of bowel.

COMBANK Insight : Commonly, patients with volvulus are elderly, debilitated, and bedridden.

For more information, see:
UpToDate

Link: UpToDate


Patient Presentation: Sensory Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Gastrointestinal System
Discipline: Surgery
A 72-year-old female presents to the emergency department with the complaint of sudden-onset abdominal pain. She is a long-term resident at a nearby nursing home. History reveals that the pain is diffuse in nature and associated with nausea and vomiting. Physical examination reveals a rigid abdomen and tenderness to palpation. You note paravertebral muscle spasms and increased warmth from T12-L2, and a tender nodule on the left thigh just anterior to the IT band.  A radiograph of the abdomen is obtained as shown in the exhibit.
Question 2 of 2 in this set
The most common etiologic agent of this diagnosis is

1. chronic constipation
2. viral infection
3. history of intussusception
4. large hernia incarceration
5. prior abdominal surgeries

The correct answer is: A
The most common cause of sigmoid volvulus is due to chronic constipation. Chronic constipation causes lengthening and dilation of the bowel. Elongation of the sigmoid colon can lead to creation of a redundant loop. Patients who develop sigmoid volvulus from chronic constipation include those in nursing homes, institutionalized patients with neuropsychiatric disorders, and those who use an excess amount of laxatives, cathartics and enemas. Diagnosis can be made with an abdominal x-ray, which often demonstrate a "coffee bean" appearance and evidence of obstruction at the point of rotation.

Answer B: Viral diseases are thought to play a role in intussusception, although studies have not conclusively established this. Intussusception is a rare cause of intestinal obstruction in adults. Sigmoid volvulus is a much more likely diagnosis.


Answer C: Intussusception is the most common cause of intestinal obstruction in children less than two years old. Intussusception is telescoping of a proximal portion of the bowel into a distal segment of the bowel at the ileocecal junction. Entrapment of the mesentery leads to the development of congestion, edema and ultimately ischemia of the intestine. Perforation and peritonitis may result if it not immediately addressed. Most cases of intussusception are idiopathic, however there have been some association with the rotavirus vaccine. Clinical manifestations include acute onset of severe, crampy and worsening abdominal pain. As the obstruction progresses patients report bilious emesis. Physical examination is remarkable for a sausage-shaped abdominal mass. History of intussusception is not a common cause of developing sigmoid volvulus.


Answer D: Large hernias are actually less likely to strangulate than small hernias and lead to less bowel ischemia. In addition, internal herniations are a less common cause of sigmoid volvulus.


Answer E: Prior abdominal surgeries are a less common cause of sigmoid volvulus. However, prior bowel surgery is a risk factor to develop a small bowel obstruction. Most small bowel obstructions occur in patients with prior abdominal surgeries secondary to intra-abdominal adhesion formation. Surgical adhesions begin immediately following surgery, and are part of the tissue healing process. Adhesions become dangerous when they impair peristalsis and result in obstruction. Other etiologies of small bowel obstruction include malignant tumors (20%), hernias (10%), inflammatory bowel disease (5%) and volvulus (3%). Risk factors for mechanical obstruction include anything that increases the chance of scar tissue development in the GI tract such as the following: previous abdominal surgeries (most common), history of peptic ulcer disease, diverticulitis, Crohn’s disease, cancers of the gastrointestinal tract, and Hirschsprung’s disease (in infants and children).




Bottom Line: Sigmoid volvulus is a common cause of intestinal obstruction in an elderly patient with chronic constipation. The most appropriate first step in imaging is to order an abdominal x-ray.

COMBANK Insight : It is important to be able to recognize volvulus on abdominal x-ray.

For more information, see:
UpToDate


Last Updated: 12/3/2016

Link: "Status epilepticus" on Medscape.


Patient Presentation: Sensory & CNS Difficulties
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Management
Body System: Nervous System
Discipline: Emergency Medicine
A 45-year-old male is brought to the emergency department by ambulance after having a witnessed seizure. He is currently alert, but not oriented to himself or his surroundings. After his initial assessment, he begins to seize violently. 5mg of midazolam is administered intravenously yet the patient continues to seize. An additional 10mg of midazolam is administered intravenously, yet the patient continues to shake violently for 6 minutes. Vital signs reveal a blood pressure of 190/100 mmHg, a heart rate of 130/min, a respiratory rate of 44/min, and a room air oxygen saturation of 88%. The most appropriate course of action is

1. call for a stat neurology consultation
2. continue using benzodiazepines intravenously until his seizure stops
3. prepare to intubate the patient using propofol as the induction agent
4. provide bag-valve-mask ventilation until his seizure stops
5. roll him on his side to prevent aspiration

The correct answer is: C
This is an example of status epilepticus (SE). Definitions vary, but generally speaking, SE is continuous seizures for longer than 5 minutes or recurrent seizures for longer than 30 minutes. This patient had two sequential seizures without clearing from his post-ictal state, followed by continued seizure activity following high dose benzodiazepines. Benzodiazepines are the first line treatment for seizures. When this fails, one must resort to the ABC’s of patient care. This patient has no ability to control his airway if he continues to seize. Immediate recognition of this is critical. Intubation is indicated for airway protection. Propofol is the induction agent of choice because it is a powerful anti-epileptic drug (AED) as well as a commonly used induction agent for rapid-sequence-intubation.

Answer A: Call for a stat neurology consultation is incorrect. The patient needs immediate stabilization of his airway. Remember the ABCs. Airway, breathing, and circulation come before all else. Once the patient is stabilized (via intubation and sedation), you have a high priority to call neurology for continuation of care.


Answer B: Benzodiazepines are the first line treatment for seizures, and are effective in the majority of cases. This patient has failed high dose treatment with a benzodiazepine, and continues to seize. Therefore a different medication should be used. Frequently used alternatives are phenobarbital and propofol, which are both powerful medications used to suppress seizure activity. When using either of these medications, the respiratory drive will be decreased and therefore intubation is usually required.


Answer D: Bag-valve-mask (BVM) ventilation will not help this patient. In fact, forcing breaths into this patient will likely only induce aspiration and possibly produce aspiration pneumonitis. His oxygen saturation should be maintained by simple nasal cannula or non-rebreather mask that will not force air into the trachea or esophagus.


Answer E: This patient requires immediate protection of his airway. Rolling him onto his side may transiently help prevent aspiration, but has done nothing to treat the underlying pathology. A patient in SE can inhale saliva, blood and gastric contents regardless of the way they are positioned, thus making this answer incorrect.




Bottom Line: Status epilepticus is defined as more than 30 minutes of continuous seizure activity or two or more sequential seizures without full recovery of consciousness between seizures. Management includes high dose benzodiazepines and management of the ABCs.

COMBANK Insight : Remember the ABCs in all patients who are critically ill. You will have distractions and other management possibilities. When in doubt, be aggressive about protecting the patient by ensuring they have adequate airway protection/patency, spontaneous breathing, and adequate circulation. Do not move on until these are stable.

For more information, see:
"Status epilepticus" on Medscape.


Last Updated: 12/8/2016

Link: Step-Up to USMLE Step 2 CK


Patient Presentation: Digestive Difficulties
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Gastrointestinal System
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 26-year-old female presents to the emergency department with nausea and vomiting of 4 days duration. Past medical history is remarkable for a recent urinary tract infection for which she was treated with Bactrim one week prior. Physical examination reveals jaundice. Laboratory analysis is below:

white blood cell count 5.2 K/microliter
hemoglobin 8.2 g/dL
platelets 111 K/microliter
total bilirubin 11.0 mg/dL
direct bilirubin 5.7 mg/dL
AST 64 U/L
ALT 17 U/L
albumin 2.3 g/L
INR 1.5
Coombs  negative
Parvovirus IgM negative
LDH 409 U/L
Haptoglobin <20 mg/dL
ANA negative
Hepatitis A IgM negative
Hepatitis B core IgM  negative
Hepatitis B core IgG positive
Hepatitis B surface antibody     positive
Hepatitis C PCR negative
Ceruloplasmin  9 (Normal range 20-50 mg/dL)
   



Question 1 of 2 in this set
The most likely diagnosis is

1. alpha-1 antitrypsin deficiency
2. cholecystitis
3. hemochromatosis
4. primary biliary cirrhosis
5. Wilson's disease

The correct answer is: E
The patient in the above scenario is most likely suffering from Wilson’s disease, an autosomal-recessive disorder of copper secretion. The excess copper deposits in the brain, liver and cornea lead to psychiatric disturbances, liver dysfunction and Kayser-Fleischer rings in the cornea. Laboratory analysis will reveal decreased ceruloplasmin in the serum and increased urinary excretion of copper. Ceruloplasmin is a glycoprotein produced in the liver. It carries more than 95 percent of copper in blood plasma. This patient presents with a Coombs negative hemolytic anemia with evidence of liver dysfunction. She has evidence of prior exposure to hepatitis B with decreased levels of ceruloplasmin. Therefore, Wilson's disease is the most likely diagnosis for this patient.

Answer A: Alpha 1-antitrypsin (AAT) deficiency is a rare genetic disorder that causes early-onset panacinar emphysema. A mutation in the AAT gene prevents its release from hepatocytes where it is manufactured. AAT is responsible for protecting the alveoli from an enzyme that destroys the connective tissue of the lungs. As a result, there is an increase in alveolar destruction and patients develop emphysema. In addition, the excess AAT in the liver can destroy the hepatocytes leading to liver disease. Chronic liver disease is evident by the fourth decade of life and emphysema is seen by the fifth decade. Patients typically report life long pulmonary symptoms and often were misdiagnosed with asthma as a child. The initial symptoms of AAT deficiency include cough, sputum production, dyspnea and wheezing. This patient presents with signs and symptoms of liver disease without evidence of pulmonary involvement. Therefore, AAT deficiency is less likely.


Answer B: Cholecystitis is inflammation of the gallbladder that results from obstruction of the cystic duct from gallstones. Accumulation of bile in the gallbladder leads to bacterial proliferation and inflammation, pressure and swelling. Risk factors include age, female sex, pregnancy, obesity and rapid weight loss. A history of ingestion of fatty, greasy or fried foods one hour before the onset of symptoms is suggestive. Nausea, vomiting and fever are typically present. Lack of fever and risk factors in this patient makes this diagnosis less likely. In addition, hepatomegaly, jaundice and ascites are not commonly associated with cholecystitis.



Answer C: Hemochromatosis is an autosomal recessive disorder caused by iron accumulation in the liver leading to cirrhosis. Patients with hereditary hemochromatosis have an inborn error in iron metabolism, which results in progressive iron overload. The liver, heart, pancreas and skin are most commonly involved. Iron deposition in organs leads to tissue damage. Most patients are asymptomatic and are diagnosed on routine laboratory analysis. Development of diabetes mellitus occurs in almost half of patients due to iron deposition in the pancreas. Symptoms include severe fatigue, impotence and arthralgia. Hepatomegaly, cirrhosis with skin bronzing is most common physical examination signs of hemochromatosis. This patient does not present with hyperpigmentation or diabetes mellitus. Thus, hereditary hemochromatosis is not the likely diagnosis.


Answer D: Primary biliary cirrhosis (PBC) is an autoimmune disease that causes progressive cholestasis and end-stage liver disease. Females are more susceptible to disease and it commonly manifests around the fourth and sixth decades of life. Clinical manifestations of PBC include fatigue, pruritis, and right upper quadrant pain. Patients may also complain of dry eyes and dry mouth, resembling sicca syndrome. As the disease progresses, physical findings include hepatomegaly, hyper pigmentation, splengomegaly and jaundice. Signs of advanced disease include those of liver failure: ascites, spider nevi, palmar erythema and peripheral edema. In PBC, laboratory analysis is relevant for significant elevations in alkaline phosphatase, bilirubin and y-glutamyl transpeptidase. Laboratory analysis in this patient is not suggestive of PBC and her presentation does not suggest a diagnosis of PBC.





Bottom Line: Wilson’s disease is a genetic disorder of copper secretion leading to a deposition of copper in the liver, cornea and brain.

For more information, see:
Step-Up to USMLE Step 2 CK

Patient Presentation: Digestive Difficulties
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Management
Body System: Gastrointestinal System
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 26-year-old female presents to the emergency department with nausea and vomiting of 4 days duration. Past medical history is remarkable for a recent urinary tract infection for which she was treated with Bactrim one week prior. Physical examination reveals jaundice. Laboratory analysis is below:

white blood cell count 5.2 K/microliter
hemoglobin 8.2 g/dL
platelets 111 K/microliter
total bilirubin 11.0 mg/dL
direct bilirubin 5.7 mg/dL
AST 64 U/L
ALT 17 U/L
albumin 2.3 g/L
INR 1.5
Coombs  negative
Parvovirus IgM negative
LDH 409 U/L
Haptoglobin <20 mg/dL
ANA negative
Hepatitis A IgM negative
Hepatitis B core IgM  negative
Hepatitis B core IgG positive
Hepatitis B surface antibody     positive
Hepatitis C PCR negative
Ceruloplasmin  9 (Normal range 20-50 mg/dL)
   



Question 2 of 2 in this set
The most appropriate adjuvant therapy for this patient's current treatment is

1. phlebotomy
2. prophylactic antibiotics
3. methotrexate
4. supplemental zinc
5. ursodeoxycholic acid

The correct answer is: D
Wilson’s disease is a genetic disorder of copper excretion, causing copper to accumulate and deposit in tissue, especially hepatic, brain and ocular tissues. Treatment involves copper chelators, such as trientene or penicillamine, a low copper diet, vitamin B6 and zinc supplementation. Zinc decreases copper absorption from the GI tract.

Answer A: Phlebotomy is used to treat hemochromatosis and polycythemia vera.


Answer B: Prophylactic antibiotics are used occasionally in immunosuppressed patients, such as those with AIDS or sickle-cell anemia but they play no role in the treatment of Wilson’s disease.


Answer C: Methotrexate is an immunosuppressive medication, which is used in autoimmune diseases such as rheumatoid arthritis, psoriasis and occasionally primary biliary cirrhosis (PBC).


Answer E: Ursodeoxycholic acid decreases bile acid production and is used in the treatment of PBC and primary sclerosis cholangitis (PSC).




Bottom Line: Supplemental zinc is part of the treatment for Wilson’s disease.

Link: "Autoimmune Hepatitis" on Medscape.


Patient Presentation: Fatigue & Weakness
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Hepatobiliary System
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 22-year-old female presents to the office with the complaints of fatigue and low-grade fevers. History reveals for the past 6 months she has also had generalized body aches, anorexia, and progressive abdominal distention. Vital signs reveal a blood pressure of 118/80 mmHg, a heart rate of 72/min, a respiratory rate of 14/min, an oxygen saturation of 94% on room air, and a temperature of 38°C (100.4°F). Physical examination reveals scleral icterus, an enlarged liver, splenomegaly, and abdominal ascites. Laboratory examination reveals aspartate an aminotransferase of 200 U/L, alanine aminotransferase of 297 U/L, and the presence of anti-smooth muscle antibodies. The most likely diagnosis is

1. autoimmune hepatitis
2. hepatolenticular degeneration
3. primary biliary cirrhosis
4. primary sclerosing cholangitis
5. viral hepatitis

The correct answer is: A
While the differential diagnosis for the above presentation is broad, this case describes one possible presentation of autoimmune hepatitis. Autoimmune hepatitis is an autoimmune process of unknown etiology and is characterized by hepatocellular inflammation and necrosis. Autoimmune hepatitis may present as acute or chronic hepatitis, established cirrhosis, or rarely as fulminant hepatic failure. While this disease can present in patients of any age or gender, most often it presents in young women (70-80% of patients are women). The most common symptoms are malaise, fatigue, upper abdominal pain, and pruritus. Physical exam findings include hepatomegaly (83%) and jaundice (69%) in addition to other stigmata of liver disease. Serum aminotransferase levels are typically elevated in the 200-300 U/L range. The diagnosis of autoimmune hepatitis is made by laboratory serum testing including antibody testing and serum immunoglobulin levels. Liver biopsy is also helpful in making the diagnosis. The most specific serum antibody test for autoimmune hepatitis is the presence of anti-smooth muscle antibodies.

Answer B: Hepatolenticular degeneration, also known as Wilson’s disease, is an autosomal recessive genetic disorder caused by mutations in the ATP7B gene causing copper accumulation and deposition in tissues and manifests as neurological, psychiatric symptoms, and liver disease. This patient has only signs of cirrhosis and no mention of neurological problems. Wilson’s disease is diagnosed by a low serum ceruloplasmin level and high urinary copper excretion.


Answer C: Primary biliary cirrhosis is an autoimmune disease characterized by slow, progressive destruction of the small bile ducts of the liver resulting in cholestasis which overtime leads to scarring, fibrosis, and cirrhosis. Primary biliary cirrhosis is associated with the presence of antimitochondrial antibodies.


Answer D: Primary sclerosing cholangitis is an autoimmune disease characterized by inflammation and subsequent obstruction of both intra- and extrahepatic bile ducts resulting in cholestasis which can progress to cirrhosis, liver failure, and is associated with increased risk of hepatocellular carcinoma. 70-80% of people with primary sclerosing cholangitis have ulcerative colitis, however only 5% of people with ulcerative colitis develop primary sclerosing cholangitis. Primary sclerosing cholangitis is associated with the presence of serum perinuclear anti-neutrophil cytoplasmic antibodies (p-ANCA), which are also associated with a variety of other rheumatologic conditions, including ulcerative colitis.


Answer E: Viral hepatitis can either be acute or chronic due to hepatitis viral infections, most commonly hepatitis B and C viruses in the United States. Viral hepatitis is associated with elevated transaminases and a mixed hyperbilirubinemia (both indirect and direct bilirubin elevation). In addition, the patient may be positive for one of several virus-specific antibodies, not anti-smooth muscle antibodies.




Bottom Line: Autoimmune hepatitis should be considered in the differential of hepatitis. The most specific serum antibody test for autoimmune hepatitis is the presence of anti-smooth muscle antibodies.

COMBANK Insight : Memorizing specific antibodies associated with the most common rheumatologic diseases can score you easy points on test day.

For more information, see:
Le T, Bhushan V, Gagga HS. First Aid for the USMLE Step 2 CK: A Student-to-Student Guide. New York, NY: McGraw-Hill; 2010: 177-183.
"Autoimmune Hepatitis" on Medscape.

Patient Presentation: Musculoskeletal Difficulties And Muscular Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Management
Body System: Musculoskeletal System
Discipline: Family Medicine
A 30-year-old male presents to the urgent care clinic with right ankle pain and swelling.  History reveals that the patient suffered an inversion injury to his ankle while playing basketball a few hours prior.  Immediately after the injury the patient was able to bear weight with only minimal discomfort and ambulates into the clinic without an assistive device.  Evaluation reveals mild ecchymosis and minimal swelling over the lateral malleolus without tenderness to palpation.  There is also no tenderness to palpation over the base of the fifth metatarsal or dorsum of the foot.  The patient is able to evert, invert, dorsiflex and plantarflex the foot with only mild discomfort.  Giveway weakness is demonstrated with eversion against resistance.  You note tenderness around the fibular head and determine that the fibular head prefers posterior translation.  The most appropriate next step would be

1. to begin early range of motion exercises and physical therapy
2. to inject the ankle with a corticosteroid
3. to obtain a MRI of the ankle
4. to obtain x-ray imaging of the ankle
5. to place the patient in a CAM walker (fracture boot)

The correct answer is: A
Examination for this patient is most consistent with a Grade I ankle sprain.  The anterior talofibular ligament is the most commonly affected ligament and patients typically present with pain, mild swelling, and possibly mild ecchymosis over the sinus tarsi and possibly distal fibula. Based on the "Ottawa Ankle Rules" it is very unlikely that this patient has a fracture and thus does not require immediate imaging modalities.  According to the  "Ottawa Ankle Rules", X-Rays are only required if there is any pain in the malleolar zone and any one of the following: bone tenderness along the distal 6 cm of the posterior edge of the fibula or tip of the lateral malleolus, or bone tenderness along the distal 6 cm of the posterior edge of the tibia or tip of the medial malleolus, or an inability to bear weight both immediately and in the emergency department for four steps. There are also a set of "Ottawa Ankle Rules" which require X-Ray imaging only if there is any pain in the midfoot zone and any one of the following: bone tenderness at the navicular bone, bone tenderness at the base of the fifth metatarsal, or an inability to bear weight both immediately and in the emergency department for four steps. He was able to bear weight immediately after injury and he is not presenting with pain overlying the malleoli, over the navicular or at the base of the 5th metatarsal.  Early ROM and PT would be most appropriate in this case scenario.  Close followup is also important. 

Remember that inversion ankle sprains, no matter the severity, often result in somatic dysfunction 'up the chain' starting with a posterior fibular head and often even causing innominate rotations and sacral torsions as well.  These things should be addressed with OMT once clinically appropriate.

Answer B: Corticosteroid injection would not be an appropriate option for such an acute injury. Grade I ankle sprains do not routinely require this treatment modality.


Answer C: Advanced imaging should be reserved for persistent pain or a motor deficit demonstrated on exam not likely secondary to pain-related weakness as was in this patient's case with giveway weakness.


Answer D: Per the Ottawa ankle rules this patient would not require x-ray imaging at this time.


Answer E: For higher grade sprains, immobilization of the ankle joint with a CAM walker would be a good consideration. For a Grade I ankle sprain, early ROM and PT are the mainstays of treatment.




Bottom Line: Appropriate management of ankle sprains includes early ROM/PT and knowing which patients are appropriate candidates for imaging.

For more information, see:
"Ankle Sprain" on Medscape.

Patient Presentation: Musculoskeletal Difficulties And Muscular Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Scientific Understanding of Health & Disease Mechanisms
Body System: Musculoskeletal System
Discipline: Family Medicine
A 23-year-old female presents to the office with the complaint of right knee pain. History reveals the pain began while training for the local half-marathon. Physical examination reveals and increased Q-angle, pain with patellar grind, and lateral tracking with quadriceps contraction. This patient's symptoms are due to weakness of

1. patellar tendon
2. sartorius
3. vastus intermedius
4. vastus lateralis
5. vastus medialis

The correct answer is: E
Patellofemoral pain syndrome is a commonly seen disorder that presents as anterior knee pain and is the result of either overuse or misalignment. The idea of overloading the knee’s extensor mechanism is the fundamental principle thought to play a role in this syndrome, as noted in the patient above who has recently increased her exercise regimen. A wide Q-angle causes abnormal strain to the lateral patella during flexion and extension. Having weaker medial quadricep muscles (vastus medialis) than lateral quadricep muscles causes the patella to be pulled laterally during quadriceps contraction and cause uneven wear of the patellar articular cartilage.

Answer A: The patellar tendon can become inflamed in jumping athletes and cause patellar tendonitis, or jumper's knee. It does not cause lateral tracking.


Answer B: The sartorial attaches to the ASIS and the pes anserinus at the medial knee. It does not attach directly to the patella.


Answers C & D: Lateral tracking of the patella is caused by relative weakness of the vastus medialis and overactivity of the vastus lateralis and vastus intermedius.




Bottom Line: Weakness of the vastus medialis may lead to lateral patellar tracking resulting in patellofemoral pain syndrome. 

For more information, see:
"Patellofemoral pain syndrome" on Medscape

Link: "Atrial fibrillation" UpToDate.


Patient Presentation: Abnormal Findings
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Management
Body System: Cardiovascular System
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 57-year-old male presents to the emergency department with palpitations, chest pain, and lightheadedness for two hours’ duration. Two days prior the patient states he had a normal physical examination and a normal ECG. Vital signs reveal a blood pressure of 80/60 mmHg, and a heart rate of 178/min. Physical examination reveals clear lungs and an irregular cardiac rhythm upon auscultation. Osteopathic examination is significant for a posterior Chapman reflex point located at the T2 transverse process. An ECG is obtained as shown in the exhibit. The most appropriate initial management is

1. cardioversion
2. diltiazem
3. emergent cardiac catheterization
4. nitroglycerin
5. pacemaker placement

The correct answer is: A
The patient has atrial fibrillation with rapid ventricular response. The restoration of sinus rhythm is performed in many patients with atrial fibrillation to improve symptoms. Indications to convert a patient to sinus rhythm include unstable hemodynamics, first episode within 48 hours, infrequent episodes that do not convert spontaneously. Unstable patients include patients with severe acute left heart failure, myocardial ischemia, or hypotension. Stable patients who are candidates include patients with new onset atrial fibrillation, patients with persistent atrial fibrillation who are limited symptomatically (worsening heart failure due to atrial fibrillation or tachycardia induced cardiomyopathy). Reasons in which it is reasonable to avoid cardioversion in new onset atrial fibrillation include patients who are minimally symptomatic with multiple comorbidities, where the risks of undergoing cardioversion outweigh the benefits of restoring sinus rhythm. Benefits tend to decline after age 80. Patients with paroxysmal atrial fibrillation are usually not cardioverted because it is usually of short duration and resolves spontaneously. There are also factors which decrease the likelihood of success of cardioversion including: atrial fibrillation has been present continuously for more than one year, left atrium enlargement, elderly, associated cardiovascular dysfunctions (left ventricular dysfunction), patients with recurrence while on antiarrhythmic drug therapy. Cardioversion is also less likely to be successful if the cause is thyrotoxicosis, pericarditis, and mitral stenosis. In these cases, the underlying disease processes should be corrected or optimized prior to cardioversion. 

Osteopathic pearl: A Chapman point represents the somatic manifestation of a visceral dysfunction (for example, cardiomyopathy). Most posterior Chapman reflex points are located between the spinous and transverse processes; they have a rubbery texture when palpated. The posterior Chapman reflex point related to the myocardium is located at the T2 transverse process. Once a Chapman point is localized, firm pressure is applied to the point. Pressure is applied in a circular pattern with the attempt to flatten the mass, and is continued until the lesion is resolved or the patient can no longer tolerate treatment.

Answer B: Diltiazem is indicated in rapid atrial fibrillation to slow ventricular response when there is no hypotension and there is no indication for cardioversion. Diltiazem is often used in patients with chronic atrial fibrillation when presenting with symptomatic rapid atrial fibrillation.


Answer C: Emergent cardiac catheterization is indicated in ST elevation MI (STEMI). This patient will eventually need evaluation of the coronary arteries for coronary artery disease either with stress test or cardiac catheterization, but it is not emergently indicated because there is no evidence that the patient is having an STEMI on the EKG.


Answer D: Nitroglycerin is indicated in patients with chest pain on the acute coronary syndrome spectrum without hypotension. This patient does have chest pain, and unstable angina or Non- ST elevation MI (NSTEMI) could be the cause of this patient's new onset atrial fibrillation, but at this point the patient is too hypotensive to be given nitroglycerin.


Answer E: Pacemaker placement is indicated in disease processes that involve the AV node or His-Purkinje system. Atrial fibrillation is a problem with the electrophysiology of the atrial myocardium.




Bottom Line: It is important to know the indications and contraindications to cardioversion for atrial fibrillation and it is especially important to know when to employ emergent cardioversion for atrial fibrillation.

For more information, see:
"Atrial fibrillation" UpToDate.
"Restoration of sinus rhythm in atrial fibrillation" on UpToDate.
Nye, Z. (2009). First aid for the COMLEX: An osteopathic manipulative medicine review (2nd ed.). New York: McGraw-Hill Medical.

Patient Presentation: Respiratory Difficulties
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Asymptomatic & General Symptoms
Discipline: Family Medicine
A 64-year-­old female presents to the office with a persistent cough that is worse at night for five months' duration. Review of symptoms is positive for chronic indigestion and a metallic taste in her mouth. Her past medical history includes hypertension for which she takes ramipril daily. Physical examination is unremarkable. The most likely diagnosis is

1. asthma
2. congestive heart failure
3. gastroesophageal reflux
4. iatrogenic
5. upper respiratory infection

The correct answer is: C
Gastroesophageal reflux (GERD) is a chronic syndrome characterized by gastric juice that leaks into the esophagus. It is primarily caused by impaired relaxation of the lower esophageal sphincter (LES) tone. Other causes include decreased esophageal motility, gastric outlet obstruction, hiatal hernia or diet. Foods such as alcohol, tobacco, chocolate, coffee and high fat foods may decrease LES pressure. Risk factors include obesity, age and consumption of a western diet. Clinical features include heartburn, dyspepsia or cough. Patients may report "water brash", which is a bitter, metallic taste in the mouth. Water brash is an accumulation of saliva and gastric acid that has been regurgitated. Hoarseness or sore throat, otitis media and chest pain are less common symptoms. Diagnostic test of choice in the diagnosis of GERD is upper gastrointestinal endoscopy.

Answer A: Asthma is a chronic condition characterized by airway inflammation, bronchial hyperresponsiveness and reversible airway obstruction. Most cases of asthma are an exaggerated response to numerous exogenous and endogenous stimuli. Stimuli can be environmental, infectious, or exercise induced. Signs and symptoms of asthma include wheezing, coughing, shortness of breath and chest tightness/pain. Symptoms are usually triggered by exposure to antigen and worse at night. Asthma is diagnosed by pulmonary function tests. This patient presents with a normal physical examination, which makes the diagnosis of asthma unlikely.


Answer B: Congestive heart failure is a clinical syndrome that develops when cardiac function is impaired and cardiac output is compromised. Heart failure develops due to reduced contractility and /or impaired filling defects. Most common causes of developing heart failure include ischemic heart disease, myocardial infarction, hypertensive cardiomyopathy, valvular disease or restrictive cardiomyopathy. Signs and symptoms of heart failure include dyspnea, orthopnea, nocturnal cough and cool extremities with diaphoresis at rest. Signs of fluid overload are manifest by jugular venous distention, rales and/or wheezing, presence of hepatojugular reflux and/or S3 or S4 gallop. Diagnosis of heart failure requires the presence of the Framingham criteria. Although this patient presents with a nocturnal cough and history of hypertension, her normal physical examination lessen the possibility of this diagnosis.


Answer D: A major side effect of Angiotensin Converting Enzyme (ACE) inhibitors is coughing. Approximately, 20 percent of patients taking ACE inhibitors develop a dry cough. Women, particularly of black or Asian ancestry, are most susceptible. ACE inhibitor cough is linked to the suppression of an enzyme, which results in the accumulation of substances thought to promote cough. Although this patient takes an ACE inhibitor daily, she presents with other symptoms that suggest a reflux disorder.


Answer E: An upper respiratory infection (URI) is an acute illness that is used to describe the common cold. The common cold is a benign self-limited syndrome caused by a viral infection. The Rhinovirus is the most common etiologic agent accounting for up to 50 percent of cases. The common cold is spread by hand contact and droplet transmission. Risk factors for increased severity include malnutrition, chronic illness, cigarette smoking and immunodeficiency. It is characterized by nasal congestion and discharge, sneezing, sore throat, cough and low-grade fever. Rhinitis and nasal congestion are the most common. Colds usually persist for up to 10 days in the normal host. A URI is unlikely in this patient. Her symptoms have been present for months. In addition, she denies nasal symptoms and has a normal physical examination.




Bottom Line: Classic symptoms of gastroesophageal reflux include pyrosis, regurgitations and cough.

Link: "Kohler Disease" on Medscape


Patient Presentation: Musculoskeletal Difficulties And Muscular Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Musculoskeletal System
Discipline: Pediatrics
A 7-year-old male presents to the urgent care clinic in the company of his mother for evaluation of a limp as well as left foot pain. History reveals symptom onset approximately one week ago with the presentation of midfoot pain. The patient's mother thought the child had stepped on one of his many toys and did not think to bring him in for evaluation. History also reveals that the child is healthy without any congenital issues. Physical examination is notable for pain over the dorsum of the left midfoot without visual evidence of ecchymosis over the affected area. Pain is not elicited with palpation along the base of the fifth metatarsal, medial or lateral malleoli. The patient is able to bear weight but demonstrates an antalgic gait. Radiographs of the left lower extremity including the left foot are obtained and reveal navicular sclerosis and flattening. The most likely diagnosis is

1. Freiberg's infarction
2. Kienbock's disease
3. Kohler's disease
4. Panner's disease
5. Sever's disease

The correct answer is: C
Kohler's disease involves osteonecrosis of the navicular tarsal bone. This condition presents frequently in childhood and the etiology is not well understood. Most patients present with persistent midfoot pain, as the patient in the above scenario. Radiographic imaging will reveal sclerosis and flattening of the navicular bone. Treatment includes immobilization and activity modification. Most patients will recover within 6 to 8 weeks. Recognizing cartilage problems will facilitate early intervention and minimize debilitating long-term sequelae.

Answer A: Freiberg's infarction involves osteochondrosis of the second metatarsal head. It is most commonly encountered in adolescent and young adult women. Patients complain of dull, achy pain on the plantar aspect of the affected metatarsal. Patients may have difficulty walking barefoot. Physical examination reveals tenderness to palpation of the affected area with swelling and/or crepitus on passive range of motion. Radiograph of the foot shows widening of the metatarsophalangeal joint with flattening of the metatarsal head. Sclerosis of the joint may also be appreciated. This patient has navicular involvement which makes Freiberg's infarction less likely.


Answer B: Kienbock's disease involves avascular necrosis (AVN) and collapse of the lunate. It is frequently encountered in individuals who overuse the wrist through repetitive compressive loading. It can also occur with fracture or dislocation to the wrist which causes damage to the blood vessels that supply this region. This patient presents with foot pain so therefore a diagnosis of Kienbock's disease is unlikely.


Answer D: Panner's disease involves osteochondrosis of the capitellum, which is part of the elbow. It is caused by overuse of the area during development when the epiphysis is growing. It is most often seen in adolescents during ages 7 to 12 years, particularly in young boys and is seen often in little league baseball players. Symptoms include tenderness to the lateral aspect of the elbow with decreased extension on passive range of motion. Swelling with a possible effusion may be observed on physical examination. Radiograph of the elbow show fragmentation with sclerosis of the capitellum and irregular appearance of the joint surface. This patient presents with foot pain which makes the diagnosis of Panner's disease unlikely.


Answer E: Sever's disease involves osteochondrosis at the insertion of the growth plate of the Achilles tendon on the calcaneal tuberosity. It is frequently observed in skeletally immature patients especially during periods of rapid growth. During these periods, there is increased metabolic activity in this area which makes it vulnerable to injury if overused. It is commonly seen in young females who are participating in sports that involve running or jumping. These activities can cause microtrauma to the area leading to infarct. Sever's disease is the most common cause of heel pain in active children. This patient presents with navicular injury which makes this diagnosis less likely.




Bottom Line: Kohler's disease involves osteonecrosis of the navicular tarsal bone.

For more information, see:
"Kohler Disease" on Medscape

Link: Medscape


Patient Presentation: Musculoskeletal Difficulties And Muscular Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Musculoskeletal System
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 61-year-old male with a past medical history of coronary artery disease presents to the emergency department with generalized weakness, fever and difficulty breathing. He reports a recent upper respiratory tract infection that seemed to get progressively worse. Review of symptoms is positive for occasional slurred speech and blurred vision over the past few years that has always been transient.  He also reports that he has
no problem when he begins to eat his meal but does have difficulty finishing a meal in the same sitting due to chewing fatigue, especially when eating meats. Vital signs reveal a temperature of 36.7C (98.0F), blood pressure of 124/88 mmHg, a heart rate of 84/min and a respiratory rate of 20/min.  Physical examination is remarkable for eyelid droop with sustained upgaze, weakness of eye adduction, upper extremity muscle strength 3 out of 5, with complete muscle strength in lower extremities. Neurological reflexes are two out of two throughout. He appears to have difficulty holding up his head upright in the neutral position for a prolong amount of time.
Question 1 of 5 in this set
What is the most likely underlying diagnosis?

1. amyotrophic lateral sclerosis
2. basilar artery thrombosis
3. Lambert-Eaton myasthenic syndrome
4. multiple sclerosis
5. myasthenia gravis

The correct answer is: E
Myasthenia gravis (MG) is a rare autoimmune neuromuscular disorder. In the disease, antibodies attack the acetylcholine nicotinic postsynaptic receptors in the neuromuscular junction of skeletal muscles. Patients typically present in second and third decades as well as from sixth to eight decades as well. Symptoms of MG include generalized weakness and muscle fatigue. The fatigue is worsened by activity and symptoms worsen after exercise or at the end of the day. This fluctuation in weakness can help differentiate MG from other neuromuscular disorders. The disease can attack any muscle group although the ocular, bulbar and facial muscles are more likely affected. Approximately 50 percent of patients present with ocular symptoms. Proximal limbs are affected more than distal in MG. Physical examination may present with a wide range of signs and symptoms. Ocular complaints include ptosis, diplopia, and internuclear opthalmoplegia or vertical gaze paresis with pupil sparing. The ptosis may increase with sustained upward gaze or by holding up the opposite eyelid. Patients with bulbar symptoms are fatigable chewing, dysarthria and dysphagia or nasal speech. Facial muscle fatigue can cause an expressionless appearance. Respiratory muscle weakness is the most severe form of MG and can lead to respiratory failure. Respiratory failure is often precipitated by upper respiratory infection. Patients with MG often present with transient symptoms that eventually become more persistent and worsen over years.

Answer A: Amyotrophic lateral sclerosis (ALS) is a common neurodegenerative disease. Approximately 75 percent of patient present with limb involvement. The cause is unknown. There are many types of ALS but the classic form affects upper and lower motor neurons. Upper motor signs include muscle weakness with spasticity and hyperreflexia. Findings of a lower motor neuron lesion include muscle weakness with atrophy and fasiculations. Symptoms of extremity involvement include tripping, stumbling or foot drop. Classic history includes a "slapping gait". Wrist drop and cramping of muscles in the hands are reported. Patients may complain of difficulty buttoning a shirt or turning a key. Only a quarter of patients have bulbar weakness characterized by slurred speech and/or hoarseness, choking while eating and difficulty drinking liquids. Emotional and cognitive difficulties, such as depression, emotional liability, and impaired social behavior, can also manifest in ALS. Serious complications of respiratory insufficiency and aspiration pneumonia develop as the disease progresses. Patients eventually become bedridden. Physical examination is remarkable for muscle atrophy with spasticity and fasiculations, abnormal reflexes, nasal voice change and drooling. Extraocular movements are spared in ALS making this diagnosis less likely in this patient.


Answer B: Basilar artery thrombosis causes ischemia in the area of the pons. Occlusion in the basilar artery results in motor and oculomotor symptoms. Motor symptoms include paresis and corticospinal tract abnormalities. The weakness is initially lateralized and can be transient or persistent. Movements can be adventitious and resemble fasciculations. Patients can show repetitive jerking and twitching in limbs contralateral to a hemiparesis. Incoordination is a common motor finding. Bilateral ataxia is witnessed but it can be asymmetric and more severe on the weaker side. Weakness of the bulbar muscles, such as facial weakness, dysphonia, dysarthria, dysphagia, and limited jaw movements, are common. Weakness is seen on the side of the face and contralateral side of the body. Bulbar weakness is bilateral. Oculomotor symptoms and signs are common in basilar artery thrombosis with ischemia of the pons. Somatosensory abnormalities are not seen. Patients present with altered mental status with motor abnormalities. This patient presents with bilateral symptoms making the diagnosis of basilar artery thrombosis less likely.


Answer C: Lambert-Eaton myasthenia syndrome is a rare autoimmune neuromuscular disorder caused by impaired acetylcholine release from the presynaptic receptors. In the disease, antibodies attack the voltage-gated calcium channels. Calcium flux across the membranes causes the release of acetylcholine. Symptoms of patients with Lambert-Eaton myasthenia syndrome present with proximal muscle weakness, hyporeflexia, and autonomic changes. Typically, patients report symptoms with gait or difficulty getting out of bed, chairs and/or climbing stairs. Muscle atrophy is not apparent and muscle weakness improves with activity. Autonomic dysfunction is unique to Lambert-Eaton myasthenia syndrome. Oropharyngeal symptoms of dysarthria, dysphagia and difficulty chewing are less common but can be present in Lambert-Eaton. Extraocular weakness is less common in Lambert-Eaton and respiratory muscle weakness is usually not a feature of the disease.


Answer D: Multiple sclerosis (MS) is an immune-mediated inflammatory disease of the central nervous system characterized by demyelination. The cause is unknown, however a combination of environmental, immunologic and genetic factors are thought to be accountable. In the disease, multifocal zones of demyelination, known as plaques are scattered throughout the white matter. White matter of the brain and spinal cord are exclusively involved in MS. Gray matter, axons and the peripheral nervous systemic are spared. Women are more commonly affected and they typically present in their third or fourth decades. Symptoms can be monofocal or multifocal and most patients report a history of asymptomatic periods in between exacerbations. Clinical features include decreased sensory, muscle weakness with spasticity, and loss of bladder and bowel control. Optic neuritis is typically one of the first presenting symptoms in which patients complain of partial or complete vision loss. Internuclear ophthalmoplegia is also seen in MS. This patient reports a steady progression of symptoms and lacks autonomic complaints making MS an unlikely diagnosis.




Bottom Line: Myasthenia gravis is an autoimmune disease characterized by skeletal muscle weakness with preserved sensation and reflexes.

For more information, see:
Medscape


Last Updated: 12/11/2016
