insert Link
Patient Presentation: Musculoskeletal Difficulties And Muscular Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Musculoskeletal System
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 61-year-old male with a past medical history of coronary artery disease presents to the emergency department with generalized weakness, fever and difficulty breathing. He reports a recent upper respiratory tract infection that seemed to get progressively worse. Review of symptoms is positive for occasional slurred speech and blurred vision over the past few years that has always been transient.  He also reports that he has
no problem when he begins to eat his meal but does have difficulty finishing a meal in the same sitting due to chewing fatigue, especially when eating meats. Vital signs reveal a temperature of 36.7C (98.0F), blood pressure of 124/88 mmHg, a heart rate of 84/min and a respiratory rate of 20/min.  Physical examination is remarkable for eyelid droop with sustained upgaze, weakness of eye adduction, upper extremity muscle strength 3 out of 5, with complete muscle strength in lower extremities. Neurological reflexes are two out of two throughout. He appears to have difficulty holding up his head upright in the neutral position for a prolong amount of time.
Question 2 of 5 in this set
Which of the following electrodiagnostic results are characteristic of this patient's diagnosis?

1. action potential activity correlates to muscle strength
2. decremental response to repetitive stimulation
3. fibrillation potentials and positive sharp waves
4. large amplitude is observed after maximal activation of muscle
5. prolonged distal latencies with conduction slowing

The correct answer is: B
Myasthenia gravis is a neuromuscular disease caused by antibodies against nicotinic acetylcholine receptors. In patients with myasthenia gravis, repetitive nerve stimulation to an affected muscle shows increasing decrement in amplitude of compound muscle action potential. This progressive decline in amplitude is seen with the first few stimuli to the muscle. A normal muscle will show no change in amplitude with repetitive nerve stimulation.

Answer A: It is important to know the electromyography (EMG) results of a normal muscle. A normal muscle at rest is relatively electronically inactive. Action potentials are elicited as the muscle begins to contract. As the strength of the muscle contraction increases the number of action potentials increase. An abnormal EMG result can originate from a myopathy, neuropathy or neuromuscular junction disease. This patient has a neuromuscular disorder caused by antibodies called Myasthenia gravis.


Answer C: Amyotrophic lateral sclerosis (ALS) is a degenerative disease of the motor neuron system. Needle electromyography results can be useful in the diagnosis of ALS. Electromyography shows fibrillation and fasciculation potentials indicating denervation. The motor units may be polyphasic and have high amplitude. Other signs of active and chronic denervation are fibrillation potentials and positive sharp waves. Neurological examination in this patient is normal; therefore, the likelihood of this EMG result is not likely.


Answer D: Repetitive nerve stimulation studies are used to confirm the diagnosis of Lambert-Eaton myasthenic syndrome. In the study, a large amplitude is observed after maximal activation of muscle. Whereas, resting muscles in patients with Lambert-Eaton myasthenic syndrome have a significantly reduced amplitude. This patient lacks proximal limb weakness and hyporeflexia, which are classic signs of Lambert-Eaton myasthenic syndrome. Therefore, this is not the most specific test.


Answer E: Electrodiagnostic findings of Guillain Barre Syndrome (GBS) are used to diagnose the inflammatory neuropathic disease. GBS is an acute immune mediated demyelinating polyradiculoneuropathy caused by a viral infection. Electromyography (EMG) studies show a slowing of motor conduction velocities with decreased amplitude. Increased distal motor latencies are often seen in the peroneal and tibial nerves, which indicate neurologic demyelination in the lower extremities. This patient has a neuromuscular junction disease and lacks lower extremity complaints.




Bottom Line: Patients with Myasthenia gravis show a decreased response to repetitive stimulation of motor nerves on EMG.

For more information, see:
Agabegi S, Agabegi E. Step-Up To Medicine. 3rd ed. Philadelphia, PA. Lippincott: Williams & Wilkins; 2013: 223-225.

Patient Presentation: Respiratory Difficulties
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Respiratory System
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 61-year-old male with a past medical history of coronary artery disease presents to the emergency department with generalized weakness, fever and difficulty breathing. He reports a recent upper respiratory tract infection that seemed to get progressively worse. Review of symptoms is positive for occasional slurred speech and blurred vision over the past few years that has always been transient.  He also reports that he has
no problem when he begins to eat his meal but does have difficulty finishing a meal in the same sitting due to chewing fatigue, especially when eating meats. Vital signs reveal a temperature of 36.7C (98.0F), blood pressure of 124/88 mmHg, a heart rate of 84/min and a respiratory rate of 20/min.  Physical examination is remarkable for eyelid droop with sustained upgaze, weakness of eye adduction, upper extremity muscle strength 3 out of 5, with complete muscle strength in lower extremities. Neurological reflexes are two out of two throughout. He appears to have difficulty holding up his head upright in the neutral position for a prolong amount of time.
Question 3 of 5 in this set
Which of the following is associated with this patient's diagnosis?

1. Camplobacter jejuni
2. hypertension
3. north European descent
4. small cell lung cancer
5. thymoma

The correct answer is: E
The thymus is involved in the pathogenesis of Myasthenia Gravis (MG) and is seen in a majority of patients with the disease. The thymus is an organ located in the anterior mediastinum that normal atrophies as a person ages. Acetylcholine receptors are present on the particular cells that make up the thymus. Thymic hyperplasia is common but thymic tumors can occur. These tumors can be noninvasive or invasive. Patients with a thymoma can be asymptomatic. If the size of the tumor is large, superior vena cava syndrome can manifest. Pleural and/or pericardial effusions will present as the disease disseminates. Thymic carcinomas are more aggressive and can cause more symptoms from its invasion into nearby structures. Cough, chest pain, and phrenic nerve paralysis are common symptoms. Thymomas can be seen on CT or MRI. Thymectomy can be curative and provide immediate symptomatic relief.

Answer A: Camplobacter jejuni is a gram-negative bacterium that is a common cause of human gastroenteritis. Camplobacter jejuni is often associated with Guillain-Barre syndrome. Guillain-Barre syndrome is an inflammatory demyelinating polyneuropathy that causes motor nerve paralysis. The syndrome typically starts abruptly and affects the peripheral nervous system. The paralysis is ascending and eventually compromises the respiratory system. Patients report a recent infection of the upper respiratory tract or gastrointestinal system. Other common bacteria associations with the disease include cytomegalovirus, hepatitis, and human immunodeficiency virus. However, Guillain-Barre can also occur in the setting of Hodgkin's disease, lupus or post-operatively. This patient has full lower extremity motor strength, which makes Guillain-Barre an unlikely diagnosis.


Answer B: Hypertension is the single greatest risk factor for stroke. High blood pressure promotes the formation of atherosclerotic lesions. Blood pressure reduction is recommended for the prevention of recurrent stroke and vascular events once the stroke is stabilized. The target blood pressure and degree of reduction is uncertain. Treatment should be individualized. However, those with initial blood pressure less than 120/70 mmHg should not receive antihypertensive therapy. For all patients, blood pressure reduction should be gradual. Also, lifestyle modifications that reduce blood pressure, weight loss, salt restriction and aerobe physical activity, should be recommended. This patient lacks signs and symptoms indicating stroke.


Answer C: Although the cause of Multiple Sclerosis (MS) is unknown, the current belief is that it is environmental. MS is more common in people who live far from the equator. Northern Europeans are the greatest population at risk for contracting the disease. Vitamin D deficiency from decreased sunlight exposure is the current hypothesis explaining the geographic variation. This regional risk is most relative before the age of 15. If a person moves to a different region after 15 years of age, the person retains the risk of their region of origin. Other risk factors of MS include infectious agents, occupational exposures, toxins and smoking. This patient lacks the classic history of relapsing and remitting symptoms that have progressed over time making this an incorrect answer choice.


Answer D: Small cell lung cancer is a malignant cancer that most commonly originates within the lung. Approximately 40 percent of patients with Lambert-Eaton myasthenia syndrome present with small cell lung cancer at diagnosis. The cancer is usually diagnosed within two years of symptoms onset. Patients with Lambert-Eaton and small cell lung cancer usually have a fairly aggressive and rapidly progressive course of symptoms compared to those without cancer. Presence of antibodies to voltage-gated calcium changes is present in almost 100 percent of patients who also have small cell lung cancer.




Bottom Line: Patients with myasthenia gravis can develop thymomas that can be asymptomatic or symptomatic.

For more information, see:
Agabegi S, Agabegi E. Step-Up To Medicine. 3rd ed. Philadelphia, PA. Lippincott: Williams & Wilkins; 2013: 223-225.

Patient Presentation: Respiratory Difficulties
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Diagnostic Technologies
Body System: Musculoskeletal System
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 61-year-old male with a past medical history of coronary artery disease presents to the emergency department with generalized weakness, fever and difficulty breathing. He reports a recent upper respiratory tract infection that seemed to get progressively worse. Review of symptoms is positive for occasional slurred speech and blurred vision over the past few years that has always been transient.  He also reports that he has
no problem when he begins to eat his meal but does have difficulty finishing a meal in the same sitting due to chewing fatigue, especially when eating meats. Vital signs reveal a temperature of 36.7C (98.0F), blood pressure of 124/88 mmHg, a heart rate of 84/min and a respiratory rate of 20/min.  Physical examination is remarkable for eyelid droop with sustained upgaze, weakness of eye adduction, upper extremity muscle strength 3 out of 5, with complete muscle strength in lower extremities. Neurological reflexes are two out of two throughout. He appears to have difficulty holding up his head upright in the neutral position for a prolong amount of time.
Question 4 of 5 in this set
The most appropriate initial step in diagnosis is

1. acetylcholine receptor antibody test
2. computed tomography of the head without contrast
3. magnetic resonance imaging
4. repetitive nerve stimulation
5. tensilon test

The correct answer is: A
An acetylcholine receptor antibody test is a quick, easy, non-invasive, and cost-effective study to aid in the diagnosis of Myasthenia Gravis (MG).  It is the most appropriate first step in determining whether a patient indeed has MG or not. These antibodies are present in about 85 to 95% of those with MG, but titers correlate poorly with the severity of the disease. If you are highly suspicious for MG, but the acetylcholine receptor antibody test is negative, there are multiple other more costly, invasive, or time-consuming follow-up diagnostic tests that could be completed (ie: Tensilon test, electromyography, etc).

Answer B: Computed tomography (CT) of the brain is the first study performed in a patient with symptoms suggestive of basilar artery thrombosis. It is a noninvasive diagnostic imaging procedure to give more detailed information of the brain tissue and structures. CT without contrast is sensitive for diagnosis of hemorrhage. However, the study has a low sensitivity for detecting ischemia. Spiral CT angiography is more useful in identifying patients with basilar artery thrombosis. This patient does not present with altered mental status, vertigo, nausea and vomiting suggestive of hemorrhagic stroke.


Answer C: Magnetic resonance imaging (MRI) is the test of choice in diagnosing Multiple Sclerosis (MS). MRI is able to detect brain lesions in 90 to 95 percent of patients and 75 percent of those with spinal lesions. The characteristic plaque of MS shows cerebral or spinal demyelination with preservation of axons. Brain MRI is also used to monitor progression of the disease-showing amount of axonal injury and death. MRI is more sensitive and specific than CT in monitoring the evolution of the disease.


Answer D: Repetitive nerve stimulation may be used to confirm a suspected diagnosis of MG after antibody testing has been completed. An electrical stimulus is applied to nerves and should result in muscle contraction. In MG, muscles fatigue easily and the muscular contraction gets weaker and weaker with each electrical stimulus.


Answer E: The tensilon test, also known as the Edrophonium test, can be used as an initial test or as a follow-up test to acetylcholine receptor antibody testing to help confirm diagnosis of MG. While this test is quick, it is more invasive than a blood draw and edrophonium may result in serious adverse effects. Contraindications to performing the tensilon test include cardiac disease or bronchial asthma. This test would not be used for initial testing due to the patient's age and history of coronary artery disease. Additionally, negative acetylcholine receptor antibody testing occurs most often in patients with ocular MG. Thus, the tensilon test may aid in diagnosis of these patients as well as others you remain highly suspicious of despite negative acetylcholine receptor antibodies (given only about 85-95% have positive acetylcholine receptor antibodies). Edrophonium is an acetylcholinesterase inhibitor that decreases the destruction of acetylcholine. The drug has a short onset of action, and effects are seen within seconds and last approximately less than 10 minutes. In addition, there is a high false-positive rate and the test lacks specificity compared to other tests.




Bottom Line: Testing for serum acetylcholine receptor antibodies is the most appropriate initial diagnostic test for Myasthenia gravis.

COMBANK Insight : Simple, non-invasive, and cost-effective diagnostic tests are often the preferred initial step in diagnosis, particularly on COMBANK.

For more information, see:
Myasthenia gravis in Neurologic disorders section of Step up to USMLE Step 2, page 184.

Patient Presentation: Musculoskeletal Difficulties And Muscular Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Management
Body System: Musculoskeletal System
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 61-year-old male with a past medical history of coronary artery disease presents to the emergency department with generalized weakness, fever and difficulty breathing. He reports a recent upper respiratory tract infection that seemed to get progressively worse. Review of symptoms is positive for occasional slurred speech and blurred vision over the past few years that has always been transient.  He also reports that he has
no problem when he begins to eat his meal but does have difficulty finishing a meal in the same sitting due to chewing fatigue, especially when eating meats. Vital signs reveal a temperature of 36.7C (98.0F), blood pressure of 124/88 mmHg, a heart rate of 84/min and a respiratory rate of 20/min.  Physical examination is remarkable for eyelid droop with sustained upgaze, weakness of eye adduction, upper extremity muscle strength 3 out of 5, with complete muscle strength in lower extremities. Neurological reflexes are two out of two throughout. He appears to have difficulty holding up his head upright in the neutral position for a prolong amount of time.
Question 5 of 5 in this set
The most appropriate pharmaceutical treatment for the underlying diagnosis is

1. acetylcholinesterase inhibitors
2. aspirin
3. immunoglobulin
4. interferon beta
5. riluzole

The correct answer is: A
Acetylcholine esterase inhibitors are the treatment of choice in patients with Myasthenia Gravis (MG). Pyridostigmine, neostigmine, and edrophonium are common agents. Pyridostigmine is the agent of choice used for maintenance therapy. By inhibiting acetylcholine esterase, the concentration of acetylcholine is increased. Acetylcholine esterase inhibitors improve symptoms of MG only and do not stop the progression of the disease.

Answer B: A cerebrovascular accident (CVA) or stroke can be caused by an embolus, thrombosis or hemodynamic compromise. Ischemic strokes account for 87% of CVAs. Sudden loss of focal brain function is a core feature of ischemic stroke. Headache with nausea and vomiting are common symptoms of subarachnoid hemorrhage. Risk factors include advanced age, smoking, hypertension and diabetes mellitus. Initial assessment includes an accurate history and physical, neurological examination and immediate laboratory studies to rule out other causes for stroke like symptoms. In the evaluation of the acute stroke patient, imaging is imperative to confirm the type of stroke and most importantly exclude hemorrhagic stroke and stroke mimickers. Furthermore, imaging studies identify the specific vascular lesion and the extent of injury. Computed tomography (CT) of the brain without contrast is the first imaging study that should be obtained. The advantages of CT are accessibility and low cost. Additionally, contrast should not be used in order to exclude hemorrhage. This patient is not having a cerebrovascular event, therefore aspirin or anti platelet therapy is not indicated.


Answer C: Intravenous Immunoglobulin (IVIG) is used in the treatment of Guillain-Barre syndrome. IVIG is a plasma protein given for immune deficient patients with immune deficiencies. By administering IVIG, the body is able to maintain adequate antibody levels to prevent infections. They can also neutralize harmful antibodies and inflammation produced by the disease. IVIG is as effective as plasmapheresis in treatment of Guillain-Barre syndrome. Either therapy is administered for non-ambulatory patients with the disease who present within four weeks of onset.


Answer D: Interferon therapy with recombinant Interferon beta-1a is used for relapsing-remitting multiple sclerosis. This drug modulates an immune response that is able to reduce relapse rates of approximately 37 percent. Response rates can vary. Interferon therapy should be started early in the course of the disease. Side effects of the interferons include flu-like symptoms and liver dysfunction. However, the long-term benefit is still unclear.


Answer E: Riluzole is a medication used to slow the progression of Amyotrophic Lateral Sclerosis (ALS). It is an antagonist of the glutamate pathway and has shown to reduce excitability. Riluzole is the only medication that has shown to increase survival in patients with ALS. However, ALS is incurable and average span of disease is approximately 3 years from onset of symptoms.




Bottom Line: Acetylcholine esterase inhibitors, such as pyridostigmine, provide symptomatic treatment in Myasthenic gravis.

For more information, see:
Agabegi S, Agabegi E. Step-Up To Medicine. 3rd ed. Philadelphia, PA. Lippincott: Williams & Wilkins; 2013: 223-225.

Patient Presentation: Musculoskeletal Difficulties And Muscular Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Management
Body System: Musculoskeletal System
Discipline: Osteopathic Principle and Practice
A 23 year-old female presents to the office reporting neck pain after sleeping "funny" two nights ago.  She reports worsening pain with active and passive extension.  She denies radiation of pain, numbness, tingling, or weakness.  Upon physical exam you identify that she has a deep suboccipital sulcus on the right that becomes more symmetrical with flexion.  You decide to treat with muscle energy, which direction would you ask the patient to push?

1. You would place your hand under their chin and ask them to push down.
2. You would place your hand on top of their chin and ask them to push up.
3. You would place your hand on their right cheek and ask them to rotate right.
4. You would place your hand on the right check and ask them to extend, rotate right and side-bend left.
5. You would place your hand on the left check and ask them to extend, rotate left and side-bend right.

The correct answer is: A
Muscle energy is a technique that involves placing the patient into their barrier of motion and then asking them to engage an isometric contraction towards the freedom of motion.  In this case the deep sulcus is on the right which become symmetrical with flexion, therefore the patients diagnosis is OA FSIRr.  The patient would be placed into the barrier of motion (ESlRr) and then asked to engage one plane of motion into the freedom.  Typically that is the flexion/extension component with the OA for which we us the chin to engage.

Answer B: By placing your fingers on top of the patients chin and asking them to push up, you are asking them to extend not flex.


Answer C: You would have her push into the freedom not the barrier.


Answer D: It would be difficult to ask you patient to engage all three barriers of motion. Fryette's third principle of motion is that one plane of motion engages all other planes. Therefore, we ask typically ask them to engage one plane of motion which will engage all the planes of motion. In addition, with muscle energy you engage the barrier and ask them to push into the freedom of motion. This would be the opposite of this answer choice.


Answer E: Same as with answer choice D.




Bottom Line: In this case the deep sulcus is on the right which become symmetrical with flexion, therefore the patients diagnosis is OA FSlRr.  The patient would be placed into the barrier of motion (ESlRr) and then asked to engage one plane of motion into the freedom.  Typically that is the flexion/extension component with the OA for which we us the chin to engage.

For more information, see:
DiGiovanna, E. An Osteopathic Approach to Diagnosis and Treatment. 3rd Ed. Lippincott Williams and Wilkins; 2005. pp. 125, 143-144.

Patient Presentation: Pregnancy/Childbirth/Postpartum/Neonatal Assessment
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Diagnostic Technologies
Body System: Asymptomatic & General Symptoms
Discipline: Obstetrics/Gynecology
A 21-year-old female, G2P1 at 42 weeks’ gestation presents to labor and delivery with the complaint of decreased fetal movement. History reveals her pregnancy has been complicated by gestational diabetes mellitus and inadequate prenatal care. The patient is placed on external fetal monitoring as shown in the exhibit. The most likely cause of the finding revealed in exhibit is

1. head compression
2. normal fetal status
3. umbilical cord compression
4. uteroplacental insufficiency
5. vagus nerve stimulation

The correct answer is: D
The fetal heart rate tracing shown in the exhibit demonstrates late decelerations. These decelerations will exhibit a gradual decrease in the fetal heart rate with the nadir of the deceleration occurring after the peak of the contraction. Such decelerations are a sign of fetal compromise, uteroplacental insufficiency, and acidemia.

Answers A & E: Early decelerations indicate fetal head compression or stimulation of the vagus nerve. These decelerations will exhibit a gradual decrease in the fetal heart rate with the nadir of the deceleration occurring at the same time as the peak of the contraction.


Answer B: Normal fetal status is indicated by the absence of late or variable decelerations.


Answer C: Variable decelerations are the most common deceleration pattern and indicate umbilical cord compression. These decelerations will exhibit an abrupt decrease in the fetal heart rate and are seen unrelated to contractions.




Bottom Line: A late deceleration is a sign of uteroplacental insufficiency and displays a gradual decrease in the fetal heart rate with the nadir occurring after the peak of the contraction.

For more information, see:
Practice Bulletin Number 116, Management of Intrapartum Fetal Heart Rate Tracings. The American College of Obstetricians and Gynecologists; 2010.

Link: Morgan, et al. 2006. Interactions of doctors with the pharmaceutical industry. J Med Ethics. 32(10): 559–563.


Patient Presentation: General Concepts
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Health Care Delivery Issues
Body System: Asymptomatic & General Symptoms
Discipline: Psychiatry
You are a medical student invited to a dinner, which is sponsored by a pharmaceutical company, to inform you of a new drug that has been designed for the treatment of hypertension. There are several attending physicians present, one of whom is scheduled to give a presentation. During the dinner you are offered two tickets to an upcoming sporting event and a new stethoscope, by one of the pharmaceutical representatives. Which of the following statements is correct regarding this situation?

1. It is unethical to attend lectures where dinner is provided by pharmaceutical companies
2. Gifts such as tickets to sporting events are allowed but medical devices are not
3. It is unethical to accept all gifts, regardless if they are medically-related or not
4. It is okay to accept the stethoscope but not the tickets
5. It is okay to accept all gifts from pharmaceutical companies, as long as they are not in the form of money

The correct answer is: C
Billions of dollars are spent each year by pharmaceutical companies to influence the prescribing patterns of physicians. Within the past ten years, increasing governmental regulations have tightened up on the incentives offered to health care providers. When placed in a situation similar to the one in the above case it is important to use good judgment in determining what is ethical and what is unethical. In this case, you are being offered dinner, a stethoscope, and tickets to a sporting event in return for your time to sit through a lecture marketing a new medication for hypertension. Basically, it is okay to accept a dinner that accompanies medical education. Ethical questions on the COMLEX must follow a clear rule and will not be ambiguous.  

Answer A: It is not unethical to attend lectures where dinner is provided by pharmaceutical companies.


Answers B & C: It is not okay to accept gifts unrelated to medicine (like sporting tickets).


Answer D: While a stethoscope is a useful medical instrument, accepting it as a gift would generally not be ethically advisable.


Answer E: Gifts do not have to be monetary to be inappropriate.




Bottom Line: It is considered ethical to accept dinner that is a part of the educational experience provided by pharmaceutical companies. Medical, as well as non-medical gifts from pharmaceutical companies are considered unethical. 

COMBANK Insight : Physicians should beware of gifts or other incentives offered by manufacturers or their representatives if the underlying intent is to create business billable to the federal government. 

For more information, see:
Morgan, et al. 2006. Interactions of doctors with the pharmaceutical industry. J Med Ethics. 32(10): 559–563.

Link: Tuberculosis on Medscape


Patient Presentation: Disease Detection & Monitoring
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Management
Body System: Asymptomatic & General Symptoms
Discipline: Family Medicine
A 42-year-old nurse from a nearby hospital is being evaluated regarding her PPD test that revealed an area of induration measuring 7mm.  She has a past medical history significant for lupus resulting in renal damage for which she has been on prolonged steroids. A chest radiograph is obtain and is unremarkable.  The most appropriate management at this time is

1. combination therapy with rifampin, isoniazid, pyrazinamide, and ethambutol
2. nine months of isoniazid
3. no treatment necessary as her PPD test is not considered positive
4. repeat the PPD test within one to two weeks
5. three months of isoniazid

The correct answer is: B
In a patient with a positive PPD skin test and a negative chest x-ray excluding active tuberculosis, isoniazid should be initiated and continued for nine months.  Isoniazid has been shown to decrease the lifetime risk of TB with a positive PPD skin test by as much as 90%.  Once the skin test is positive it should never be repeated because it will always remain positive.  This patient is considered to have a positive PPD skin test because of her prolonged use of steroids and an area of induration greater than 5mm.

Answer A: This combination would be indicated if the patient had active tuberculosis. All four are maintained for the first two months at which point ethambutol and pyrazinamide are discontinued and rifampin and isoniazid are continued for the next four months for a total of six months of treatment. Treatment may be extended to nine months in the setting of meningitis, osteomyelitis, miliary tuberculosis, or when pyrazinamide cannot be used (as in pregnancy). Active tuberculosis classically presents with fever, cough, sputum, weight loss, hemoptysis, and night sweats. The best initial test is a chest radiograph, which would classically demonstrate a cavitary lesion, usually in the upper lobe. Sputum stain and culture for acid-fast bacilli should also be obtained. Three negative results are required to exclude active tuberculosis infection completely. The most accurate diagnostic test is a pleural biopsy.


Answer C: Due to the extended use of steroids, this patient's PPD skin test is considered positive. Remember that positivity is determined by the size of induration, NOT erythema. The PPD skin test is considered positive when induration is greater than the following:

5mm: Glucocorticoid users, abnormal calcifications on chest radiography, HIV-positive patients, organ transplant recipients, and those in close contact with someone who has active tuberculosis.

10 mm: Prisoners, close contacts of someone with tuberculosis, recent immigrants (past five years), health-care workers, patients with hematologic malignancy, alcoholics, or diabetes mellitus.

15mm: Those with no risk factors.


Answer D: Two-stage testing is only required for those who have never had a PPD skin test before. If this is the case, a second test is indicated within one to two weeks if the first test is negative.


Answer E: This patient will require treatment with isoniazid, however treatment duration is nine months, not three months.




Bottom Line: A patient with a positive PPD skin test as indicated by the area of induration, with a negative chest radiograph to exclude active tuberculosis, should be initiated on isoniazid therapy and continued for nine months.

For more information, see:
Tuberculosis on Medscape

Patient Presentation: Cognitive Difficulties
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Psychiatry
Discipline: Family Medicine
For each numbered item (patient presentation), select the one heading (associated diagnosis) most closely associated with it. Each lettered heading may be selected once, more than once, or not at all. 

A. Dementia
B. Depression
C. Hypoglycemia
D. Hypothyroid
E. Vitamin B12 deficiency
Question 1 of 5 in this set
A 65-year-old male presents to the office with decreased appetite, malaise and insomnia.  You note a diminished amplitude cranial rhythmic impulse and alternating superior and inferior vertical strains upon palpation of the vault.

1. A
2. B
3. C
4. D
5. E

The correct answer is: B
Major depression disorder is a mental disorder characterized by anhedonia, low mood and sadness. Late-life depression is typically under diagnosed and inadequately treated. Risk factors for developing late life depression include female sex, social isolation, insomnia, uncontrolled pain or comorbidities. Dysmorphic mood is a hallmark sign in the elderly. Irritability, mood swings, sadness or heaviness may be expressed. Hopelessness or helplessness may occupy their thoughts. Most can present with a normal appearance. However, severe depression can cause a decline in grooming and hygiene with noticeable weight change. Other signs may be a flat affect, restlessness or psychomotor retardation. Untreated late life depression can increase a patient's risk to develop dementia.  

SBS compression (palpated as low/absent CRI and/or alternating vertical strains) is a common cranial finding among patients with depression.

Answer A: Dementia is a neurodegenerative disease that is defined as a nonspecific syndrome of reduced cognitive ability in a previous unimpaired person. There are several forms of dementia: Alzheimer's disease, vascular dementia, Fronto-temporal dementia, and Dementia with Lewy bodies. Dementia is most common in elderly persons with age being the biggest risk factor. The disease is characterized by a loss of memory, with a marked reduction in the ability to learn, reason, retain, or recall past experiences. Eventually thoughts, feelings, and activities are affected. Altered mental status and behavioral changes are noted on exam.


Answer C: Hypoglycemia is characterized by a syndrome of altered mental status and/or sympathetic nervous system stimulation due to a reduced plasma glucose concentration. Most patients become symptomatic at a serum glucose less than 50 mg/dL. Risk factors include diabetes mellitus, renal insufficiency, hepatic cirrhosis or other endocrine diseases. Symptoms include headache, confusion, nausea and vomiting, and fatigue. Neurogenic affects include diaphoresis, tremor, paresthesias, dizziness and diplopia. Hypoglycemia is confirmed by a low blood sugar, presence of symptoms and reversal of symptoms when blood glucose returns to normal.


Answer D: Hypothyroidism is an endocrine disorder in which the thyroid cannot produce sufficient thyroid hormones. Iodine deficiency or Hashimoto's disease are the most common causes of primary hypothyroidism. Classic symptoms include lethargy, weight gain, dry skin, cold intolerance, and constipation, however signs and symptoms can be subtle. Physical examination is remarkable for slurred speech and slow movements, weight gain, dry, coarse hair or hair loss. Bradycardia is usually present. Diagnosis is made by thyroid function studies, including thyroid stimulating hormone, thyroxine and trioothryroxidine levels.


Answer E: Vitamin B12 is a water soluble vitamin that is excreted through the urine. However, it can be stored in the liver for up to one year and a deficiency does not always manifest itself for one year or more. Vitamin B12 deficiency is most often due to inadequate dietary intake, malabsorption associated with pernicious anemia and other gastric abnormalities such as Crohn's disease or Celiac disease. Vegans and vegetarians are particularly susceptible to vitamin B12 deficiency. In addition, medications such as oral contraceptive pills and proton pump inhibitors, can reduce absorption of the vitamin. Clinical manifestations include a classic triad of weakness, sore tongue and paresthesias. As the disease progresses, defects in the peripheral nervous system become apparent. These include impaired vibration sense, ataxia and paresthesias due to posterior spinal cord demyelination.




Bottom Line: Depression is characterized by dysmorphic mood in the elderly.

They also note she often stays in her room for meals and participates in fewer activities. Review of symptoms is positive for multiple somatic complaints including headache, low back and joint pain, abdominal fullness and constipation. Her past medical history is only significant for diabetes mellitus and she recently suffered a ground level fall resulting in a hip fracture. Her mini mental status exam is 27 and her medical evaluation is within normal limits. She has four children who visit her regularly in the nursing home. 1. A
2. B
3. C
4. D
5. E

Answer A: Dementia is a neurodegenerative disease that is defined as a nonspecific syndrome of reduced cognitive ability in a previous unimpaired person. Patient Presentation: General Concepts
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Health Care Delivery Issues
Body System: Asymptomatic & General Symptoms
Discipline: Surgery
You are an employee at a hospital run by a managed care plan that has recently introduced minimally invasive robotic surgery. Although many benefits exist over conventional surgery, the downside of this is the high cost. You have recently seen a patient in your office in need of a total abdominal hysterectomy that would benefit from this new technology; however, you have recently received written instruction from the insurance company not to offer the service to managed care patients because of the high cost. In addition, you have been told by your chief medical officer that your future salary will be dependent on your documented expenditures per patient. Based on this scenario, the best course of action is

1. advise the patient to contact the managed care provider herself to discuss the surgery
2. consult your medical director for “special permission” to allow the surgery on a need basis
3. inform the patient of the new robotic surgery only if the patient inquires about his/her surgical options
4. offer information regarding the robotic surgery and discuss the risks and benefits with the patient
5. refer your patient to a gynecologic oncologist for further discussion on this topic

The correct answer is: D
In all specialties of medicine, the physician has a duty to put the patient’s best interest first at all times. You cannot withhold information from a patient if it may provide beneficial treatment. In order for the patient to make “informed consent,” he/she must be fully informed and this is not possible if the patient is unaware of new technologies that would better his/her care. It is your responsibility, as a physician, to educate your patients on the best treatment options, regardless of their expense. It is not ethical to tell patients about certain treatments only if asked about them. Even if ordered by the institution to withhold this information, it is still your ethical responsibility to inform the patient. Taking your personal needs into account is inappropriate and violates your physician responsibility.

Answer A: It is the physician's responsibility to discuss treatment options, risks and benefits, and to obtain informed consent from the patient.


Answer B: Consulting the medical director may be appropriate after the patient makes a decision regarding their treatment, but a conversation with the medical director cannot dictate what treatment options are given to the patient.


Answer C: It is unethical for a physician to withhold information from a patient if it may provide beneficial treatment.


Answer E: Referral is not appropriate for strictly personal financial purposes.




Bottom Line: Always inform patient of all treatment options regardless of cost.

COMBANK Insight : When answering the medical ethics questions, always remember that beneficence outweighs all other motives, including institutional directives. If you keep your patient’s best interests in mind, you can never go wrong. Do not get tripped up by answer choices that promote your personal needs or those of the hospital.

Last Updated: 1/14/2016

Patient Presentation: Digestive Difficulties
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Scientific Understanding of Health & Disease Mechanisms
Body System: Endocrine System
Discipline: Internal Medicine
For each patient presentation, select the one acid base disorder most closely associated with it. Each acid base disorder may be selected once, more than once, or not at all

A. Acute respiratory acidosis on chronic respiratory acidosis
B.    High anion gap metabolic acidosis and metabolic alkalosis
C. Non-anion gap metabolic acidosis and metabolic alkalosis
D. Respiratory acidosis and metabolic alkalosis
E. Respiratory alkalosis and non-anion gap metabolic acidosis
Question 1 of 5 in this set
An 18-year-old female with diabetes mellitus type 1 presents with vomiting. Laboratory analysis reveals a pH 7.20, pCO2 29 mm Hg, HCO3 15 mEq/L, Na 132 mEq/L, K 3.5 mEq/L, and Cl 86 mEq/L.

1. A
2. B
3. C
4. D
5. E

The correct answer is: B
Approach acid-base disorders in a systematic way.

1) First, look at the pH and determine if its acidemia or alkalemia. Here the pH is 7.2 which points to acidemia.
2) Determine if it is primarily metabolic or respiratory disorder. With a very low HCO3, the primary disorder is metabolic.
3) Calculate the anion gap via [Na - (Cl + HCO3)] with a normal anion gap being 12-16. This case displays a high anion gap of 31.
4) Use Winters formula [(1.5 x HCO3) + 8 ± 2] to calculate an expected value for pCO2 to determine whether pCO2 is low due to an appropriate respiratory compensation or if its lower than expected indicating a concomitant primary respiratory alkalosis. In this case, the expected pCO2 is 28.5 - 32.5 which corresponds to the actual pCO2 indicating an appropriate respiratory compensation for the high anion gap metabolic acidosis.
5) Lastly, calculate a corrected HCO3 to determine if there is a combination of acid-base disorders via [(measured anion gap - normal anion gap of 12) + HCO3]. In this case, [(31 - 12) + 15] = 34. The corrected HCO3 is greater than a normal HCO3 of about 24 indicating a concomitant metabolic alkalosis with the high anion gap acidosis.

This patient has a high anion gap metabolic acidosis as a result of diabetic ketoacidosis likely caused by an infection and a metabolic alkalosis from vomiting.

Answer A: Respiratory acidosis is characterized by an elevated arterial pCO2 and a low arterial pH. Acute respiratory acidosis is defined as an increase in arterial pCO2 concentration above the reference range of 35-45 mmHg with a reduction in pH whereas patients with chronic respiratory acidosis may not have an alteration in pH. An acute respiratory acidosis superimposed on chronic respiratory acidosis would result in an elevated pCO2, a mildly elevated bicarbonate and a low pH.


Answer C: Metabolic alkalosis is characterized by an increase in serum bicarbonate concentration and pH. The most common causes of metabolic alkalosis include diuretic use and gastrointestinal losses such as vomiting. A mixed disorder of metabolic alkalosis and non-anion gap metabolic acidosis could cause the pH to equilibrate to normal or near normal levels.


Answer D: Respiratory acidosis is an acid base disorder that is caused by alveolar hypoventilation. Alveolar hypoventilation results in an increase in pCO2 or hypercapnia. In turn, hypercapnia decreases the HCO3 and pH. The compensatory response to acute respiratory acidosis should increase the serum HCO3 by about 1 mEq/L for every 10 mmHg elevation in the pCO2. The higher than expected value of bicarbonate is mostly due to a superimposed metabolic alkalosis which has increased the serum HCO3 from the expected value in a simple respiratory acidosis. In addition, the pH may normalize from this mixed disorder. Metabolic alkalosis is an increase in serum HCO3 concentration and pH.


Answer E: Metabolic acidosis is an acid-base disorder that results from a primary decrease in serum HCO3 with a subsequent decrease in pH (less than 7.35). The compensation for a drop in pH is hyperventilation which results in a decrease in pCO2 of 1-1.3 mmHg for every 1 mEq/L drop in serum HCO3 concentration. Respiratory alkalosis is characterized by a low arterial pCO2 and increases arterial pH.




Bottom Line: Approach acid-base disorders in a systematic way. You will need to memorize the above equations.

For more information, see:
Acid-base disorders in Step-Up to USMLE Step 2 CK
Acid-base physiology in Renal section of First Aid for the USMLE Step 1: A Student-to-Student Guide


Last Updated: 12/12/2016

Patient Presentation: Consciousness Alterations
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Scientific Understanding of Health & Disease Mechanisms
Body System: Respiratory System
Discipline: Internal Medicine
For each patient presentation, select the one acid base disorder most closely associated with it. Each acid base disorder may be selected once, more than once, or not at all

A. Acute respiratory acidosis on chronic respiratory acidosis
B.    High anion gap metabolic acidosis and metabolic alkalosis
C. Non-anion gap metabolic acidosis and metabolic alkalosis
D. Respiratory acidosis and metabolic alkalosis
E. Respiratory alkalosis and non-anion gap metabolic acidosis
Question 2 of 5 in this set
A 71-year-old male with a history of emphysema presents with a brief loss of consciousness. Physical examination is remarkable for diaphoresis, tachypnea and decreased breath sounds on the left side. Laboratory analysis reveals a pH of 7.22, pCO2 72 mmHg, and HCO3 32 mEq/L.

1. A
2. B
3. C
4. D
5. E

The correct answer is: A
Respiratory acidosis is characterized by an elevated arterial pCO2 and a low arterial pH. Acute respiratory acidosis is defined as an increase in arterial pCO2 concentration above the reference range of 35-45 mmHg with a reduction in pH whereas patients with chronic respiratory acidosis may not have an alteration in pH. An acute respiratory acidosis superimposed on chronic respiratory acidosis would result in elevated pCO2, a mildy elevated bicarbonate and a low pH. This patient presents with a pneumonia superimposed on a history of chronic obstructive pulmonary disease which increases the already mildly elevated pCO2 and serum bicarbonate levels.

Answer B: Metabolic acidosis is an acid-base disorder that results from a primary decrease in serum bicarbonate ion, HCO3, (normal range is 22-28 mmHg) with a subsequent decrease in pH (less than 7.35). The anion gap is the difference between the concentration of the major measured cation, Na, and the major measured anions, Cl and HCO3. A normal anion gap is 3 to 10 meq/L. Non-anion gap, or hyperchloremic acidosis is a state in which the acidosis is not accompanied by an accumulation of anions. Causes of non-AG metabolic acidosis include gastrointestinal losses such as diarrhea or a renal acidification defect. An increased anion gap metabolic acidosis is a state in which the accumulating acid is not the HCl ion, leading to a greater number of unmeasured anions in the serum. The major causes of increased anion gap metabolic acidosis are lactic acidosis, ketoacidosis, acute or chronic kidney disease, salicylate poisoning or toxic alcohol ingestion. A mixed acid base disorder of anion gap metabolic acidosis and normal anion gap acidosis should decrease the concentration of bicarbonate further, relative to the change in the anion gap. This signifies that there is another process present requiring buffering by bicarbonate. Some examples of this disorder include progressive renal failure, lactic acidosis superimposed on severe diarrhea and diabetic ketoacidosis.


Answer C: Metabolic alkalosis is characterized by an increase in serum bicarbonate ion concentration and pH. The most common causes of metabolic alkalosis include diuretic use and gastrointestinal losses such as vomiting. A mixed disorder of metabolic alkalosis and metabolic acidosis would cause the pCO2 and HCO3 to equilibrate with a pH near normal levels. The anion gap should still be elevated.


Answer D: Respiratory acidosis is an acid base disorder that is caused by alveolar hypoventilation. Alveolar hypoventilation results in an increase in pCO2 or hypercapnia. In turn, hypercapnia decreases the HCO3 and pH. The compensatory response to acute respiratory acidosis should increase the serum HCO3 by about 1 mEq/L for every 10 mmHg elevation in the pCO2. The higher than expected value of bicarbonate is mostly due to a superimposed metabolic alkalosis which has increased the serum HCO3 from the expected value in a simple respiratory acidosis. In addition, the pH may normalize from this mixed disorder. Metabolic alkalosis is an increase in serum HCO3 concentration and pH. The most common causes of metabolic alkalosis include diuretic use and gastrointestinal losses such as vomiting.


Answer E: Metabolic acidosis is an acid-base disorder that results from a primary decrease in serum bicarbonate ion, HCO3, (normal range is 22-28 mmHg) with a subsequent decrease in pH (less than 7.35). The compensation for a drop in pH is alveolar hyperventilation which results in a decrease in pCO2 of 1-1.3 mmHg for every 1 mEq/L drop in serum HCO3 concentration. Respiratory alkalosis is characterized by a low arterial pCO2 and a high arterial pH. An acute respiratory alkalosis superimposed on a metabolic acidosis would result in a pCO2 lower than observed.




Bottom Line: Acute on chronic respiratory acidosis is characterized by a low pH and a higher than expected pCO2.

For more information, see:
Acid base on Uptodate

D. Respiratory acidosis and metabolic alkalosis
E. Respiratory alkalosis and non-anion gap metabolic acidosis
Question 3 of 5 in this set
A 21-year-old male presents to the emergency department with chest pain and dyspnea. Physical examination revealed an oxygen saturation of 85 percent and unilateral inspiratory crackles. Laboratory analysis reveals a pH of 7.30, pCO2 20 mmHg, HCO3 19 mEq/L, Na 135 mEq/L, K 5.6 mEq/L and Cl 104 mEq/L.

1. A
2. B
3. C
4. D
5. E

The correct answer is: E
This patient has acidosis given pH is 7.30 (< 7.35). The HCO3 is low at 19 indicating a metabolic acidosis. Calculating the anion gap [Na of 135 - (Cl of 104 + HCO3 of 19)] = 12 determines this is a non-anion gap metabolic acidosis as this is within normal anion gap range. The compensation for metabolic acidosis is hyperventilation resulting in decrease in pCO2 of 1-1.3 mmHg for every 1 mEq/L drop in HCO3. Winter's formula calculates the expected value of pCO2 to be 34.5 - 38.5 mmHg via (1.5 x 19 + 8 ± 2). The pCO2 is
 Winters' equation predicts a pCO2 of 33-37 mm Hg (1.5 * 18 + 8 + 2). The actual pCO2 of 20 is less than the expected value, therefore a concurrent respiratory alkalosis is present.

Answer A: Respiratory acidosis is characterized by an elevated arterial pCO2 and a low arterial pH. Acute respiratory acidosis is defined as an increase in arterial pCO2 concentration above the reference range of 35-45 mmHg with a reduction in pH whereas patients with chronic respiratory acidosis may not have an alteration in pH. An acute respiratory acidosis superimposed on chronic respiratory acidosis would result in elevated pCO2, a mildy elevated bicarbonate and a low pH.


Answer B: Metabolic acidosis is an acid-base disorder that results from a primary decrease in serum HCO3 with a subsequent decrease in pH (less than 7.35). The anion gap is the difference between the concentration of the major measured cation, Na, and the major measured anions, Cl and HCO3. A normal anion gap is typically 12-16 meq/L. The major causes of high anion gap metabolic acidosis are lactic acidosis, ketoacidosis, acute or chronic kidney disease, salicylate poisoning or toxic alcohol ingestion. Metabolic alkalosis is characterized by an increase in corrected serum bicarbonate concentration. The most common causes of metabolic alkalosis include diuretic use and gastrointestinal losses such as vomiting.


Answer C: Metabolic alkalosis is characterized by an increase in serum bicarbonate concentration and pH. The most common causes of metabolic alkalosis
include diuretic use and gastrointestinal losses such as vomiting. A mixed disorder of metabolic alkalosis and non-anion gap metabolic acidosis could cause the pH to equilibrate to normal or near normal levels.


Answer D: Respiratory acidosis is an acid base disorder that is caused by alveolar hypoventilation. Alveolar hypoventilation results in an increase in pCO2 or hypercapnia. In turn, hypercapnia decreases the HCO3 and pH. The compensatory response to acute respiratory acidosis should increase the serum HCO3 by about 1 mEq/L for every 10 mmHg elevation in the pCO2. The higher than expected value of bicarbonate is mostly due to a superimposed metabolic alkalosis which has increased the serum HCO3 from the expected value in a simple respiratory acidosis. In addition, the pH may normalize from this mixed disorder. Metabolic alkalosis is an increase in serum HCO3 concentration and pH.




Bottom Line: A mixed disorder of respiratory alkalosis and metabolic acidosis will reduce the arterial pCO2 lower than expected.

For more information, see:
Acid-base disorders in First Aid for the USMLE Step 2 CK: A Student-to-Student Guide

Patient Presentation: Respiratory Difficulties
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Scientific Understanding of Health & Disease Mechanisms
Body System: Respiratory System
Discipline: Internal Medicine
For each patient presentation, select the one acid base disorder most closely associated with it. Each acid base disorder may be selected once, more than once, or not at all

A. Acute respiratory acidosis on chronic respiratory acidosis
B.    High anion gap metabolic acidosis and metabolic alkalosis
C. Non-anion gap metabolic acidosis and metabolic alkalosis
D. Respiratory acidosis and metabolic alkalosis
E. Respiratory alkalosis and non-anion gap metabolic acidosis
Question 4 of 5 in this set
An 83-year-old female with an unknown medical history presents to the emergency department in respiratory distress. She is intubated and placed on mechanical ventilation. Laboratory analysis reveals a pH of 7.32, pCO2 72 mmHg, HCO3 43 mEq/L, and pO2 77 mmHg.

1. A
2. B
3. C
4. D
5. E

The correct answer is: D
Respiratory acidosis is an acid base disorder that is caused by alveolar hypoventilation. Alveolar hypoventilation results in an increase in pCO2 or hypercapnia. In turn, hypercapnia decreases the HCO3 and pH. The compensatory response to acute respiratory acidosis should increase the serum HCO3 by about 1 mEq/L for every 10 mmHg elevation in the pCO2. The higher than expected value of bicarbonate is mostly due to a superimposed metabolic alkalosis which has increased the serum HCO3 from the expected value in a simple respiratory acidosis. In addition, the pH may normalize from this mixed disorder. Metabolic alkalosis is an increase in serum HCO3 concentration and pH. The most common causes of metabolic alkalosis include diuretic use and gastrointestinal losses such as vomiting.

Answer A: Respiratory acidosis is characterized by an elevated arterial pCO2 and a low arterial pH. Acute respiratory acidosis is defined as an increase in arterial pCO2 concentration above the reference range of 35-45 mmHg with a reduction in pH whereas patients with chronic respiratory acidosis may not have an alteration in pH. An acute respiratory acidosis superimposed on chronic respiratory acidosis would result in elevated pCO2, a mildy elevated bicarbonate and a low pH.


Answer B: Metabolic acidosis is an acid-base disorder that results from a primary decrease in serum bicarbonate ion, HCO3, (normal range is 22-28 mmHg) with a subsequent decrease in pH (less than 7.35). The anion gap is the difference between the concentration of the major measured cation, Na, and the major measured anions, Cl and HCO3. A normal anion gap is 3 to 10 meq/L. Non-anion gap, or hyperchloremic acidosis is a state in which the acidosis is not accompanied by an accumulation of anions. Causes of non-AG metabolic acidosis include gastrointestinal losses such as diarrhea or a renal acidification defect. An increased anion gap metabolic acidosis is a state in which the accumulating acid is not the HCl ion, leading to a greater number of unmeasured anions in the serum. The major causes of increased anion gap metabolic acidosis are lactic acidosis, ketoacidosis, acute or chronic kidney disease, salicylate poisoning or toxic alcohol ingestion. A mixed acid base disorder of anion gap metabolic acidosis and normal anion gap acidosis should decrease the concentration of bicarbonate further, relative to the change in the anion gap. This signifies that there is another process present requiring buffering by bicarbonate. Some examples of this disorder include progressive renal failure, lactic acidosis superimposed on severe diarrhea and diabetic ketoacidosis.


Answer C: Metabolic alkalosis is characterized by an increase in serum bicarbonate ion concentration and pH. The most common causes of metabolic alkalosis include diuretic use and gastrointestinal losses such as vomiting. A mixed disorder of metabolic alkalosis and metabolic acidosis would cause the pCO2 and HCO3 to equilibrate with a pH near normal levels. The anion gap should still be elevated.


Answer E: Metabolic acidosis is an acid-base disorder that results from a primary decrease in serum bicarbonate ion, HCO3, (normal range is 22-28 mmHg) with a subsequent decrease in pH (less than 7.35). The compensation for a drop in pH is alveolar hyperventilation which results in a decrease in pCO2 of 1-1.3 mmHg for every 1 mEq/L drop in serum HCO3 concentration. Respiratory alkalosis is characterized by a low arterial pCO2 and a high arterial pH. An acute respiratory alkalosis superimposed on a metabolic acidosis would result in a pCO2 lower than observed.




Bottom Line: A metabolic alkalosis superimposed on a respiratory acidosis inappropriately increases the HCO3.

For more information, see:
Acid base on Uptodate

Link: "Type 2 diabetes mellitus" on Medscape.


Patient Presentation: Genitourinary Disorders/Issues
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Diagnostic Technologies
Body System: Endocrine System
Discipline: Family Medicine
A 42-year-old female presents to the office with the complaints of fatigue and frequent urination. History reveals the patient eats a poor diet and gets little exercise. Past medical history includes obesity, hypertension, and hypercholesterolemia. Family history reveals coronary artery disease, hypertension, and diabetes. Vital signs reveal a blood pressure of 152/90 mmHg, a heart rate of 84/min, and a respiratory rate of 18/min. Physical examination reveals an obese female with trace edema in bilateral lower extremities. Osteopathic examination is significant for a posterior Chapman reflex point located at the right T7 intertransverse space. A 2-hour oral glucose tolerance test is obtained. Which of the following results of the oral glucose tolerance test is required for a positive diagnosis of diabetes?

1. greater than 126mg/dL
2. greater than 126mg/dL on two separate occasions
3. greater than 300mg/dL with symptoms
4. greater than 200mg/dL with symptoms
5. greater than 200mg/dL with or without symptoms

The correct answer is: E
Glucose tolerance testing (GTT) is used to evaluate the ability to regulate glucose metabolism and is indicated when random/fasting blood glucose testing alone is insufficient in establishing or ruling out the diagnosis of diabetes mellitus. The reference range of serum or plasma glucose is less than 140 mg/dL at 2 hours after a 75-g glucose load. Approximately 25% of patients with either impaired glucose tolerance or impaired fasting glucose will develop type-2 diabetes over the following 3–5 years. However, the results of a glucose tolerance test cannot distinguish between type-1 and type-2 diabetes mellitus, and cannot be used to predict response to therapy, or likelihood of future complications. Although glucose tolerance testing is more specific than other options, fasting blood glucose and haemoglobin A1c testing are usually more convenient and thus are used more frequently for screening. Higher plasma glucose levels are linked to increased cardiovascular risk, with this risk increase extending into the non-diabetic range. Impaired glucose tolerance (as demonstrated on a GTT) has also been demonstrated to be a better predictor of increased cardiovascular risk and mortality than fasting glucose. the presence of any one of the following criteria supports the diagnosis of diabetes mellitus: 1) Hemoglobin A1C (HbA1C) = 6.5% 2) Fasting plasma glucose = >126 mg/dL (7.0 mmol/L) 3) A 2-hour plasma glucose level = 200 mg/dL (11.1 mmol/L) during a 75-g OGTT 4) A random plasma glucose level = 200 mg/dL (11.1 mmol/l) in a patient with classic symptoms of hyperglycemia or hyperglycemic crisis

Answers A & B: A fasting plasma glucose greater than 126mg/dL on 1 occasion can be used as provisional diagnostic criteria for DM. However this question asks for the diagnostic criteria for a 2-hour glucose tolerance test.


Answer C: A glucose level above 300mg/dL is too high as a cut-off for any of the diagnostic criteria for DM.


Answer D: A positive diagnostic criteria for DM after a 75g oral glucose tolerance test is a glucose level above 200mg/dL. It does not require the patient to have symptoms. On the other hand, a random plasma glucose that is above 200mg/dL and the patient has symptoms is considered a positive diagnostic criteria for DM.




Bottom Line: The diagnosis of DM can be made with a fasting plasma glucose greater than 126mg/dL, a random plasma glucose greater than 200mg/dL with symptoms, or a 2-hour glucose tolerance test glucose greater than 200mg/dL. Each of the diagnostic tests should be confirmed on a second occasion if there is doubt of hyperglycemia. If there is clear evidence such as hyperglycemic episode there is no need to repeat diagnostic testing.

For more information, see:
"Type 2 diabetes mellitus" on Medscape.
Nye, Z. (2009). First aid for the COMLEX: An osteopathic manipulative medicine review (2nd ed.). New York: McGraw-Hill Medical.

Link: Braunwald E et al. ACC/AHA guidelines for the management of patients with unstable angina and NSTEMI: executive summary and recommendations. Circulation. 2000;102(10):1193-1209.


Patient Presentation: Sensory Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Management
Body System: Cardiovascular System
Discipline: Emergency Medicine
A 56-year-old male with a history of hypertension presents to the emergency department with crushing, substernal chest pain that radiates to his left arm. The pain has been intermittent with a substernal pressure sensation that awoke him from his sleep. The patient is a non-smoker and his only medication is to control his blood pressure. Electrocardiogram is normal. Laboratory analysis is remarkable for a troponin I of 4.8 ng/mL. 
Question 1 of 3 in this set
What would be the most beneficial next step?

1. Administer diltiazem
2. Administer low molecular weight heparin
3. Administer morphine
4. Administer thrombolytics
5. Obtain a creatine kinase MB

The correct answer is: B
This patient presents with a non-ST-segment elevation myocardial infarction (NSTEMI), as indicated by the chest pain with onset at rest and elevated troponin I. In addition to many other medications, administration of an anticoagulant is indicated in NSTEMI. Anticoagulant medications, such as unfractionated heparin and low molecular weight heparin (enoxaparin), help prevent further thrombus formation and reinfarction. Overall, clinical trials have shown that low molecular weight heparin results in a statistically significant reduction in the rate of death or urgent revascularization and is superior to unfractionated heparin and, thus, is usually the preferred anticoagulant in unstable angina and NSTEMI. Additionally, in both low and high-risk patients with unstable angina or NSTEMI, pharmacologic treatment should be started immediately with consideration for surgical intervention in patients that do not respond to drug therapy. Other important medications for NSTEMI include antiplatelet drugs, such as aspirin and clopidogrel.

Answer A: Diltiazem is a calcium channel blocker used in the treatment of hypertension and angina pectoris, as well as some types of arrhythmia. Calcium channel blockers cause coronary vasodilation, afterload reduction and also have an effect on heart contractility. Calcium channel blockers are considered second-line treatment compared to beta blocker therapy. These agents have not shown to lower mortality in coronary artery disease.


Answer C: Administration of morphine is often used in patients with unstable angina or NSTEMI whose symptoms are not relieved by sublingual nitroglycerin. While pain control is important, the American College of Cardiology and American Heart Association deem anticoagulant and antiplatelet therapies a level A recommendation to be implemented immediately, while morphine is only a level C recommendation to be considered after nitroglycerin is attempted. Additionally, morphine offers no mortality reduction.


Answer D: While thrombolytics reduce mortality in ST-segment elevation myocardial infarction (STEMI), they are not indicated in patients with NSTEMI. Overall, data suggests thrombolytic therapy is harmful in patients with NSTEMI and may cause reinfarction or hemorrhage. On the other hand, thrombolytics should be administered to a STEMI patient if percutaneous coronary intervention cannot be performed within 90 minutes, chest pain began within the past 12 hours, and the patient has no contraindications to fibrinolytic therapy.


Answer E: Creatine kinase MB (CK-MB) is a cardiac marker used to assist in the diagnosis of an acute myocardial infarction. CK-MB is released into the serum after myocardial damage. CK-MB should not be used for the diagnosis of acute myocardial infarction. It is less sensitive and specific than troponin. Given we already know the patient has an elevated troponin I, a CK-MB is of no benefit at this time.




Bottom Line: Low molecular weight heparin is a first-line treatment in NSTEMI, in addition to antiplatelet therapy.

For more information, see:
Braunwald E et al. ACC/AHA guidelines for the management of patients with unstable angina and NSTEMI: executive summary and recommendations. Circulation. 2000;102(10):1193-1209.

Link: Stress testing on Uptodate


Patient Presentation: Sensory Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Diagnostic Technologies
Body System: Cardiovascular System
Discipline: Emergency Medicine
A 56-year-old male with a history of hypertension presents to the emergency department with crushing, substernal chest pain that radiates to his left arm. The pain has been intermittent with a substernal pressure sensation that awoke him from his sleep. The patient is a non-smoker and his only medication is to control his blood pressure. Electrocardiogram is normal. Laboratory analysis is remarkable for a troponin I of 4.8 ng/mL. 
Question 2 of 3 in this set
The most appropriate diagnostic procedure is

1. cardiovascular magnetic resonance
2. coronary angiography
3. dobutamine echocardiography
4. doppler echocardiography
5. exercise electrocardiogram

The correct answer is: B
Coronary angiography is the gold standard for the diagnosis of atherosclerosis. The extent and severity of coronary heart disease can be identified on angiography. Specifics, such as patient prognosis as well as optimal management are provided by the test. However, the functional capacity of a single vessel cannot be ascertained with angiography. Coronary angiography is indicated in patients with symptoms in spite of maximal medical treatment and for those considered high risk. This patient presents with active symptoms of non ST-segment elevation myocardial infarction and therefore should proceed to coronary angiography to determine whether revascularization is needed.

Answer A: Cardiovascular magnetic resonance (CMR) uses a 3-dimensional analysis to view the heart and its structures with high-resolution. CMR provides a greater sensitivity compared to dobutamine stress echocardiography. The negative drawbacks to CMR are patient intolerance, cost and contraindications to use. These contraindications include medical instability and/or medical devices. However, CMR has limited utility as the initial stress imaging modality due to lack of availability and associated expertise.


Answer C: Stress echocardiography demonstrates the extent of coronary artery disease and ischemia by observation of cardiac wall abnormalities. Pharmacological stress echocardiography is preferred in patients that are unable to perform treadmill exercise. Dobutamine is a pharmacological agent that increases heart rate and myocardial contractility. In contrast to exercise echocardiography, this technique is able to see the heart at variable stress levels and at peak stress. Although stress test without imaging is the preferred initial test in a patient who presents with cardiac chest pain, stress test with imaging is preferred in a certain subset of patients. Assessment of myocardial viability and visualization of cardiac lesions provides more information than a stress test without imaging. One indication for additional imaging includes those with preexisting electrographic abnormalities that may interfere with ECG monitoring such as a left bundle branch block or those with a ventricular paced rhythm. Also, patients that are unable to physically attempt the exercise component of the stress test are recommended to take this test. Patients with unstable angina have a higher risk of adverse effects during stress testing. This patient presents with symptoms suggestive of non ST-segment elevation myocardial infarction, therefore this patient should proceed to coronary angiography.


Answer D: Doppler echocardiography is used to measure left ventricular systolic function, valvular function and pulmonary artery pressure. Most patients with stable angina do not need a doppler echocardiography. Use of this imaging device is indicated in patients with prior myocardial infarction, undiagnosed heart murmur, signs and symptoms of heart failure, and complex ventricular arrhythmias. Doppler echocardiography is utilized in individuals to assess the presence of a valvular heart disease. This patient does not present with signs or symptoms that indicate the need for a doppler echocardiography.


Answer E: Exercise with electrocardiogram (ECG) monitoring is the initial cardiac stress test of choice in patients with exertional chest pain. The exercise stress test is preferred to pharmacological stress test because it provides the most information regarding a patients' symptoms and hemodynamic response during normal forms of activity. Furthermore, the inability to perform an exercise stress test is a negative predictor of chronic heart disease. Although this test has low sensitivity and specificity compared to other options, exercise ECG testing is cost-effective and able to identify patients at low and high risk. A 1-mm depression of the ST segment during exercise is characteristic of ischemic disease. A depression greater than 2 mm is a sign of severe ischemic disease and represents a poor prognosis. However, exercise with ECG is not appropriate in patients who cannot perform the appropriate work load and/or individuals with baseline ECG abnormalities that may obscure the final diagnosis. Other absolute contraindications include acute myocardial infarction within 2 days, unstable angina, and/or uncontrolled heart failure. This patient would be an inappropriate candidate and therefore should not undergo exercise electrocardiogram testing.




Bottom Line: Coronary angiography is the most accurate method to diagnosis coronary artery disease and is recommended in high risk or symptomatic patients.

For more information, see:
Stress testing on Uptodate

Link: CABG on Uptodate


Patient Presentation: Disease Detection & Monitoring
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Management
Body System: Cardiovascular System
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 56-year-old male with a history of hypertension presents to the emergency department with crushing, substernal chest pain that radiates to his left arm. The pain has been intermittent with a substernal pressure sensation that awoke him from his sleep. The patient is a non-smoker and his only medication is to control his blood pressure. Electrocardiogram is normal. Laboratory analysis is remarkable for a troponin I of 4.8 ng/mL. 
Question 3 of 3 in this set
The selected test is significant for blockage in three coronary vessels. What is the most appropriate next step?

1. Balloon angioplasty
2. Bare metal stenting
3. Coronary artery bypass grafting
4. Drug-eluting stenting
5. Medical therapy

The correct answer is: C
Coronary artery bypass grafting (CABG) is performed on patients with coronary artery disease to improve the quality of life and reduce cardiac-related mortality. Patients with diabetes are more likely to have coronary heart disease. Coronary heart disease is a major cause of morbidity and mortality among diabetics. These patients are more likely to have multivessel disease and episodes of silent ischemia compared to non-diabetics. Revascularization is recommended in patients that are considered high-risk. The main indications for CABG include three-vessel disease with greater than 70 percent stenosis, left main coronary disease with greater than 50 percent stenosis and left ventricular dysfunction. For most patients with diabetes and multivessel disease, CABG is preferred over percutaneous coronary intervention with stenting due to better outcomes.

Answer A: Balloon angioplasty, otherwise known as percutaneous transluminal coronary angioplasty (PTCA), is a non-surgical alternative to coronary artery bypass graft surgery. Balloon angioplasty is not the best option due to complications. Acute and subacute closure and restenosis are the most significant problems seen in patients with balloon stenting. Furthermore, patients with multivessel disease have shown reduced survival rates compared to those who received coronary artery bypass surgery.


Answer B: Bare metal coronary artery stents are used in percutaneous coronary intervention to treat stable and unstable angina, acute myocardial infarction, and multivessel disease. Although mortality is not significantly different, bare metal stents have a higher rate of repeat revascularization due to restenosis. Thus, bare metal stenting is the not the best option in a patient with multivessel disease.


Answer D: Drug-eluting stents are coated with an antiproliferative drug, which allows drug elution into the coronary wall for weeks after stent implantation. Drug-eluting stents were developed to reduce restenosis rates that were seen in bare metal stents. Complications include stent thrombosis. In addition, drug-eluting stents had a higher rate of target lesion revascularization in diabetics. Furthermore, most studies have shown that event-free survival is worse after revascularization with percutaneous cutaneous intervention in diabetic patients compared to non-diabetic patients.


Answer E: Medical therapy is indicated in patients with short-term risk of death or low probability of recurrent myocardial infarction. For those with recurrent angina or evidence of ischemic disease on stress testing, invasive therapy is more appropriate. This is a high-risk patient who presents with lesions on coronary angiography, therefore, revascularization is indicated.




Bottom Line: Coronary artery bypass grafting is recommended in patients with diabetes and multivessel disease who require revascularization.

For more information, see:
CABG on Uptodate

Link: "The metabolic syndrome (insulin resistance syndrome or syndrome X)" on UpToDate.


Patient Presentation: Disease Detection & Monitoring
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Scientific Understanding of Health & Disease Mechanisms
Body System: Endocrine System
Discipline: Family Medicine
A 53-year-old male presents to the office for a physical examination and age-appropriate screening labs. Physical examinations reveal an obese patient with high blood pressure. Laboratory analysis reveals impaired glycemic control and dyslipidemia. Which of the following fulfill the criteria for metabolic syndrome in this patient?

1. creatinine 2.5 mg/dL, HDL 24 mg/dL and fasting glucose 106 mg/dL
2. triglycerides 190 mg/dL, HDL 24 mg/dL and ALT 260 u/L
3. triglycerides 198 mg/dL, HDL 28 mg/dL and blood pressure 125/85
4. triglycerides 198 mg/dL, HDL 28 mg/dL and fasting glucose 103 mg/dL
5. triglycerides 198 mg/dL, HDL 55 mg/dL and fasting glucose 118 mg/dL

The correct answer is: D
The most likely diagnosis in this patient is metabolic syndrome.  Metabolic syndrome is also known as syndrome X or insulin resistance syndrome.  It is associated with an increased risk of coronary artery disease and mortality from an MI or CVA.  Patients may be completely asymptomatic but will most often be obese with hypertension, hyperglycemia and elevated lipids.  Three out of five criteria must be met to make the diagnosis of metabolic syndrome.  Criteria for making the diagnosis include: abdominal obesity defined as greater than 40 inches in men or greater than 35 inches in females, triglycerides > 150 mg/dL, HDL < 40 mg/dL in men and < 50 mg/dL in women, blood pressure > 130/85 or on hypertensive medication and fasting glucose > 100 mg/dL.  Treatment for metabolic syndrome consists of weight loss, exercise and optimizing cholesterol and blood pressure levels.  Metformin has been shown to slow the onset of diabetes in this high-risk patient population.

Answers A & B & C & E: See explanation above.




Bottom Line: Criteria for making the diagnosis include: abdominal obesity defined as greater than 40 inches in men or greater than 35 inches in females, triglycerides > 150 mg/dL, HDL < 40 mg/dL in men and < 50 mg/dL in women, blood pressure > 130/85 or on hypertensive medication and fasting glucose > 100 mg/dL.

COMBANK Insight : Some questions on your level 2 exam will test specific definitions and criteria needed to make the diagnosis.  Specific numbers for making the diagnosis of hyperlipidemia, diabetes, metabolic syndrome and hypertension should be memorized.

For more information, see:
"The metabolic syndrome (insulin resistance syndrome or syndrome X)" on UpToDate.
