Link: De Quervains on Medscape


Patient Presentation: Sensory & CNS Difficulties
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Diagnostic Technologies
Body System: Nervous System
Discipline: Family Medicine
A 42-year-old female presents to the office with the complaints of wrist pain. History reveals she is right hand dominant, and the symptoms are worse at night and while she is typing on her computer at work. Physical examination reveals reproduction of pain in the wrist by ulnar deviation of the thumb. You also note tenderness over the coronoid process and lateral epicondyle of the right elbow.  The most appropriate diagnosis is

1. carpal tunnel syndrome
2. De Quervain tenosynovitis
3. cubital tunnel syndrome
4. C4 spondylosis
5. Guyon's canal syndrome

The correct answer is: B
De Quervain tenosynovitis is a painful stenosing tenosynovitis involving the first extensor compartment of the wrist. Most cases are associated with overuse. Patients present with pain on thumb and wrist movement with focal tenderness over the radial styloid which is along the radial border of the hand. The abductor pollicis longs and extensor pollicis brevis are typically involved. The Finkelstein's test is provocative test for the diagnosis of the disease. Sharp pain is elicited along the distal radius by downward deflection of the thumb and ulnar bone. Plain films are non-diagnostic but may show soft-tissue swelling over the radial styloid. Focal abnormalities such as cortical erosion, sclerosis or periosteal reaction may be seen.

Due to compensations that develop secondary to pain in the APL or EPB muscles, you often see counterstain points arise around the elbow as the compensating muscles become overworked and irritated.

Answer A: Carpal tunnel syndrome is due to median nerve entrapment at the flexor retinaculum and patients are often unable to grip normally and cannot oppose the thumb and index finger normally. Carpal tunnel syndrome symptoms are typically felt in the thumb through 4th finger. Over time, patients can develop muscle atrophy of the thenar eminence. The most common presentation is numbness and tingling over the first three digits of the hand. Pain is usually worst at night. Phalen's maneuver and Tinel's sign can reproduce the patient's symptoms. However, the Finkelstein's test is diagnostic for De Quervain tenosynovitis.


Answer C: Cubital tunnel syndrome is caused by ulnar nerve entrapment at the medial edge of the elbow. Cubital tunnel syndrome presents with similar symptoms of Guyon's canal syndrome. Symptoms include tingling and/or pain along the 4th and 5th digits of the corresponding hand. Altered sensation in these fingers may also be reported. Muscle wasting of the hypothenar eminence may be noted on physical examination. Although, cubital tunnel syndrome and Guyon's canal syndrome are both types of ulnar neuropathy, cubital tunnel syndrome is less likely in this patient. This patient denies pain in the elbow or history of trauma to the area. In addition, the Finkelstein's test is diagnostic for De Quervain tenosynovitis.


Answer D: Cervical disc disease can cause referred pain to the shoulder, neck and or back. Examination of the cervical spine should be included in all evaluations of back, shoulder and or neck pain. Patients often complain of pain that started in the neck area. Pain that radiates down the arms passed the elbow is suggestive of cervical disc disorder. The Spurling's test is used in the evaluation of a patient with these complaints. Reproduction of the pain with extension and rotation of the cervical spine with axial loading is highly suggestive of the disease.


Answer E: The ulnar nerve is the terminal branch of the brachial plexus. It is responsible for innervation to the muscles of the forearm and hand. It also is responsible for sensation to the 4th and 5th digits. Ulnar nerve entrapment at Guyon's canal may cause denervation and/or paralysis of the muscles. Anatomically, the ulnar nerve is entrapped as it passes through the tunnel formed by the pisiform and hamate bones and the pisohamate ligament at the level of the wrist. This produces atrophy of the hypothenar eminence and impairs both adduction and abduction of the 4th and 5th fingers. Muscle wasting of these digits may occur but is rare. Usually patients also complain of a “pins and needles” sensation of the 5th digit and the medial aspect of the palmar surface of the 4th digit. Physical examination will be evident for sensory loss along the ulnar distribution in the hand with preservation in the dorsal aspect of the hand.




Bottom Line: De Quervain tenosynovitis is inflammation of the first dorsal compartment of the wrist.

For more information, see:
De Quervains on Medscape

Patient Presentation: Musculoskeletal Difficulties And Muscular Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Management
Body System: Musculoskeletal System
Discipline: Osteopathic Principle and Practice
A 26-year old left-handed female with no significant past medical history presents with neck pain over the last few weeks. The neck pain is described as a stiff, sore sensation localized around T3-T4 around the spinous processes. NSAIDs alleviate the pain and she has increased her medication from 1 tab as needed to 1 tab everyday in the last few weeks. Warm compresses and stretching help her neck pain as well. She admits that she has been stressed out more than usual at work trying to get her projects complete on time; she works as a graphic designer and spends a lot of time in front of a computer. She denies radiating pain to her upper extremities, fevers, chills, or recent illnesses. 

On physical exam, the patient appears to be well nourished and in no apparent distress. She is afebrile. Upper extremities are neurovascularly intact bilaterally, with appropriate and equal muscle strength. Physical exam findings are notable for the following: AA RR, hypertonic left trapezius muscle, increased thoracic kyphosis, and a right posterior transverse process at T4 that becomes more symmetric with flexion. What is the most appropriate initial patient positioning in order to address her thoracic somatic dysfunction with post-isometric relaxation MET?


1. The patient’s head and neck are extended, sidebent left, and rotated left until the edge of the restrictive barriers are addressed at T4
2. The patient’s head and neck are flexed, sidebent right, and rotated right until the edge of the restrictive barriers are addressed at T4
3. The trunk is extended, sidebent left, and rotated left until the edge of the restrictive barriers are addressed at T4
4. The patient’s trunk is flexed, sidebent right, and rotated right until the edge of the restrictive barriers are addressed at T4
5. The patient’s head and neck are extended, sidebent left, and rotated left until the edge of the restrictive barriers are addressed at T4

The correct answer is: A
Muscle energy technique is a form of direct osteopathic manipulative treatment where the patient’s muscles are actively contracted from a precisely controlled position, in a specific direction, and against an executed physician counterforce. The majority of thoracic MET involves direct post-isometric relaxation. In this type of MET, the physician instructs the patient to isometrically contract the agonist muscle. Immediately following an isometric contraction, the neuromuscular apparatus is in a refractory state during which enhanced passive stretching may be performed. The physician may take up the myofascial slack during this relaxed refractory period.

Upper thoracic somatic dysfunctions (T1-T4) use the head and neck as a lever to induce motion at the segment; lower thoracic somatic dysfunctions (T5-T12) use the trunk as a lever. On physical exam, this patient has a T4 FSRRR; at T4, there is a restriction in extension, left sidebending, and left rotation. With the patient seated, the physician monitors the T4-T5 interspace with one hand. With the other hand, the physician uses the patient’s head and neck as a lever to induce extension, left sidebending, and left rotation until the edge of the restrictive barrier is reached at T4. The patient is instructed to flex the head and neck and turn right while the physician provides an unyielding counterforce. The isometric contraction provided by the patient is maintained for 3 to 5 seconds and then the patient is to stop and relax. When the patient is relaxed, the physician will reposition the head and neck in order to engage the edge of the new restrictive barriers in extension, left sidebending, and left rotation. The series of isometric contraction, relaxation, and re-positioning is repeated 3 to 5 times or until motion is improved at T4.

Answer B: This is the patient's position of ease. Post-isometric relaxation MET is a direct technique.


Answer C: The patient positioning is correct. However, the head and neck as a lever is better suited to adequately address the upper thoracic segment compared to the trunk.


Answer D: This is the patient's position of ease. Post-isometric relaxation MET is a direct technique. Also, the head and neck as a lever is better suited to adequately address the upper thoracic segment compared to the trunk.




Bottom Line: When utilizing thoracic MET to treat an upper thoracic somatic dysfunction (T1-T4), the head and neck function as a lever in order to induce motion at the desired segment; for a lower thoracic somatic dysfunction (T5-T12), the trunk is the lever.

COMBANK Insight : Do not forget the guideline or rule of three in order to locate the thoracic transverse process from the thoracic spinous processes:

T1-T3: the transverse process is located at the level of the corresponding spinous process

T4-T6: the transverse process is located ½ of a segment above the corresponding spinous process (i.e. the transverse process of T4 is located halfway between T3 and T4)

T7-T9: the transverse process is located one segment above the corresponding spinous process (i.e. the transverse process of T8 is located at the level of T7)

T10: same rule as T7-T9*

T11: same rule as T4-T6*

T12: same rule as T1-T3

* - Some texts that state that the transverse processes of T10-T12 are at the same level as the spinous process

For more information, see:
Muscle Energy Techniques. In: Nicholas AS, Nicholas EA. Atlas of Osteopathic Techniques. 3rd ed. Philadelphia, PA: Lippincott Williams & Wilkins; 2015.
Normal Vertebral Motion. In: DeStefano, LA. Greenman’s Principles of Manual Medicine. Philadelphia, PA: Wolters Kluwer; 2017.
Thoracic Spine Technique. In: DeStefano, LA. Greenman’s Principles of Manual Medicine. Philadelphia, PA: Wolters Kluwer; 2017.
Savarese RG. OMT Review: A Comprehensive Review in Osteopathic Medicine. 2009:19,136,138.

Patient Presentation: Respiratory Difficulties
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Diagnostic Technologies
Body System: Cardiovascular System
Discipline: Internal Medicine

A 43-year-old African-American male with past medical history of GERD, Hypertension, Diabetes mellitus and Chronic Kidney disease presents to the office with the complaint of palpitations. History reveals dizziness, heart palpitations, shortness of breath and 1 presyncopal episode in the last six months. Patient states he is healthy and denies any similar episodes in the past. He states the presyncopal episode was sudden and was not followed by any confusion. Patient is adopted and unsure about his family history. Physical exam reveals a normal cardiac and pulmonary exam. An electrocardiogram shows  initial slurring of the QRS complex. He goes to the cardiologist who advises of the risk associated with this condition.


Question 1 of 3 in this set
The initial slurring of the QRS complex is known as a

1. Delta wave
2. J wave
3. Q wave
4. RSR’ complex
5. Sawtooth appearance

The correct answer is: A
This is the result of the wave of depolarization. It starts in the atrium, arrives at the ventricles early, and had begun to partially depolarize earlier, which gives rise to the characteristic initial slurring of the QRS complex, also known as a delta wave.

Answer B: A J wave, also known as an Osborn wave, is a positive deflection occurring at the junction between the QRS complex and the ST segment. J waves are typically associated with hypothermia but may also be seen in patients with hypercalcemia, brain injury, vasospastic angina and ventricular fibrillation.


Answer C: A Q wave is the initial negative deflection of the QRS complex. Q waves may be normal or pathologic depending on size and may be associated with myocardial infarction.


Answer D: An RSR’ complex is also known as “rabbit ears” and may be associated with a right bundle branch block.


Answer E: A sawtooth appearance refers to the appearance of rapidly repeating P waves and is associated with atrial flutter.




Bottom Line: As a result of the bypass tract in patients with WPW, the ventricles begin to partially depolarize earlier which gives rise to the characteristic initial slurring of the QRS complex, also known as a delta wave.

COMBANK Insight : You may be given a description of an EKG finding or an actual EKG tracing and asked to interpret it. Make sure you review the most common EKG findings. Take your studying one step further and make sure you know the treatment for each specific EKG finding.

For more information, see:
"Wolff Parkinson White" UpToDate

Link: UpToDate Wolf Parkinson White Syndrome


Patient Presentation: Respiratory Difficulties
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Diagnostic Technologies
Body System: Cardiovascular System
Discipline: Internal Medicine

A 43-year-old African-American male with past medical history of GERD, Hypertension, Diabetes mellitus and Chronic Kidney disease presents to the office with the complaint of palpitations. History reveals dizziness, heart palpitations, shortness of breath and 1 presyncopal episode in the last six months. Patient states he is healthy and denies any similar episodes in the past. He states the presyncopal episode was sudden and was not followed by any confusion. Patient is adopted and unsure about his family history. Physical exam reveals a normal cardiac and pulmonary exam. An electrocardiogram shows  initial slurring of the QRS complex. He goes to the cardiologist who advises of the risk associated with this condition.


Question 2 of 3 in this set
The most likely diagnosis is

1. Brugada syndrome with risk of sudden cardiac death
2. hypothermia with risk of sudden cardiac death
3. Mobitz type I atrioventricular block with risk of sudden cardiac death
4. Mobitz type II atrioventricular block with risk of sudden cardiac death
5. Wolff-Parkinson-White syndrome with risk of sudden cardiac death

The correct answer is: E
The most likely diagnosis is Wolff-Parkinson-White syndrome. WPW is a type of AV junction disorder that can lead to atrioventricular reciprocating tachycardia (AVRT). AVRT results when there is a circular movement of an impulse between the AV node and the atrium through a bypass tract, known as the bundle of Kent. Patients can present with palpitations, shortness of breath, angina, syncope or lightheadedness. Treatment may consist of carotid massage, the Valsalva maneuver, adenosine and cardioversion if the patient is unstable. WPW may also be known as pre-excitation syndrome. As a result of the bypass tract, the ventricles begin to partially depolarize earlier which gives rise to the characteristic initial slurring of the QRS complex, also known as a delta wave. Treatment may also consist of class IA or class III antiarrhythmics.

Answer A: Brugada syndrome is a genetic disease with a characteristic EKG pattern and can be a cause of sudden cardiac death. The pattern seen on the ECG is persistent ST elevations in the electrocardiographic leadsV1-V3 with a right bundle branch block (RBBB) appearance with or without the terminal S waves in the lateral leads that are associated with a typical RBBB.


Answer B: J waves are associated with hypothermia. A j wave, also known as an Osborn wave, is a positive deflection occurring at the junction between the QRS complex and the ST segment. J waves are typically associated with hypothermia but may also be seen in patients with hypercalcemia, brain injury, vasospastic angina and ventricular fibrillation.


Answer C: Mobitz type I (Wenckebach) is a type of second degree heart block. EKG will show a progressive PR lengthening until a dropped beat occurs.


Answer D: Mobitz type II is a type of second degree heart block. EKG will show unexpected dropped beats without a change in the PR interval.




Bottom Line: WPW is a type of AV junction disorder that can lead to atrioventricular reciprocating tachycardia (AVRT). It has a characteristic delta wave which can be observed on an EKG tracing. Risk stratification is important and associated with sudden cardiac death in symptomatic patients. Symptoms include pre-syncope, palpitation and shortness of breath.

COMBANK Insight : On the exam, EKG finding or an actual EKG tracing may be presented. It is imperative to review the most common EKG findings and take the preparation further by being familiar with the treatment of each specific EKG finding.

For more information, see:
UpToDate Wolf Parkinson White Syndrome

Patient Presentation: Respiratory Difficulties
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Management
Body System: Cardiovascular System
Discipline: Internal Medicine

A 43-year-old African-American male with past medical history of GERD, Hypertension, Diabetes mellitus and Chronic Kidney disease presents to the office with the complaint of palpitations. History reveals dizziness, heart palpitations, shortness of breath and 1 presyncopal episode in the last six months. Patient states he is healthy and denies any similar episodes in the past. He states the presyncopal episode was sudden and was not followed by any confusion. Patient is adopted and unsure about his family history. Physical exam reveals a normal cardiac and pulmonary exam. An electrocardiogram shows  initial slurring of the QRS complex. He goes to the cardiologist who advises of the risk associated with this condition.


Question 3 of 3 in this set
Which of the following treatments should be avoided?

1. Amiodarone
2. Carotid massage
3. Digoxin
4. Procainamide
5. Valsalva maneuver

The correct answer is: C
The most likely diagnosis is Wolff-Parkinson-White syndrome. WPW is a type of AV junction disorder that can lead to atrioventricular reciprocating tachycardia (AVRT). AVRT results when there is a circular movement of an impulse between the AV node and the atrium through a bypass tract, known as the bundle of Kent. Patients can present with palpitations, shortness of breath, angina, syncope or lightheadedness. Treatment of WPW may consist of carotid massage, the Valsalva maneuver, adenosine and cardioversion if the patient is unstable. WPW may also be known as pre-excitation syndrome. As a result of the bypass tract, the ventricles begin to partially depolarize earlier which gives rise to the characteristic initial slurring of the QRS complex, also known as a delta wave. Treatment may also consist of class IA or class III antiarrhythmics. Digoxin, beta-blockers and calcium channel blockers should be avoided because these medications may block the AV node further, thereby increasing the risk of developing re-entry leading to supraventricular tachycardia.

Answer A: Treatment of WPW may consist of carotid massage, the Valsalva maneuver, adenosine and cardioversion if the patient is unstable. Amiodarone is a class III antiarrhythmic used in the treatment of WPW.


Answers B & E: Treatment of WPW may consist of carotid massage, the Valsalva maneuver, adenosine and cardioversion if the patient is unstable.


Answer D: Treatment of WPW may consist of carotid massage, the Valsalva maneuver, adenosine and cardioversion if the patient is unstable. Procainamide is a class IA antiarrhythmic used in the treatment of WPW.




Bottom Line: Treatment of WPW may consist of carotid massage, the Valsalva maneuver, adenosine and cardioversion if the patient is unstable.

Patient Presentation: Digestive Difficulties
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Scientific Understanding of Health & Disease Mechanisms
Body System: Endocrine System
Discipline: Internal Medicine
While on a medical mission trip, you see a 5-year-old male at the local clinic in a remote village of the Dominican Republic. Vital signs are unremarkable. Physical examination reveals a very thin malnourished child younger than stated age. He is able to speak appropriately but is unable to stand and walk. There is visible deformity of the wrists noted. You note paravertebral hypertonicity that is reportedly tender to palpation from T1-4.  Radiographs of the lower extremities and wrists are obtained, as seen in the exhibit. Which of the following represent the most likely laboratory findings in this patient?

1. decreased alkaline phosphatase, decreased PTH, decreased serum phosphate
2. elevated alkaline phosphatase, decreased PTH, decreased serum phosphate
3. elevated alkaline phosphatase, elevated PTH, decreased serum phosphate
4. elevated alkaline phosphatase, elevated PTH, elevated serum phosphate
5. elevated alkaline phosphatase, normal PTH, decreased serum phosphate

The correct answer is: C
This patient is most likely suffering from nutritional rickets. Nutritional rickets will show decreased to normal levels of serum calcium, decreased serum phosphate, elevated parathyroid hormone (PTH), and elevated alkaline phosphatase. Nutritional rickets is due to a dietary deficiency of vitamin D (most common) or calcium. Exhibit A demonstrates key radiographic findings of nutritional rickets, mainly bowing of long bones as well as cupping and fraying of the metaphyseal region in the wrist.

This patient is exhibiting TART changes from T1-4 which can represent a variety of different pathologies and affected tissues.  In this case, the viscerosomatic reflex is in response to the skeletal and endocrine changes taking place in the upper extremities and the parathyroid glands.

Answer A: Primary hypophosphatemia is the most common cause of non-nutritional rickets. Hypophosphatemia is also seen in refeeding syndrome and respiratory alkalosis. In primary hypophosphatemia, lab results will show decreased serum calcium, low serum phosphate, elevated (not decreased) alkaline phosphatase, as well as increased PTH.


Answer B: The different causes of rickets can either present with decreased or normal levels of calcium. When calcium levels are low, PTH levels become elevated which allow the activation of 1-alpha-hydroxylase, thus allowing the conversion of 25-hydroxyvitamin D to the active form of vitamin D (calcitriol). Therefore, PTH levels will always be elevated with rickets.


Answer D: Elevated serum phosphate levels (hyperphosphatemia) normally result in increased levels of PTH, which leads to increased phosphate excretion by the kidneys into the urine. Concurrently, calcium levels are usually decreased due to precipitation of calcium and phosphate into tissues. As a result of low serum calcium levels, as mentioned previously, this also will lead to increased PTH levels.


Answer E: Hypophosphatemic rickets or X-linked hypophosphatemia (XLH) is a dominant X-linked disorder characterized by the inability for the proximal tubules of the kidney to reabsorb phosphate due to a mutation in the PHEX gene. It is currently theorized that PHEX protects the extracellular matrix glycoproteins from proteolysis. It is associated with decreased serum phosphate, elevated alkaline phosphatase, normal PTH, and normal serum calcium.




Bottom Line: Nutritional rickets will present with an elevated alkaline phosphatase, elevated PTH, and decreased phosphate.

COMBANK Insight : The key differentiating laboratory component of nutritional from X-linked hypophosphatemic rickets is an elevated parathyroid hormone.

For more information, see:
"Rickets" on Medscape.

Question 1 of 4 in this set
A 47-year-old female nurse recovered from a past infection with hepatitis B virus after suffering a needle-stick injury 20 years ago.

1. HBsAg + anti-HBs
2. HBsAg + HBeAg + anti-HBc (IgM)
3. Anti-HBc (IgM) only
4. Anti-HBs only
5. Anti-HBs + anti HBc (IgG)

The correct answer is: E
The presence of antibodies to the core and surface antigens is consistent with recovery from a previous infection.

Answer A: Positive serology for both surface antigen and surface antibody is not seen under any conditions.


Answer B: This serology is consistent with acute, active infection. IgM antibodies to the core antigen indicate that the infection is acute. The absence of other antibodies indicates that the infection is active and transmissible.


Answer C: Isolated antibodies to the core antigen (with no other positive serology) is the hallmark of the window period.


Answer D: The presence of antibodies to the surface antigen but not the core antigen is the hallmark of a successful vaccination.

A 54-year-old male with elevated liver enzymes appearing 8 weeks following exposure.

1. HBsAg + anti-HBs
2. HBsAg + HBeAg + anti-HBc (IgM)
3. Anti-HBc (IgM) only
4. Anti-HBs only
5. Anti-HBs + anti HBc (IgG)

The correct answer is: B
This serology is consistent with acute, active infection. IgM antibodies to the core antigen indicate that the infection is acute. The absence of other antibodies indicates that the infection is active and transmissible. Hepatitis B surface antigen (HBsAg) is the first antigen to be detected in hepatitis B infection, followed by hepatitis B e antigen (HBeAg). As an immune response is mounted, anti-Hbc becomes detected. This occurs between 2 and 12 weeks after infection.

Answer A: Positive serology for both surface antigen and surface antibody is not seen under any conditions. Once an antibody is produced against the surface antigen, the surface antigen is no longer detectable.


Answer C: Isolated antibodies to the core antigen (with no other positive serology) is the hallmark of the window period.

Patient Presentation: Disease Detection & Monitoring
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Diagnostic Technologies
Body System: Hepatobiliary System
Discipline: Internal Medicine
For each numbered item (patient presentation), select the one heading (serologic hepatitis B marker) most closely associated with it. Each lettered heading may be used once, more than once, or not at all.
Question 3 of 4 in this set
A 28-year-old woman presents for her annual visit. She has no complaints and states she has been in good health this year. Patient history is positive for hepatitis B infection. Laboratory analysis shows HBV DNA is greater than 200,000 with an INR of 0.9, alanine aminotransferase of 18 units/L and aspartate aminotransferase of 14 units/L.

1. Anti-HBc (IgM) only
2. Anti-HBs + anti HBc (IgG)
3. Anti-HBs only
4. HBsAg + anti-HBc (IgG)
5. HBsAg + HBeAg + anti-HBc (IgM)

The correct answer is: D
This patient presents with a chronic hepatitis B virus (HBV) infection in the immune tolerant state, as evidenced by a high circulating viral load with no evidence of liver inflammation. Patients with immune tolerant HBV have positive hepatitis B surface antigen (HBsAg) and Anti-HBc IgG titers. The HBe antigen or antibody can be positive or negative in these patients. This is a typical presentation of a person born in a hepatitis B-endemic area, such as Southeast Asia or Africa. In this patient, HBV was most likely acquired perinatally. As long as these patients maintain normal aminotransferase levels, they are considered low-risk for progression of the disease. However, as she ages she is at increased risk for progression of the disease. Therefore, aminotransferases should be monitored. If the levels of aminotransferases increase, then the patient should receive a liver biopsy and treatment.

Answer A: Isolated antibodies to the core antigen, with no other positive serology, is the hallmark of the window period. The window period is the point at which the assays cannot yet detect the hepatitis B surface antigen and hepatitis B e surface antigen in the blood. The window period is typically six months after infection of the disease.


Answer B: A positive titer of anti-HBs can indicate previous immunization, as well as those who have recovered from an actual hepatitis B infection. Anti-HBs represents the production of antibodies to the hepatitis B virus. However, a positive titer of anti-HBs can indicate previous immunization, as well as those who have recovered from an actual hepatitis B infection. To differentiate between the two diagnosis, one should check the level of anti-HBc (IgG). Anti-HBc is antibodies produced in reaction to the core antigen of the virus. A positive anti-HBc (IgG) level, in conjunction with a positive anti-HBs titer, indicate a resolved hepatitis B viral infection.


Answer C: Patients with only a positive anti-HBs titer have been previously immunization to the hepatitis B virus. Vaccination against the hepatitis B virus initiates the production of antibodies against HBsAg. These antibodies, which are labeled as Anti-HBs, confer passive immunity to the virus. However, a positive titer of anti-HBs can indicate previous immunization, as well as those who have recovered from an actual hepatitis B infection. To differentiate between the two diagnosis, one should check the level of anti-HBc (IgG). Anti--HBc is antibodies produced in reaction to the core antigen of the virus. A positive anti-HBc (IgG) level in conjunction with a positive anti-HBs titer indicate a resolved hepatitis B viral infection.


Answer E: This serology is consistent with acute, active infection. Patients with acute, active hepatitis B virus (HBV) infection present with elevated aminotransaminase. Viral markers that can be identified in the serum to support an acute diagnosis include the presence of hepatitis B surface antigen (HBsAg) and hepatitis B e antigen (HBeAg). IgM antibodies to the core antigen also support that the infection is acute. The absence of other antibodies indicates that the infection is active and transmissible.




Bottom Line: High HBV titers with normal aminotransferases indicate an immune tolerant state. These patients have positive HBsAg and anti-HBc (IgG) titers.

COMBANK Insight : Be prepared to know the timeline of hepatitis serologic markers, as this question is a favorite on the COMLEX examination.

Patient Presentation: Musculoskeletal Difficulties And Muscular Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Diagnostic Technologies
Body System: Musculoskeletal System
Discipline: Family Medicine
A 23-year-old female gymnast presents to the urgent care center with the complaint of acute onset lower back pain that began earlier in the day after a dismount from the uneven bars.  She is otherwise healthy except for chronic “on and off” lower back pain for which she has been evaluated multiple times for. She was told it was a strain and was prescribed physical therapy.  She describes this pain as achy and similar to her past experiences, although ibuprofen is not helping today as it usually does.  She states that it is worse when she bends backward and may radiate to her buttocks but denies any other neurological symptoms. Physical examinations reveal her gait is stiff and antalgic.  There is bilateral paraspinal hypertonicity with hyperlordosis and hamstring tightness with a positive step-off sign. The remainder of the neuromusculoskeletal exam is unremarkable. The most appropriate course of action is

1. corticosteroid injection
2. immediate neurosurgical intervention
3. obtain a plain radiograph
4. obtain magnetic resonance imaging
5. osteopathic manipulative treatment

The correct answer is: C
Given this patient’s reported history and physical exam findings, including a positive “step-off” sign, spondylolisthesis should be suspected and the initial diagnostic study of choice is a plain radiograph looking for forward slippage (anterolisthesis) of one vertebrae in relation to the one below it as seen best in a lateral view.  This is caused by bilateral defects of the pars interarticularis with resultant vertebral subluxation, most commonly between L4 and L5.  It is commonly seen in athletes who perform repetitive back bending.  Most patients are asymptomatic, but when symptomatic, usually present with mid low back pain that may radiate to the buttocks or thighs and is worse with extension.   Radicular symptoms are usually absent, but if the listhesis is severe and unstable enough, they may be present due to nerve root compression.  Treatment is mainly conservative including physical therapy, activity modification, weight loss, and analgesics.  Spondylolisthesis is graded based on the degree of slippage.  See Exhibit.

Answer A: Given the acuity of her injury and its relation to known trauma (the dismount from the uneven bars), one would want to be sure that there are no fractures or dislocations before initiating any specific therapies.


Answer B: There are no reported symptoms and no exam findings to indicate significant neurologic involvement that would require immediate intervention. If she were having symptoms of cauda equina syndrome (bilateral leg weakness, saddle anesthesia, or incontinence), then neurodiagnostic imaging and immediate neurosurgical evaluation would be warranted.


Answer D: A plain radiograph should be obtained first. Magnetic resonance imaging would be appropriate if she were having radicular symptoms in dermatomal distributions that would indicate nerve root or spinal cord compression. She does complain of radiation to the buttocks, however, this is a nonspecific radiation pattern and not necessarily indicative of neural foraminal stenosis or nerve root impingement.


Answer E: This patient would definitely benefit from osteopathic manipulative treatment; however, it is important to obtain imaging in this case first to rule out serious injury. Of note, high velocity low amplitude manipulation is contraindicated in persons with spondylolisthesis.




Bottom Line: Spondylolisthesis is an anterior displacement of one vertebra in relation to the one below it.  It is graded based on degree of slippage as evaluated by imaging.  Plan films are generally the first diagnostic image of choice.  Magnetic resonance imaging may be obtained if there are signs or symptoms of neurologic compromise.

COMBANK Insight : It has been shown that up to 52% of asymptomatic patients may have disc pathology on magnetic resonance imaging.  Therefore, it is important to keep in mind that abnormalities discovered on magnetic resonance imaging should be correlated clinically; if the abnormality would not cause the patient’s symptoms it is likely coincidental.   Be sure to develop good physical exam skills to help guide your decision-making as to which diagnostic imaging modalities to utilize and when.

For more information, see:
Spondylolisthesis, Spondylolysis, and Spondylosis on Medscape

Patient Presentation: Sensory Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Diagnostic Technologies
Body System: Cardiovascular System
Discipline: Emergency Medicine
A 63-year-old male presents to the emergency department with the complaint of sudden-onset chest tightness radiating to the left axilla. Past medical history reveals hypertension and hypercholesterolemia. Physical examination reveals a diaphoretic male with tachycardia with muscles spasms from T1-4 bilaterally and an exquisitely tender nodule overlying the left transverse process of T2. An ECG is obtained and reveal ST elevation in leads V1-V6, with reciprocal ST depression in leads II, III, and aVF.

Question 1 of 2 in this set
The most likely diagnosis is

1. acute anterior myocardial infarction
2. acute inferior myocardial infarction
3. acute pericarditis
4. old inferior myocardial infarction
5. unstable angina

The correct answer is: A
This patient is suffering from an acute anterior wall myocardial infarction (MI). Patients with an acute MI may present with chest pain, tightness or pressure, shortness of breath, and diaphoresis. Female patients are more likely to have an atypical presentation with nausea, light-headedness, and anxiety. An ECG can indicate the location of the MI. Anterior wall MIs produce ST elevation in the precordial leads, with reciprocal ST depression in the inferior leads. Anterior wall MIs are due to occlusion in the left anterior descending coronary artery.

Though this question is fairly clear-cut on the cardiac origin of this chest pain, other questions will be more vague and you will need to differentiate between cardiac chest pain and chest pain of non cardiac origin (GI, MSK, etc.).  Viscerosomatic reflexes will often be given in a test, along with Chapman points, that will help you make this distinction.  The sympathetic innervation for cardiac tissue comes from T1-4 and therefore you will see viscerosomatic changes reflected through TART changes in this region.  In addition, chapman point in the 2nd intercostal space anteriorly, and overlying the transverse processes of T2 or T3 posteriorly, are representative of cardiac pathology.

Answer B: An acute inferior MI would present with ST elevation present in inferior leads II, III and aVF with reciprocal ST depression in anterior leads I and aVL on ECG.


Answer C: Pericarditis presents with fever and chest pain exacerbated by leaning forward and relieved by leaning back. Diffuse ST elevation is seen on ECG in patients suffering from acute pericarditis.


Answer D: The presence of Q waves leads II, III, and aVF would suggest old inferior wall MI.


Answer E: Unstable angina may or may not have positive findings on ECG that may include ST depression, which is indicative of ischemia. However, clinically the diagnosis is made by history of chest pain at rest or acutely worsening exertional angina.




Bottom Line: Patients who are suffering from acute anterior wall MI often have ECG findings or ST elevation in leads V1-V6, with reciprocal ST depression in leads II, III, and aVF.

COMBANK Insight : Locating MIs based on ECG findings is one of the favorite questions on COMLEX levels 2 and 3. Most of the time the ECG will be shown, however, sometimes you will be given the findings in the stem. Know the different patterns that you would expect to find in new versus old MIs, as well as the leads involved with each location. Atrial fibrillation, MAT, SVT and AV nodal blocks are also favorite COMLEX questions.

For more information, see:
"Myocardial infarction" on Medscape.

Patient Presentation: Sensory Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Scientific Understanding of Health & Disease Mechanisms
Body System: Cardiovascular System
Discipline: Emergency Medicine
A 63-year-old male presents to the emergency department with the complaint of sudden-onset chest tightness radiating to the left axilla. Past medical history reveals hypertension and hypercholesterolemia. Physical examination reveals a diaphoretic male with tachycardia with muscles spasms from T1-4 bilaterally and an exquisitely tender nodule overlying the left transverse process of T2. An ECG is obtained and reveal ST elevation in leads V1-V6, with reciprocal ST depression in leads II, III, and aVF.

Question 2 of 2 in this set
The vessel most likely occluded is the

1. anterior descending branch of the left coronary artery
2. circumflex branch of the left coronary artery
3. right coronary artery
4. right marginal branch of the right coronary artery
5. right ventricular branch of the right coronary artery

The correct answer is: A
The patient is presenting with signs of an acute anterior wall myocardial infarction which is supported by his ECG findings. The anterior wall of the left ventricle receives its arterial supply from the anterior branch of the left coronary artery (left anterior descending artery). Occlusion of this vessel results in acute anterior wall MI, which is represented by ECG findings including ST elevation in leads V1-V6, with reciprocal ST depression in leads II, III, and aVF.

Answer B: The circumflex branch of the left coronary artery supplies the left atrium, lateral wall of the left ventricle, and posterior wall of the left ventricle.


Answers C & E: The right coronary artery splits off into the posterior descending branch, marginal branch, and SA and AV nodal branches. The posterior descending branch supplies the inferior wall of the left ventricle as well as the posterior 1/3 of the interventricular septum.


Answer D: The marginal branch of the right coronary artery supplies the rights atrium and ventricle.




Bottom Line: Occlusion of the anterior descending branch of the left coronary artery results in acute anterior wall myocardial infarction.

COMBANK Insight : Knowing which vessels supply each section of the heart as well as the corresponding ECG findings are both fair game for the exam.

For more information, see:
"Myocardial infarction" on Medscape.

Patient Presentation: Sensory Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Cardiovascular System
Discipline: Osteopathic Principle and Practice
A 52-year-old female presents to the emergency department with complaints of shortness of breath and chest pain. She describes the pain as sharp and located around the epigastric region. She has mild improvement of her pain when she leans forward. History reveals she takes oral estrogen replacement therapy for peri-menopausal hot flashes and antacids for chronic indigestion. She admits to a thirty pack year history of smoking. She has no history of illicit drug use. Vital signs reveal a temperature of 99.6 F, blood pressure of 92/63 mm Hg, a heart rate of 130/min, a respiratory rate of 28, and an oxygen saturation of 87% on room air. No murmurs are appreciated. Lung auscultation reveals clear breath sounds bilaterally. An ECG is obtained and shows sinus tachycardia. A positive Homan's sign is present. What level would the hypersympathetic tone most likely be palpated in the paraspinal musculature?

1. T2-T7
2. T5-T9
3. T10-T11
4. T12
5. T12-L2

The correct answer is: A
The above patient is presenting with signs and symptoms consistent with the diagnosis of pulmonary embolism. Her risk factors include estrogen replacement therapy and a history of smoking. Sinus tachycardia, shortness of breath, poor oxygen saturation and chest pain are all supporting factors for this diagnosis. Therefore, you would expect sympathetic findings at the level of T2-T7, which corresponds to the level of sympathetic innervation of the lungs. All of the other answer choices supply areas that are not involved with the patient’s presentation.

Answer B: The level of T5-T9 supplies the region of the upper GI tract.


Answer C: T10-T11 supplies the mid-portion of the GI tract, as well as the testes and ovaries.


Answer D: The level of T12 corresponds to the appendix.


Answer E: T12-L2 supplies the left colon, lower ureter, bladder, uterus, prostate, and genitals.




Bottom Line: Patients presenting with disorders involving the lung will most likely have viscerosomatic reflexes at the level of T2-T7.

For more information, see:
Pulmonary Embolism

Patient Presentation: Skin, Nail & Hair Disorders
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Diagnostic Technologies
Body System: Genitourinary System
Discipline: Obstetrics/Gynecology
A 65-year-old female presents to the office with the complaint of intense pruritus in her genital area that is accompanied by burning with urination.  The patient denies any trauma to the area.  In addition, she denies any recent changes of her laundry detergent or bathing soap.  Her past medical history is significant for hypothyroidism, treated with levothyroxine.  In addition, she admits to being treated for genital herpes in the past.  Her last menstrual period was 13 years ago. Vital signs reveal a blood pressure of 119/71 mmHg, a heart rate of 71/min, a temperature of 37.0°C (98.6°F), and a respiratory rate of 13/min. Pelvic examination reveals a white plaque measuring 1 cm with several surrounding white papules on the vulva.
Question 1 of 2 in this set
The next most appropriate step to make the diagnosis is

1. colposcopy
2. urinalysis
3. vulvar punch biopsy of lesion
4. vulvar culture
5. vulvar cytology

The correct answer is: C
This woman’s vulvar lesion is suspicious for lichen sclerosus, and the next best step in diagnosis would be to obtain a vulvar punch biopsy. The differential diagnosis for such a lesion on the vulva includes lichen sclerosus, lichen simplex chronicus, vulvovaginal candidiasis, and psoriasis. Vulvar cultures are also routinely taken to rule out candidal infection as this may develop secondary to chronic pruritis.

Answer A: Colposcopy is typically used for diagnosis of cervical disease and would not aid in the diagnosis of a vulvar lesion.


Answer B: Urinalysis is not indicated in the absence of urinary symptoms such as dysuria.


Answer D: Vulvar culture is a part of the work-up of a vulvar lesion; however, this is secondary to a punch biopsy, which will provide a definitive diagnosis.


Answer E: Vulvar cytology may aid in the diagnosis of an associated vulvar malignancy, but punch biopsy is the best tool for diagnosis of a lesion.




Bottom Line: Punch biopsy is the standard tool used in the diagnosis of a vulvar lesion.

Patient Presentation: Skin, Nail & Hair Disorders
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Management
Body System: Genitourinary System
Discipline: Obstetrics/Gynecology
A 65-year-old female presents to the office with the complaint of intense pruritus in her genital area that is accompanied by burning with urination.  The patient denies any trauma to the area.  In addition, she denies any recent changes of her laundry detergent or bathing soap.  Her past medical history is significant for hypothyroidism, treated with levothyroxine.  In addition, she admits to being treated for genital herpes in the past.  Her last menstrual period was 13 years ago. Vital signs reveal a blood pressure of 119/71 mmHg, a heart rate of 71/min, a temperature of 37.0°C (98.6°F), and a respiratory rate of 13/min. Pelvic examination reveals a white plaque measuring 1 cm with several surrounding white papules on the vulva.
Question 2 of 2 in this set
The most likely diagnosis is confirmed with the preceding diagnostic test.  Which of the following is the best treatment option?

1. vulvar surgery
2. valacyclovir
3. prednisone
4. tacrolimus
5. topical corticosteroids

The correct answer is: E
Lichen sclerosus is a benign, progressive dermatologic condition characterized by inflammation, epithelial thinning, and dermal changes (usually white papules that coalesce into plaques). Symptoms include vulvar pruritis and pain. Lichen sclerosus is associated with squamous cell carcinoma (SCC), but it is unknown whether early treatment stops progression to SCC. If SCC is suspected, then an ablative or excisional procedure is necessary. Topical corticosteroids are the first-line therapy for lichen sclerosus. Potent topical agents are usually used to slow progression of disease as lichen sclerosus can lead to anatomical destruction of the vulva.

Answer A: Vulvar surgery is not recommended unless malignancy is present.


Answer B: Valacyclovir is helpful in the treatment and prevention of herpes. However, it has not been shown to be useful in treating lichen sclerosus.


Answer C: Prednisone is a glucocorticoid that is not as effective as topical corticoids for the treatment of lichen sclerosus.


Answer D: The calcineurin inhibit (such as tacrolimus or pimecrolimus) have been found to be helpful in some patients, but do not work as quickly or as effectively as topical corticosteroids.




Bottom Line: First-line medical management of lichen sclerosus is potent topical corticosteroids. 

For more information, see:
"Lichen Sclerosis et Atrophicus" on Medscape.

Link: "Fetal fibronectin for prediction of preterm labor and delivery" on UpToDate.


Patient Presentation: Pregnancy/Childbirth/Postpartum/Neonatal Assessment
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Diagnostic Technologies
Body System: Reproductive System
Discipline: Obstetrics/Gynecology
A 31-year-old G4P3 female presents to the emergency department with the complaint of contractions. History reveals she is at 33 weeks' gestation and has had a normal pregnancy so far. The best laboratory test to determine the likelihood of premature labor is

1. 21-alpha hydroxylase
2. alkaline phosphatase
3. alpha-fetoprotein
4. fetal fibronectin
5. fetal hemoglobin

The correct answer is: D
Fetal fibronectin (fFN) is used as a marker to assess the likelihood of preterm labor between 22-34 weeks of pregnancy. Although a positive test is useful in predicting preterm labor in patients, a negative test is even more beneficial. Studies have demonstrated that patients with a negative fetal fibronectin test have less than a 1% chance of going into labor within two weeks of the exam. This is especially useful for women who present during this time-frame with questionable labor. A negative fetal fibronectin is often used to triage the patient home and can be reassuring for both physicians and patients.

Answer A: There are no data suggesting that other laboratory data, such as 21-alpha hydroxylase, plays a significant role in determining preterm labor.


Answer B: There are no data suggesting the other laboratory data, such as alkaline phosphatase, plays a significant role in determining preterm labor. Alkaline phosphatase may be used to aid in the diagnosis of certain cancers and diseases of bone.


Answer C: There are no data suggesting the other laboratory data, such as alpha fetoprotein, plays a significant role in determining preterm labor. AFP can be used in conjunction with other studies to diagnose conditions such as Down's syndrome.


Answer E: There are no data suggesting the other laboratory data, such as fetal hemoglobin, plays a significant role in determining preterm labor.




Bottom Line: Order a fetal fibronectin assay to rule out preterm labor in women between 22-34 weeks' gestation. If negative, the patient has a less than 1% chance of going into labor within two weeks of the test.

For more information, see:
"Fetal fibronectin for prediction of preterm labor and delivery" on UpToDate.

Link: Medscape


Patient Presentation: Sensory Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Management
Body System: Head & Neck
Discipline: Osteopathic Principle and Practice
A 32-year-old female with past medical history of diabetes and chronic neck pain presents to the office with the complaint of recurrent headaches. The pain is worse in the morning upon waking up from sleep. On review of symptoms, the patient reports occasional earaches with restriction associated with eating. She describes the pain as sharp, radiating down the face and bilateral. She denies any major trauma or recent infection.  She denies any similar episodes in the past. She states that she is otherwise healthy and runs 1 mile every day. Physical examination is remarkable for chin deviation towards the side of the pain. She states she has not tried any medication for the pain as it is mostly intermittent in nature and usually resolves on its own. The most likely diagnosis is

1. acute otitis media
2. trigeminal neuralgia
3. temporal arteritis
4. temporomandibular joint syndrome
5. tension headache

The correct answer is: D
The temporomandibular joint (TMJ) is a synovial joint that connects the jaw to either side of the skull. The etiology of TMJ dysfunction is multifactorial but most cases are linked to anterior displacement of the joint. TMJ dysfunction is a common problem and is a frequent cause of toothache and facial pain. Patients typically complain of pain that is periauricular and is exacerbated by chewing. Patients may complain of headaches, neck pain, and jaw soreness with audible "clicking" when opening or closing the jaw. The physical examination may be evident for lateral tracking of the jaw on opening or closing. Restricted range of motion of the jaw may also be noted. Patients usually report tenderness at the joint and may have palpable crepitus or clicking when opening or closing her jaw on examination. Conservative measures are the mainstay of treatment for TMJ dysfunction.

Answer A: Otitis media is an infection of the middle ear caused by Eustachian tube dysfunction. Patients complain of ear pain. Fever may or may not be present. Most patients report recent history of an upper respiratory infection. Physical examination is evident for visible inflammation and effusion of the eardrum with signs of fullness, bulging, cloudiness and redness behind the ear. This patient presents with deviation of the mandible on physical examination, therefore otitis media is not likely diagnosis.


Answer B: Trigeminal neuralgia pain is usually unilatral and is described as "shock like", lancinating unbearable pain. It is not made worse with chewing and does not cause deviation of the jaw.


Answer C: Temporal arteritis, or giant cell arteritis, is a systemic inflammatory vasculitis. Infiltration of inflammatory cells causes mural thickening leading to luminal narrowing and distal ischemia. Patients present with headache, jaw pain, visual disturbances and scalp tenderness. If not treated, temporal arteritis can lead to permanent vision loss. Although temporal arteritis should be considered in this patient, her physical examination points towards a diagnosis of temporomandibular joint syndrome.


Answer E: Tension headache is a common cause of recurring headaches that is precipitated by muscular and psychogenic factors. Most patients with tension headaches admit to a large amount of daily stress. This stress is thought to cause muscle contraction of the scalp and neck muscles. Other causes include depression and poor posture. Patients with tension headaches describe a non-pulsatile type of head pain that wraps around their head in a band like distribution. Physical examination may be normal but tight cervical muscles may be appreciated. This patient presents with unilateral pain that is exacerbated by chewing with evidence of jaw deviation on physical examination, therefore tension headache is unlikely.




Bottom Line: Temporomandibular joint syndrome is a common cause of headache with ear pain and jaw soreness.

For more information, see:
Medscape

Patient Presentation: Disease Detection & Monitoring
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Management
Body System: Cardiovascular System
Discipline: Family Medicine
A 54-year-old male presents to the office for an annual examination.  History reveals he has a twenty-pack year smoking history and drinks alcohol occasionally.  Vital signs reveal a blood pressure of 162/84 mmHg. His BMI is 28.  Baseline laboratory analysis shows a blood creatinine test 1.5 milligrams per deciliter, a fasting blood glucose of 110 milligrams per deciliter with an albumin to creatinine ration of 115 milligrams per gram. The patient is counseled on the importance of lifestyle changes and dietary modifications and is seen in the office again for a follow-up.  His blood pressure is 164/82 mmHg at the second visit. In addition to further workup, the best next step in pharmacological management is

1. hydrochlorothiazide
2. isosorbide mononitrate
3. lisinopril
4. metoprolol
5. spironolactone

The correct answer is: C
This patient has isolated systolic hypertension, as diagnosed on two separate readings. Hypertension is defined as a systolic pressure over 140 mmHG or a diastolic pressure over 90 mmHg. It is usually asymptomatic, unless it has begun to cause end organ damage. The choices provided are all used in the treatment of hypertension. The key to answering hypertension questions will be to look for the existence of co-morbidity such as diabetes or COPD that will make a medication either preferred or contraindicated. In this case, this patient presents with an elevated creatinine and proteinuria, therefore first line treatment for elevated blood pressure is an ACE inhibitor or angiotensin receptor blocker (ARB).  Lisinopril is an ACE-inhibitor and is quite effective in lowering blood pressure. ACE-inhibitors would be the choice in a patient who is hypertensive and also has evidence of chronic kidney disease, as they protect kidney function and lower cardiovascular risk. They are also agents to add in congestive heart failure because they lower mortality.

Answer A: According to the Eighth Joint National Committee, goal blood pressure is adult's ages 18-59 is less than 140/90 mmHg. Therefore, this patient
presents with elevated blood pressure and needs to be started on a hypertensive agent. The first line agent in treating hypertension in the general non-black population without evidence of diabetes or chronic kidney disease can include an ACE inhibitor, angiotensin receptor blocker, calcium channel blocker, or thiazide.


Answer B: Isosorbide mononitrate is a vasodilator. The drug stimulates cGMP production, resulting in vascular smooth muscle relaxation. According to the
Eighth Joint National Committee 8, use of nitrates should not be considered first line treatment of adult hypertension.


Answer D: Metoprolol is a beta-blocker that is also used to lower blood pressure. The indication for beta-blockers is in congestive heart failure and in patients who are post myocardial infarction, where they will reduce mortality. They are also relatively contraindicated in patients with COPD or asthma, as they will exacerbate the pulmonary symptoms. Medications that lower mortality are crucial to remember. Look for relative contraindications and co-morbidity to select the drug of choice.


Answer E: Spironolactone is a potassium sparing diuretic that is hardly ever used as a diuretic. It is used in congestive heart failure because of its antagonistic effects on aldosterone. Its other major use is as an anti-androgen, as it is a potent inhibitor of the androgen receptors and an inhibitor of androgen production. In patients who develop gynecomastia while taking spironolactone, eplerenone is the alternative.




Bottom Line: ACE inhibitors or ARBs should be used in hypertensive patients with evidence of proteinuria and chronic kidney disease.

For more information, see:
"Hypertension Treatment and Management" on Medscape.

Patient Presentation: Musculoskeletal Difficulties And Muscular Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Musculoskeletal System
Discipline: Osteopathic Principle and Practice
A 25-year-old G2P2 female presents to the office complaining of low back pain. History reveals she had a spontaneous vaginal delivery two weeks ago. The pain is described as achy, and worse with prolonged sitting. She has tried over the counter pain medicine without relief. She denies saddle anesthesia, bladder incontinence, and focal weakness of the lower extremities. She has no history of IV drug use or known malignancy. Vital signs reveal a temperature of 98.6 F, a blood pressure of 122/81 mmHg, a heart rate of 65/min, a respiratory rate of 16/min, and an oxygen saturation of 100% on room air. Physical exam reveals 5/5 strength of the lower extremities bilaterally. Two point discrimination and pinprick sensation is intact bilaterally. Structural examination reveals a negative standing and seated flexion test and deep sacral sulci bilaterally. What is the most likely somatic dysfunction?

1. Backward sacral rotation on a transverse axis
2. Forward sacral rotation on a transverse axis
3. Left sacral rotation on a right oblique axis
4. No osteopathic dysfunction
5. Right sacral rotation on a right oblique axis

The correct answer is: B
Bilateral sacral flexion is the most common somatic dysfunction in the postpartum patient due to the mechanics of delivery. The patient typically has direct tenderness at the lumbosacral junction and the inferior lateral angles. With both bilateral sacral flexion and bilateral sacral extension, the sacrum rotates about a middle transverse axis. With sacral flexion, the sacral sulci are rotated forward or anterior. Consequently, the patient has deep sacral sulci and her inferior lateral angles are shallow and posterior. The lumbosacral spring test is only positive if the sacral base has moved posteriorly. Additionally, since both SI joints are restricted, asymmetry at the sacral base cannot be appreciated and the patient has a falsely negative seated flexion test.

Answer A: The findings of bilateral sacral extension are opposite those of bilateral sacral flexion. The seated flexion test is still negative, but the lumbosacral spring test is positive. Additionally, the patient has shallow or posterior sacral sulci and her inferior lateral angles are deep or anterior.


Answer C: With left on right rotation, there is a positive seated flexion test on the left and a positive lumbosacral spring test. The left sulcus is shallow and the right inferior lateral angle is anterior (deep) and superior. With right on left rotation, there is a positive seated flexion test on the right and a positive lumbosacral spring test. The right sulcus is shallow and the left inferior lateral angle is anterior (deep) and superior.


Answer D: Bilateral sacral flexion produces a false negative seated flexion test. A deep sacral base is evidence of dysfunction.


Answer E: This is an example of forward sacral torsion. There is a positive seated flexion test on the left and a negative lumbosacral spring test. The left sulcus is anterior/deep and the right inferior lateral angle is posterior/shallow and inferior.




Bottom Line: Bilateral sacral flexion is the most common somatic dysfunction in the postpartum patient due to the mechanics of delivery.

For more information, see:
Nicholas A, Nicholas E. Atlas of Osteopathic Techniques. 1st Ed. LLW; 2008. Muscle Energy Techniques 260-268.

Patient Presentation: Respiratory Difficulties
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Management
Body System: Musculoskeletal System
Discipline: Osteopathic Principle and Practice
You are called to evaluate a patient in the hospital who was admitted a couple of days ago for pneumonia.  On physical exam you identify that she is restricted in motion with inhalation on the right lower ribs (ribs 7-10).  Which of the following statements it TRUE with regards to this somatic dysfunction.

1. The ribs favor pump handle motion during the respiratory cycle.
2. When treating this somatic dysfunction, you would start by treating rib 10.
3. These are all considered typical ribs.
4. When treating this somatic dysfunction with a direct technique, you would restrict exhalation and encourage inhalation.
5. These ribs articulate directly with the sternum.

The correct answer is: D
When diagnosing and treating ribs it is important to review the different anatomic considerations of the rib cage.  There are true and false ribs which are defined by the anterior articulation.  True ribs articulate directly with the sternum (1-7) and false ribs articulate with either the costal arch or are floating ribs.  When treating a somatic dysfunction of the rib cage, it is crucial to treat the key rib; Inhalation somatic dysfunction you treat the bottom ribs and exhalation somatic dysfunction you treat the top ribs (BITE pneumonic).  Remember that with direct techniques the physician encourages motion into the barrier and restricts motion into the freedom.  Remember when name somatic dysfunctions, we always name for the freedom of motion.

Answer A: All ribs have a certain amount of pump handle and bucket handle motion, however the upper ribs favor pump handle motion and the lower ribs favor bucket handle motion.


Answer B: In the case of an exhalation somatic dysfunction (restriction in inhalation) the ribs are stuck down and the key rib is the top rib.


Answer C: Ribs one, two, ten, eleven and twelve are considered atypical ribs (any ribs the one or two). Ten is an atypical rib because it only has a single articular facet with T10 vertebra.


Answer E: Ribs 8-10 are considered vertebrochondral ribs and articulate with the costal arch which then joins the cartilage of rib 7 to join the sternum. These ribs (along with ribs 11 and 12) are considered false ribs. Ribs 1-7 are considered true ribs because the articulate directly through the costal cartilage to the sternum.




Bottom Line: In an exhalation somatic dysfunction the top rib is the key rib to treat, with a direct technique like muscle energy you would encourage inhalation and restrict exhalation.

For more information, see:
DiGiovanna, E. An Osteopathic Approach to Diagnosis and Treatment. 3rd Ed. Lippincott Williams and Wilkins; 2005. pp. 363-369.

Patient Presentation: Fatigue & Weakness
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Diagnostic Technologies
Body System: Hematology/Oncology
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 56-year-old male presents to the emergency department via ambulance. Earlier that day he complained of lethargy, nausea and muscle cramps before having a witnessed seizure.  Physical examination is remarkable for splenomegaly. Laboratory analysis shows white blood cell count of 80,000 cells/mL, red blood cell count of 5 million cells/mcL, hemoglobin of 12 g/dl, hematocrit of 35% and platelet count of 365,000/mL. Peripheral smear shows basophilia and granulocytosis with neutrophils. The most likely diagnosis is

Question 1 of 3 in this set
The most likely diagnosis is

1. chronic lymphocytic leukemia
2. chronic myelogenous leukemia
3. hairy cell leukemia
4. Hodgkin lymphoma
5. multiple myeloma

The correct answer is: B
The most likely diagnosis is chronic myelogenous leukemia. CML involves clonal expansion of myeloid progenitor cells. A leukocytosis with excess granulocytes and basophils may be seen. CML involves a BCR-ABL t(9;22) translocation which forms the classic Philadelphia chromosome. CML if often diagnosed with routine testing while many patients are asymptomatic. Patients may present with anemia, splenomegaly, LUQ pain, early satiety and constitutional symptoms. There are three phases of CML including chronic, accelerated and blast phases. CBC analysis in patients with CML usually shows a very high WBC, sometimes greater than 500,000 and signs of anemia. Other abnormal labs include a low leukocyte alkaline phosphatase and high LDH, uric acid and B12 levels. Treatment for chronic CML is imatinib or allogenic stem cell transplant while the treatment for the blast phase of CML is dasatinib or stem cell transplant. Below is the appearance of a typical blood smear in patients with CML.



Answer A: CLL is a malignant clonal proliferation of functionally incompetent lymphocytes. This is the most common type of leukemia and is commonly a diagnosis of elderly males. Most cases of CLL involve well differentiated B lymphocytes, although a very rare T cell type of CLL has been described. First degree relatives of patients with CLL are three times more likely to develop the disorder. CLL is often asymptomatic but many patients may present with infection, fatigue, lymphadenopathy, splenomegaly, anemia, granulocytopenia and thrombocytopenia. CBC usually shows an abundance of small, normal appearing lymphocytes and ruptured smudge cells on peripheral smear. Bone marrow biopsy is rarely required for diagnosis or staging but may provide prognostic information and help assess response to therapy. Treatment is usually withheld until the patient becomes symptomatic. When symptomatic, CLL is treated with chemotherapy and radiation. The Rai staging system is commonly used for CLL which correlates with survival.


Answer C: Hairy cell leukemia is a malignant disorder of well differentiated B lymphocytes. It is a rare disease that accounts for less than 2% of adult leukemia cases which usually affects the elderly male population. HCL often presents with splenomegaly, weakness, fatigue, petechiae, abdominal pain, early satiety and weight loss. Symptoms are similar to those seen in patients with CLL except that HCL patients rarely have lymphadenopathy. HCL is diagnosed based on history, physical exam and labs but the diagnosis is confirmed through the identification of hairy cells in the blood, blood marrow or spleen. TRAP (tartrate resistant acid phosphatase) stain and flow cytometry are helpful in distinguishing the pathognomonic hairy cells. Peripheral smear shows hairy cells, also described as mononuclear cells with abundant pale cytoplasm and cytoplasmic projections. Ten percent of patients with HCL will never require treatment as they have a benign disease course. However, treatment should begin when patients become symptomatic or cytopenic. Nucleoside analogs are the initial treatment of choice.


Answer D: Hodgkin disease is predominately a B-cell malignancy. It may have an association with EBV. It usually presents in the third decade or sixth decade. Nodular sclerosing type is the most common type in patients in their thirties while lymphocyte depleted type is more common in patients in their sixties. HD usually presents with cervical adenopathy but it may also present with a mediastinal mass. Early in its presentation it is present above the diaphragm with infradiaphragmatic involvement suggesting more disseminated disease. Other presenting signs include systemic symptoms, pruritus and hepatosplenomegaly. Pel-ebstein fevers and alcohol induced nodal pain are rare but specific signs of Hodgkin disease. Diagnosis of HD is best made with excisional lymph node biopsy because fine-needle biopsy is usually non-diagnostic. Histology is considered positive when Reed-Sternberg cells are observed which are abnormal giant cells with bilobar nuclei and eosinophilic nucleoli. The most common subtype of HD is nodular sclerosing type.


Answer E: Multiple myeloma is due to a clonal expansion of malignant plasma cells with excessive production of monoclonal immunoglobulins or immunoglobulin fragments. It is mainly a disease of the elderly population and has a peak incidence in the seventh decade. It usually presents with signs of anemia, bone pain, lytic bone lesions, signs of hypercalcemia such as poyluria, constipation and confusion. The classic triad for MM is greater than 10% plasma cells in the bone marrow, M proteins in the urine and evidence of lytic bone lesions. Because multiple myeloma is an osteoclastic process, a bone scan which detects osteoblastic activity, may be negative. MM treatment consists of chemotherapy, often with melphalan and prednisone.




Bottom Line: CML involves clonal expansion of myeloid progenitor cells. A leukocytosis with excess granulocytes and basophils may be seen

For more information, see:
"Chronic Myelogenous Leukemia" on Medscape.

A 56-year-old male presents to the emergency department via ambulance. Earlier that day he complained of lethargy, nausea and muscle cramps before having a witnessed seizure.  Physical examination is remarkable for splenomegaly. Laboratory analysis shows white blood cell count of 80,000 cells/mL, red blood cell count of 5 million cells/mcL, hemoglobin of 12 g/dl, hematocrit of 35% and platelet count of 365,000/mL. Peripheral smear shows basophilia and granulocytosis with neutrophils. The most likely diagnosis is

Question 2 of 3 in this set
Which of the following translations is associated with the patient’s diagnosis?

1. t(11;14) bcl-1
2. t(14;18) bcl-2
3. t(8;14) c-myc
4. t(8;14) n-myc
5. t(9;22) bcr-abl

The correct answer is: E
The most likely diagnosis is chronic myelogenous leukemia. CML involves clonal expansion of myeloid progenitor cells. A leukocytosis with excess granulocytes and basophils may be seen. CML involves a BCR-ABL t(9;22) translocation which forms the classic Philadelphia chromosome. CML if often diagnosed with routine testing while many patients are asymptomatic. Patients may present with anemia, splenomegaly, LUQ pain, early satiety and constitutional symptoms. There are three phases of CML including chronic, accelerated and blast phases. CBC analysis in patients with CML usually shows a very high WBC, sometimes greater than 500,000 and signs of anemia. Other abnormal labs include a low leukocyte alkaline phosphatase and high LDH, uric acid and B12 levels. Treatment for chronic CML is imatinib or allogenic stem cell transplant while the treatment for the blast phase of CML is dasatinib or stem cell transplant.

Answer A: t(11;14) bcl-1 is associated with mantle cell lymphoma.


Answer B: t(14;18) bcl-2 is associated with follicular lymphoma.


Answers C & D: The translation in Burkitt’s lymphoma is t(8;14) c-myc. The c-myc oncogene on chromosome 8 is translated next to the heavy chain Ig gene on chromosome 14.




Bottom Line: CML involves a BCR-ABL t(9;22) translocation which forms the classic Philadelphia chromosome.

COMBANK Insight : Certain concepts tested on level 1 may appear again on your level 2 exam. Translations involved in the most common cancers are important to know. Make sure you are able to recognize the translations for Ewing’s sarcoma, Burkitt’s lymphoma, CML and Follicular lymphoma.

Patient Presentation: Fatigue & Weakness
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Management
Body System: Hematology/Oncology
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 56-year-old male presents to the emergency department via ambulance. Earlier that day he complained of lethargy, nausea and muscle cramps before having a witnessed seizure.  Physical examination is remarkable for splenomegaly. Laboratory analysis shows white blood cell count of 80,000 cells/mL, red blood cell count of 5 million cells/mcL, hemoglobin of 12 g/dl, hematocrit of 35% and platelet count of 365,000/mL. Peripheral smear shows basophilia and granulocytosis with neutrophils. The most likely diagnosis is

Question 3 of 3 in this set
The most appropriate pharmacological management is

1. abciximab
2. busulfan
3. imatinib
4. rituximab
5. trastuzumab

The correct answer is: C
The most likely diagnosis is chronic myelogenous leukemia. CML involves clonal expansion of myeloid progenitor cells. A leukocytosis with excess granulocytes and basophils may be seen. CML involves a BCR-ABL t(9;22) translocation which forms the classic Philadelphia chromosome. CML if often diagnosed with routine testing while many patients are asymptomatic. Patients may present with anemia, splenomegaly, LUQ pain, early satiety and constitutional symptoms. There are three phases of CML including chronic, accelerated and blast phases. CBC analysis in patients with CML usually shows a very high WBC, sometimes greater than 500,000 and signs of anemia. Other abnormal labs include a low leukocyte alkaline phosphatase and high LDH, uric acid and B12 levels. Treatment for chronic CML is imatinib or allogenic stem cell transplant while the treatment for the blast phase of CML is dasatinib or stem cell transplant.

Answer A: Abciximab is a monoclonal antibody that binds to the glycoprotein receptor IIb/IIIa on activated platelets thereby preventing platelet aggregation. It is used in the treatment of acute coronary syndrome and PCTA.


Answer B: Busulfan is an alkylating agent used in the treatment of CML. It can also be use for ablating bone marrow in hematopoietic stem cell transplant patients.


Answer D: Rituximab is the treatment for Burkitt’s lymphoma.


Answer E: Trastuzumab is a monoclonal antibody against HER-2 (erb-B2) used in the treatment of HER-2 positive breast cancers. A major side effect of trastuzumab is cardiotoxicity.




Bottom Line: Treatment for chronic CML is imatinib or allogenic stem cell transplant while the treatment for the blast phase of CML is dasatinib or stem cell transplant.
