Patient Presentation: Bleeding
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Health Care Delivery Issues
Body System: Hematology/Oncology
Discipline: Obstetrics/Gynecology
A 50-year-old male with a past medical history of COPD is going to the operating room for acute appendicitis and has one peripheral IV and needs a second IV. Nursing has been having a hard time establishing a second IV after multiple attempts.  Anesthesia evaluates the patient and determine that he should not be difficult to extubate after surgery but does need an additional IV line. The patient is given the option and consents to a subclavian central line for venous access instead continuing peripheral IV attempts. Although the patient was told about the chance of several complications associated with the procedure, he was not informed of the possibility of iatrogenic pneumothorax. The patient unfortunately gets a pneumothorax requiring chest tube placement and is not able to be extubated after surgery. He remained intubated for 7 days and was extubated after his pneumothorax resolved and his chest tube was withdrawn. The patient files suit against the physician. The most likely outcome is

1. the patient wins the case because nursing failed to obtained IV access resulting in an unnecessary procedure
2. the patient wins the case because the risk of pneumothorax was not discussed with the patient
3. the physician wins and was not found negligent for causing a pneumothorax as this is a common possible complication from this procedure
4. the physician wins the case because the pneumothorax is a rare complication that the physician could not foresee happening
5. the physician wins the case because the pneumothorax was recognized, treated and the patient survived to discharge

The correct answer is: B
When obtaining consent for any procedure or medical treatment, it is important to discuss all potential complications and potential adverse outcomes. Although iatrogenic pneumothorax from central line placement is not common, it is a well-known possible complication.  The pneumothorax resulted in a chest tube, prolong hospital stay and possible loss wages from being hospitalized for an additional week. The patient in this study had COPD and the risk of a pneumothorax has greater consequences than a healthier patient. Had he been told of this possible complication, he may have elected to have nursing continue to try to gain an additional peripheral IV line.

Answer A: While the patient would likely win this case, it is reasonable to place a central line in a patient that needs multiple IV lines and has failed multiple attempts of peripheral access.


Answer C: While this is a common complication of a subclavian central line placement, the patient still has the right to consent weather he would like the line placed or if he would like the alternative option of having nursing continue try to establish an additional peripheral IV line.


Answer D: Pneumothorax is a well-known complication of central line placement. The risk of a pneumothorax is higher in a subclavian central line. An internal jugular central line has a slightly lower risk of pneumothorax and would possibly have been a better option than a subclavian central line. However, placement of central lines comes down to what the physician is comfortable placing, access to ultrasound, etc., and this physician would not have been faulted for placing a subclavian line when an internal jugular line still carries risk of pneumothorax.


Answer E: The patient would still win because of this common complication was not given in the consent. Even though the patient was treated for the pneumothorax and survived he would still be able to sue the physician for the prolong hospitalization stay and for loss wages.




Bottom Line: When obtaining consent for a procedure, always make sure to disclose ALL potential major risks, not matter how unlikely you feel they may be. 

COMBANK Insight : As the physician providing or performing the treatment and/or procedure (not a delegated representative), you should disclose and discuss with your patient: The patient's diagnosis, if known the nature and purpose of a proposed treatment or procedure, the risks and benefits of a proposed treatment or procedure, alternatives (regardless of their cost or the extent to which the treatment options are covered by health insurance), the risks and benefits of the alternative treatment or procedure, and the risks and benefits of not receiving or undergoing a treatment or procedure.

For more information, see:
"Informed Consent" from the University of Washington School of Medicine

Link: Achieving Toilet Continenance


Patient Presentation: Physiologic Processes
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Health Promotion & Disease Prevention
Body System: Asymptomatic & General Symptoms
Discipline: Pediatrics
A concerned mother brings her 18-month-old son to your office for evaluation. She has questions regarding toilet training and states that her child has no interest in using the “potty.” The mother would like to know the timeline that most children will have complete urinary continence.  The timeline that most children will have achieved complete urinary continence is:

1. 6-12 months
2. 12-18 months
3. 18-24 months
4. 36-48 months
5. 48-60 months

The correct answer is: D
Toilet training is the process of achieving both urinary and stool continence. Infant (less than 1 year old) or early toilet training is not directly comparable with late toilet training (children aged 18 months or over), since both the timing and expected outcome are different. In early toilet training, the goal is to learn when the child needs to void, so that he or she can be brought to the toilet, while the goal of late toilet training is for the child to be completely independent in the entire process of toileting.

In the United States, 26 percent of children achieve daytime continence by 24 months of age, 85 percent by 30 months, and 98 percent by 36 months of age. Most children achieve bowel and bladder control by 24 to 48 months of age. Diagnostic workup or treatment is generally not indicated for children under the age of 5. 

It is an important distinction in this question that the majority of children achieve complete urinary continence by 48 months (hence making answer D correct) but a diagnostic workup would be recommended if enuresis persists after the age of 5. 

Answer A: Babies will be able to babble at 6 months, be able to sit without support at 9 months, and be able to stand with or without support by 12 months.


Answer B: Babies will develop separation anxiety at 12 months and have hand preference by 18 months.


Answer C: Babies will be able to stack 6 cubes and use 2-word sentences at 24 months of age.


Answer E: Children will be able to copy a cross and tell stories at 4 years of age and use abstract words and copy a square at 5 years of age.




Bottom Line: Be able to know the major milestones that babies and children will develop from 6 months until 5 years of age.

For more information, see:
Achieving Toilet Continenance
Toilet training. UpToDate. Literature review current through: Jul 2017

Patient Presentation: Sensory Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Management
Body System: Musculoskeletal System
Discipline: Osteopathic Principle and Practice
A 30-year-old female presents to the office with the complaint of anterior chest pain after pitching for a baseball game. The pain is described as achy when she pulls her right arm out as if she is going to pitch the ball, and worsens when she coughs or takes a deep breath. She denies any radiation of pain, jaw pain, and shortness of breath.  The patient is able to reproduce the pain upon pressing the area along the anterior mid-axillary line at the level of the ribs 4-6 on the right. The classic technique for counterstrain for this area is

1. flex cervicals, sidebend and rotate the thorax toward the left
2. flex cervicals, sidebend and rotate the thorax to the right
3. extend cervicals, sidebend and rotate the thorax to the right
4. extend cervicals, sidebend and rotate the thorax to the left
5. place cervicals in neutral position, sidebend thorax to the right, rotation to the left

The correct answer is: B
Classic counterstrain treatment for ribs 3-6 is slight flexion, sidebend and rotate toward the point.

Answer A: Slight flexion is correct. However, the classic treatment for Ribs 3-6 is to the side of the tenderpoint, in this case to the right side.


Answer C: Extension is not the classic position. The sidebending and rotation are correct.


Answer D: Again, extension is not correct. In addition you must sidebend and rotate toward the point.


Answer E: Neutral may also affect the treatment as much as slight flexion. The sidebending and rotation must be to the same side.




Bottom Line: Remember the saying "wrapping" or "covering" the tenderpoint applies to ribs. Positioning for the treatment involves bending head and thorax toward the tenderpoint.

Patient Presentation: Trauma
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Management
Body System: Integumentary System
Discipline: Pediatrics
A 7-year-old male presents to the emergency department after being bit on the arm by a neighbor's dog. History reveals the bite occurred 8 hours ago. The patient has no significant past medical history. Physical examination reveals four small, tender puncture wounds on the right forearm with surrounding erythema and induration. There is no active bleeding from the puncture sites and no motor or sensory dysfunction is present. The most appropriate antibiotic for treatment of this injury is

1. amoxicillin-clavulanate
2. ampicillin
3. cephalexin
4. ciprofloxacin
5. trimethoprim-sulfamethoxazole

The correct answer is: A
Approximately 1% of annual emergency department visits are due to animal bites. The first line choice of antibiotic for animal or human bites is amoxicillin-clavulanate. In patients with an allergy to penicillin, the options include moxifloxacin or clindamycin plus ciprofloxacin. Wound infections from animal bites are usually polymicrobial with Pasteurella multocida being isolated in 80% of cat bites and 50% of dog bites. Another common microorganism found in the mouth and saliva of healthy canines is Capnocytophaga canimorsus, a gram-negative bacillus that is also susceptible to amoxicillin-clavulanate.

Answer B: Ampicillin is generally used for genitourinary tract infections, some respiratory infections and endocarditis prophylaxis; however, it does not cover the common organisms responsible for infection due to animal bites.


Answer C: Cephalexin is a first generation cephalosporin commonly used for uncomplicated skin infections, respiratory tract infections, otitis media and bone infections. It will not cover pathogens from bites caused by animals.


Answer D: Ciprofloxacin is a fluoroquinolone commonly used for urinary tract infections, acute sinusitis, skin and intra-abdominal infections as well as pseudomonal ear infections. It is not an appropriate antibiotic for animal bites unless it is combined with clindamycin.


Answer E: Sulfamethoxazole and trimethoprim combination is used to treat infections of the urinary tract, middle ear, and for bronchitis, traveler's diarrhea, and shigellosis.




Bottom Line: The drug of choice for treatment of animal bites is amoxicillin-clavulanate. Pasteurella multocida is a common pathogen responsible for wound infections in both dog and cat bites.

For more information, see:
"Pasteurella Multocida Infection" on Medscape.

Patient Presentation: Sensory Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Scientific Understanding of Health & Disease Mechanisms
Body System: Head & Neck
Discipline: Osteopathic Principle and Practice
A 42-year-old male presents to the office with the complaint of difficulty concentrating. History reveals the patient has also been having headaches and fatigue after being hit in the head with a falling rock two weeks ago. The patient works as a construction worker and was wearing a hard hat at the time of the injury. Where is the ring finger placed in order to properly palpate the motion of the cranium using a vault hold?

1. Greater wing of the sphenoid
2. Mastoid process
3. Occipital condyle
4. Portion of the temporal bone
5. Squamous portion of the occiput

The correct answer is: B
The cranial rhythmic impulse is a palpable experience that can be appreciated by the physician while palpating a patient's cranium or sacrum. There are numerous hand placements for palpation of the cranium. The vault hold is a common method used to evaluate the cranial rhythmic impulse, as well as treat. The finger placement for the vault hold is the index finger is located on the greater wing of the sphenoid, the middle finger is located on the preauricular portion of the temporal bone, the ring finger is located on the mastoid process, and the little finger is located on the squamous portion of the occiput.

Answer A: The index finger is located on the greater wing of the sphenoid


Answer C: The occipital condyles are not monitored with any finger when using the vault hold.


Answer D: The middle finger is located on the preauricular portion of the temporal bone.


Answer E: The little finger is located on the squamous portion of the occipital bone.




Bottom Line: When palpating a patient’s cranium using the vault hold, the ring finger is placed on the mastoid process of the temporal bone.

Patient Presentation: Digestive Difficulties
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Gastrointestinal System
Discipline: Surgery
A 78-year-old male presents to the office with the complaint of difficulty swallowing and weight loss over the last month. History reveals vomiting of undigested food 30 minutes after eating. Physical examination reveals halitosis, normal bowel sounds, and a soft and nontender abdomen. 

Question 1 of 2 in this set
The most likely diagnosis is

1. Achalasia
2. Boerhaave's syndrome
3. Gastroesophageal reflux disease (GERD)
4. Tracheoesophageal fistula
5. Zenker's diverticulum

The correct answer is: E
This is a classic presentation of Zenker's diverticulum. Patients with this disorder usually present with esophageal dysphagia, voice changes, regurgitation of undigested food and bad breath (halitosis). Due to impaired food digestion, patients also frequently experience weight loss. Zenker's diverticulum occurs secondary to abnormal cricopharyngeal muscular activity that results in increased mucosal pressure. Since the weakest part of the mucosa is posterior, a pouch (diverticulum) forms and food gets lodged in this area.

Answer A: Achalasia refers to the stricture of the esophagus due to failure of the lower esophageal sphincter to relax. Patients with achalasia also present with dysphagia and regurgitation, but in addition to these symptoms, patients also experience heartburn and do not have halitosis.


Answer B: Although Boerhaave's syndrome can also present with dysphagia of solid foods, the month-long history makes this diagnosis unlikely. Boerhaave's syndrome is rupture of the esophagus, usually due to prolonged retching. This patient has no history of sudden onset of symptoms that would be typical of Boerhaave's syndrome.


Answer C: Individuals with GERD usually present with heartburn, persistent belching, and complain of sour-tasting regurgitation of liquids and food particles.


Answer D: A tracheoesophageal fistula is a phenomenon observed in neonates during their first year of life and would not be seen in this age group.




Bottom Line: Zenker's diverticulum is a diagnosis that should be suspected in an elderly patient experiencing dysphagia, regurgitation, and halitosis. Weight loss and hoarseness are also commonly seen presentations. 

For more information, see:
"Zenker's diverticulum" on UpToDate.

Patient Presentation: Digestive Difficulties
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Diagnostic Technologies
Body System: Gastrointestinal System
Discipline: Surgery
A 78-year-old male presents to the office with the complaint of difficulty swallowing and weight loss over the last month. History reveals vomiting of undigested food 30 minutes after eating. Physical examination reveals halitosis, normal bowel sounds, and a soft and nontender abdomen. 

Question 2 of 2 in this set
The best test to confirm the diagnosis is

1. abdominal x-ray
2. barium swallow
3. endoscopy
4. esophageal manometry
5. lateral chest x-ray

The correct answer is: B
Barium swallow is the confirmatory test for Zenker's diverticulum. This study clearly shows the diverticulum as it fills up with radio-opaque barium during the procedure. Patients with this diagnosis typically have a > 2-cm posterior midline diverticulum just above the cricopharyngeus.

Answer A: Abdominal films would likely be normal and of little use.


Answer C: If a neoplasm is suspected or if there are mucosal irregularities found on barium swallow, endoscopy should be performed.


Answer D: Esophageal manometry is recommended only if an alternative motility disorder is suspected after performing the contrast study.


Answer E: Lateral chest films would not be useful for this diagnosis, as it would not clearly show the diverticulum. A lateral chest film is a useful study to assess cardiac and pulmonary structures.




Bottom Line: The confirmatory test for Zenker's diverticulum is barium swallow.

COMBANK Insight : A significant proportion of COMLEX 2 and 3 questions are set up in multi-step format. One method of answering these questions is by an “all or none” strategy, where you pick your diagnosis and ride it through the remaining questions pertaining to the case, such as diagnostic imaging, laboratory findings, etc. This strategy can be huge if you are correct from the very beginning, or it can easily cause you to miss 3 or 4 questions that appear straight-forward and easy. Just like the stock market, you can also try to “hedge” your bets if you are unsure of the initial diagnosis. Picking one diagnosis and following up with an alternate treatment modality can sometimes benefit your overall score if you are deciding between answer choices. The best way to decide which strategy is for you is to follow your intuition.

For more information, see:
"Zenker's diverticulum" on UpToDate.

Patient Presentation: Cognitive Difficulties
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Scientific Understanding of Health & Disease Mechanisms
Body System: Head & Neck
Discipline: Osteopathic Principle and Practice
A 25-year-old female presents to her primary care physician with the complaint of extreme anxiety. History reveals the patient is preparing to give a speech to a large audience. Vital signs reveal a blood pressure of 132/72 mmHg and a heart rate of 96/min. Osteopathic structural examination reveals restriction and fullness at both occipitomastoid suture. A V-spread technique is performed to balance the autonomic nervous system.

Question 1 of 3 in this set
Which cranial nerve is most likely affected by this technique?

1. III
2. VII
3. IX
4. X
5. XII

The correct answer is: D
A V-spread technique can be performed at any suture within the cranium. Its intention is to open any suture that may be impacted or restricted in its normal range of motion. It is beneficial at the occipitomastoid suture between the occipital and temporal bones because of its effects on the vagus nerve. The occipital and temporal bones come together to form the jugular foramen. The vagus nerve originates in the medulla and exits the cranium through the jugular foramen. Dysfunction of the vagus nerve can occur secondary to a compression at the occipitomastoid suture. The vagus nerve is responsible for the parasympathetic innervation of many organ systems, including the heart. Stimulation of the vagus nerve can aid in the stabilization of the heart rate.

Answer A: Cranial nerve III is the oculomotor nerve that arises in the midbrain and exits the cranium through the superior orbital fissure.


Answer B: Cranial nerve VII is the facial nerve. It arises from the pons and enters the internal acoustic meatus exiting the cranium through the stylomastoid foramen.


Answer C: Cranial nerve IX is the glossopharyngeal nerve. It arises from the medulla and exits the skull through the jugular foramen. It can be affected by an occipitomastoid V-spread technique, but it does not influence heart rate.


Answer E: Cranial nerve XII is the hypoglossal nerve. It arises in the medulla and exits the cranium through the hypoglossal canal. Dysfunction of this nerve occurs more frequently with somatic dysfunction at the occipital condyles. It is especially prominent in infants after passage through the birth canal and results in suckling disorders.




Bottom Line: A V-spread technique at the occipitomastoid suture is performed to affect the vagus nerve to address the parasympathetic nervous system.

Patient Presentation: Cognitive Difficulties
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Scientific Understanding of Health & Disease Mechanisms
Body System: Head & Neck
Discipline: Osteopathic Principle and Practice
A 25-year-old female presents to her primary care physician with the complaint of extreme anxiety. History reveals the patient is preparing to give a speech to a large audience. Vital signs reveal a blood pressure of 132/72 mmHg and a heart rate of 96/min. Osteopathic structural examination reveals restriction and fullness at both occipitomastoid suture. A V-spread technique is performed to balance the autonomic nervous system.

Question 2 of 3 in this set
The v-spread technique at the occipitomastoid suture affects the

1. Foramen magnum
2. Foramen ovale
3. Jugular foramen
4. Stylomastoid foramen
5. Superior orbital fissure

The correct answer is: C
The occipital and temporal bones come together to form the jugular foramen. Therefore, a V-spread technique performed at the occipitomastoid suture will decrease restriction at the jugular foramen. The nerves that exit the cranium through the jugular foramen are IX, X, and XI - the glossopharyngeal, vagus, and spinal accessory nerves. Dysfunction between the occipital and temporal bones can lead to restriction at the occipitomastoid suture or jugular foramen. This can lead to problems with any of the nerves traversing through here.

Answer A: Foramen magnum is within the occipital bone. The brainstem forms the spinal cord and exits the cranium through the foramen magnum. The spinal accessory nerve that originates at C1-C6 spinal nerves and comes through the foramen magnum entering the cranium and exiting through the jugular foramen.


Answer B: Foramen ovale is within the sphenoid bone. The third division of the trigeminal nerve traverses through this foramen.


Answer D: The stylomastoid foramen is within the temporal bone. Cranial nerve VII, the facial nerve, exits the cranium through here. Dysfunction of the facial nerve or temporal bone can lead to Bell’s palsy.


Answer E: The superior orbital fissure is within the sphenoid bone. Cranial nerves III, IV, V1, and VI exit the cranium through here. Dysfunction of these nerves or the sphenoid bone can result in diplopia, esotropia, ptosis, and accommodation problems.




Bottom Line: The temporal and occipital bones come together to form the jugular foramen and the occipitomastoid suture. The cranial nerves that exit the cranium through the jugular foramen are IX, X, and XI.

insert Link
Patient Presentation: Cognitive Difficulties
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Scientific Understanding of Health & Disease Mechanisms
Body System: Head & Neck
Discipline: Osteopathic Principle and Practice
A 25-year-old female presents to her primary care physician with the complaint of extreme anxiety. History reveals the patient is preparing to give a speech to a large audience. Vital signs reveal a blood pressure of 132/72 mmHg and a heart rate of 96/min. Osteopathic structural examination reveals restriction and fullness at both occipitomastoid suture. A V-spread technique is performed to balance the autonomic nervous system.

Question 3 of 3 in this set
What are the two cranial bones that form the occipitomastoid suture?

1. Occiput and ethmoid
2. Occiput and frontal
3. Occiput and parietal
4. Occiput and sphenoid
5. Occiput and temporal

The correct answer is: E
The occipital and temporal bones come together to form the occipitomastoid suture and the jugular foramen. The nerves that exit the cranium through the jugular foramen are IX, X, and XI - the glossopharyngeal, vagus, and spinal accessory nerves. Dysfunction between the occipital and temporal bones can lead to restriction at the occipitomastoid suture or jugular foramen. This can lead to problems with any of the nerves traversing through here. A V-spread technique can be performed with any suture within the cranium. Its intention is to open any suture that may be impacted or restricted in its normal range of motion. It is beneficial at the occipitomastoid suture between the occipital and temporal bones mainly because of its effects on the motion of the occipital and temporal bones, as well as the function of the vagus nerve. The vagus nerve originates in the medulla and exits the cranium through the jugular foramen. Dysfunction of the vagus nerve can occur secondary to a compression at the occipitomastoid suture. The vagus nerve is responsible for the parasympathetic innervation of many organ systems.

Answer A: There is no articulation between the occipital and ethmoid bones.


Answer B: There is no articulation between the frontal and occipital bones.


Answer C: The occipital and parietal bones come together at the lambdoidal suture.


Answer D: The occiput and the sphenoid bone come together at the sphenobasilar synchondrosis.




Bottom Line: The occipital bone and temporal bone articulate at the occipitomastoid suture.

Link: Child Abuse in Emergency Medicine on Medscape.


Patient Presentation: Trauma
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Health Care Delivery Issues
Body System: Asymptomatic & General Symptoms
Discipline: Pediatrics
A 4-year-old female is brought to the emergency department by her worried aunt. The child was found to be lethargic after being picked up from a new daycare. Physical examination reveals multiple bruises on the buttocks, a small circular burn mark on the arm, and retinal hemorrhages. A chest radiograph is obtained and reveals a right-sided rib fracture. A CT scan is obtained and reveals a subdural hematoma. The most appropriate next step in management is

1. admit the child to the hospital to protect her from further harm and contact child protective services upon discharge
2. admit the child to the hospital to protect her from further harm and contact the police immediately
3. admit the child to the hospital to protect her from further harm and discharge when her wounds have healed
4. release the child into the care of the aunt and follow-up with primary care
5. release the child into the care of the parents and follow-up with primary care

The correct answer is: B
When child abuse is suspected immediate contact must be made to child protective services, law enforcement, or both. Each state varies in the law as to whether one or both need to be notified. For clinical purposes please review the recommendations for the state in which you live/practice. For didactic purposes it is necessary to admit the child and immediately report the incident. The physician is pardoned if allegations are made on the basis of clinical findings and child abuse is not actually the cause. Conversely, the physician faces felony charges if child abuse is suspected and not reported. The same is true for elder abuse. These children should be admitted into the care of the hospital to care for the wounds that have been sustained.

Answers A & C & D & E: When child abuse is suspected the child should be admitted to the hospital when safety cannot be guaranteed and immediate contact with the police or child protective services should be made.




Bottom Line: Serious injuries as a result of abuse or neglect must be reported. Failure to do so as a healthcare worker results in felony charges.

For more information, see:
Child Abuse in Emergency Medicine on Medscape.

Link: "Gastrointestinal Disease and Pregnancy" on Medscape.


Patient Presentation: Digestive Difficulties
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Health Promotion & Disease Prevention
Body System: Gastrointestinal System
Discipline: Obstetrics/Gynecology
A 26-year-old G2P1 female at 26 weeks' gestation presents to the office with the complaint of difficulty sleeping. History reveals she has multiple nighttime awakenings with coughing spells. In the morning her throat is sore and she has a sour taste in her mouth. She eats dinner one hour before she goes to bed, but lays down once she finishes eating. She enjoys spicy foods for dinner and has chocolate most evenings. The most appropriate initial step in management is

1. inhaled beta agonist prior to bed
2. oral diphenhydramine prior to bed
3. oral omeprazole every morning
4. oral zolpidem prior to bed
5. raise the head of the bed at night

The correct answer is: E
The most likely diagnosis is gastroesophageal reflux disease. This can commonly occur in pregnant patients due to relaxation of the gastroesophageal sphincter. Food choices can exacerbate symptoms and patients should be counseled on refraining from spicy or acidic foods as well as caffeine and chocolate. Patients should remain upright after eating or elevate the head of the bed, if adjustable, or add an extra pillow to avoid reflux of gastric contents into the esophagus. Initial management consists of these lifestyle modifications.

Answer A: Inhaled beta agonists are used to treat asthma.


Answer B: Diphenhydramine is an antitussive medication that can also be used as a sleep aid. This would not address the underlying problem.


Answer C: Proton pump inhibitors are the next step in management once lifestyle modifications have failed in the non-pregnant patient. In the pregnant patient H2 receptor blockers are preferred because of the increased safety for the fetus.


Answer D: Zolpidem is a sleep aid that would not address the underlying problem.




Bottom Line: Heartburn is a normal consequence of pregnancy and is initially treated with lifestyle modifications such as elevation of the head of the bed and avoidance of trigger foods.

COMBANK Insight : The three most common causes for a chronic cough are; gastroesophageal reflux, asthma, and allergies.

For more information, see:
"Gastrointestinal Disease and Pregnancy" on Medscape.

Link: "Personality disorders" on UpToDate.


Patient Presentation: Cognitive Difficulties
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Psychiatry
Discipline: Psychiatry
A 19-year-old Caucasian male is sent to your office for a court-mandated psychiatric evaluation after assaulting his neighbor with a knife. The patient has a long-standing history of violence and was labeled a “juvenile delinquent” during his mid-adolescent years. He also has a long history of being arrested for car-theft. This patient is demonstrating behavior characteristic of

1. Antisocial personality disorder
2. Avoidant personality disorder
3. Borderline personality disorder
4. Conduct disorder
5. Schizoid personality disorder

The correct answer is: A
Anti-social personality is the adult counterpart of conduct disorder and cannot be diagnosed until after the patient becomes 18 years-old. Its characteristics include aggressive behavior toward people and animals, destruction of property, illegal activity, and lying. Treatment options include a structured environment with psychotherapy.

According to the DSM 5, the diagnostic criteria for antisocial personality disorder include:

A. Disregard for and violation of others rights since age 15, as indicated by one of the seven sub features:
  1) Failure to obey laws and norms by engaging in behavior which results in criminal arrest, or would warrant criminal arrest
  2) Lying, deception, and manipulation, for profit tor self-amusement
  3) Impulsive behavior
  4) Irritability and aggression, manifested as frequently assaults others, or engages in fighting
  5) Blatantly disregards safety of self and others,
  6) A pattern of irresponsibility
  7) Lack of remorse for actions

B. The person is at least age 18

C. Conduct disorder was present by history before age 15

D. and the antisocial behavior does not occur in the context of schizophrenia or bipolar disorder





Answer B: Avoidant personality disorder is characterized by the fear of criticism and embarrassment, social withdrawal, and a preoccupation by fear of rejection. Treatment options include psychotherapy (initially individualized and then followed by group therapy) combined with antidepressants or anxiolytics.


Answer C: Borderline personality is characterized by unstable moods, high impulsivity, and self-destructive behavior. Another key finding is splitting, seeing others as either all good or all bad. They feel alone inside and have multiple suicide attempts over seemingly trivial things. Self-mutilation is another common finding in borderline patients. Treatment options include extensive psychotherapy combined with low-dose antipsychotics, SSRIs, or mood stabilizers.


Answer D: Although conduct disorder also presents in a similar manner, this diagnosis is reserved for minors, and at 19 years of age, this patient is considered an adult. Conduct disorder is a childhood disorder characterized by anti-social behavior including stealing, fighting, destroying property and forced sexual activity. It is more common in children with ADHD.


Answer E: Schizoid personality disorder is characterized by the inability to form close relationships, social withdrawal, and lack of sexual interests. Typically, they are unable to show emotion and are happy to be alone (contrast this with avoidant personality disorder). Treatment options include antipsychotics initially to resolve behavior as well as supportive psychotherapy.




Bottom Line: Antisocial personality disorder is associated with criminal behavior. Look for patients who show disregard for others and engage in activities such as drugs, theft and assault. Remember that conduct disorder is basically the same thing in a minor (patient less than age 18).

COMBANK Insight : Sometimes there are only one or two clues that give an answer away. For psychiatric disorders, it is usually the duration of symptoms or age of the patient.

For more information, see:
"Personality disorders" on UpToDate.
Antisocial Personality Disorder

135 of 200

Link: Polymyositis on Medscape


Patient Presentation: Musculoskeletal Difficulties And Muscular Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Management
Body System: Musculoskeletal System
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 50-year-old female with a history of poorly controlled diabetes presents to the office with the complaint of pain and stiffness in her shoulders, fatigue and weight loss. Physical examination reveals 3/5 motor strength in the proximal upper extremities bilaterally and 4/5 throughout the lower extremities bilaterally. Laboratory analysis includes erythrocyte sedimentation rate 56 mm/h, a serum creatine kinase level of 1123 units/L, and thyroid-stimulating factor is 2.0 micro units/mL. Anti-Jo-1 antibodies are positive. Muscle biopsy shows lymphocytic infiltration of the endomysium with invasion of intact myofibers.

The most likely diagnosis is


1. dermatomyositis
2. hypothyroid myositis
3. inclusion body myositis
4. polymyositis
5. scleroderma

The correct answer is: D
The most likely diagnosis for this patient is polymyositis. Polymyositis is a progressive, systemic connective tissue disorder characterized by immune mediated muscle inflammation primarily affecting striated muscle fibers. It usually affects patients between the ages of 50-70 and affects females more than males. It may present with symmetric, progressive, proximal muscle weakness. Patients with polymyositis may develop myocarditis and cardiac conduction defects and their condition may be associated with an underlying malignancy, especially breast and lung carcinomas. Lab analysis reveals an increased serum creatine kinase, increased aldolase and positive anti-Jo-1 antibodies. A muscle biopsy reveals predominantly lymphocytic muscle infiltration with evidence of necrosis and regeneration.

Answer A: Dermatomyositis is an idiopathic inflammatory myopathy that affects both the skin and muscles. It may also affect the joints. In the disease, antibodies form and attack the muscle capillaries and small arterioles. As the disease progresses, micro infarction occurs within the muscles. Skin disease is one of the initial presenting signs. Heliotrope rash and Gottron's papules are pathognomonic of the disease. Heliotrope rash is an erythematous rash involving the periorbital area. Gottron's papules are erythematous nodules palpated over the bony prominences. Muscle biopsy is similar to polymyositis in that is shows lymphocytic infiltration of the endomysium. However, cell markers in dermatomyositis are positive for CD4-positive cells.


Answer B: Hypothyroidism is an endocrine disorder in which the thyroid cannot produce sufficient thyroid hormones. Classic symptoms include lethargy, weight gain, dry skin, cold intolerance, and constipation; however, signs and symptoms can be subtle. Neuromuscular and musculoskeletal complaints can occur in hypothyroidism. Patients report pain, stiffness and aches of the proximal muscles, which is similar to the complaints seen in patients with polymyositis. Distribution is symmetrical. Etiology of hypothyroid myopathy is caused by slowed muscle contraction due to impaired mitochondrial metabolism. Laboratory analysis in hypothyroid myositis is remarkable for elevated creatine kinase and abnormal thyroid function tests. Hypothyroid myopathy is an unlikely cause of muscle pain in this patient. Laboratory analysis reveals a normal thyroid-stimulating factor. In addition, muscle biopsy is suggestive of an inflammatory process. Therefore, a diagnosis of hypothyroid myopathy is not likely in his patient.


Answer C: Inclusion body myositis is an insidious inflammatory muscle disorder that affects older patients. Patients develop proximal and distal muscle weakness. The disease follows a slow progressive course. Dysphagia is present in over half of the cases. Pathology of a muscle biopsy in inclusion body myositis is remarkable for vacuoles and filamentous inclusions. Inflammatory markers are present as well. Inclusion body myositis is an unlikely diagnosis in his patient based on muscle biopsy results. Also, this patient lacks symptoms of distal muscle involvement.


Answer E: Scleroderma is a chronic systemic autoimmune disease marked by fibrosis. There are two types of scleroderma: limited and diffuse. Limited scleroderma, or CREST syndrome, affects the hands, arm and face. Diffuse scleroderma is characterized by organ involvement in addition to the skin. 90% of patients present with Raynaud's syndrome. Anti-Scl-70 antibodies are associated with diffuse cutaneous scleroderma and have a high risk of lung disease. Anti-Scl-70 antibody, or anti-topoisomerase I, attacks the topoisomerase 1 antigen, which is responsible for links between DNA. It is approximately 96% specific for scleroderma. The sensitivity varies with cutaneous involvement. This patient does not have symptoms suggestive of scleroderma making the utility of this assay unhelpful.




Bottom Line: Anti-Jo-1 antibody is specific in inflammatory myopathies such as polymyositis and dermatomyositis.

For more information, see:
Polymyositis on Medscape

Link: "Patient Confidentiality" from the American Medical Association.


Patient Presentation: General Concepts
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Health Care Delivery Issues
Body System: Asymptomatic & General Symptoms
Discipline: Obstetrics/Gynecology
A 15-year-old girl is brought to the physician by her mother requesting that she have a gynecological examination. The mother is concerned that her daughter is sexually active. The pelvic examination is normal and a private conversation with the girl reveals that she is sexually active and is on oral contraceptives. The most appropriate action at this time is to

1. bring the mother back into the room and discuss the daughter's condition as a group
2. recommend to the daughter that she keep her sexuality private from her mother
3. talk to the mother privately about her daughter's condition
4. tell the mother that her daughter is healthy
5. tell the mother that her daughter is sexually active

The correct answer is: D
The general rule for health privacy of minors is that if a minor has the right to control a health care decision, then he or she has the right to control the information associated with that decision.  In this case, a minor is able to request and take oral contraceptives without parental permission.

Physicians have always had a duty to keep their patients' confidences. In essence, the physician's duty to maintain confidentiality means that a physician may not disclose any medical information revealed by a patient or discovered by a physician in connection with the treatment of a patient without the patient's permission to share this information.

Answers A & B & C & E: As discussed above, no other choices protect the minors’ autonomy and privacy.




Bottom Line: Minors have the right to protect their medical information from their family members.

COMBANK Insight : Exceptions to confidentiality include mandated reporting (e.g., child abuse, violent injuries, and sexually transmitted infections) and mandatory parental notification. Some states also permit parental notification for specific services if the health care provider believes it is in the best interest of the patient.

For more information, see:
"Patient Confidentiality" from the American Medical Association.
"Adolescent Confidentiality" on UpToDate.

Link: "Cholelithiasis" on Medscape.


Patient Presentation: Sensory Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Diagnostic Technologies
Body System: Hepatobiliary System
Discipline: Surgery
A 28-year-old female presents to the emergency department with the complaints of nausea and abdominal pain. History reveals the pain has been intermittent over the past three weeks and she states it is much worse after eating. Past medical history reveals gastritis, obesity, and hypercholesterolemia, for which she is taking omeprazole and simvastatin. Vital signs reveal a blood pressure of 120/70 mmHg, a heart rate of 60/min, a respiratory rate of 12/min, and a temperature of 36.5°C (97.7°F). Physical examination reveals an obese woman in no apparent distress. She has a tender right upper quadrant exam but without guarding or rebound tenderness, and a negative Murphy’s sign. There is no scleral icterus. Serum aminotransferases, bilirubin, and lipase are tested and results are within normal limits. A bedside ultrasound is obtained of her right upper quadrant as shown in the exhibit. The most likely diagnosis is

1. acute cholecystitis
2. ascending cholangitis
3. choledocolithiasis
4. cholelithiasis
5. gallstone pancreatitis

The correct answer is: D
Cholelithiasis is the presence of gallstones within the gallbladder, which this patient has as demonstrated by the ultrasound image. She is at high risk given her age, sex, and body habitus to develop gallstones. Remember the memory tool "fat, female, forty, fertile, and flatulent". She has typical biliary colic given her right upper quadrant (RUQ) pain radiating to the right shoulder that is intermittent and worse after meals. Patients develop biliary colic due to contraction of the gallbladder due to cholecystokinin (CCK) release following a meal. The ultrasound image shows the liver surrounding a fluid filled, anechoic (dark) structure that is the gallbladder. Within this dark sac, there is a large round echogenic mass that represent a gallstone. Notice that the stone casts a dark shadow as the highly dense stone blocks the soundwaves from the ultrasound transducer. This patient has cholelithiasis without any evidence of acute cholecystitis given the lack of fever, normal bilirubin, and negative Murphy’s sign.

Answer A: Acute cholecystitis is the inflammatory and often infectious result of blockage of the cystic duct. Over time an impacted gallstone in the cystic duct results in obstruction, inflammation, and infection and causes symptoms of biliary colic. Cholecystitis is detected on physical exam by the presence of a fever, mild jaundice, Murphy’s sign, or any signs of peritonitis such as rebound or guarding. By history and physical exam alone, this patient is unlikely to have cholecystitis. Ultrasound findings in cholecystitis are pericholecystic fluid, a thickened gallbladder wall, gas within the gallbladder, or a positive sonographic Murphy’s sign (pain over sonographic compression of gallbladder).


Answer B: Ascending cholangitis is a bacterial infection of the biliary tree that usually occurs due to obstruction of the common bile duct (choledocolithiasis). These patients are generally ill appearing and may have Charcot’s triad: RUQ pain, jaundice, and fever. Reynolds’ pentad (Charcot’s triad plus septic shock and altered mental status) is present when systemic infection is present and suggests critical illness. This patient has no fever, jaundice, or other signs of sepsis to suggest ascending cholangitis.


Answer C: Choledocolithiasis defines gallstones in the common bile duct (CBD). Symptoms can vary widely but are usually consistent with biliary colic. Ultrasound is somewhat useful in this condition to evaluate the size of the CBD, and is used in conjunction with laboratory tests such as elevated alkaline phosphatase and total bilirubin levels in diagnosis. This patient has obvious stones in the gallbladder but without elevation of serum bilirubin/transaminases, and symptoms more consistent with biliary colic.


Answer E: Gallstone pancreatitis occurs when a gallstone becomes lodged in the pancreatic duct causing obstruction of outflow from the pancreas. This obstruction causes inflammation, infection, and clinical pancreatitis. Pancreatitis usually presents with searing epigastric pain that radiates to the back and is worse with food, especially fats and alcohol. Diagnosis of gallstone pancreatitis begins with serum lipase which should be elevated (in this case it is normal). This patient is unlikely to have gallstone pancreatitis given her normal lipase level.




Bottom Line: Cholelithiasis describes gallstones within the gallbladder and presents classically in an overweight female of childbearing age.

COMBANK Insight : Hepatobiliary problems are commonly tested on Step 2. Be able to differentiate between subtleties in exam findings, lab tests, and imaging modalities for the gallbladder, liver, and pancreas.

For more information, see:
"Cholelithiasis" on Medscape.

Patient Presentation: Digestive Difficulties
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Scientific Understanding of Health & Disease Mechanisms
Body System: Gastrointestinal System
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 22-year-old female presents to the emergency department with a fever, headache, and watery diarrhea.  History reveals her stomach has been hurting her since last night and she noticed a small amount of blood in her most recent bowel movement. She states she went on a picnic with her boyfriend 2 days ago. They ate peanut butter sandwiches, chips, and she had a couple cups of water from the bathroom's faucet. A stool sample is obtained and is positive for lactose-negative, hydrogen sulfide-producing gram-negative bacteria.
Question 1 of 3 in this set
Which of the following is the most likely infectious agent causing these symptoms?

1. Escherichia coli
2. Proteus mirabilis
3. Pseudomonas aeruginosa
4. Salmonella enterica typhimurium
5. Shigella dysenteriae

The correct answer is: D
Salmonella enterica typhimurium is lactose-negative on MacConkey agar and produces hydrogen sulfide, a black precipitate noted on culture. Salmonella is primarily transmitted through contaminated food, such as undercooked chicken or fruits and vegetables. In 2009 (and again in fall of 2012) this organism was responsible for a major U.S. food-borne outbreak in peanut butter. In addition to contaminated food, Salmonella spp. can be transmitted from reptiles and amphibians, as it is part of the normal flora. Symptoms of non-typhi Salmonella include fever, diarrhea that can contain red blood cells and abdominal cramps 12-72 hours after infection. Salmonella typhi, which is not currently found endemically in the United States, is transmitted directly between people and causes constipation with bacteremia.

Answer A: Escherichia coli are lactose-positive bacteria on MacConkey agar that do not produce hydrogen sulfide. Strains of E. coli can cause different types of diarrhea, including watery diarrhea in travelers (enterotoxigenic E. coli - ETEC) or bloody diarrhea from contaminated food or water (enterohemorrhagic E. coli - EHEC, Enteroinvasive E. coli - EIEC).


Answer B: Proteus mirabilis is lactose-negative and produces hydrogen sulfide in culture, but is most commonly associated with urinary tract infections, not diarrhea. These bacteria produce high levels of urease, which alkalinizes the urine and can lead to struvite stone formation.


Answer C: Pseudomonas aeruginosa is a non-fermenting bacteria (including lactose), and also does not produce hydrogen sulfide. P. aeruginosa causes many clinical manifestations including, pneumonia in cystic fibrosis patients, wound contamination in burn patients, hot tub folliculitis, otitis externa, and endocarditis in intravenous drug abusers (to name a few), but is not a common cause of diarrhea.


Answer E: Shigella dysenteriae is lactose-negative but does not produce hydrogen sulfide. This lack of hydrogen sulfide is a major distinguishing factor between Salmonella and Shigella spp, particularly since both genera of bacteria can cause watery or bloody diarrhea. In addition, Shigella is more commonly found in children who attend daycare and patients in mental institutions.




Bottom Line: Salmonella typhimurium are lactose-negative bacteria on MacConkey agar that produce hydrogen sulfide in culture. Non-typhi salmonella manifests as watery or bloody diarrhea and can be acquired from contaminated peanut butter.

Last Updated: 12/3/2016

insert Link
Patient Presentation: Digestive Difficulties
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Scientific Understanding of Health & Disease Mechanisms
Body System: Gastrointestinal System
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 22-year-old female presents to the emergency department with a fever, headache, and watery diarrhea.  History reveals her stomach has been hurting her since last night and she noticed a small amount of blood in her most recent bowel movement. She states she went on a picnic with her boyfriend 2 days ago. They ate peanut butter sandwiches, chips, and she had a couple cups of water from the bathroom's faucet. A stool sample is obtained and is positive for lactose-negative, hydrogen sulfide-producing gram-negative bacteria.
Question 2 of 3 in this set
Which of the following mechanisms is most likely responsible for causing the symptoms associated with the causative bacteria?

1. direct activation of the immune response resulting in increased intracellular cAMP
2. exotoxin that irreversibly nicks 60S ribosomes thus inhibiting protein synthesis
3. inactivation of elongation factor-2 leading to inhibition of protein synthesis by an exotoxin
4. secreted toxin that binds to stimulatory G proteins and decreases intracellular cAMP
5. direct activation of the immune response resulting in decreased intracellular cAMP

The correct answer is: A
Salmonella enterica typhimurium is gram-negative, lactose-negative rod that causes watery to bloody diarrhea. Salmonella does not have an enterotoxin, but elicits an immune response that leads to increased intracellular cAMP levels. This accounts for the watery diarrhea associated with non-typhi salmonellosis.

Answer B: The Shiga toxin of Shigella spp, and the Shiga-like toxin of enterohemorrhagic Escherichia coli (EHEC), irreversibly nick the 60S ribosome and result in inhibition of protein synthesis. This leads to cellular death and bloody diarrhea.


Answer C: Both Corynebacterium diphtheriae and Pseudomonas aeruginosa produce an exotoxin that inactivates elongation factor-2, leading to inhibition of protein synthesis and cell death.


Answer D: There is no known toxin that binds to G proteins and decreases cAMP. However, the toxin produced by Vibrio cholerae binds to stimulatory G proteins and increases intracellular cAMP and is directly responsible for the symptoms of cholera.


Answer E: Salmonella enterica typhimurium does not have an enterotoxin, but elicits an immune response that leads to increased (not decreased) intracellular cAMP levels.




Bottom Line: Salmonella typhimurium is a lactose-negative, oxidase-negative rod that causes watery to bloody diarrhea by eliciting a host immune response that upregulates intracellular cAMP.

Last Updated: 12/3/2016

Patient Presentation: Digestive Difficulties
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Scientific Understanding of Health & Disease Mechanisms
Body System: Gastrointestinal System
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 22-year-old female presents to the emergency department with a fever, headache, and watery diarrhea.  History reveals her stomach has been hurting her since last night and she noticed a small amount of blood in her most recent bowel movement. She states she went on a picnic with her boyfriend 2 days ago. They ate peanut butter sandwiches, chips, and she had a couple cups of water from the bathroom's faucet. A stool sample is obtained and is positive for lactose-negative, hydrogen sulfide-producing gram-negative bacteria.
Question 3 of 3 in this set
Which of the following organisms can cause similar clinical symptoms but is Gram-negative, more virulent, lactose-negative, and propels itself through actin polymerization?

1. Borrelia burgdorferi
2. Leptospira interrogans
3. Listeria monocytogenes
4. Salmonella typhi
5. Shigella dysenteriae

The correct answer is: E
Shigella dysenteriae is lactose-negative and propels itself between cells by actin polymerization. Shigella is more virulent, needing only ten organisms to cause infections, and can invade the colon to produce bloody diarrhea and an immune response, as well as produce a toxin that inhibits protein synthesis and kills enterocytes.

Answer A: Borrelia burgdorferi is a Gram-negative spirochete, and it is the cause of Lyme disease. Patients with this infection should present with a Bulls-eye rash, but not diarrhea.


Answer B: Leptospira is a Gram-negative, question-mark-shaped spirochete found in animal urine. These bacteria may cause flu-like symptoms, headache, fever, and abdominal pain. Unique symptoms to this organism include jaundice and photophobia.


Answer C: Listeria monocytogenes uses actin polymerization, and can cause diarrhea, but are Gram-positive bacteria. Listeria is found in contaminated dairy meats, milk, and cheese. Due to the harmful effects of Listeria monocytogenes on a fetus, these foods should be avoided by pregnant women to protect from contamination.


Answer D: Salmonella typhi is similar to Salmonella typhimurium and is more virulent, but does not cause diarrhea. Most patients present with bacteremia, constipation, and 20% of patients may have rose spots on the trunk. Salmonella are Gram-negative, lactose-negative, and are motile due to the presence of flagella, but do not use actin polymerization to infect new cells.




Bottom Line: Shigella dysenteriae is a virulent, lactose-negative rod that directly invades the colon and produces a protein-synthesis inhibiting toxin.

Patient Presentation: Skin, Nail & Hair Disorders
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Diagnostic Technologies
Body System: Endocrine System
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 38-year-old obese female presents to your office complaining of a dirty band around the back of her neck that does not wash off. She has no significant past medical history. She denies pain or itching. Upon physical examination, you note a symmetric, brown, palpable plaque along the posterior aspect of her neck, as shown in the exhibit. The most important tests to perform next are

1. 24-hour urinary free cortisol level, complete blood count, comprehensive metabolic panel, urinalysis, late-afternoon ACTH level
2. ACTH stimulation test, CT scan abdomen/pelvis, complete blood count, comprehensive metabolic panel, plasma cortisol level
3. glycosylated hemoglobin A1C level, plasma insulin level, complete blood count, comprehensive metabolic panel, lipid panel
4. serum aldosterone level, serum renin level, complete blood count, comprehensive metabolic panel, urinalysis
5. 24-hour urinary free cortisol level, complete blood count, comprehensive metabolic panel, urinalysis, late-afternoon ACTH level

The correct answer is: C
This patient is suffering from acanthosis nigricans (AN), characterized by symmetric, brown, velvety plaques in intertriginous areas. It is usually benign and associated with hyperinsulinemia and insulin-resistance, such as diabetes, obesity, and polycystic ovarian syndrome. It can be a sign of underlying malignancy, and, in a non-obese patient, a work-up for GI adenocarcinoma is warranted. The pathophysiology of AN pertains to the excessive amounts of insulin which bind to insulin-like growth factor receptors on keratinocytes, leading to their overgrowth. Work-up for this patient should include hemoglobin A1C levels to rule out diabetes, plasma insulin levels (should be high) to look for insulin resistance, complete blood cell count to look for anemia (as clue to a GI cause), comprehensive metabolic panel, and lipid panel. There is no treatment for AN, except control of underlying conditions.

Answer A: These lab tests are used to determine if the patient suffers from Cushing’s syndrome. There are many causes of Cushing’s syndrome, including ACTH hypersecretion from a pituitary adenoma (Cushing’s disease), ectopic ATCH secretion from neoplasm, excessive adrenal secretion of cortisol (bilateral adrenal hyperplasia, adenoma), and iatrogenic from exogenous corticosteroid administration. The patient can present with central obesity, hypertension, thin skin with striae, moon facies, muscle wasting (especially extremities), and hirsutism. The above patient does not have any of these signs, except obesity. The first screening test is a 24-hour urinary free cortisol, which will be elevated, or a low-dose dexamethasone suppression test (A.M. cortisol persistently elevated after overnight suppression). If late afternoon ACTH levels are elevated, Cushing’s disease or ectopic ATCH production is likely.


Answer B: These tests are used to determine if primary adrenal insufficiency (e.g., Addison’s disease) is a cause of her skin hyperpigmentation (remember there is no skin hyperpigmentation in secondary causes of adrenal insufficiency). There are no signs/symptoms in the question stem to suggest Addison’s disease as the underlying cause of her neck discoloration, e.g., hypotension, nausea, vomiting, fatigue, poor appetite, weight loss, among others. In primary adrenal insufficiency, the ACTH stimulation test should not show a rise in cortisol and a random cortisol level should be <20 ug/dL. Hyperkalemia, hyponatremia, and hypercalcemia can be seen on CMP.


Answer D: These lab tests are used to determine if the patient suffers from hyperaldosteronism secondary to adrenocortical hyperplasia or unilateral adrenal adenoma (Conn’s disease). The clinical picture above does not depict hyperaldosteronism. Signs/symptoms of hyperaldosteronism include hypertension (usually diastolic), headache, polyuria, and muscle weakness. Labs show hypokalemia and an increased aldosterone/plasma renin activity (>30 = primary hyperaldosteronism). CT scan can reveal an adrenal mass. Treat with adrenalectomy (unilateral mass) or spironolactone for bilateral hyperplasia.




Bottom Line: Acanthosis nigricans is a benign or malignant skin condition that manifests as symmetric, dark brown, velvety plaques in intertriginous areas. This is usually a result of hyperinsulinemia and insulin resistance secondary to diabetes, obesity, or polycystic ovarian syndrome. In non-obese patients, a work-up for possible GI malignancy should be pursued.

For more information, see:
"Acanthosis Nigricans" on Medscape.

Link: "Amenorrhea" on Medscape.


Patient Presentation: Sensory Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Reproductive System
Discipline: Obstetrics/Gynecology
A 21-year-old female presents to the office complaining of irregular periods. She states she has not had a period in four months and is sexually active. The patient reports having normal monthly periods until four months ago although she will occasionally miss a month if she is "stressed out." Review of symptoms is positive for weight gain, fatigue and increased vaginal discharge. The most likely diagnosis is

1. Asherman’s syndrome
2. ectopic pregnancy
3. intrauterine pregnancy
4. pelvic inflammatory disease
5. polycystic ovarian disease

The correct answer is: C
Primary amenorrhea is defined as the absence of menses at age 15 years in the presence of normal growth and secondary sexual characteristics, whereas secondary amenorrhea is defined as the absence of menstruation for more than three months in women who previously had regular menstrual cycles or six months in women who had irregular menses. All the answer choices pertain to possible causes of secondary amenorrhea, of which an intrauterine pregnancy is the most common cause and should be ruled out with a urine pregnancy test in all females of reproductive age.

Answer A: Asherman’s syndrome is a less common cause of secondary amenorrhea that occurs secondary to the presence of intrauterine adhesions, which usually follow infection or uterine surgery.


Answer B: Although ectopic pregnancy should be considered in this case, the patient has not had her period in four months. It is more likely she is carrying an intrauterine pregnancy, as an ectopic pregnancy would have an earlier presentation.


Answer D: Pelvic inflammatory disease (PID) usually presents with fever and cervical motion tenderness on physical examination (Chandelier sign) and is not considered in the differential diagnosis of secondary amenorrhea.


Answer E: Patients with polycystic ovarian syndrome (PCOS) have irregularities in their menstrual cycle attributed to chronic anovulation.




Bottom Line: The most common cause of secondary amenorrhea is intrauterine pregnancy.

COMBANK Insight : Secondary amenorrhea is defined as the absence of menstruation for more than three months in women who previously had regular menstrual cycles or six months in women who had irregular menses. An intrauterine pregnancy is the most common cause of secondary amenorrhea.

For more information, see:
"Amenorrhea" on Medscape.

Link: "Treatment of Acute Pericarditis." on UpToDate.


Patient Presentation: Sensory Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Management
Body System: Cardiovascular System
Discipline: Emergency Medicine
A 27-year-old male presents to the emergency department with chest pain that has been constant for twenty-four hours' duration. History reveals the pain is exacerbated by lying flat, taking a deep breath, or coughing. The patient denies any associated dyspnea, nausea, or diaphoresis. He is an otherwise healthy male with no past medical history and no current medications. He reports a recent upper respiratory infection. Vital signs reveal a blood pressure of 120/75 mmHg, a heart rate of 82/min, a respiratory rate of 14/min, and an oxygen saturation of 99% on room air. Physical examination reveals a pericardial friction rub. An ECG is significant for diffuse ST elevation and PR depression and a chest x-ray is normal. Blood work is all within normal limits. The next best step in management is

1. aspirin
2. corticosteroids
3. NSAIDs
4. colchicine
5. sublingual nitroglycerin

The correct answer is: C
The patient in the above scenario has acute pericarditis. Pericarditis is inflammation of the pericardial sac surrounding the heart. Pericarditis is characterized by chest pain that is constant and pleuritic in nature. It is characteristically exacerbated by lying flat and relieved by sitting and leaning forward. The pain this patient describes is more likely to be pericarditis than angina because of its constant nature (angina is typically exertional), its' worsening with lying flat, and its' lack of associated symptoms (shortness of breath, diaphoresis, nausea). Pericarditis is often (but not always) preceded by an upper respiratory viral infection. Pericarditis is also associated with systemic lupus erythematosus, uremia, radiation, neoplasms, open heart surgery, post-MI Dressler's syndrome, and neoplasms. Classic ECG findings in pericarditis include diffuse ST elevation PR depression. The physical examination in acute pericarditis will be significant for a pericardial friction rub. The initial treatment of pericarditis is with NSAIDs. In cases of pericarditis due to an identifiable cause, management is focused upon the underlying disorder and, if necessary, drainage of an associated pericardial effusion.

Answer A: Aspirin therapy in combination with colchicine is used in patient with pericarditis after an acute myocardial infarction.


Answer B: Corticosteroids are not the first line of treatment for acute viral pericarditis. Initial management with systemic glucocorticoid therapy should be restricted to patients with pericarditis due to connective tissue disease, immune-mediated pericarditis, uremic pericarditis not responding to dialysis, and to patients who have contraindications to NSAID therapy. Glucocorticoid therapy is also used for patients with idiopathic or viral pericarditis that is refractory to combination therapy with NSAIDs and colchicine.


Answer D: Colchicine can be used in addition to NSAIDs to treat pericarditis. However, it is usually added if the patient has not responded to NSAIDs alone.


Answer E: Sublingual nitroglycerin is a vasodilator used to treat angina or ischemic chest pain. Nitroglycerin would not be an appropriate treatment choice for pericarditis.




Bottom Line: Initial treatment of acute pericarditis is with NSAIDs.

For more information, see:
"Treatment of Acute Pericarditis." on UpToDate.

Link: "Amebiasis" on Medscape.


Patient Presentation: Digestive Difficulties
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Gastrointestinal System
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 35-year-old female immigrant from Mexico presents to the emergency department with the complaints of fever, right upper quadrant pain, diarrhea, and weight loss for three weeks' duration. Osteopathic examination is significant for posterior Chapman reflex points located at the right T5-T6 intertransverse space. Laboratory analysis reveals leukocytosis, elevated liver enzymes, and elevated alkaline phosphatase. A CT was obtained and reveals a hypodense avascular liver mass with well-defined margins. The most likely diagnosis is

1. Entamoeba histolytica
2. Enterobius vermicularis
3. Hepatitis C
4. liver metastasis
5. Taenia solium

The correct answer is: A
The most likely diagnosis is a liver abscess caused by the intestinal protozoa Entamoeba histolytica. This is endemic in Mexico thus be aware for patients who have recently traveled there or are immigrants from the region. Entamoeba histolytica has two forms, a cyst form and a trophozoite form. The disease is spread via the cyst form, which infects the colon and divides into eight trophozoites. This leads to ulcer formation in the colon causing bloody diarrhea. The trophozoites are motile; therefore, they have the propensity to enter into the portal vein. From here, they may seed onto the liver causing the formation of a hepatic abscess. Diagnosis can be made by ova and parasite analysis of a stool sample. Imaging of the liver will reveal a hepatic abscess if the infection has seeded onto the liver. The treatment of choice is metronidazole. 

Osteopathic pearl: A Chapman point represents the somatic manifestation of a visceral dysfunction. Most posterior Chapman reflex points are located between the spinous and transverse processes; they have a rubbery texture when palpated. The Chapman reflex points related to the liver are located at the right T5-T6 intertransverse spaces.

Answer B: Enterobius vermicularis (pinworm) is a parasite that lives in the cecum and migrates down to the anus to lay eggs. The classic vignette is a child with intense anal itching due to the eggs hatching near the anal skin.


Answer C: Hepatitis C may have a similar clinical presentation but is not associated with a liver mass.


Answer D: Liver metastasis usually presents with multiple masses in the liver and also has a more chronic indolent course.


Answer E: Taenia solium is a tapeworm that is acquired through ingestion of undercooked infected pork. This parasite is associated with neurocysticercosis, which can cause cystic lesions in the brain and death. It is the most common parasitic disease of the central nervous system.




Bottom Line: Entamoeba histolytica is an intestinal protozoa that causes bloody diarrhea and may lead to the formation of a hepatic abscess.

COMBANK Insight : Amebiasis is caused by Entamoeba histolytica, an intestinal protozoa. The highest prevalence of amebiasis is in developing countries where barriers between human feces and food and water supplies are inadequate.  Transmission is via the fecal oral route or sexually.

For more information, see:
"Amebiasis" on Medscape.
Nye, Z. (2009). First aid for the COMLEX: An osteopathic manipulative medicine review (2nd ed.). New York: McGraw-Hill Medical.

Patient Presentation: Cognitive Difficulties
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Health Care Delivery Issues
Body System: Asymptomatic & General Symptoms
Discipline: Family Medicine
A 92-year-old male presents to the office with his son. His son explains that the patient suffers from Alzheimer's type dementia of moderate severity. The son states that the patient lives at home with the son and the son's wife and two children. The son desires to keep the patient at home with the rest of his family as long as possible because the entire family enjoys spending time together and the two children enjoy spending time at home after school with their grandfather. However, the son describes to you, that due to the progressive nature of his father's dementia, more and more time is needed during the day to care for the patient and the son is now starting to miss workdays. The son asks you for advice about what he should do. Which of the following is the appropriate initial advice?

1. a hospital admission for a dementia work up is needed
2. consider a home health aid
3. consider a nursing home facility
4. consider an assisted living facility
5. this decision should be made only by the care-giver, not the physician

The correct answer is: B
Home health care agencies provide nursing aids and personal care providers to assist families who have an adult family member that cannot be left alone during the day, yet does not need 24-hour care in an Assisted Living Facility or nursing home. Changing the environment or removing dementia patients from settings, which they are familiar, can potentially exacerbate dementia symptoms. Thus, a home health aid would be ideal to provide this patient's son with the assistance he needs during the day and would still enable the patient to live at home with his family. An appropriate alternative to home health care would be an adult day care center; however, this would require removing the patient from his familiar setting and placing him in a new setting.

Answer A: This patient is not suffering from an acute illness that requires a hospital admission. He has already been diagnosed with dementia and active family support.


Answer C: Nursing Home Facility placement is indicated for patients that require constant, 24-hour skilled nursing supervision and is most appropriate for patients that are bed-bound or suffer from severe cognitive impairments due to advanced dementia or mental illness. The 24-hour skilled nursing care provided at a Nursing Home Facility is not indicated for this patient.


Answer D: Assisted Living Facility placement is most appropriate when the patient needs assistance with performing his/her basic ADLs such as administration of medication, supervision due to behavioral problems, and/or food preparation, but is unable to receive this care in their own home. Oftentimes this option is ideal for patients that do not have family or resources that would enable them to stay in their own home and thus must move to an assisted living facility for patient safety. In the above scenario, the son wishes to have his father living at home as long as possible.


Answer E: Refusing to offer advice to a patient is not appropriate in this scenario.




Bottom Line: The least restrictive environment available that meets the needs of the patient is the most appropriate choice.

COMBANK Insight : Grant money from the Administration on Aging is pouring into developing comprehensive training programs for geriatrics. Questions involving the management of geriatric patients will be showing up more and more on COMLEX exams and will be encountered in clinical practice. High yield geriatric topics include osteoporosis, falls and fall prevention, malnutrition, delirium, dementia, and placement issues.

Guillain-Barre Syndrome on Medscape


Patient Presentation: Sensory & CNS Difficulties
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Diagnostic Technologies
Body System: Nervous System
Discipline: Family Medicine
A 72-year-old female presents to the emergency department with the complaint of progressive weakness and numbness. History reveals that her symptoms started about three days ago with tingling in her fingers and toes.  She then began to notice that she was having trouble getting up from her chair. Past medical history reveals recent gastroenteritis. Physical examination reveals bilateral hip flexor weakness and absent patellar deep tendon reflexes. A lumbar puncture in this patient would be expected to show

1. extremely elevated cell count with decreased glucose
2. extremely elevated protein with lymphocytosis
3. increased protein with a normal cell count
4. normal protein and glucose with lymphocytosis
5. oligoclonal bands

The correct answer is: C
This patient is presenting with Guillain-Barre Syndrome, which is an acute inflammatory demyelinating polyradiculoneuropathy (AIDP) that classically presents as an ascending paralysis. It is caused by inflammation and demyelination of peripheral nerves and motor fibers of the ventral roots and is often associated with recent mild upper respiratory or gastrointestinal tract infections, classically caused by Campylobacter jejuni. The pathophysiology is that of molecular mimicry resulting in autoimmune demyelination. Most patients survive with the majority reaching complete recovery after weeks to months. Physical examination reveals loss of deep tendon reflexes and there may be some mild sensory changes. The most specific diagnostic test is electromyography and nerve conduction studies. Lumbar puncture is not routinely done, however, will classically reveal an increased protein with normal cell count, also known as albuminocytologic dissociation.

Answer A: This would be seen in bacterial meningitis. The pleocytosis has a strong neutrophil predominance.


Answer B: This is typically seen in tuberculosis meningitis.


Answer D: This is typically seen in a viral meningitis.


Answer E: Oligoclonal bands are found in roughly 85% of patients with multiple sclerosis; however, they are not specific to that disease.




Bottom Line: Guillain-Barre syndrome classically presents as an ascending paralysis with loss of deep tendon reflexes about two to four weeks after an upper respiratory or gastrointestinal infection. Cerebrospinal fluid analysis reveals albuminocytologic dissociation.

For more information, see:

Guillain-Barre Syndrome on Medscape

Link: Emergency Medical Treatment & Labor Act (EMTALA). Centers for Medicare and Medicaid Services.


Patient Presentation: Trauma
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Health Care Delivery Issues
Body System: Asymptomatic & General Symptoms
Discipline: Emergency Medicine
A 40-year-old farmer is brought to the emergency department by ambulance after suffering an accident on his tractor. The patient arrives unconscious and is in need of immediate medical care. He does not have identification on him and there are no family members present upon his arrival. Advanced life support measures are deemed necessary and his resuscitation status is not immediately known. 

Which of the following is not considered an emergency medical condition that mandates care according to the Emergency Medical Treatment and Active Labor Act?

1. acute left-sided weakness
2. chest pain and shortness of breath
3. generalized seizures
4. suicidal ideations
5. 33 week female for prenatal care

The correct answer is: E
Consent is always implied in an emergency setting. When the patient is unable to speak for him/herself, and the family cannot be reached, consent is assumed and treatment should begin without delay. The only situation in which this would not apply is if there is a prior DNR order in the chart and the patient is in need of cardiopulmonary resuscitation. The Emergency Medical Treatment and Active Labor Act (EMTALA) is an act of the United States Congress, which was passed in 1986. It requires hospitals that accept Medicare to provide emergency health care treatment to any person regardless of citizenship, legal status or ability to pay. Hospitals that participate in this act may not transfer or discharge patients needing emergency treatment until considered stable. The act goes on to define conditions that require emergency treatment as those that are manifesting acute symptoms of severity that will jeopardize the patients health if medical attention is not given. Prenatal care is not considered a medical emergency according to EMTALA.

Answer A: Acute left-sided weakness is suggestive of a new onset stroke. This condition requires emergency medical treatment including possible thrombolysis. This condition is considered a medical emergency according to the Emergency Medical Treatment and Active Labor Act.


Answer B: Chest pain and shortness of breath is suggestive of a myocardial infarction and/or pulmonary embolus. Without medical treatment, these conditions could result in death. Both of these conditions are covered under the Emergency Medical Treatment and Active Labor Act.


Answer C: Generalized seizures are considered an emergency medical condition under the Emergency Medical Treatment and Active Labor Act.


Answer D: A patient with suicidal ideations requires direct supervision to ensure the patient does not deliver harm to himself or herself. This condition is covered under the Emergency Medical Treatment and Active Labor Act.




Bottom Line: Prenatal care is not considered a medical emergency according to the Emergency Medical Treatment and Active Labor Act.

For more information, see:
Emergency Medical Treatment & Labor Act (EMTALA). Centers for Medicare and Medicaid Services.
