Patient Presentation: Fatigue & Weakness
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Management
Body System: Cardiovascular System
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 27-year-old male visiting Connecticut on vacation presents to the emergency department with the complaint of fatigue and palpitations. Several weeks prior he had been camping and hiking while on vacation and suffered from fever and a “round rash” to the proximal thigh that has since resolved. An ECG is obtained as shown in the exhibit.
Question 1 of 2 in this set
The next best step in management is

1. administer atropine
2. administer doxycycline
3. admit to ICU
4. attach transcutaneous pacing pads
5. discharge to follow up with cardiology

The correct answer is: D
This patient most likely has a third degree AV block as shown on the ECG. Of all the choices, the first thing to do would be to control the circulatory system, therefore attaching the transcutaneous pacing pads in case his third degree block deteriorates and the patient becomes unstable.

Answer A: Although atropine is indicated in a patient with symptomatic bradycardia, it is not indicated in this situation because the patient is not symptomatic. The patient complains of fatigue and palpitations, but that is not symptomatic. In this setting, symptomatic would be defined as altered mental status, alteration of vital signs, or chest pain. Also, lyme carditis is rarely responsive to atropine and these patients usually need transvenous pacing for a short time.


Answer B: Although doxycycline is the antibiotic of choice for Lyme disease, it is not the next step in management for this patient with these presenting symptoms.


Answer C: Although the patient may need admission to the ICU, it is not the next step in management. Remember the "ABCs", then diagnostics, treatment, and disposition.


Answer E: Patients with third degree AV block are at risk of their block degenerating and requiring immediate intervention. The patient will need to be seen by cardiology as an inpatient.




Bottom Line: The ECG reveals third degree AV block. Applying the pacer pads is the next step in management because of the "ABCs" of patient care.

Patient Presentation: Fatigue & Weakness
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Scientific Understanding of Health & Disease Mechanisms
Body System: Cardiovascular System
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 27-year-old male visiting Connecticut on vacation presents to the emergency department with the complaint of fatigue and palpitations. Several weeks prior he had been camping and hiking while on vacation and suffered from fever and a “round rash” to the proximal thigh that has since resolved. An ECG is obtained as shown in the exhibit.
Question 2 of 2 in this set
The most likely diagnosis is

1. infectious mononucleosis
2. rocky mountain spotted fever
3. spirochete infection, stage 1
4. spirochete infection, stage 2
5. syphilis

The correct answer is: D
Lyme disease is the most common vector-borne disease in the United States. It is associated with outdoor activities in wooded areas (hiking, camping) in the Northeast U.S. and in the Wisconsin/Minnesota area. Stage 1 is early localized infection, with classical signs including erythema migrans presenting as a large, painless, target-shaped lesion. Stage 2 is disseminated infection and typically presents after several weeks to months in 15% of patients with presentations including meningitis/encephalitis, cranial neuritis, radiculopathy and cardiac manifestations like pericarditis, myocarditis, and AV block. The patient in the above scenario has third degree AV block as shown on the ECG.

Answer A: The characteristic description of mononucleosis is a patient with fever, sore throat, lymphadenopathy, hepatosplenomegaly and a maculopapular rash.


Answer B: Rocky Mountain spotted fever would present as a papular rash after 5 days of fever. The rash starts peripherally on the wrists, forearms, palms, ankles and soles and spreads centrally, becoming maculopapular and eventually petechial.


Answer C: Spirochete infection stage 1 or the first stage of Lyme disease is early localized infection, with classical signs including erythema migrans presenting as a large, painless, target-shaped lesion.


Answer D: Stage 2 Lyme disease, cause by the spirochete B. burgdorferi is disseminated infection and typically presents after several weeks to months in 15% of patients with presentations including meningitis/encephalitis, cranial neuritis, radiculopathy and cardiac manifestations like pericarditis, myocarditis, and AV block. The patient in the above scenario has third degree AV block as shown on the ECG.


Answer E: Syphilis is described as a painless, crater like lesion that appears on the genitalia approximately four weeks after exposure, then a maculopapular rash develops. Eventually 1/3 of patients untreated will develop cardiovascular syphilis, neurosyphilis, and gummas. This only happens years after the primary infection.




Bottom Line: Target lesion rash, fever, and ECG changes has a high suspicion for Lyme’s disease/carditis and should be treated as such until proven otherwise. 

For more information, see:
Medscape

Link: "Osteomyelitis" on Medscape.


Patient Presentation: Musculoskeletal Difficulties And Muscular Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Musculoskeletal System
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 32-year-old male presents to the office with the complaint of left foot pain. History reveals the patient works in construction and he recently stepped on a nail. It pierced through his shoe and into his foot. Physical examination reveals a healing 2mm wound on the bottom of the left foot near the 4th metatarsal, along with warmth and erythema. There is significant tenderness to palpation over the 4th metatarsal.  You note paravertebral muscle spasms from T11-L2 that are tender to palpation.
Question 1 of 3 in this set
The patient is up to date on all of his immunizations. Imaging suggests an invasive infection. The most likely diagnosis is

1. cellulitis
2. cutaneous anthrax
3. erysipelas
4. osteomyelitis
5. tetanus

The correct answer is: D
The most likely diagnosis is osteomyelitis. Osteomyelitis is a bone or bone marrow infection. In children it is usually caused from hematogenous seeding whereas in adults it is usually caused from direct spreading from a soft tissue infection or direct inoculation from penetrating trauma. It usually presents with localized bone pain and tenderness in addition to swelling, redness, and increased warmth. Laboratory analysis usually reveals an increased white blood cell count, elevated sedimentation rate, positive blood cultures, and elevated C reactive protein levels. The most common overall cause of osteomyelitis is Staphylococcus aureus. Intravenous drug abusers are common infected with S. aureus or Pseudomonas aeruginosa. Pseudomonas is also the most common cause of osteomyelitis resulting from a foot puncture wound. Patients with sickle-cell disease are often infected with Salmonella. Osteomyelitis is most effectively treated with surgical debridement of necrotic, infected bone tissue followed by 4-6 weeks of intravenous antibiotics.  As with any other pathology in the lower extremity, in this case we see TART changes from T11-L2 that are representative of a viscerosomatic reflex from the leg pathology.

Answer A: Cellulitis is an acute painful spreading infection of the dermis and subcutaneous tissues. It causes warm, erythematous, and swollen tissues but does not lead to bone pain.


Answer B: Cutaneous anthrax presents 1-7 days after being exposed to spores of anthrax. It presents with an itchy papule that evolves into a black eschar.


Answer C: Erysipelas resembles cellulitis however it is typically bright red with a peau d' orange appearance. It classically presents on the cheeks but can occur on other body regions. The borders are typically raised and well demarcated.


Answer E: Tetanus is caused by Clostridium tetani and may be caused by puncture wounds. However it usually presents with muscle rigidity at the site of infection. It will occur in individuals who have not been properly immunized. The patient in the above scenario should receive the tetanus vaccination if his immunization status is not up-to-date.




Bottom Line: Osteomyelitis usually presents with localized bone pain and tenderness in addition to swelling, redness and increased warmth.

For more information, see:
"Osteomyelitis" on Medscape.

Link: "Osteomyelitis" on Medscape.


Patient Presentation: Musculoskeletal Difficulties And Muscular Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Diagnostic Technologies
Body System: Musculoskeletal System
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 32-year-old male presents to the office with the complaint of left foot pain. History reveals the patient works in construction and he recently stepped on a nail. It pierced through his shoe and into his foot. Physical examination reveals a healing 2mm wound on the bottom of the left foot near the 4th metatarsal, along with warmth and erythema. There is significant tenderness to palpation over the 4th metatarsal.  You note paravertebral muscle spasms from T11-L2 that are tender to palpation.
Question 2 of 3 in this set
The most accurate diagnostic test is 

1. blood cultures
2. bone biopsy
3. computed tomography
4. conventional radiography
5. erythrocyte sedimentation rate

The correct answer is: B
Osteomyelitis is the inflammation of bone caused by an infection. The most common causes are trauma, presence of a foreign body or prosthesis and/or bacteremia. A bone biopsy is necessary for the definitive diagnosis of osteomyelitis by isolation of the pathogens from the bone lesion. The biopsy should be obtained prior to or within 48 hours of antibiotic therapy to be diagnostic. Histologic findings of acute osteomyelitis include edema, acute inflammatory cells, and small-vessel thrombosis with compromise of surrounding soft tissue. Histology of chronic osteomyelitis includes evidence of necrotic bone, presence of inflammatory cells and the formation of new bone.

Answer A: Labs in patients with acute osteomyelitis usually show an increased white blood cell count, elevated sedimentation rate, and elevated CRP levels. CRP can be used to monitor the efficacy of antibiotic therapy. Blood cultures are only positive 50% of the cases of osteomyelitis.


Answer C: Imaging studies are useful in the presentation of osteomyelitis. Computed tomography (CT) may assist the clinician in the assessment of osteomyelitis through the detection of bony abnormalities or necrotic bone and/or soft tissue. It is also useful for needle guided bone biopsy. Although useful in the workup of osteomyelitis, CT does not provide a definitive diagnosis.


Answer D: Conventional radiology should be the initial imaging study to be obtained if osteomyelitis is suspected. Periosteal thickening or elevation, presence of necrotic bone and/or loss of trabecular architecture may be seen. However, conventional radiographs do not provide the definitive diagnosis of osteomyelitis.


Answer E: Labs in patients with osteomyelitis usually show an increased white blood cell count, elevated sedimentation rate, and elevated CRP levels. An elevated sedimentation rate is indicative of inflammation but is a nonspecific marker and is not diagnostic of osteomyelitis.




Bottom Line: Definitive diagnosis of osteomyelitis is obtained by bone biopsy.

For more information, see:
"Osteomyelitis" on Medscape.

Patient Presentation: Musculoskeletal Difficulties And Muscular Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Diagnostic Technologies
Body System: Musculoskeletal System
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 32-year-old male presents to the office with the complaint of left foot pain. History reveals the patient works in construction and he recently stepped on a nail. It pierced through his shoe and into his foot. Physical examination reveals a healing 2mm wound on the bottom of the left foot near the 4th metatarsal, along with warmth and erythema. There is significant tenderness to palpation over the 4th metatarsal.  You note paravertebral muscle spasms from T11-L2 that are tender to palpation.
Question 3 of 3 in this set
The best imaging modality to confirm the diagnosis is

1. bone scan
2. CT scan
3. MRI
4. plain radiograph
5. ultrasound

The correct answer is: C
The test of choice for diagnosing osteomyelitis is an MRI which will show increased signal within the bone marrow with or without surrounding tissue edema. X-rays are typically negative within the first two weeks but may progress to show periosteal elevation. The definitive diagnosis is made with bone marrow aspiration with gram staining and cultures.

Answer A: Bone scans may be used to help support the diagnosis due to their high sensitivity but they lack specificity, making them a less helpful test.


Answer B: CT scan does not effectively visualize the soft tissues.


Answer D: Plain radiographs are typically negative within the first two weeks but may progress to show periosteal elevation.


Answer E: Ultrasound is not effective in supporting the diagnosis of osteomyelitis as it does not sufficiently allow for visualization of bone.




Bottom Line: The test of choice for diagnosing osteomyelitis is an MRI which will show increased signal within the bone marrow with or without surrounding tissue edema.

For more information, see:
"Osteomyelitis" on Medscape.

Patient Presentation: Fever
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Head & Neck
Discipline: Emergency Medicine
A 19-year-old female presents to the emergency department with sudden onset of fever, nausea and headache starting twelve hours ago. History reveals the patient has never experienced a headache this severe before and over-the-counter analgesics have provided no relief. Physical examination reveals the patient is alert and oriented, there is no lymphadenopathy, cough or tenderness to percussion over her frontal or maxillary sinuses. The headache is worse when sitting and slightly better lying down. The most likely cause of her symptoms is

1. classic migraine
2. influenza
3. subarachnoid hemorrhage
4. tension headache
5. viral meningitis

The correct answer is: E
This patient is exhibiting signs and symptoms of viral meningitis, an inflammation of the meninges. There are five types of meningitis including viral, bacterial, fungal, parasitic and non-infectious. Most commonly seen is viral meningitis which is rarely fatal in patients with healthy immune systems. This patient states that her headache is worse with sitting, which causes flexion of the hips. This position pulls down on the inflamed meninges and can worsen the headache. Sudden onset of symptoms with the addition of fever make the other options less likely.

Answer A: Classic migraines are preceded by an aura approximately ten to thirty minutes prior to the onset of the headache. Auras include phenomena such as visual disturbances including flashing lights, spots or shapes. Auras can also include tactile sensations such as pins and needles or difficulty speaking. Patients with migraines usually have photophobia and the peak prevalence is in women ages thirty-five to forty-five.


Answer B: Although this patient's symptoms could all be caused by influenza virus, typically influenza presents with dry cough, nasal congestion and diffuse myalgias. It rarely causes severe headaches as described in this case.


Answer C: The most common cause of subarachnoid hemorrhage (SAH) is a ruptured berry aneurysm. This is usually seen in patients over the age of forty. Other causes include arteriovenous malformations and head trauma. Patients will usually describe SAH as "the worst headache of their life." Other clinical symptoms to look for include new onset diplopia, transient loss of consciousness and pain reaching maximal intensity within one hour.


Answer D: Tension headaches are described as bilateral, tight, band-like distribution of pain across the front of the head. Tight posterior neck muscles are common and the pain usually builds slowly. Tension headaches do not generally present with fever.




Bottom Line: Viral meningitis presents as severe headache with constitutional symptoms such as fever.

For more information, see:
Kasper DM., et al. Harrison's Principles of Internal Medicine. 16th Ed. New York, NY: McGraw-Hill Professional; 2005: pp. 85-93.

Link: Medscape


Patient Presentation: Trauma
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Diagnostic Technologies
Body System: Nervous System
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 23-year-old male presents to the emergency department for a 3-day history of mild dizziness. Review of symptoms is positive for neck pain, difficulty concentrating and low back pain. The patient was in a motor vehicle accident 1 week ago. He denies focal weakness, numbness, ringing in his ears, or vision changes. Vital signs reveal a temperature of 36.7ºC (98.0ºF), blood pressure of 124/74 mmHg, a heart rate of 89/min and a respiratory rate of 18/min. Pertinent physical examination findings are seen in the video. 

The most appropriate next step is

1. broad spectrum antibiotics
2. CT scan of head without contrast
3. cyclobenzaprine
4. meclizine
5. physical therapy

The correct answer is: B
CT of the head should be the first step in the management of this patient. This patient presents with neurological symptoms of dizziness, mental cloudiness and a positive Romberg test on physical examination. The Romberg test is a test for ataxia and proprioceptive loss in which the patient stands with his or her feet together and eyes closed. If the patient sways or loses balance, this is a positive test. A positive Romberg test supports the diagnosis of ataxia but is not localizing. Presence of a subdural hematoma is a consideration in a patient with recent head or neck trauma with cognitive symptoms. A subdural hematoma is common after a minor injury to the cervical spine or head. It develops slowly over days to weeks as the disrupted bridging veins between the brain parenchyma and dura bleed into the space. A CT scan of the head without contrast is the diagnostic tool of choice for acute head trauma.

Answer A: Broad spectrum antibiotics are the appropriate therapy for bacterial meningitis. Bacterial meningitis is an inflammatory disease of the tissues surrounding the brain and spinal cord. The classic triad of bacterial meningitis is high fever (>38°C), nuchal rigidity and change in mental status. However, patients are typically quite ill and therefore present soon after symptom onset. Although this may be likely in a young patient with neck pain, the absence of fever and the patient’s history of less acute onset of the disease make it a less likely diagnosis.


Answer C: Cyclobenzaprine is a muscle relaxant that could be utilized in the treatment of muscle spasms causing neck and back pain. However, the patient reports mental cloudiness and dizziness after suffering trauma to his head. This patient also has a positive Romberg test on physical examination. These neurologic abnormalities are concerning for intracranial pathology. A muscle relaxant should not be administered at this time. Although it would address the patient's complaints of neck and back pain, this medication would exacerbate the cognitive and gait dysfunction in this patient.


Answer D: Meclizine is a medication that is effective in the management of dizziness of vestibular origin. Vestibular injury causing dizziness can follow whiplash injuries. However, the physical examination findings in this patient suggest an alternative diagnosis to explain his complaints. A CT scan of the head is imperative to rule out the presence of a possible hematoma before the administration of any medication.


Answer E: Physical therapy is the incorrect choice. Although physical rehabilitation is important to improve a patient's mobility and functional ability, this is a premature step at this point. Dizziness, mental cloudiness and a positive Romberg test on physical examination suggest a neurological cause of this patient's complaints. Neuroimaging should be completed first in a patient with this history and these examination findings.




Bottom Line: The next best step in management of a patient with head trauma is a CT of the head without contrast.

For more information, see:
Medscape

Link: "Malignant Melanoma"


Patient Presentation: Skin, Nail & Hair Disorders
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Diagnostic Technologies
Body System: Integumentary System
Discipline: Family Medicine
A 55-year-old male presents to the office for an annual physical examination. Past medical history is significant for hypertension. Physical examination reveals a dark pigmented lesion on the patient’s back. The lesion has several different colors including tan, dark brown and nearly black sections. The lesion measures 6 mm in diameter and is asymmetric in shape. The next best step in management is

1. excisional biopsy
2. observation with close follow up
3. punch biopsy
4. shave biopsy
5. wide surgical excision

The correct answer is: A
Melanoma is a malignant melanocyte tumor that accounts for approximately 5% of all skin cancers but is responsible for >15% of skin cancer related deaths annually. The classic description of a melanoma is a pigmented skin lesion that is painless with recent changes in appearance. Features that distinguish melanoma from benign skin lesions can remembered by the acronym, ABCD which stand for:
A - Asymmetry
B - Border irregularity
C - Color that is variable including dark, black, blue
D - Diameter ≥ 6 mm
Suspicious lesions should be biopsied and examined with microscopy for diagnosis and grade. Excisional biopsy is the preferred method of biopsy. If melanoma is diagnosed, wider surgical excision is required (0.5 cm if in situ, 1cm margin if < 2 mm thick, and 2 cm margin if >2 mm thick).

Answer B: Observation of suspicious lesions is not appropriate because of the fast growing nature of these lesions and risk for metastasis.


Answer C: Although excisional biopsy is preferred, punch or incisional biopsies may be considered for lesions in cosmetic places or those that are very large. The above lesion is not large and is not in a cosmetically sensitive area.


Answer D: Shave biopsy is contraindicated because melanomas are graded by their depth of invasion and all skin layers need be present in the biopsy sample.


Answer E: Depending on the lesion, wide surgical excision may be required; however is not the appropriate initial mode of biopsy.




Bottom Line: Pigmented lesions suspicious for malignant melanoma should be initially managed and diagnosed by excisional biopsy.

For more information, see:
"Malignant Melanoma"

Link: "Menopausal hot flashes" on UpToDate.


Patient Presentation: Genitourinary Disorders/Issues
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Management
Body System: Reproductive System
Discipline: Obstetrics/Gynecology
A 50-year-old female presents to the office with the complaint of a sensation of feeling warm around her chest and face especially at night. History reveals she has also been having irregular menstrual cycles for the first time in her life for the last few months. She denies prolonged or heavy menstrual cycles, but states he cycles began "skipping around" a few months ago. Past medical history is significant for breast cancer that was treated with a mastectomy 6 years ago. The most appropriate treatment for her nocturnal symptoms is

1. black cohosh
2. combination selective estrogen receptor modulator and conjugated estrogen
3. estrogen
4. phytoestrogens
5. selective serotonin reuptake inhibitor

The correct answer is: E
Hot flashes occur in about 75% of menopausal women in the United States, and these symptoms most commonly begin the perimenopausal stage. They typically last for two to four minutes and are often associated with profuse perspiration and occasionally palpitations. The cause of hot flashes is unknown, but is thought to be the result of thermoregulatory dysfunction, initiated at the hypothalamus by estrogen withdrawal. Selective serotonin reuptake inhibitors are one of the first non hormonal choices for treatment of vasomotor symptoms in women unable to take estrogen, such as in those with a history of breast cancer.

Answers A & D: Alternative therapies such as black cohosh or phytoestrogens can be used to manage vasomotor symptoms, however their use is not recommended for women with a history of breast cancer.


Answer B: A combination selective estrogen receptor modulator and conjugated estrogen is under investigation for the treatment of postmenopausal vasomotor symptoms and osteoporosis prevention. Its effects on breast tissue are still under investigation.


Answer C: Though estrogen therapy remains the gold standard for relief of menopausal symptoms, including hot flashes, it is contraindicated in women with a history of breast cancer, coronary heart disease, or history of venous thromboembolic event.




Bottom Line: SSRIs are the preferred treatment of vasomotor symptoms in patients unable to use estrogen.

COMBANK Insight : The efficacy of SSRIs and SNRIs for vasomotor symptoms associated with perimenopause/menopause has been demonstrated in individual placebo-controlled trials, a pooled analysis of seven trials of and a meta-analysis of 43 trials of non-estrogen therapies.

For more information, see:
"Menopausal hot flashes" on UpToDate.

Link: "Acute Compartment Syndrome" on Medscape.


Patient Presentation: Trauma
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Asymptomatic & General Symptoms
Discipline: Emergency Medicine
A 45-year-old male is brought into the emergency department after a motor vehicle accident. Physical examination findings reveal extreme pain with passive dorsiflexion and plantarflexion of the right ankle joint. The patient reports associated numbness and weakness in the same leg. An X-ray reveals a fracture of the right tibia. The patient is casted and despite analgesic medication complains of severe worsening pain with paresthesia of the right lower extremity. Physical examination is likely to reveal

1. bounding pulses
2. hypersensitivity
3. muscle atrophy
4. vascular insufficiency
5. warmth to palpation

The correct answer is: D
The patient in the above scenario has compartment syndrome secondary lower extremity fracture and casting. This is a common scenario for the formation of compartment syndrome, which is characterized by the following physical examination findings: pain, paresthesia, pallor, pulselessness, and paralysis. Clinical findings of acute compartment syndrome generally correlate with increasing compartment pressures. Pain develops as tissue pressures reach 20-30 mmHg. Capillary blood flow becomes compromised when tissue pressure increases to 25-30 and ischemia occurs when tissue pressure approaches systemic diastolic blood pressure. In severe instances of compartment syndrome, an emergent fasciotomy is required.

Answer A: Bounding pulses can be seen in hypertensive situations. Conversely, in compartment syndrome pulses are more likely to be diminished.


Answer B: There is paresthesia, or numbness not hypersensitivity in compartment syndrome


Answer C: Muscle atrophy is more commonly seen in lower and upper motor neuron deficiencies. Compartment syndrome is unlikely to reveal signs and symptoms of muscle atrophy.


Answer E: Compartment syndrome is more likely to have cyanosis due to lack of blood flow rather than warmth to palpation.




Bottom Line: Compartment syndrome is a serious condition that occurs due to increased pressure in a muscle compartment. It can lead to severe nerve and muscle damage if not diagnosed and treated properly. Compartment syndrome commonly presents with severe pain that is not resolved with analgesic therapy or elevation of the extremity. Other symptoms include decreased sensation, paleness of the skin, pulselessness, weakness, swollen and shiny skin, severe pain with movement of the affected area.

For more information, see:
"Acute Compartment Syndrome" on Medscape.
"Acute Compartment Syndrome of the Extremities" on UpToDate.

insert Link
Patient Presentation: Musculoskeletal Difficulties And Muscular Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Musculoskeletal System
Discipline: Osteopathic Principle and Practice
A 29-year-old female presents to the office with complaints of low back pain since she gave birth to her first child three months ago. The infant weighed 7lbs 8oz at birth and was born at 41 weeks gestation via spontaneous vaginal delivery. She denies lower extremity weakness and urinary incontinence or retention. Vital signs reveal a temperature of 98.6 F, a blood pressure of 120/81 mmHg, a heart rate of 63, a respiratory rate of 16 and an oxygen saturation of 99%. Physical exam reveals 5/5 strength of the lower extremities bilaterally and intact two point discrimination. Structural examination reveals both the standing and seated flexion tests are negative. There is an increased lumbar curve, and both the right and left superior sulci of the sacrum are deep. Upon spring testing, the ILAs are restricted bilaterally. What is the most likely diagnosis?

1. Sacral base posterior
2. Sacral base anterior
3. Forward sacral torsion
4. No osteopathic dysfunction
5. Backward sacral torsion

The correct answer is: B
This patient has symptoms and physical examination findings consistent with a bilaterally flexed sacrum (Sacral base anterior). The key to this question is the negative seated flexion test. Typically, the seated flexion test is positive on the opposite side of the oblique axis. The seated flexion test is negative when there is no sacral dysfunction and when there is a sacral dysfunction in which both sides of the sacral base move symmetrically in the same direction. In this case, a springing (anterior) motion of the sacral base is free, indicating that the sacral base has moved anteriorly. A springing motion of both inferolateral angles (ILA) is restricted, indicating that the ILAs have moved posteriorly. Therefore, the sacrum prefers flexion and resists extension.

Answer A: A bilateral sacral extension (sacral base posterior) would also present with negative seated and standing flexion tests, however, the lumbosacral spring test would demonstrate no spring at the sacral base.


Answer C: A forward sacral torsion can be one of two possibilities: A left on left sacral torsion would have a positive seated flexion test on the right, a deep right sacral sulcus, and a deep right ILA. A right on right would have a positive seated flexion test on the left, shallow right sacral sulcus, and shallow right ILA.


Answer D: The seated and standing flexion tests in this case are "falsely" negative. Increased lumbar curve, deep superior sulci bilaterally and restricted ILAs bilaterally are consistent with bilateral sacral flexion.


Answer E: A backward sacral torsion can be one of two possibilities: A right on left sacral torsion would have a positive seated flexion test on the right, a deep left sacral sulcus, and a deep left ILA. A left on right sacral torsion would have a positive seated flexion test on the left, a shallow left sacral sulcus, and shallow left ILA.




Bottom Line: The seated flexion test is negative when there is no sacral dysfunction or when there is a symmetric sacral dysfunction, such as bilateral sacral flexion or extension.

COMBANK Insight : In addition to diagnosing sacral dysfunctions, there are classic scenarios that are associated with particular sacral dysfunctions. Bilateral sacral flexion is commonly associated with childbirth. As the baby traverses the birth canal, pressure is exerted against the apex (bottom) of the sacrum, causing both ILAs to move posteriorly and the sacral base to move anteriorly.

Link: "Mullerian Duct Anomalies" on Medscape.


Patient Presentation: Bleeding
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Reproductive System
Discipline: Obstetrics/Gynecology
A 24-year-old female presents to the emergency department with the complaint of vaginal bleeding. History reveals she has a long-standing history of dysmenorrhea and has had two prior spontaneous abortions. Her last menstrual period was 14 weeks ago. She is sexually active and uses condoms on occasion for contraception. A transvaginal ultrasound is obtained as shown in the exhibit. The most likely etiology of the most likely diagnosis is

1. bicornuate uterus
2. pelvic inflammatory disease
3. septate uterus
4. urinary tract infection
5. uterine leiomyoma

The correct answer is: A
The patient is suffering from a bicornuate uterus, which occurs secondary to incomplete fusion of the paramesonephric (Mullerian) ducts resulting in two uterine cavities. The limited size of the uterine horn ultimately restricts fetal development and leads to 1st or 2nd trimester abortions. Patients with this diagnosis often have a history of recurrent abortions, along with dysmenorrhea, dyspareunia, cyclic pelvic pain, and infertility.

Answer B: PID can be caused by Gonorrhea or Chlamydia infections that go untreated. It usually presents with fever, cervical motion tenderness and an elevated WBC.


Answer C: Because the presence of a septate uterus can also lead to recurrent abortions, 2D ultrasound is often performed. The most important finding on ultrasound is a concave fundus with a fundal cleft greater than 1 cm. This has been shown to be a reliable means of distinguishing bicornuate from septate uteri.


Answer D: A UTI would not be compatible with the ultrasound findings in this patient.


Answer E: Uterine fibroids can be seen as discrete masses or polyps on ultrasound. They rarely cause pain but commonly cause bleeding episodes.




Bottom Line: A bicornuate uterus can lead to recurrent spontaneous abortions and should be considered in patients with a long-standing history of dyspareunia, dysmenorrhea, cyclic pelvic pain, and infertility issues.

For more information, see:
"Mullerian Duct Anomalies" on Medscape.

Link: Ménière’s Disease


Patient Presentation: Sensory & CNS Difficulties
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Head & Neck
Discipline: Family Medicine
A 34-year-old man presents to his primary care doctor with several episodes of vertigo, tinnitus, hearing loss of the left ear, and a feeling of fullness in the left ear. He denies any loss of consciousness. The patient states these symptoms come and go for the past 2 years but recently have been getting more frequent. The Weber test is performed and it lateralizes to the left ear. The Rinne test is positive bilaterally. The rest of the head and neck exam is completely normal. An audiogram slopes from the right to the left of a loss of low frequency hearing in the left ear; the right ear is normal. What is the most likely diagnosis?

1. Presbyacusis
2. Ménière’s Disease
3. Bilateral mild sensorineural hearing loss
4. Conductive hearing loss
5. Vertigo

The correct answer is: B
Ménière’s Disease is a disease that causes vertigo, tinnitus, hearing loss, a sense of fullness and hearing loss in the affected ear. It is a difficult diagnosis to make as symptoms can come and go and patients typically don't seek treatment until symptoms are more severe. A audiogram can be ordered for patients to help distinguish what frequencies patients have a hard time hearing as Ménière’s Disease typically results in loss of ability to hear low frequencies with preservation of hearing in high frequencies. This unique presentation is typically only found in Ménière’s Disease type of hearing loss. The clinical presentation, signs, and symptoms with the audiogram demonstrating loss of low-frequency hearing is highly suggestive of Ménière’s Disease.

Answer A: Presbyacusis is also known as age related hearing loss. With age related hearing loss a person can usually hear low-pitched sounds better than high-pitched sounds. Clarity of sound is affected – ‘I can tell that someone is talking but cannot understand what they have said’. The audiogram typically slopes from the left to the right, which means they lose hearing ability of high-pitched sounds.


Answer B: Typically, the audiogram of someone with Ménière’s Disease slopes from right to left, this indicates that the person has trouble hearing low pitched sounds rather then high pitched sounds. This is very typical of Ménière’s Disease.


Answer C: Sensorineural hearing loss typically results in loss of hearing across a diverse range of frequencies. The audiogram of a patient with sensorineural hearing loss will show diminished hearing ability across both low and high frequencies.


Answer D: A patient with conductive hearing loss audiogram looks similar to a sensorineural hearing loss audiogram, in that hearing is depressed across a vast array of frequencies. Often times conductive hearing loss with affect one ear versus both as is more typical in sensorineural hearing loss. The clinical presentation of how the hearing loss occured, how long ago was it noticed, acute vs. chronic etc can help to distinguish between the two.


Answer E: Although vertigo can produce tinnitus is rare occasions, it does not affect hearing so an audiogram in someone with vertigo should be normal.




Bottom Line: Ménière’s Disease is a disease that commonly presents with vertigo, tinnitus, hearing loss of the affected ear, and also a sense of fullness in the ear. A key distinction about Ménière’s Disease is that it results in loss of hearing of low frequencies but normal hearing of high frequencies. This sets Ménière’s Disease apart from other types of hearing loss.

For more information, see:
Ménière’s Disease

Link: "Clinical manifestations and diagnosis of early pregnancy" on UpToDate.


Patient Presentation: Pregnancy/Childbirth/Postpartum/Neonatal Assessment
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Reproductive System
Discipline: Obstetrics/Gynecology
A 21-year-old female presents the office for her first prenatal visit. History reveals she has had multiple sexual partners in the past three months and is unclear who the father is. She has a history of sexually transmitted infections but has been treated with antibiotics. She has no medical problems and takes no medications. She has never had a Pap smear or pelvic examination. Laboratory analysis is obtained. A urinalysis is obtained and is positive for B-hCG. A pelvic examination with Pap smear is performed. Examination of the cervix is most likely to reveal

1. bleeding from the cervical os
2. bluish discoloration
3. cervical enlargement and hypertrophy
4. hardening of the cervical wall
5. retraction of the squamocolumnar junction into the cervical canal

The correct answer is: B
During early pregnancy, several physiologic changes are noted. It is typical for the vagina and cervix to turn bluish in color. This is referred to as "Chadwick's sign." Common early signs of pregnancy include the following: Breast swelling and tenderness, development of the linea nigra from the umbilicus to the pubis, telangiectasias, and palmar erythema. Goodell's sign is softening of the vaginal portion of the cervix. Ladin's sign is softening at the junction of the cervix and uterus.

Answers A & C & D & E: These are not typical findings in pregnancy.




Bottom Line: Bluish discoloration of the cervix and vagina is one of the earliest signs of pregnancy and is referred to as "Chadwick's sign."

COMBANK Insight : Normal changes that occur throughout the course of pregnancy including changes in physical exam and physiology are important to know for test day.

Link: "Nephrolithiasis" on Medscape.


Patient Presentation: Musculoskeletal Difficulties And Muscular Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Scientific Understanding of Health & Disease Mechanisms
Body System: Genitourinary System
Discipline: Emergency Medicine
A 65-year-old male with no past medical history presents to the office with the complaint of right flank pain. History reveals the pain was rapid in onset, and radiates to his groin. Physical examination reveals an agitated male who cannot seem to find a position of ease. Urine analysis reveals microscopic hematuria. A noncontrast CT scan is obtained and confirms the most likely diagnosis. What is the most likely etiology?

1. calcium oxalate stone
2. calcium phosphate stone
3. cysteine stone
4. struvite stone
5. uric acid stone

The correct answer is: A
Nephrolithiasis, or renal calculi are usually composed of calcium oxalate but may also be composed of calcium phosphate, struvite, uric acid or cysteine. Risk factors include a positive family history, low fluid intake, gout, medications such as allopurinol, chemotherapy and loop diuretics, enzyme deficiencies, type I RTA and hyperparathyroidism. It usually presents in older males but may also occur in other age groups. It presents with acute onset of severe, colicky flank pain that may radiate to the testes or vulva and can be associated with nausea and vomiting. Patients are often unable to get comfortable and shift position frequently. UA may show gross or microscopic hematuria and altered urine pH. A KUB identifies radiopaque stones but will miss 10% of stones that are radiolucent. A noncontrast abdominal CT scan is the gold standard for the diagnosis of kidney stones. Hydration and analgesia are the initial treatments.

Answer B: Calcium phosphate stones are the second most common type of renal stones. They are radiopaque and may be seen on plain radiography. Treatment consists of hydration, sodium and protein restriction, thiazide diuretics and avoiding a decrease in calcium intake.


Answer C: Cysteine stones account for less than 1% of nephrolithiasis. They are associated with defects in the renal transport of cysteine leading to its accumulation in the renal tubules. These renal crystals have a unique hexagonal shape.


Answer D: Struvite stones are also called triple phosphate stones and are associated with urease-producing organisms such are proteus. They can form staghorn calculi, which may need to be removed surgically.


Answer E: Uric acid stones account for about 7% of nephrolithiasis. They are associated with gout, xanthine oxidase deficiency and high purine states, which can be seen in chemotherapy patients. Uric acid stones are radiolucent and will not be seen with plain radiography. Treatment consists of hydration, potassium citrate of sodium bicarbonate.




Bottom Line: Renal calculi are usually composed of calcium oxalate but may also be composed of calcium phosphate, struvite, uric acid or cysteine. 

For more information, see:
"Nephrolithiasis" on Medscape.

Link: Pathology and pathogenesis of necrotizing enterocolitis in newborns


Patient Presentation: Pregnancy/Childbirth/Postpartum/Neonatal Assessment
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Gastrointestinal System
Discipline: Pediatrics
An 18 year old female with no past medical history comes into the emergency room in active labor. She has recieved all of her prenatal care at her OBGYN office. She gives birth to male at 32 weeks gestation. The infant has a lengthy neonatal intensive care unit stay however he progresses well. The mother decides that she wants to start breast feeding her infant. After approximately 24 hours of breast feeding she notices that the infant is not as hungry as before. He appears more bloated, anxious, and has started to vomit. The mother also notes some bloody stools. On physical exam, the infant's vital signs are all within normal limits, however is abdomen is distended and tympanic. A abdominal xray shows pneumatosis intestinalis. What is the most likely diagnosis?

1. Necrotizing enterocolitis
2. Pyloric stenosis
3. Volvulus
4. Intussusception
5. Hirschsprung disease

The correct answer is: A
This infant has necrotizing enterocolitis. A premature infant that develops feeding intolerance, bloating, and development of pneumatosis intestinalis on abdominal xray is pathognomonic for necrotizing enterocolitis (NEC). The treatment at this time would be to make the patient NPO, fluid rehydration, and bowel rest. Some cases of NEC can resolve on their own with conservative treatment but a large number of patients with NEC end up developing bowel perforation that requires a partial bowel resection. Development of free intra abdominal air is a surgical emergency. The overall prognosis in patient is full blown NEC is poor.

Answer B: Pyloric stenosis is incorrect because the presentation does not fit it. Typical pyloric stenosis is a first born male that develops projectile bilious emesis after being fed. There is also a palpable mass (typically described as an olive) in the upper abdomen.


Answer C: A volvulus can present with bloating, anxiety, and bloody stools. In severe cases of volvulus, pneumatosis can be seen. However, given the fact that the patient developed feeding intolerance, as well as being born prematurely suggests NEC more likely then volvulus.


Answer D: Intussusception can develop classic "currant jelly" stool, however they typically do not have bloating or more serious symptoms. The majority of cases of intussusception can resolve with a air contrast enema and would classicaly show up as a "lead pipe" appearance on xray.


Answer E: Hirschsprung disease will develop after an infant fails to pass meconium. It is due to a loss of aganglionic cells in the distal colon.




Bottom Line: When a premature infant develops pneumatosis intestinalis after feeding, you must always suspect necrotizing enterocolitis.

For more information, see:
Fischer - Master of Surgery, pediatric surgery.
Pathology and pathogenesis of necrotizing enterocolitis in newborns

Patient Presentation: Musculoskeletal Difficulties And Muscular Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Management
Body System: Musculoskeletal System
Discipline: Osteopathic Principle and Practice
A 32-year old female with a history of hypertension controlled with lisinopril presents with mild, intermittent lower right leg pain over the last 6 weeks. She first noticed the leg pain at work, which involves standing up for long periods of time. The leg pain improves with rest. Physical exam shows the following: +2/4 femoral, popliteal, posterior tibial, and dorsalis pedis pulses bilaterally and equal; muscle strength 5/5 in the lower extremities bilaterally; normal gait; right tibia internally rotated; right fibular head that is motion restricted with supination of the right foot. Muscle energy technique to the tibia resolves its somatic dysfunction. What is the optimal hand placement and direction of the thrust in order to treat the fibular head with HVLA?

1. Metacarpophalangeal joint on the posterior fibular head; anterior to posterior thrust
2. Metacarpophalangeal joint on the anterior fibular head; posterior to anterior thrust
3. Thenar eminence on the anterior fibular head; anterior to posterior thrust
4. Thenar eminence on the posterior fibular head; anterior to posterior thrust
5. Thenar eminence on the anterior fibular head; posterior to anterior thrust

The correct answer is: C
The patient’s fibular head has decreased motion when the foot is supinated. Supination of the foot causes the distal fibula to move anteriorly, resulting in the fibular head moving posterior. Therefore, the fibular head is restricted in posterior glide and its diagnosis is an anterior fibular head. To treat an anterior fibular head with HVLA, the thenar eminence is placed over the anterior aspect of the proximal fibula, with the thrust moving from anterior to posterior.

Answer A: Correct hand placement to treat a posterior fibular head but incorrect thrust.


Answer B: Incorrect hand placement and thrust.


Answer D: Incorrect hand placement but correct thrust.


Answer E: Correct hand placement but incorrect thrust.




Bottom Line: Supination of the foot causes the talofibular ligament to pull anteriorly on the distal fibula, resulting in the fibular head moving in the posterior direction. HVLA treatment of an anterior fibular head involves placing the thenar eminence over the anterior fibular head and providing a thrust in an anterior to posterior direction.

For more information, see:
Savarese, R.G. OMT Review: A Comprehensive Review in Osteopathic Medicine. 2009: 74-76.
DeStefano, L.A. Greenman's Principles of Manual Medicine. Philadelphia, PA: Wolters Kluwer; 2017: 439-444.

Link: "Clinical features, laboratory manifestations, and diagnosis of multiple myeloma" on ?UpToDate.


Patient Presentation: Musculoskeletal Difficulties And Muscular Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Diagnostic Technologies
Body System: Hematology/Oncology
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 70-year-old man presents to your office complaining of shortness of breath, constipation, swelling in his feet and "pain in his bones." Laboratory analyses the patient is found to have an increasing creatinine and abnormal proteins on urinalysis. Radiographs demonstrate multiple lytic lesions in the lumbar spine. The most accurate method of diagnosing this condition is:

1. A bone scan
2. Urine electrophoresis
3. MRI
4. Bone marrow biopsy
5. Urinalysis

The correct answer is: D
The most likely diagnosis is multiple myeloma (MM). MM is due to a clonal expansion of malignant plasma cells with excessive production of monoclonal immunoglobulins or immunoglobulin fragments. The diagnosis of MM requires ≥10 percent clonal plasma cells in the bone marrow.  Plasma cell dyscrasias such as monoclonal gammopathy of uncertain significance, smoldering multiple myeloma, Waldenstrom macroglobulinemia, solitary plasmacytoma, and AL amyloidosis can present with similar symptoms and thus it is important to remember that bone marrow biopsy is the gold standard to rule in multiple myeloma.

Answer A: Bone scan is an important part of the workup for multiple myeloma to determine the extent of lytic lesion involvement. However, many other conditions such as Paget's disease and metastatic renal cell carcinoma can cause similar findings to multiple myeloma.


Answer B: Urine electrophoresis may be positive in conditions such as monoclonal gammopathy of uncertain significance and AL amyloidosis. However, this test is important in the initial workup for patients with suspected multiple myeloma.


Answer C: MRI may be a part of the diagnostic workup in addition to bone scan to help identify focal lesions but is not necessarily part of initial workup for patients with suspected multiple myeloma.


Answer D: Because multiple myeloma is an osteoclastic process, a bone scan that detects osteoblastic activity, may be negative.


Answer E: Urinalysis may reveal muddy casts, hematuria, and proteinuria in patients with acute kidney injury secondary to multiple myeloma but these are non-specific findings.




Bottom Line: Because multiple myeloma is an osteoclastic process, a bone scan that detects osteoblastic activity, may be negative.

COMBANK Insight : Remember that the most specific test to diagnose multiple myeloma is bone marrow biopsy showing ≥10 percent clonal plasma cells in the bone marrow.  Other common tests such as urinalysis, MRI, urine electrophoresis, and bone scan are done to show the extent of involvement as well as target end-organ damage.

For more information, see:
"Clinical features, laboratory manifestations, and diagnosis of multiple myeloma" on ?UpToDate.

Link: Manual of Ocular Diagnosis and Therapy


Patient Presentation: Sensory & CNS Difficulties
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Head & Neck
Discipline: Emergency Medicine
A 32 year old otherwise healthy male presents to the emergency room with sudden onset pain in his right eye and blurry vision. He states he woke up this morning and noticed that his vision was blurry in the right eye and became painful. He denies any trauma to the eye and does not wear any glasses or contact lenses. On physical exam his temperature is 98.9, pulse 88, blood pressure 116/76, and oxygen saturation of 99% on room air. He is awake and alert, in no acute distress. He has conjunctival injection on the right eye and a mildly dilated non reactive pupil on the right. His intraocular pressure on the right is 23 mmHG. The most likely diagnosis is

1. Acute angle glaucoma
2. Cataracts
3. Macular degeneration
4. Retinal detachment
5. Uveitis

The correct answer is: A


Answer A: Acute angle glaucoma occurs is an ocular emergency. Acute angle closure is defined as at least 2 of the following symptoms: ocular pain, nausea/vomiting, and a history of intermittent blurring of vision with halos; and at least 3 of the following signs: IOP greater than 21 mm Hg, conjunctival injection, corneal epithelial edema, mid-dilated nonreactive pupil, and shallower chamber in the presence of occlusion.


Answer B: A cataract is a clouding of the lens in your eye. It affects your vision. Cataracts are very common in older people. A cataract can occur in either or both eyes. It cannot spread from one eye to the other. Cataracts usually develop slowly.


Answer C: Age-related macular degeneration — also called macular degeneration, AMD or ARMD — is deterioration of the macula, which is the small central area of the retina of the eye that controls visual acuity. There are 2 types of ARMD - wet and dry. The dry form is more common than the wet form, with about 85 to 90 percent of AMD patients diagnosed with dry AMD. The wet form of the disease usually leads to more serious vision loss. Dry AMD is an early stage of the disease and may result from the aging and thinning of macular tissues, depositing of pigment in the macula or a combination of the two processes.Dry macular degeneration is diagnosed when yellowish spots known as drusen begin to accumulate in and around the macula. It is believed these spots are deposits or debris from deteriorating tissue. With wet macular degeneration, new blood vessels grow beneath the retina and leak blood and fluid. This leakage causes permanent damage to light-sensitive retinal cells, which die off and create blind spots in central vision.


Answer D: The retina is the light-sensitive layer of tissue that lines the inside of the eye and sends visual messages through the optic nerve to the brain. When the retina detaches, it is lifted or pulled from its normal position. If not promptly treated, retinal detachment can cause permanent vision loss. There are three different types of retinal detachment: Rhegmatogenous —A tear or break in the retina allows fluid to get under the retina and separate it from the retinal pigment epithelium (RPE), the pigmented cell layer that nourishes the retina. These types of retinal detachments are the most common. Tractional—In this type of detachment, scar tissue on the retina’s surface contracts and causes the retina to separate from the RPE. This type of detachment is less common. Exudative—Frequently caused by retinal diseases, including inflammatory disorders and injury/trauma to the eye. In this type, fluid leaks into the area underneath the retina, but there are no tears or breaks in the retina.


Answer E: Uveitis is a general term describing a group of inflammatory diseases that produces swelling and destroys eye tissues. These diseases can slightly reduce vision or lead to severe vision loss. The term “uveitis” is used because the diseases often affect a part of the eye called the uvea. Nevertheless, uveitis is not limited to the uvea. These diseases also affect the lens, retina, optic nerve, and vitreous, producing reduced vision or blindness. Uveitis may be caused by problems or diseases occurring in the eye or it can be part of an inflammatory disease affecting other parts of the body.




Bottom Line: Sudden onset blindness or blurry vision and raised intraocular pressure is highly characteristic of acute angle glaucoma. It is an ocular emergency and must be treated promptly to prevent blindness.

For more information, see:
Manual of Ocular Diagnosis and Therapy

Link: "Clinical manifestations and diagnosis of infective endocarditis" on UpToDate.


Patient Presentation: Respiratory Difficulties
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Management
Body System: Cardiovascular System
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 68-year-old female presents to the emergency department with shortness of breath, fever, and lethargy for the past 2 days.  She has had a history of refractory arrhythmia which is managed with a pacemaker that was placed a year ago.  Vital signs reveal a blood pressure of 120/83 mmHg, a heart rate of 97/min, respiratory rate of 18/min, and a temperature of 390C. Physical exam shows retinal hemorrhages with pale centers of the left and right eyes with direct fundoscopy, holosystolic murmur at the apex which is a new finding compared to earlier visits to the hospital, and clear lungs.  Blood cultures from the left and right arms are drawn which show Staphylococcus epidermis bacteremia. Echocardiography is obtained and reveals vegetations on the mitral valve. The next best step in management is

1. intravenous ceftriaxone
2. intravenous ciprofloxacin
3. intravenous methicillin
4. intravenous vancomycin
5. oral vancomycin

The correct answer is: D
This patient has the classic signs of infective endocarditis which occurred from her contaminated pacemaker.  Patients present with new-onset heart murmurs fevers, flame-shaped retinal hemorrhages (Roth's spots), painless erythematous papules and macules on the palms and soles of the feet (Janeway lesions), painful erythematous subcutaneous papules (Osler's nodes), and subungual splinter hemorrhages (microemboli).  Diagnosis is based on 2 major criteria being met: 1) 2 positive blood cultures done in separate sites or multiple positive blood cultures at the same site 12 hours apart 2) Positive echocardiogram showing vegetations or abscesses.  Similarly diagnosis can be made with 1 major or 3 minor criteria (fever, the presence of a predisposing valvular condition or intravenous drug abuse; a predisposing valvular condition is defined as a prosthetic heart valve or a valve lesion that leads to significant regurgitation or turbulence of blood flow, vascular phenomenon" such as emboli to organs or the brain, hemorrhages in the mucous membranes around the eyes, immunologic phenomenon" such as glomerulonephritis, or lesions such as Roth's spots (in the retina of the eyes) or "Osler's nodes (nodules on the fingers or toes), and positive blood cultures that do not meet the strict definitions of a major criterion), or 5 minor criteria being met.  Treatment depends on the bacteria but IV vancomycin is treatment of choice for staphylococcus bacteremia.

Answer A: IV ceftriaxone would be a good treatment option for streptococci-associated endocarditis.


Answer B: IV ciprofloxacin would not be used in the treatment of infective endocarditis.


Answer C: It is reasonable to assume that the organism is resistant to methicillin. Time is crucial for the patient's survival or prevention of complications.


Answer E: PO vancomycin is not sufficient for the patient; they need parenteral antibiotics.




Bottom Line: Treat staphylococcus infective endocarditis with IV vancomycin.


For more information, see:
"Clinical manifestations and diagnosis of infective endocarditis" on UpToDate.

Link: "Subarachnoid Hemorrhage" on Medscape.


Patient Presentation: Sensory Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Nervous System
Discipline: Emergency Medicine
A 46-year-old male presents to the emergency department with the complaint of a severe posterior headache. History reveals the pain woke him from sleep three hours prior and is associated with nausea, vomiting, and neck stiffness. He denies fever, photophobia or recent trauma. Past medical history is significant for gastroesophageal reflux disease. A CT scan of the head is obtained and reveals no abnormalities. A lumbar puncture is performed and cerebrospinal fluid analysis reveals: 

opening pressure 250 mm H2O, yellow color
WBC 4/mm3 
RBC 1,255/mm3 
Protein 20 mg/dL
Glucose 60 mg/dL

The most likely diagnosis is

1. epidural hematoma
2. intraparenchymal hemorrhage
3. subarachnoid hemorrhage
4. subdural hematoma
5. traumatic tap

The correct answer is: C
The patient is presenting with symptoms of a subarachnoid hemorrhage (SAH). The most common causes of SAH are trauma and ruptured berry aneurysms. Classically patients will present with a severe posterior headache associated with nausea and vomiting due to the increased intracranial pressure and neck pain due to meningeal irritation caused by the blood in the subarachnoid space. A non-contrast CT scan of the head is the first-line diagnostic imaging of choice, however, it can be negative in small hemorrhages. In cases where SAH is suspected a lumbar puncture with cerebrospinal fluid (CSF) analysis is necessary. A classic sign of SAH is xanthochromia, a yellow discoloration of the CSF, which occurs when red blood cells have been present for at least 2 hours. It is caused by the breakdown of red blood cells and the release of heme pigments. Opening pressure of the CSF can be elevated or normal in SAH.

Answer A: Epidural hematoma generally occurs in the setting of trauma when an arterial collection of blood forms between the inner table of the skull and the dural lining. This type of injury is typically seen on CT scan of the head and often accompanies an overlying skull fracture. The patient in this question denies trauma making epidural hematoma unlikely.


Answer B: Intraparenchymal hemorrhage is usually visible on CT scan of the head but will not cause xanthochromia of the CSF.


Answer D: Subdural hematomas are a venous accumulation of blood beneath the dura, usually due to damage to the bridging veins. Like epidural hematomas, they are typically caused by trauma and appear as a crescent-shaped collection of blood along the periphery of the brain.


Answer E: A traumatic tap occurs when a vessel is inadvertently damaged during the lumbar puncture causing red blood cells to contaminate the CSF as it is collected. The patient in this question has other symptoms concerning for SAH so the RBCs in the CSF should be considered accurate. Another method used to differentiate between traumatic tap and SAH is to see that the RBCs decrease from tube 1 to tube 4.




Bottom Line: Xanthochromia is a yellow discoloration of CSF caused by degraded red blood cells in the subarachnoid space; it is a classic finding in SAH.

For more information, see:
"Subarachnoid Hemorrhage" on Medscape.

Link: ?Notification of child abuse


Patient Presentation: General Concepts
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Health Care Delivery Issues
Body System: Musculoskeletal System
Discipline: Pediatrics
A 4-year-old boy is brought to the emergency room by his mother. She states he started crying suddenly when he was playing with his father. The mother states she ran into the room and found the child on the floor crying and holding onto his right leg in pain. The child is up to date with all his immunizations, and is developmentally appropriate for his age. Physical exam shows the child appears uncomfortable and crying while holding his right leg. An x-ray of the child’s right leg shows a spiral fracture of the right femur. In addition to proper orthopedic care for the child, what else should be done?

1. Notify child protective services.
2. Confront the child’s mother as to why she is abusing her child.
3. Contact the hospital’s ethics committee.
4. Notify the social worker for look for alternative housing for the child.
5. Discharge the patient after getting orthopedic care.

The correct answer is: A
As a physician you have the duty to report any abuse that is occurring in a vulnerable population. This child has a spiral fracture of the femur, which is very uncommon without a significant amount of force. Although a spiral fracture in itself is not diagnostic of child abuse, as a clinician if you see a spiral fracture without a history of significant trauma, or the story seems suspicious, you must always suspect child abuse. If you are suspect, then you must notify the proper authorities, in this case child protective services. They will conduct an investigation to determine if child abuse is indeed occurring. Just because you report a case to child protective services it does not mean that child abuse has occurred, it’s just that the pattern of injury is suspicious and should be looked into further. This helps to protect the patient and make sure proper care is being provided and it is your duty to report to the authorities the abuse of any vulnerable population.

Answer B: Confrontation with a parent is never advised. If you are suspicious child abuse has occurred, you must report it to the proper authorities. Also advisable is to speak to the parents and discuss your findings. This helps to open up a channel of communication between the parents and the clinician.


Answer C: There is no need to contact the hospital’s ethics committee. There is a clear suspicion of child abuse occurring, the next thing to do for this patient is to notify child protective services so they can conduct an investigation to see if indeed abuse is occurring.


Answer D: A social worker’s job is to address any social issues that may be contributing to a child’s health. An example would be if a child lives in a house with lead paint, the social worker’s duty would include how to find alternative housing or what else can be done to facilitate moving the child to a safer environment. In this case, there is no role for a social worker. Child protective services must be notified first, an investigation conducted to see whether abuse has occurred and make recommendations based on the investigation.


Answer E: This is inappropriate because there is clear suspicion abuse is occurring. Without notifying child protective services, the physician can be liable for any abuse that is occurring because they did not notify the proper authorities and breached his duty. If you are suspecting abuse, you must notify the proper authorities.




Bottom Line: If you suspect child abuse if occuring, you must notify child protective services.

For more information, see:
?Notification of child abuse

Patient Presentation: Trauma
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Scientific Understanding of Health & Disease Mechanisms
Body System: Asymptomatic & General Symptoms
Discipline: Emergency Medicine
A 54-year-old male presents to the emergency department by ambulance after overdosing on methanol. History is unobtainable. Physical examination reveals an incoherent male with Glasgow coma scale of 12. 

Question 1 of 3 in this set
An arterial blood gas sample is most likely to reveal

1. anion gap metabolic acidosis
2. metabolic alkalosis
3. non anion gap metabolic acidosis
4. respiratory acidosis
5. respiratory alkalosis

The correct answer is: A
Methanol poisoning leads to metabolic acidosis, which is manifested by a low serum bicarbonate level. Anion-gap metabolic acidosis results from elevated lactate and ketone levels, which is likely due to the accumulation of formic acid. This may necessitate the administration of bicarbonate as well as necessitate the need for assisted ventilation. Bicarbonate has been shown to potentially be effective in the reversal of the visual deficits associated with ingestion of methanol. The administration of bicarbonate may also help to decrease the amount of active formic acid.

Answer B: Metabolic alkalosis may be caused by diuretic use, vomiting and excessive bicarbonate consumption.


Answer C: Non-anion gap metabolic acidosis may be caused by excessive diarrhea, renal tubular acidosis, and diuretic use with carbonic anhydrase inhibitors.


Answer D: Respiratory acidosis may be caused by COPD, barbiturate ingestion, or chronic lung disease. It results from any process that causes the lungs to retain CO2.


Answer E: Respiratory alkalosis results from any process that causes the lungs to excessively exhale CO2. Examples include pulmonary embolism and breathing at high altitudes.




Bottom Line: Methanol poisoning leads to an anion-gap metabolic acidosis.

For more information, see:
"Metabolic Acidosis" on Medscape.

Link: Methanol Toxicity" on Medscape.


Patient Presentation: Trauma
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Management
Body System: Asymptomatic & General Symptoms
Discipline: Emergency Medicine
A 54-year-old male presents to the emergency department by ambulance after overdosing on methanol. History is unobtainable. Physical examination reveals an incoherent male with Glasgow coma scale of 12. 

Question 2 of 3 in this set
The best antidote for management is

1. ethanol
2. glucagon
3. methylene blue
4. n-acetylcysteine
5. naloxone

The correct answer is: A
This patient is suffering from methanol poisoning and should be immediately treated with ethanol or fomepizole (which is not an answer choice). Antidote therapy works by delaying methanol metabolism until the methanol can be eliminated from the system either naturally or via dialysis. Ethanol is believed to compete with methanol for ADH, therefore preventing the conversion of methanol to its toxic byproducts. ADH has been shown to have a 10- to 20-fold increased affinity for ethanol over methanol. By slowing methanol metabolism, ethanol is used to prevent accumulation of formic acid. Fomepizole is also metabolized by ADH but has limited use because of high cost and lack of availability.

Answer B: Glucagon is used as an antidote for beta-blocker toxicity.


Answer C: Methylene blue is the antidote for methemoglobinemia that should be watched for in a patient thought to have overdosed on nitrates, nitroprusside, or antimalarials.


Answer D: N-acetylcysteine regenerates glutathione and is used to prevent the toxic metabolism of acetaminophen.


Answer E: Opioids such as heroin cause euphoric behavior, miosis, constipation, and ultimately can lead to respiratory depression and even death. Withdrawal leads to abdominal cramps, nausea, and vomiting. Treatment options include methadone therapy, naltrexone to prevent euphoria, and naloxone for acute overdose with significant respiratory depression.




Bottom Line: Ethanol and fomepizole are both antidotes for methanol toxicity and are thought to compete with methanol for ADH, which prevents methanol metabolism.

COMBANK Insight : Knowing antidotes to the most common toxic substances is important for test day. Also, be prepared to determine the most likely toxicity based on presenting symptoms.

For more information, see:
Methanol Toxicity" on Medscape.

Link: "Methanol Toxicity" on Medscape.


Patient Presentation: Trauma
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Management
Body System: Asymptomatic & General Symptoms
Discipline: Emergency Medicine
A 54-year-old male presents to the emergency department by ambulance after overdosing on methanol. History is unobtainable. Physical examination reveals an incoherent male with Glasgow coma scale of 12. 

Question 3 of 3 in this set
Several hours after arrival to the hospital the patient complains of blurred vision and is noted to have a serum methanol level of 24 mg/dL. The most beneficial treatment at this time is

1. administration of active charcoal
2. gastric decontamination
3. gastric lavage
4. hemodialysis
5. ipecac induced emesis

The correct answer is: D
The most appropriate treatment for this patient would be hemodialysis, given new clinical and laboratory findings. In cases of methanol poisoning, there are four indications for hemodialysis: 1) Ingestion of a methanol volume greater than 30 mL, 2) a serum methanol level of 20 mg/dL or greater, 3) development of visual changes, and 4) lack of improvement in acidosis despite multiple sodium bicarbonate infusions. This patient has a serum methanol level of 24 mg/dL, therefore warranting hemodialysis.

Answer A: It is doubtful that activated charcoal would have the ability to absorb such large amounts of methanol but may be useful if a co-ingestant is suspected.


Answer B: Gastric decontamination is unlikely to be beneficial due to the rapid and complete absorption of methanol from the gut.


Answer C: Gastric lavage would need to be performed immediately after ingestion to be beneficial.


Answer E: Ipecac-induced emesis is contraindicated due to the risk of rapid loss of consciousness.




Bottom Line: Hemodialysis should be performed in patients with methanol poisoning who meet the following criteria: 1) Ingestion of methanol volumes greater than 30 mL 2) Serum methanol levels of 20 mg/dL or greater 3) Development of visual changes 4) Lack of acidotic improvement despite multiple sodium bicarbonate infusions

COMBANK Insight : Indications for hemodialysis also include: hyperkalemia refractory to medical therapy, uremic pericarditis and end stage renal disease.

For more information, see:
"Methanol Toxicity" on Medscape.

Patient Presentation: Disease Detection & Monitoring
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Health Promotion & Disease Prevention
Body System: Endocrine System
Discipline: Family Medicine
A 42-year-old male presents to the clinic for a well visit. He is a non-smoker with a past medical history of hypertension, kidney stones and was recently diagnosed with Type II diabetes mellitus. The patient is currently taking metformin and his most recent A1C level was 5.2. During your interview, he expresses extreme concern about the possibility developing foot ulcers because his father has diabetes and had to have a limb amputation secondary to the development of a decubitus ulcer. You explain to him that the best preventative measure in avoiding the development of decubitus ulcers in diabetes is

1. applying a deep moisturizer to the feet before bed each night
2. checking the urine for microalbuminuria every 6 months
3. control of blood glucose levels
4. making sure to dry the feet appropriately after bathing
5. wearing shoes with good padding

The correct answer is: C
While applying a deep moisturizer to the feet before bed each night, making sure to dry the feet appropriately after bathing, and wearing shoes with good padding are all good answer choices in helping to prevent decubitus ulcers, the best preventative measure is to self-monitor blood glucose levels. This allows the patient to know day by day how their diabetes is being controlled. By appropriately adjusting their meds to better control blood glucose, they will prevent decubitus ulcers as well as other related complications such as peripheral neuropathy, retinopathy, incontinence, impotence etc.

Answers A & D & E: While important adjuncts, none of these foot care methods are better than good blood glucose control at preventing diabetic foot ulcers.


Answer B: Checking the urine for microalbuminuria every 6 months is important in monitoring for the prevention of diabetic nephropathy.




Bottom Line: The best preventative measure for avoiding decubitus ulcers in diabetic patients involves the self-monitoring of blood glucose levels.

COMBANK Insight : It is important to note that whenever the question is regarding a "preventative" measure, the best possible management option is to control the etiology of the problem. Pressure redistribution, hygienic measures, wearing appropriate shoe wear are excellent measures that accompany adequate medical management of the etiology, but these measures would not be directed at the root of the diagnosis. 

When medical management has been optimized, many stage I and stage II pressure sores heal spontaneously. However, stage III and stage IV ulcers almost always require a surgical approach.

For more information, see:
"Diabetic ulcers" on Medscape.
"Evaluation of the diabetic foot" on UpToDate.

Patient Presentation: Respiratory Difficulties
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Health Care Delivery Issues
Body System: Respiratory System
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 57-year-old Caucasian male presents with his wife to your office for a routine primary care visit. He is without complaints and review of symptoms is negative. His medical history includes chronic obstructive pulmonary disease, hypertension and hyperlipidemia. He has a 15-pack-year history of tobacco abuse but quit over two years ago. He denies a history of alcohol abuse or illegal drugs. He is compliant with his medications and is stable on inhaled albuterol as needed and amlodipine. Vital signs reveal a temperature of 98.6º F, blood pressure 126/82 mm Hg, heart rate of 76 bpm, and a respiratory rate of 15. Physical examination is remarkable for a slightly overweight male in no acute distress. On review of his records he received the influenza vaccination one year ago with no adverse events. He denies recent emergency room visits and has never been hospitalized. He is also up to date on his colonoscopy screening. Which of the following is the most appropriate immunization at this time?






1. Pneumococcal vaccination only
2. Trivalent influenza and pneumococcal vaccinations
3. Trivalent influenza vaccination only
4. Trivalent intranasal influenza and pneumococcal vaccinations
5. Trivalent intranasal influenza vaccination only

The correct answer is: B
Annual influenza vaccination is recommended in all patients with chronic obstructive pulmonary disease (COPD), regardless of age. The pneumococcal vaccination is recommended for all adults older than 65 years and for younger patients with certain conditions such as COPD, asthma, diabetes and active smokers. Annual influenza vaccinations is recommended for all persons 6 months and older during the influenza season. The main vaccination used in the United States is the trivalent inactivated virus. There is an intranasal form that is live and attenuated. This is administered only to patients age 5-49 years who are not pregnant, immunosuppressed, or living with an immunosuppressed person. A single reactivation with the pneumococcal vaccine is recommended in adults older than 65 years if they were vaccinated more than 5 years previously at a time when they were younger than 65 and in immunosuppressed patients 5 years or more after the first dose. If this patient received the pneumococcal vaccine after 65 then he would not need another dose. Because he was younger than 65 he will need another dose of the vaccine after he turns 65 years old.

Answer A: Annual influenza vaccination is recommended in all patients greater than 6 months of age.


Answer C: The pneumococcal vaccination is recommended for all adults older than 65 years and for younger patients with certain conditions such as COPD,
asthma, diabetes and active smokers.


Answer D: The intranasal form of the influenza vaccination is live and attenuated and only recommended for healthy persons between the ages of 5 and 49 years of age.


Answer E: The pneumococcal vaccination is recommended for all adults older than 65 years and for younger patients with certain conditions such as COPD, asthma, diabetes and active smokers.




Bottom Line: The pneumococcal and influenza vaccines are recommended in patients with COPD.

For more information, see:
Vaccination information at CDC
