Link: Pelak,VS, Quan, D. Ocular Myasthenia Gravis. In Uptodate Wilterdink JL (Ed), UpToDate. Waltham, MA.


Patient Presentation: Sensory & CNS Difficulties
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Diagnostic Technologies
Body System: Nervous System
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 73-year-old male presents to the office for a two-month history of intermittent double vision. He says that he notices it more at night when he is trying to read a novel. Neurologic exam shows bilateral ptosis with sustained ninety-second up-gaze and subtle left sixth nerve palsy. He has diplopia on far left vision. The rest of his neurologic and general exam is unremarkable. Acetylcholine receptor binding antibody test is negative. Which of the following options is most specific and sensitive to diagnose his most likely condition?

1. CT scan of chest
2. Bedside edrophonium testing
3. muscle specific tyrosine kinase antibody
4. repetitive nerve stimulation of facial nerve
5. single fiber electromyography

The correct answer is: E
The patient likely has ocular myasthenia gravis (OMG). These patients typically present with significant ophthalmologic disease without significant systemic weakness, bulbar symptoms or respiratory distress (though it can progress to these).  Their symptoms typically fluctuate during the day and are fatigable as seen with systemic MG. The most common symptoms are ptosis and diplopia. Acetylcholine receptor binding antibody (AChR-ab) is typically found in about 85-95% of patients with systemic MG, but they are only seen in about 45-50% of those with OMG. Single-fiber electromyography (SFEMG) in experienced hands can have a sensitivity up to 100%. Treatment for OMG is similar to systemic MG; the anticholinesterase agent pyridostigmine is the mainstay of chronic treatment. Prednisone and other glucocorticoids are frequently used in chronic therapy and management of exacerbations. Steroid sparing agents such azathioprine, mycophenolate and cyclosporine have also been used.

Answer A: A CT scan of the chest is commonly done in any type of MG to evaluate for a thymic hyperplasia or thymoma. It is believed that thymic abnormalities produce the ACh-R antibodies. Thymectomy is then sometimes performed in appropriate patients.


Answer B: Edrophonium or Tensilon test could be performed in this patient. It is typically done only in patients with an objective finding (such as ptosis or ophthalmoparesis) as the effect of the drug can be easily observed. The sensitivity of the edrophonium test is about 95% in generalized myasthenia and reportedly similar in ocular myasthenia with ptosis. However, diplopia fails to respond in about a third of patients and long-standing myasthenia may not respond at all. Also, false-positive results have been described in other neuromuscular, neuropathic, and central conditions, including brain stem gliomas and other tumours


Answer C: Muscle specific tyrosine kinase antibody (MuSK antibody) is the second most common antibody seen in myasthenia gravis. It is found in 38-50% of patients who are AChR antibody negative. It is rarely seen in ocular MG.


Answer D: Repetitive nerve stimulation is widely used as a confirmatory test in MG, but it is less sensitive than SFEMG. It is typically done by stimulating a nerve at low rate (3 hertz) 6-10 times. The compound muscle action potentials (CMAP) are then recorded. In normal muscle, there will be no change. MG patients will have a progressive decline (a decrement).




Bottom Line: Single fiber electromyography is the most specific and sensitive test for any type of MG.

COMBANK Insight : Myasthenia gravis is commonly tested subject. The diagnostic course is usually: 1) AChR-ab 2) MuSK ab 3) RNS 4) SFEMG. A CT scan is usually done not to make the diagnosis to look for a thymoma.

For more information, see:
Pelak,VS, Quan, D. Ocular Myasthenia Gravis. In Uptodate Wilterdink JL (Ed), UpToDate. Waltham, MA.

Link: "Urinary tract injury in gynecologic surgery: Evaluation and management" on UpToDate.


Patient Presentation: Musculoskeletal Difficulties And Muscular Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Scientific Understanding of Health & Disease Mechanisms
Body System: Genitourinary System
Discipline: Obstetrics/Gynecology
A 45-year-old female is in the hospital after she underwent a total abdominal hysterectomy two days ago. This morning she complains of right flank pain. Vital signs reveal a temperature of 101ºF. Physical examination reveals right costovertebral tenderness. A surgical urinary tract injury is suspected. Which of the following is the most likely location of her injury?

1. bladder dome
2. bladder trigone
3. cardinal ligament
4. near the psoas muscle
5. pelvic brim

The correct answer is: C
Among the risks of abdominal and pelvic surgery is injury to the urinary tract, whether the ureters or the bladder. During a hysterectomy the most common location for ureteral injury is in the cardinal ligament, which attaches the cervix to the pelvic wall. Here the ureter is a few centimeters lateral to the cervix and just below the uterine artery (water under the bridge). Due to the proximity of the structures, the ureter can be damaged during ligation of the uterine artery. Intraoperative or postoperative urology consultation is usually required when an injury to the urinary system is identified.

Answer A: The bladder dome is not commonly injured during hysterectomy and is more commonly injured in bowel surgery.


Answer B: The bladder trigone is where the ureters enter the bladder. Injury to it is not as likely as the cardinal ligament.


Answer D: The location where the ureter crosses the psoas muscle is far enough away from the uterus that it is an uncommon site of injury.


Answer E: Injury at the pelvic brim can occur during ligation of the ovarian vessels but this is not as common as the injury to the ureter within the cardinal ligament.




Bottom Line: The most common location for ureteral injury during a hysterectomy is the cardinal ligament. Here the ureter courses just below the uterine artery (water under the bridge). 

COMBANK Insight : Anatomy is not as commonly tested on step 2 compared to step 1 but when it is, the question will be clinically oriented. 

For more information, see:
"Urinary tract injury in gynecologic surgery: Evaluation and management" on UpToDate.

Patient Presentation: Sensory Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Diagnostic Technologies
Body System: Cardiovascular System
Discipline: Emergency Medicine
A 58-year-old male is hospitalized for lower extremity cellulitis. Past medical history reveals hypertension, diabetes, and hyperlipidemia. On hospital day number two, the patient develops crushing substernal chest pain. Laboratory studies reveal a troponin-I level of 5 ng/mL. An ECG is obtained and reveals ST segment depression and T-wave inversion in the lateral leads.

Question 1 of 2 in this set
The most likely diagnosis is

1. non-ST segment elevation myocardial infarction
2. pericarditis
3. stable angina
4. ST segment elevation myocardial infarction
5. unstable angina

The correct answer is: A
This patient is currently experiencing a non-ST segment myocardial infarction (NSTEMI) due to active chest pain, a positive troponin, and an ECG showing ST segment depression and T-wave inversion in the lateral leads (I, aVL, V5-V6). This patient has 3 listed coronary artery disease risk factors and is at high risk for acute coronary syndrome including NSTEMI.

Answer B: Pericarditis is rarely described as an acute onset of substernal chest pain. More likely, it is a gradual progression of pain. Typical ECG findings are diffuse ST segment elevation, PR segment depression. When suspecting pericarditis, remember to look at for an elevated PR segment in aVR.


Answer C: Stable angina involves episodic pain lasting 5-15 minutes that is provoked by exertion, and relieved by rest or nitroglycerin. It is typically associated with transient ST segment depression and is due to relative cardiac ischemia (oxygen demand outweighs supply, causing ischemia and pain).


Answer D: The patient is not experiencing an acute ST segment elevation myocardial infarction (STEMI) due to lack of ST segment elevation on ECG. The fact that the troponin is positive is indicative of current or recent myocardial cell damage, which can occur for a variety of reasons. STEMI is a clinical and ECG diagnosis, therefore there needs to be ECG criteria met in the right clinical situation to diagnose STEMI.


Answer E: Unstable angina is defined as chest pain at rest without ECG findings and negative serum cardiac biomarkers. Unstable angina differs from stable angina only by patient description of severity, timing, and occurrence at rest (stable angina occurring with exertion). This patient has a positive troponin, thus making it by definition a NSTEMI.




Bottom Line: Non–ST-segment elevation myocardial infarction (NSTEMI) is distinguished from unstable angina and STEMI by elevated levels of cardiac enzymes in the setting of chest pain and the absence of ST segment elevation on ECG.

For more information, see:
"Acute Coronary Syndrome Workup" on Medscape.

Link: "Acute Coronary Syndrome Treatment & Management" on Medscape.


Patient Presentation: Sensory Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Management
Body System: Cardiovascular System
Discipline: Emergency Medicine
A 58-year-old male is hospitalized for lower extremity cellulitis. Past medical history reveals hypertension, diabetes, and hyperlipidemia. On hospital day number two, the patient develops crushing substernal chest pain. Laboratory studies reveal a troponin-I level of 5 ng/mL. An ECG is obtained and reveals ST segment depression and T-wave inversion in the lateral leads.

Question 2 of 2 in this set
In order to reduce morbidity and mortality during the acute event, the most appropriate pharmacological agent is

1. angiotensin converting enzyme inhibitor
2. aspirin
3. low molecular weight heparin
4. nitroglycerin
5. unfractionated heparin

The correct answer is: B
Aspirin is the first line mediation in all acute coronary syndromes (ACS) because it has been shown to consistently reduce morbidity and mortality. Aspirin renders platelets inactive by irreversibly inhibiting thromboxane A2 production thus decreasing the rate of thrombus formation within the coronary arteries. Antiplatelet therapy is the mainstay of ACS treatment and prevention. If a patient does not tolerate aspirin, clopidogrel should be used as an alternative.

Answer A: Angiotensin converting enzyme inhibitors (ACE-I) are indicated in the treatment of hypertension, congestive heart failure, and for prevention of diabetic nephropathy. Their use in ACS has not been shown to directly decrease morbidity or mortality. ACE-inhibitors are used post-myocardial infarction management to prevent remodeling of the myocardium and to decrease afterload.


Answers C & E: Unfractionated heparin (UFH) and low molecular weight heparin (LMWH) are indicated as prophylaxis for ischemic complications caused by unstable angina and NSTEMI. The data showing improvements in outcomes compare aspirin alone versus aspirin plus UFH or LMWH and likely show a benefit. However, the data regarding true morbidity and mortality of these agents is controversial and overall do not offer the reduction that aspirin does. New data suggests that LMWH is slightly better than UFH and is easier to use due to dosing and monitoring issues associated with UFH.


Answer D: Nitroglycerin causes relaxation of the vascular smooth muscle (including coronary arteries) via stimulation of intracellular cyclic guanosine monophosphate production. Nitrates are used to increase blood and oxygen delivery to ischemic cardiac tissue and relieve symptoms of ischemia. Nitrates have not been shown to improve mortality however.




Bottom Line: Aspirin is the first line medication in ACS including unstable angina and NSTEMI. It has been shown to reduce morbidity and mortality associated with ACS.

COMBANK Insight : Remember, aspirin is given to all patients with known or suspected ACS. It will be the correct answer unless the patient has an aspirin allergy or the question specifically asks a question regarding another medication.

For more information, see:
"Acute Coronary Syndrome Treatment & Management" on Medscape.
Le T, Bhushan V, Gagga HS. First Aid for the USMLE Step 2 CK: A Student-to-Student. New York, NY: McGraw Hill; 2010: 55-57.

Patient Presentation: Musculoskeletal Difficulties And Muscular Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Management
Body System: Musculoskeletal System
Discipline: Osteopathic Principle and Practice
A 40-year old female with a past medical history significant for hypertension and type-2 diabetes presents with 6 weeks of ongoing left forearm and left elbow pain. The pain first occurred after she slipped on a wet floor, fell backwards, and landed on her left hand. She went to the urgent care clinic the same day as accident, had x-rays taken, and was sent home with a wrist brace after the x-rays were found to be negative for fracture. The forearm pain has improved significantly since the accident, but has not completely resolved. She denies paresthesia, radiating pain, or muscle weakness. Physical exam is notable for stiffness in left elbow extension, left wrist range of motion in all directions, tenderness to palpation at the radial head, and radial head motion restriction. Using direct muscle energy treatment, the physician takes the patient’s left forearm into pronation until the restrictive barrier edge is palpated and instructs the patient to supinate against the physician’s equal counterforce. A few seconds later, the patient relaxes and the physician pronates the patient’s arm until a new restrictive barrier is appreciated. This procedure is repeated 3-5 times. Re-evaluation of the upper extremity would most likely show improvement where and in which direction?

1. Radial head; anterior glide
2. Radial head; posterior glide
3. Radial head; medial glide
4. Radial head; lateral glide
5. Elbow; flexion

The correct answer is: B
The patient has an anterior radial head; that is, the radial head is restricted in posterior glide. Anterior radial head somatic dysfunctions typically result from a backward fall, where the patient extends her arm, and lands on the palm of the hand to break the fall. The forearm is in the supinated anatomic position. The distal radius moves posterior as a result and the radial head moves anterior. Direct muscle energy treatment to treat a radial head somatic dysfunction involves pronation or supination of the forearm. As the physician pronates the patient’s forearm into the restrictive barrier, the patient’s radial head moves posterior. Re-evaluation of the radial head after direct muscle energy treatment should show improvement in posterior glide.

Answer A: Pronation of the forearm by the physician engages the radial head posterior glide barrier.


Answers C & D: Pronation and supination of the forearm does not cause motion of the radial head in this direction.


Answers E & F: Pronation and supination of the forearm does not address the elbow joint.


Answers G & H: Pronation and supination of the forearm does not address the wrist joint.




Bottom Line: A common cause of an anterior radial head somatic dysfunction is falling backward with the arm extended. Pronation of the forearm causes the radial head to move posterior.

For more information, see:
Heinking KP. Upper Extremities. In: Chila AG, executive ed, Carreiro, JE, Dowling DJ, et al. Foundations of Osteopathic Medicine. 3rd ed. Philadelphia, PA: Lippincott Williams & Wilkins; 2011:655-656.
Nicholas AS, Nicholas EA. Atlas of Osteopathic Techniques. 2nd ed. Philadelphia, PA: Lippincott Williams & Wilkins; 2012:327.
Savarese RG. OMT Review: A Comprehensive Review in Osteopathic Medicine. 2009:66,143.

Patient Presentation: Respiratory Difficulties
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Management
Body System: Respiratory System
Discipline: Pediatrics
A 7-year-old African-American female presents to the office for a well child examination. Past medical history is significant for asthma. History reveals over the last month she has had episodes of coughing and wheezing four times per week and waking from sleep with coughing spells once a week. She is currently managed on inhaled albuterol on an as needed basis.  You ask her to demonstrate how she uses her inhaler and she shows you appropriately. Vital signs reveal a blood pressure of 110/75 mmHg, a heart rate of 100/min, a temperature of 37°C (98.6°F), and a respiratory rate of 20/min. She is in the 50th percentile for height and the 80th percentile for weight. Pulmonary function testing is obtained and reveals and FEV1 > 80% expected and FEV1/FVC > 80% expected. The most appropriate management is

1. azithromycin
2. add a long acting beta agonist
3. add a low dose inhaled corticosteroids
4. add an oral steroid
5. continue albuterol on an as needed basis

The correct answer is: C
This case describes a patient with previously well-managed intermittent asthma, who is now experiencing mild persistent asthma. Intermittent asthma, which requires only a short acting beta agonist such as albuterol, is defined as symptoms no more than twice a week and night-time symptoms no more than two times a month. When symptoms progress to more than twice a week or nighttime symptoms more than twice per month asthma can be described as persistent. Despite essentially normal PFTs this patient needs acceleration or treatment. The first step in management would be to add a daily inhaled low dose corticosteroid inhaler to the current treatment and reevaluate to determine if this was effective.

Answer A: Antibiotics would be appropriate if the child was displaying signs of an infection.


Answer B: A long acting beta agonist would be added if the child's asthma symptoms do not become controlled with the low-dose inhaled corticosteroid.


Answer D: An oral steroid for a short time may be added to the current regimen if there is an exacerbation.


Answer E: Continuing albuterol on an as needed basis would be inadequate management due to the patient's increase in symptoms.




Bottom Line: Asthma is considered persistent when symptoms are present more than two days a week or night-time symptoms. The first step in escalation of treatment after albuterol in low dose inhaled corticosteroids.

For more information, see:
"Overview of Changes to Asthma Guidelines: Diagnosis and Screening" on AAFP.org.

Patient Presentation: Sensory Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Management
Body System: Cardiovascular System
Discipline: Family Medicine
A 54-year-old male patient presents to the office with the complaint of chest pain.  He has a past medical history of obesity, tobacco abuse, and hypertension.  History reveals that his pain started thirty minutes ago.  He describes it as a retrosternal heaviness with associated shortness of breath. Vitals reveal a blood pressure of 152/92 mmHg, a heart rate of 94/min, and a respiratory rate of 18/min. Physical examination reveals an anxious, diaphoretic male patient with paravertebral muscle spasms from T1-4. An electrocardiogram is obtained and reveals a first degree AV block with no ST changes.  The most appropriate next step in management is

1. check cardiac enzymes
2. exercise tolerance testing
3. immediate transfer to an emergency department
4. reassurance that his chest pain is not of cardiac origin
5. repeat electrocardiogram

The correct answer is: C
This patient is presenting with complaints that are very concerning for an acute coronary syndrome, especially given his past medical history.  Risk factors for coronary artery disease include diabetes mellitus (the most significant risk factor), tobacco smoking, hypertension (the most common risk factor), hyperlipidemia, age above 45 in men or 55 in women, and a family history of premature coronary artery disease.  Family history refers to first degree relatives; siblings and parents.  Premature disease is defined as younger than age 55 in men or 65 in women.  Ischemic cardiac chest pain is typically described as dull, sore, squeezing, or pressure-like pain located in the retrosternal area that may radiate to the left arm or neck.  Patients may also complain of shortness of breath, diaphoresis, nausea, fever, and anxiety.  Like any cardiac pathology, physical exam findings may include viscerosomatic reflexes resulting in TART changes from T1-4.

The best initial test is always an electrocardiogram.  It is important to note, that the electrocardiogram is not a 100% sensitive test, and may be normal even in the setting of ischemic chest pain.  Because this patient has multiple major risk factors for, and is presenting classically with, a potential acute coronary syndrome, the most appropriate next step is to transfer him to an emergency department where a more thorough evaluation may be undertaken.

Answer A: This would be inappropriate in an outpatient setting. The patient should be transferred to an emergency room and the enzymes may be drawn then.


Answer B: Exercise tolerance testing is done to evaluate chest pain when the etiology is not clear and the electrocardiogram is not diagnostic.


Answer D: Given his past medical history, his symptoms, and his physical appearance, acute coronary syndrome must be ruled out.


Answer E: Repeating the electrocardiogram is reasonable, however it should not delay transfer to an emergency department. Once the patient has arrived at the emergency department a repeat electrocardiogram could be completed to assess progression of ischemic change, if it is present.




Bottom Line: When evaluating a patient in an ambulatory setting with signs and symptoms concerning for acute coronary syndrome, the most important thing to do is have them transferred to an emergency department where a full evaluation can be undertaken.

For more information, see:

Fischer C, August E, Gonzalez-Mayo A, et al. Master the Boards USMLE Step 2 CK. New York, NY: Kaplan Publishing; 2011: 49-56

Patient Presentation: Masses & Edema
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Management
Body System: Gastrointestinal System
Discipline: Surgery
A 35-year-old male presents with a palpable inguinal mass. History reveals intermittent abdominal discomfort when active at work. Physical examination reveals a BMI of 38 and a palpable, reducible left inguinal hernia. The patient requests an elective herniorrhaphy. The most appropriate initial step in management would be to perform

1. counseling regarding weight loss, diet, and exercise with appropriate follow-up
2. laparoscopic totally extraperitoneal herniorrhaphy
3. open mesh and plug repair
4. pedal pump
5. sphenopalatine ganglion release

The correct answer is: A
Any condition that chronically increases intra-abdominal pressure contributes to the development and recurrence of inguinal hernias. These conditions include morbid obesity (BMI>35), chronic obstructive pulmonary disease, chronic constipation, pregnancy, severe ascites, and urinary obstruction. To decrease the risk of post-operative recurrence, patients with asymptomatic inguinal hernias should improve any factors related to increased intra-abdominal pressure before elective herniorrhaphy.

Answer B: Before surgery is performed, the patient should be counseled regarding weight loss, diet and exercise in order to increase the effectiveness of the hernia repair.


Answer C: Open mesh repair of inguinal hernias is one of the most common treatments. However, in order to decrease the hernia recurrence, the patient should be counseled regarding diet, exercise and weight loss before surgery is performed.


Answer D: Osteopathic treatment has not been shown to be useful in the treatment of inguinal hernias, given the mechanical treatment of the condition.


Answer E: Osteopathic manipulative therapy will not be useful in the treatment of this patient's hernia.




Bottom Line: Counseling patients on weight loss, diet, and exercise should be performed to prevent recurrent inguinal hernias in high-risk populations.

COMBANK Insight : COMLEX often tests materials that have an effect on patient outcomes as prevention of recurrent disease.

For more information, see:
"Inguinal and femoral hernia repair: Open techniques" on UpToDate.

Patient Presentation: Substance Abuse
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Nervous System
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 45-year-old male presents to the emergency department after being hit by a car at low speed.  Past medical history reveals hepatitis C, cirrhosis, and alcoholism. Vital signs reveal a temperature of 37.2º C (99º F), a blood pressure of 132/80 mmHg, a heart rate of 72/minute, a respiratory rate of 16/minute and an oxygen saturation of 98% on room air. Physical examination reveals an intoxicated male with slurred speech and lethargy; he is unable to cooperate with the exam fully. You note paravertebral hypertonicity from T6-9, right diaphragm restriction, and a tender nodule in the 6th intercostal space near the sternum on the right. There are several abrasions on the patient's limbs and cardiopulmonary examination is normal. The patient is admitted to the hospital for observation until a more thorough exam can be completed. 8 hours later, the patient becomes diaphoretic, agitated, and tremulous while remaining fully alert and oriented. Repeat vital signs reveal a temperature of 37°C (98.6°F), a blood pressure of 180/100 mmHg, a heart rate of 130/min, a respiratory rate of 20/min, and an oxygen saturation of 94% on room air.

Question 1 of 2 in this set
The most likely etiology of his current symptoms is

1. alcohol withdrawal
2. akathisia
3. delirium
4. hepatic encephalopathy
5. pulmonary embolism

The correct answer is: A
Alcohol withdrawal syndrome is most likely in this patient because he has a history of alcoholism, and has been in the hospital for at least 8-12 hours, away from alcohol. Alcohol withdrawal presents initially with agitation and tremors, and depending on the person and amount of daily alcohol ingestion, can even occur while there is ethanol still detectable in the bloodstream. The tremors of alcohol withdrawal are typically high frequency, low volume, and present at rest. Additionally, acute alcohol withdrawal is characterized by autonomic hyperactivity indicated by tachycardia, hypertension, tachypnea, and anxiety. Alcohol withdrawal presents soon after the cessation of alcohol ingestion in chronic alcoholics and begins first with tremor, tachycardia, and hypertension. It then progresses to seizures, delirium, and hallucinations.

On exam this patient exhibits a few osteopathic findings that are characteristic of liver disease including viscerosomatic reflex from T6-10, anterior chapman point in the right 6th intercostal space, and diaphragm restriction as a result of inflammation and hepatomegaly.

Answer B: Akathisia is a feeling of motor restlessness and inner-anxiety that occurs most commonly following the administration of medications that blocks dopamine (most commonly antipsychotics such as haloperidol, metoclopramide, and thioridazine) in the central nervous system. It is the mildest of the extrapyramidal symptoms (others being dystonia, dyskinesia, tardive dyskinesia, and neuroleptic malignant syndrome). This question does not mention the patient receiving any medications, therefore it makes medication side effect such as akathisia less likely. In addition, the question asks about the etiology of the symptoms, which is alcohol withdrawal, as some sources cite the symptoms of alcohol withdrawal as being a type of akathisia.


Answer C: Delirium is a clinical syndrome of waxing and waning levels of orientation commonly associated with visual hallucinations. The causes of delirium are myriad, but most commonly include infection, medication reaction, “sundowning”, and simply being hospitalized. It is much more common in elderly patients. Usually delirium does not involve vital sign abnormalities or tremor, as it is simply a confusional state associated with vivid visual hallucinations. Delirium tremens is a separate entity, and is the most severe form of alcohol withdrawal. It occurs usually beyond 2-3 days after the cessation of alcohol use in a person dependent on alcohol.


Answer D: Hepatic encephalopathy occurs in patients with liver dysfunction (cirrhosis) due to buildup of toxic metabolites (most commonly thought to be ammonia) in the brain. Classic symptoms include disorientation, confusion, and day-night reversal. The classic physical exam finding is called asterixis and is described as a “flapping tremor” when hands are held in extension with arms outstretched. While this patient has cirrhosis, he does not have confusion or a decreased level of arousal to suggest encephalopathy. In addition, hepatic encephalopathy does not usually cause autonomic hyperactivity (tachycardia, hypertension, and diaphoresis).


Answer E: In the right clinical context, a pulmonary embolism (PE) can cause tachycardia, diaphoresis, but should not cause a tremor. This patient is also very early in his hospital course and is not yet at risk for developing a deep vein thrombosis due to immobility. In addition, this question stem does not mention hypoxemia, chest pain, shortness of breath, or leg swelling to suggest that a PE is the cause of the symptoms.




Bottom Line: Alcohol withdrawal presents soon after the cessation of alcohol ingestion in chronic alcoholics and begins first with tremor, tachycardia, and hypertension.

COMBANK Insight : Know the stages of alcohol withdrawal - 1) 6-12 hours - tremor, tachycardia, hypertension, 2) 12-48 hours - seizures, 3) 2-7 days - delirium tremens.

For more information, see:
"Withdrawal Syndromes " on Medscape.

Patient Presentation: Substance Abuse
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Management
Body System: Nervous System
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 45-year-old male presents to the emergency department after being hit by a car at low speed.  Past medical history reveals hepatitis C, cirrhosis, and alcoholism. Vital signs reveal a temperature of 37.2º C (99º F), a blood pressure of 132/80 mmHg, a heart rate of 72/minute, a respiratory rate of 16/minute and an oxygen saturation of 98% on room air. Physical examination reveals an intoxicated male with slurred speech and lethargy; he is unable to cooperate with the exam fully. You note paravertebral hypertonicity from T6-9, right diaphragm restriction, and a tender nodule in the 6th intercostal space near the sternum on the right. There are several abrasions on the patient's limbs and cardiopulmonary examination is normal. The patient is admitted to the hospital for observation until a more thorough exam can be completed. 8 hours later, the patient becomes diaphoretic, agitated, and tremulous while remaining fully alert and oriented. Repeat vital signs reveal a temperature of 37°C (98.6°F), a blood pressure of 180/100 mmHg, a heart rate of 130/min, a respiratory rate of 20/min, and an oxygen saturation of 94% on room air.

Question 2 of 2 in this set
The best medication to prevent a seizure is

1. chlordiazepoxide
2. lactulose
3. levetiracetam
4. phenytoin
5. propofol

The correct answer is: A
The mainstay of treatment for alcohol withdrawal is with benzodiazepines. Examples of this class of medication include chlordiazepoxide, lorazepam, diazepam, midazolam, and clorazepate. Chlordiazepoxide and lorazepam are the two most commonly used medications. Benzodiazepines and ethanol are both active on the GABA receptor, therefore withdrawal from one can be treated by the other. Often patients can require extremely large doses of intravenous benzodiazepines or barbiturates to prevent seizures or autonomic instability.

Answer B: Lactulose is one of several medications given to patients with hepatic encephalopathy. Lactulose is metabolized in the colon by bacterial flora into acidic moieties, thereby acidifying the colonic contents (increasing the H+ concentration in the gut). This promotes the formation of the nonabsorbable NH4+ from NH3, trapping ammonium in the colon. This process effectively reduces the plasma NH3 concentrations that eventually end up in the CNS and affecting cognition.


Answer C: Levetiracetam is an anti-seizure medication but is used in the treatment of epilepsy. If this patient had a seizure, it would be due to alcohol withdrawal. The best way to prevent this to treat the withdrawal with benzodiazepines


Answer D: Phenytoin is another anti-seizure medication. Again, the best way to prevent seizures in a patient undergoing alcohol withdrawal is to prevent withdrawal with benzodiazepines


Answer E: Propofol is an anesthetic used in surgeries and for sedation. It is a powerful anti-seizure medication and is in refractory status epilepticus. It would not be used in this situation.




Bottom Line: Treat alcohol withdrawal syndrome with benzodiazepines.

For more information, see:
"Withdrawal Syndromes " on Medscape.

Patient Presentation: Digestive Difficulties
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Scientific Understanding of Health & Disease Mechanisms
Body System: Gastrointestinal System
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 36-year-old female presents to the emergency department complaining of nausea, vomiting, and diarrhea. History reveals the symptoms began a few hours after eating a lunch of left over Chinese food. Physical examination reveals mild diffuse abdominal tenderness with hyperactive bowel sounds in all four quadrants. Intravenous ondansetron is administered for nausea. The mechanism of action of this drug is

1. 5-HT2 antagonist
2. 5-HT3 antagonist
3. dopamine receptor agonist
4. dopamine receptor antagonist
5. serotonin receptor agonist

The correct answer is: B
Ondansetron is a selective 5-HT3 antagonist, used as an anti-emetic. It works both in the periphery and in the central nervous system with its primary site of action in the gastrointestinal tract. Since ondansetron does not have any effect on dopamine receptors, extrapyramidal side effects are not seen with its use. The most common side effect of ondansetron is headache. It is most commonly used as an anti-emetic in patients with gastroenteritis, chemotherapy or radiation-induced nausea and vomiting, or post-operative nausea and vomiting. Use with caution in patients with hepatic impairment, prolonged Q-T intervals, and phenylketonuria.

Answer A: 5-HT2A is expressed widely throughout the CNS. High concentrations of this receptor in the cortex may modulate cognitive processes, working memory, and attention. In the periphery, it is highly expressed in platelets and many cell types of the cardiovascular system, in fibroblasts, and in neurons of the peripheral nervous system. It is not implicated in GI functionality.


Answer B: The 5-HT3 receptors are present in several critical sites involved in emesis, including vagal afferents, the solitary tract nucleus (STN), and the area postrema itself. The 5-HT3 receptor antagonists suppress vomiting and nausea by inhibiting serotonin binding to the 5-HT3 receptors.


Answer C: Dopaminergic stimulation (via D2 receptors) in the GI tract is implicated in inducing nausea, vomiting, gastric irritation and reflux.


Answer D: Dopamine antagonists, such as metoclopramide, are also used as an anti-emetic as it blocks the effect of dopamine in the GI tract, especially at high doses.


Answer E: Serotonin is released by the enterochromaffin cells of the small intestine in response to chemotherapeutic agents and stimulates vagal afferents (via 5-HT3 receptors) to initiate the vomiting reflex. The 5-HT3 receptor antagonists (Ondansetron) suppress vomiting and nausea by inhibiting serotonin binding to the 5-HT3 receptors. A serotonin receptor agonist would have the opposite effect.




Bottom Line: The mechanism of action of ondansetron is a 5-HT3 receptor blocker that is used as an anti-emetic in children and adults.

For more information, see:
"Serotonin modulators: Pharmacology, administration, and side effects". Literature review current through: May 2017. | This topic last updated: May 14, 2017.


Last Updated: 6/15/2017
186 of 200

Link: "Complex Regional Pain Syndrome in Emergency Medicine" on Medscape.


Patient Presentation: Musculoskeletal Difficulties And Muscular Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Musculoskeletal System
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 57-year-old female presents to the office with right hand pain that has been persistent for one years' duration. History reveals throbbing pain and increased heat in the right hand. She has also noticed increased hair and nail growth on the right hand when compared to the left hand. The patient had been in a car accident six months ago during which her right hand was injured, but extensive imaging revealed no evidence of fracture. Electromyography (EMG) is negative for any abnormality. The most likely diagnosis is

1. complex regional pain syndrome type I
2. complex regional pain syndrome type II
3. peripheral neuropathy
4. thoracic outlet syndrome
5. venous thrombosis

The correct answer is: A
Complex regional pain syndrome (CRPS) is a disorder of the extremities that is characterized by pain, swelling, limited range of motion, vasomotor instability, skin changes, and patchy bone demineralization. Two types of CRPS are recognized: type I in which there is no definable nerve lesion and type II in which a definite peripheral nerve injury is present. The patient in the scenario above has no defined nerve lesion and a normal electromyography (EMG) study of the extremity, which would make her most likely diagnosis complex regional pain syndrome type I. Chronic regional pain syndrome often begins after a minor or major injury, although it can also occur without a precipitating event. The diagnosis is suggested by severe pain, often burning or throbbing, as well as altered skin temperature. Emotional stress may also be a contributing factor.

Answer B: Complex regional pain syndrome type II has a defined peripheral nerve injury present.


Answer C: Peripheral neuropathy would be evident on EMG. It is typically characterized by pain, numbness, and paresthesia. It is unlikely that peripheral neuropathy would present with temperature or skin changes.


Answer D: Thoracic outlet syndrome is the compression of the neurovascular bundle (subclavian artery, subclavian vein, and brachial plexus) as it exits the thoracic outlet. It is characterized by neck pain and paresthesia radiating to the arm.


Answer E: Venous thrombosis often presents with pain and swelling in a limb. The skin will often be dusky due to venous congestion in a limb affected with a venous clot. A venous thrombosis is typically a more acute diagnosis, rather than chronic. Venous thrombosis is also unlikely to present with increased hair and nail growth.




Bottom Line: Complex regional pain syndrome (CRPS) is a disorder of the extremities that is characterized by pain, swelling, limited range of motion, vasomotor instability, skin changes, and patchy bone demineralization. Two types of chronic regional pain syndrome are recognized: type I in which there is no definable nerve lesion and type II in which a definite peripheral nerve injury is present. The patient in the scenario above has no defined nerve lesion and a normal electromyography (EMG) study of the extremity, which would make her most likely diagnosis complex regional pain syndrome type I.

For more information, see:
"Complex Regional Pain Syndrome in Emergency Medicine" on Medscape.

187 of 200

Link: "Meniscal injury of the knee" on UpToDate.


Patient Presentation: Musculoskeletal Difficulties And Muscular Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Musculoskeletal System
Discipline: Family Medicine
A 19-year-old male presents to the office with the complaint of five days of right knee pain following a club basketball game. He admits to increasing pain and swelling that occurred the following day. Physical examination of his right knee demonstrates an effusion that is tender to palpation with increased tenderness along the medial joint line. You note paravertebral hypertonicity from T11-L2, hamstring spasm, and tenderness over the IT band.  Special testing reveals a positive McMurray’s test.
Question 1 of 2 in this set
The structure most likely damaged is

1. anterior cruciate ligament
2. lateral meniscus
3. medial collateral ligament
4. medial meniscus
5. posterior cruciate ligament

The correct answer is: D
Meniscal tears are the most common injury to the knee that necessitates surgery. The menisci act to primarily share the load across the knee joint by increasing contact area and decreasing contact stress. They also provide stability, absorb shock, increase articular congruity, aid in lubrication, prevent synovial impingement and limit extremes of flexion and extension. They are vascularized by the superior, inferior and middle genicular arteries. The blood supply decreases from a peripheral to central direction, creating three zones of vascularity; red-red, red-white and white-white, with the red-red zone the only area receiving direct vascularization. The medial meniscus is torn three times more often than the lateral meniscus, due to loading biomechanics across the knee. However, the lateral meniscus is torn more often with concomitant anterior cruciate ligament tears. The most common mechanism of injury in young athletes is a twisting motion or change of direction. In older patients, squatting or falling is the more common mechanism of injury. Patients will experience immediate pain followed by a delayed effusion. They may also experience locking, catching, clicking or giving way; due to the torn meniscus interfering with the articulating surfaces.

You may note the following osteopathic findings in patients with a torn meniscus: viscerosomatic reflex from T11-L2 (lower extremity), local irritation to the IT band, hamstring spasms and tenderness.

Answer A: Anterior cruciate ligament (ACL) tears occur from non-contact, cutting and twisting mechanisms that occur when landing from a jump or changing direction. Patients experience immediate pain and an early effusion as well as instability. A positive Lachman’s test is highly suggestive of an ACL tear.


Answer B: Lateral meniscal tears occur at one-third the rate as medial meniscal tears. Patients with lateral meniscal tears will complain of the same symptoms and present with the same mechanisms of injury. Without an MRI to help determine which meniscus is torn, the odds favor a medial meniscal tear in this patient.


Answer C: Medial collateral ligament (MCL) and lateral collateral ligament (LCL) injuries occur when a valgus or varus force is applied across the knee, respectively. The patient will complain of pain and swelling, but usually will not have an effusion, since these structures are extra-articular. Varus-valgus stress testing will aid in the diagnoses by demonstrating gapping of the affected knee as compared to the uninjured knee.


Answer E: Posterior cruciate ligament (PCL) injuries are less common because they require a powerful force. It usually occurs secondary to a powerful blow to a flexed knee such as in a motor vehicle accident or athletic injury. A twisting or hyperextension injury may also cause this.




Bottom Line: Meniscal tears are most commonly caused by a twisting or change of direction mechanism in young athletes, while squatting or falling cause tears in older patients. Patients present with knee pain, an effusion and may experience locking, clicking, catching or giving way. A positive McMurray’s and Apley’s test are suggestive of a meniscal tear. On average, medial meniscal tears are three times more common than lateral meniscal tears.

For more information, see:
"Meniscal injury of the knee" on UpToDate.
Miller MD, Thompson SR, Hart JA, et al. Review of Orthopaedics. Philadelphia, PA: Elseiver-Saunders; 2012: 294.

Patient Presentation: Musculoskeletal Difficulties And Muscular Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Diagnostic Technologies
Body System: Musculoskeletal System
Discipline: Family Medicine
A 19-year-old male presents to the office with the complaint of five days of right knee pain following a club basketball game. He admits to increasing pain and swelling that occurred the following day. Physical examination of his right knee demonstrates an effusion that is tender to palpation with increased tenderness along the medial joint line. You note paravertebral hypertonicity from T11-L2, hamstring spasm, and tenderness over the IT band.  Special testing reveals a positive McMurray’s test.
Question 2 of 2 in this set
The most appropriate imaging study to confirm the diagnosis is a/an

1. bone scan
2. CT with contrast
3. MRI
4. PET scan
5. x-ray

The correct answer is: C
The best imaging study to assess the meniscus for damage is an MRI. An MRI will provide detailed images of the meniscus in three planes, which can be assessed for increased signal that can indicate a tear. It may also demonstrate an effusion, bone contusion, other ligamentous damage and whether the torn meniscus is displaced. Meniscal tears may be treated non-operatively if; they are stable, longitudinal tears < 10mm in length and < 5mm of displacement, degenerative tears with significant osteoarthritis, short radial tears < 3mm or stable partial tears. These tears are treated with ice, rest, NSAIDs and physical therapy. Surgery is indicated if the tear is a vertical longitudinal tear, a radial tear >3mm, a flap, a horizontal cleavage or a complex tear. Only vertical longitudinal tears are repaired, while all other tears receive a partial meniscectomy.

Answer A: Bone scans are useful in diagnosing stress fractures and osteomyelitis, but are not particularly helpful for meniscal tears as it is primarily a soft-tissue structure.


Answer B: CT scans have been largely replaced by MRI for the evaluation of soft tissue structures, such as the meniscus. They may be used if an MRI is contraindicated for the patient. CT lacks the definition of soft-tissues that MRI provides.


Answer D: Ultrasound is beneficial for evaluating soft tissue lesions about the knee, such as patellar tendonitis, hematomas and extensor tendon ruptures. They offer little diagnostic value for the evaluation of meniscal injuries, however.


Answer E: X-rays are an initial diagnostic modality that will be performed to rule out bony injury, however they will not provide the soft tissue imagery necessary to diagnose a torn meniscus. Occasionally, an x-ray may show increased lateral joint space, which may indicate a discoid lateral meniscus.




Bottom Line: MRI is the diagnostic study of choice to evaluate meniscal injuries, as it provides excellent definition of the structure and can demonstrate if the tear is partial or complete based on the signal intensity and location.

For more information, see:
Meniscal injury of the knee UpToDate.
Miller MD, Thompson SR, Hart JA, et al. Review of Orthopaedics. Philadelphia, PA: Elseiver-Saunders; 2012: 294.

Patient Presentation: Skin, Nail & Hair Disorders
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Management
Body System: Integumentary System
Discipline: Family Medicine
A 14-year-old male presents to the office with the complaints of minor itching and a rash.  He says that there was a rash on his chest that suddenly erupted over his abdomen a couple of days later.  The patient states he had a cold a week prior to the rashes.  There is no family history of diseases and he is on no medication. Physical examination reveals there are multiple salmon-pink patches that are 4-5 mm in diameter scattered around the anterior chest and abdomen.  The borders have a collarette appearance.  VDRL and FTA-AB. tests are negative.  The best treatment at this time is

1. oral steroids
2. reassurance and follow-up
3. antibiotics
4. immunosuppressants
5. permethrin cream

The correct answer is: B
Pityriasis rosea is a self-limiting condition of unknown origin.  Sometimes patients will have had an upper respiratory infection before the rashes. Typically, a herald patch precedes an outbreak of salmon-pink patches and tends to follow a Christmas-tree pattern on the trunk of the body. Patients will oftentimes complain of pruritus as well.  It is important to rule out tinea corporis, secondary syphilis, eczema, and medication-induced rashes.  This condition requires no treatment, but patients oftentimes have relief of pruritus with calamine lotion, oral antihistamines, and topical steroids.  Interestingly, direct sun exposure or ultraviolet light within the first week of eruption can decrease the severity of the disease.

Answer A: Steroid creams can be used for pruritus in pityriasis rosea; oral steroids can be used if pruritus is severe which is not the case in this patient.


Answer C: There is no reason to use antibiotics, as pityriasis rosea is a self-limiting condition that is thought to be of viral origin.


Answer D: Immunosuppressants are not indicated for pityriasis rosea.


Answer E: Permethrin is indicated for scabies infection. There is no indication for using permethrin in pityriasis rosea patients.




Bottom Line: Key words like Christmas tree pattern, herald patch, and preceding viral upper respiratory infection almost always accompany pityriasis rosea diagnoses.  This is a self-limiting condition with medications reserved for relief of pruritus.

For more information, see:
Practical Guide to the Care of the Medical Patient. 7th Ed. Mosby Elsevier: Philadelphia, PA 2007, pp. 262-263.

Patient Presentation: Respiratory Difficulties
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Respiratory System
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 40-year-old female presents to the emergency department with a cough and dyspnea that has been worsening for 4 weeks' duration. She denies fever or chills, but notes unintentional 20-pound weight loss over the past month. Past medical history reveals the patient has no smoking history and denies alcohol use. Physical examination reveals the presence of a pleural friction rub over the right lung base, paravertebral fullness and tenderness from T2-5, and a tender nodule in the fourth intercostal space just to the right of the sternum.  A chest radiograph is obtained as shown in the exhibit. Pleural fluid analysis reveals an exudative pleural effusion.
Question 1 of 3 in this set
The most likely diagnosis is

1. adenocarcinoma
2. idiopathic pulmonary fibrosis
3. large cell carcinoma
4. squamous cell carcinoma
5. small cell carcinoma

The correct answer is: A
Non-small cell lung cancers include adenocarcinoma, squamous cell carcinoma, and large-cell carcinoma and these account for approximately 75% of all lung cancers. Adenocarcinoma is the most common type of non-small cell lung cancer and comprises up to 40% of cases. Adenocarcinoma typically occurs peripherally within the lung and arises from bronchial mucosal glands which secrete mucin. Unlike small cell and squamous cell carcinomas, adenocarcinoma is commonly found in non-smokers, particularly females.

As with any pathology in the lungs, the osteopathic findings can be helpful in determining the location of the underlying disease process.  The viscerosomatic reflex from T2-5 tells us that the lungs are involved and then we can use Chapman points if present to help determine which lung field is involved.  In this example we see the right 4th intercostal space which indicates right lower lung field.  Anteriorly, chapman points can present in the 3rd ICS for the upper lungs, and 4th ICS for lower lungs.  Posteriorly, chapman points are found around the transverse process of T3 for upper and T4 for lower.

Answer B: Idiopathic pulmonary fibrosis is where inflammation causes fibrosis of the lungs. The cause is unknown and patients usually present with progressive dyspnea and dry crackles. Patients are often over 50 years of age, unlike this case. A chest radiograph would reveal a ground glass appearance diffusely involving both lungs whereas the radiograph in this case reveals a mass in the periphery of one lung field.


Answer C: Large cell carcinomas are the least common of the non-small cell lung cancers and comprise anywhere form 5-15% of all lung cancers. They often arise in a peripheral lung field and grow rapidly resulting in frequent cavitation. Patients may present with cough and dyspnea, along with pleuritic chest pain resulting from irritation of the parietal pleura and chest wall.


Answer D: Squamous cell carcinomas are most common in smokers and are almost always centrally located. They may produce symptoms of cough, dyspnea, atelectasis, wheezing, and hemoptysis. Chest x-rays reveal a coin-like lesion that may be cavitary. This type of lung cancer can affect calcium homeostasis by producing a parathyroid like protein. This is best treated with surgery if localized.


Answer E: Small cell carcinomas are common in smokers and are often centrally located. This type of lung cancer grows rapidly which results in early metastasis. Many paraneoplastic syndromes are associated with small cell carcinomas as a result of the production and secretion of ADH (SIADH), ACTH (Cushing's syndrome), or antibodies to calcium channels (Lambert-Eaton syndrome). Primary therapy involves chemotherapy with consideration for adjuvant radiation instead of surgical resection.




Bottom Line: Adenocarcinoma is the most common non-small cell carcinoma of the lung, arises peripherally from mucin-producing glandular tissue, and is classically seen in female non-smokers.

For more information, see:
Respiratory Neoplasms section in Pulmonary Disorders, Step up to USMLE Step 2, pages 42-45.

Patient Presentation: Respiratory Difficulties
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Diagnostic Technologies
Body System: Respiratory System
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 40-year-old female presents to the emergency department with a cough and dyspnea that has been worsening for 4 weeks' duration. She denies fever or chills, but notes unintentional 20-pound weight loss over the past month. Past medical history reveals the patient has no smoking history and denies alcohol use. Physical examination reveals the presence of a pleural friction rub over the right lung base, paravertebral fullness and tenderness from T2-5, and a tender nodule in the fourth intercostal space just to the right of the sternum.  A chest radiograph is obtained as shown in the exhibit. Pleural fluid analysis reveals an exudative pleural effusion.
Question 2 of 3 in this set
The most effective method to obtain a tissue sample in this patient is

1. bronchial brushings
2. CT-guided biopsy
3. fiberoptic bronchoscopy
4. mediastinoscopy
5. thoracoscopy

The correct answer is: B
CT-guided biopsy is the preferred procedure for sampling tissue from peripheral lung masses. It is safe procedure usually performed by an interventional radiologist. A needle is placed percutaneously and guided with the aid of CT scan images. Risks include bleeding, infection, pneumothorax and nerve damage.

Answer A: Tissue can be analyzed by direct biopsy, bronchial brushings with washings, or by transbronchial biopsy depending on the size and location of the lesion.


Answer C: Fiberoptic bronchoscopy should be utilized for addressing centrally located lesions and has the advantage of providing means for direct visualization of the bronchial tree.


Answer D: Mediastinoscopy can be utilized to evaluate mediastinal lymph nodes that have been visualized on CT scanning prior to resection of the lesion.


Answer E: Thoracoscopy can be performed as a second-line procedure to further evaluate lesions that are not characterized by bronchoscopy or CT-guided biopsy.




Bottom Line: Fiberoptic bronchoscopy is the preferred method to evaluate centrally located lung lesions while CT-guided biopsy remains the preferred method for the evaluation of peripheral lesions.

COMBANK Insight : Lung cancer is a leading cause of cancer death in the United States and is a commonly tested topic on the COMLEX. When evaluating a lung mass, you should be able to form an assessment based on the radiographic description of the lesion (eg. cavitating vs. nodular), as well as its location in the chest cavity (eg. central vs. peripheral).

For more information, see:
"Non-Small Cell Lung Cancer" on Medscape.

Patient Presentation: Respiratory Difficulties
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Scientific Understanding of Health & Disease Mechanisms
Body System: Respiratory System
Discipline: Internal Medicine
A 40-year-old female presents to the emergency department with a cough and dyspnea that has been worsening for 4 weeks' duration. She denies fever or chills, but notes unintentional 20-pound weight loss over the past month. Past medical history reveals the patient has no smoking history and denies alcohol use. Physical examination reveals the presence of a pleural friction rub over the right lung base, paravertebral fullness and tenderness from T2-5, and a tender nodule in the fourth intercostal space just to the right of the sternum.  A chest radiograph is obtained as shown in the exhibit. Pleural fluid analysis reveals an exudative pleural effusion.
Question 3 of 3 in this set
Histology of the tissue sample is most likely to reveal

1. glandular formation with mucin production
2. keratin pearls
3. large and irregular nuclei with coarse nuclear chromatin
4. large cells with prominent nucleoli and no mucin production
5. sheets of small and round cells with scant cytoplasm and fine granular chromatin

The correct answer is: A
Adenocarcinoma of the lung is currently the most common type of non-small cell lung cancer, which is characterized histologically by the presence of glandular formation and mucin production. The identification of mucin is achieved by periodic acid-Schiff (PAS) staining.

Answers B & C: Keratin pearls and the presence of large, irregular nuclei with coarse nuclear chromatin are seen in cases of well-differentiated squamous cell carcinomas.


Answer D: Large-cell carcinoma is histologically described by the formation of large cells with prominent nucleoli, but without mucin production.


Answer E: Oat (small) cell carcinoma of the lung is composed of sheets of small, round cells with scant cytoplasm and fine granular chromatin. Oat cell lung cancer is thought to arise from Kulchitsky cells and is located peribronchial in the chest.




Bottom Line: Adenocarcinoma of the lung is described histologically by glandular formation with mucin production identified on PAS staining.

"Non-Small Cell Lung Cancer" on Medscape.

Patient Presentation: Genitourinary Disorders/Issues
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Scientific Understanding of Health & Disease Mechanisms
Body System: Genitourinary System
Discipline: Osteopathic Principle and Practice
A 36-year-old female presents to the office with a complaint of painful urination. History reveals she has also had blood in her urine and pain radiating from her right flank to her groin for the last two days. Past medical history reveals kidney stones. Which of the following is the most likely muscle affected as part of a viscerosomatic response?

1. Iliacus
2. Piriformis
3. Psoas major
4. Quadratus lumborum
5. Rectus abdominis

The correct answer is: C
The ureters lie directly on the psoas muscles as they pass from the kidney to penetrate the base of the bladder. Ureteral stones have been implicated in causes of psoas syndrome as have abdominal aortic aneurysm, intra-abdominal abscess, appendicitis, diverticulitis, inguinal hernia, prostate or sigmoid colon cancer, prostatitis, salpingitis, and Crohn’s disease. The inflammation of the ureter as it overlies the psoas can also cause inflammation and irritation of the psoas muscle.

Answers A & B & D: The quadratus lumborum, iliacus, and piriformis are retroperitoneal, but the ureters are not in contact with them.


Answer E: The rectus abdominis is an anterior abdominal wall muscle. The ureters do not contact the rectus abdominis.




Bottom Line: Psoas syndrome can be defined as a muscular imbalance, strain, spasm, tendonitis, or flexion contracture of the iliopsoas muscle. Ureteral stones can cause a viscerosomatic reflex causing psoas syndrome.

COMBANK Insight : Basic anatomy can help you answer many questions. Try and review some important muscle groups and their corresponding innervation. It would be time-saving to review those that correlate to common osteopathic or orthopedic complaints.

For more information, see:
Chila A, eds. Foundations for Osteopathic Medicine. 3rd Ed. Philadelphia, PA; Lippincott Williams & Wilkins; 2010: 572.

"Clinical manifestations and diagnosis of gout" on UpToDate


Patient Presentation: Musculoskeletal Difficulties And Muscular Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Scientific Understanding of Health & Disease Mechanisms
Body System: Musculoskeletal System
Discipline: Family Medicine
A 21-year-old male presents to the office with the complaint of severe pain in his right first toe.  History reveals that there has not been any trauma to the extremity. The patient reports that he was recently out in the hot sun at a baseball game. He has never experienced anything like this before and is not currently taking any  medications. Social history reveals the patient has been drinking 6-8 beers per day for the past week because he's been on spring break. Vital signs reveal a temperature of 38ºC (100.4 F), a blood pressure of 140/82 mm Hg, and a heart rate of 84/min. Physical examination reveals warmth and redness in the great toe. A radiograph is obtained as shown in the exhibit.
Question 1 of 3 in this set
Joint aspirate would most likely reveal

1. <200 WBCs
2. 2,000-50,000 WBCs, negatively birefringent rhomboids
3. 2,000-50,000 WBCs, negatively birefringent needles
4. 2,000-50,000 WBCs, positively birefringent rhomboids
5. anti-cyclic citrullinated peptide

The correct answer is: C
This is a classic presentation of tophaceous gout. The spontaneous onset of pain, redness, and swelling in the metatarsal-phalangeal joint of the great toe is characteristic of podagra, the most common presentation of gout.  Gout should be considered in any patient who presents with monoarticular arthritis of any joint (with the exception of the glenohumeral joint as this is typically indicative of pseudogout).  Risk factors for gout include excessive alcohol and protein intake in addition to dehydration.  Gout is more common in middle-aged to elderly male patients, but it can occur in younger males as well.  Radiographic findings will rule out fracture and may demonstrate soft tissue swelling.  The most accurate test for diagnosing tophaceous gout is joint aspiration revealing a white blood cell (predominantly neutrophils) count between 2,000 and 50,000 with negatively birefringent needle-shaped crystals.  Any time a patient presents with acute pain, erythema, and warmth of a joint it should be tapped to exclude septic arthritis.

Answer A: This is seen in osteoarthritis. Osteoarthritis is characterized as a chronic, slowly progressive and erosive arthritis resulting in loss of articular cartilage. It is non-inflammatory, and thus shows a low white blood cell count on joint aspiration.


Answer B: A WBC count between 2,000-50,000 and negatively birefringent needle-shaped crystals would demonstrate tophaceous gout.


Answer D: This is the most often selected incorrect answer. The inflammatory reaction is of similar intensity in both tophaceous gout and in calcium pyrophosphate deposition disease (CPDD, "pseudogout") resulting in similarly elevated white blood cell counts. These patients are typically of advanced age. In addition, the knee is affected in over 50% of all acute attacks while the first metatarsophalangeal joint is the most frequently affected in urate gout. In CPDD, joint aspiration will reveal positively birefringent rhomboid-shaped crystals.


Answer E: Anti-cyclic citrullinated peptide is the most sensitive (>80%) and specific (>95%) marker for rheumatoid arthritis (RA). In contrast, rheumatoid factor is seen in roughly 70-80% of patients with RA, however it is rather nonspecific and may be associated with many other autoimmune disorders and chronic infectious diseases.




Bottom Line: Joint aspiration in a patient with tophaceous gout would demonstrate a WBC count between 2,000-50,000 and negatively birefringent needle-shaped crystals.

For more information, see:

"Clinical manifestations and diagnosis of gout" on UpToDate

Link: "Clinical manifestations and diagnosis of gout" on UpToDate.


Patient Presentation: Musculoskeletal Difficulties And Muscular Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Scientific Understanding of Health & Disease Mechanisms
Body System: Musculoskeletal System
Discipline: Family Medicine
A 21-year-old male presents to the office with the complaint of severe pain in his right first toe.  History reveals that there has not been any trauma to the extremity. The patient reports that he was recently out in the hot sun at a baseball game. He has never experienced anything like this before and is not currently taking any  medications. Social history reveals the patient has been drinking 6-8 beers per day for the past week because he's been on spring break. Vital signs reveal a temperature of 38ºC (100.4 F), a blood pressure of 140/82 mm Hg, and a heart rate of 84/min. Physical examination reveals warmth and redness in the great toe. A radiograph is obtained as shown in the exhibit.
Question 2 of 3 in this set
The patient inquires about prevention of future attacks. The patient states his current episodes are helped with ibuprofen but consumption of which of the following medicine or food items may help to decrease this patient's risk of future episodes?

1. chlorthalidone
2. red wine
3. low fat products and vegetables
4. seafood
5. fruit juice

The correct answer is: C
Gout is caused by monosodium urate monohydrate crystals; pseudogout is caused by calcium pyrophosphate crystals and is more accurately termed calcium pyrophosphate disease. Elevated serum uric acid levels are the principal risk factor for developing gout. lIn study that compared 993 patients with asymptomatic hyperuricemia and 4,241 normouricemic patients, the odds ratio (OR) for developing gout was 32 times higher in the hyperuricemic group than in the normouricemic group. The risk was most striking in men with severe hyperuricemia, in whom the OR for developing gout was 624.8. Although gout is associated with hyperuricemia, gout attacks are triggered not by a particular level of uric acid but typically by acute changes in the level of uric acid. All individuals with gout have hyperuricemia; however, hyperuricemia is also found in patients taking diuretics and even in those taking niacin or low doses of aspirin. Because uric acid is a breakdown product of purine, high-purine foods should be either avoided or consumed only in moderation. Foods very high in purines include organ meats such as sweetbreads (eg, pancreas and thymus), smelt, sardines, and mussels. Foods moderately high in purines include anchovies, trout, haddock, scallops, mutton, veal, liver, bacon, salmon, kidneys, and turkey. Overall, purine restriction generally reduces serum uric acid levels by no more than 1 mg/mL, with modest impact, and diets with very low purine content are not palatable. Diet modifications alone are rarely able to lower uric acid levels sufficiently to prevent accumulation of urate, but they may help lessen the triggers of acute gout attacks. Patients with gout should avoid excess ingestion of alcoholic drinks, particularly beer, because alcohol use elevates uric acid levels and thus can precipitate attacks of gout. Indeed, heavy drinkers are much more likely to have recurrent gout attacks, even with allopurinol therapy. Moderate wine intake is not associated with increased development of incident gout, but excesses of any form of alcohol in gout patients are associated with acute gout flares. Patients should avoid sodas and other beverages or foods sweetened with high-fructose corn syrup. They should also limit their use of naturally sweet fruit juices, table sugar, and sweetened beverages and desserts, as well as table salt. Maintaining a high level of hydration with water (at least 8 glasses of liquids per day) may be helpful in avoiding attacks of gout. In view of the association of gout with atherosclerosis, the diagnosis of gout may afford a particularly good opportunity for the clinician to advise a low-cholesterol, low-fat diet if such a diet is otherwise appropriate for the patient. Although a diet of this type may help uric acid levels, such advice should be given primarily to help prevent atherosclerosis. Evidence has shown that low-fat dairy products have been proven to lower the incidence of gout flares. Low-fat dairy products reduce uricosuric products and are anti-inflammatory in nature.

Answer A: Some medications such as thiazide diuretics are also known to increase uric acid levels and precipitate gout. Therefore, this would be an incorrect answer choice.


Answer B: Substances rich in purines, such as alcohol, are known to precipitate acute gouty attacks. The patient should be informed that his excessive use of alcohol is likely the cause of his symptoms. Other foods that are likely to increase gout attacks include high protein foods such as fish, mussels, and meats, especially organ meats. Although wine has shown to not be as abrasive as beer, alcohol consumption in general is linked to gouty flares. Therefore, red wine is not recommended.


Answer D: Seafood, in particular, anchovies, herring, sardines, mussels, scallops, trout, haddock, mackerel and tuna have been shown to increase the chances of making gout worse.


Answer E: Intake of high-fructose beverages such as soft drinks and fruit juices are associated with gout flares. The metabolism of fructose can cause increased uric acid production.




Bottom Line: Low-fat dairy products have been shown to reduce gout flares.

For more information, see:
"Clinical manifestations and diagnosis of gout" on UpToDate.
Gout - E Medicine

Patient Presentation: Musculoskeletal Difficulties And Muscular Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Management
Body System: Musculoskeletal System
Discipline: Family Medicine
A 21-year-old male presents to the office with the complaint of severe pain in his right first toe.  History reveals that there has not been any trauma to the extremity. The patient reports that he was recently out in the hot sun at a baseball game. He has never experienced anything like this before and is not currently taking any  medications. Social history reveals the patient has been drinking 6-8 beers per day for the past week because he's been on spring break. Vital signs reveal a temperature of 38ºC (100.4 F), a blood pressure of 140/82 mm Hg, and a heart rate of 84/min. Physical examination reveals warmth and redness in the great toe. A radiograph is obtained as shown in the exhibit.
Question 3 of 3 in this set
The best medication with the least side effects used to manage acute presentations of this condition is

1. colchicine
2. cosyntropin
3. nitrates
4. allopurinol
5. indomethacin

The correct answer is: E
There are 2 approaches to treatment of gout: management of acute attacks and prophylaxis. NSAIDs such as indomethacin are the mainstay therapy for acute gout attacks. High doses are given for 2-5 days until the symptoms have subsided. Colchicine was the mainstay of treatment of acute gout flares in the past. However, it is rarely used because of its high side effect profile. Once the acute attack is resolved prophylactic agents such as allopurinol can be given.

Answer A: Colchicine decreases leukocyte motility (and thus inflammatory response) can also be used for the acute treatment of gout but it is associated with more side effects such as nausea, vomiting, diarrhea, bone marrow suppression, and thrombocytopenia.


Answer B: Cosyntropin is an adrenocorticotropic hormone that works by stimulating endogenous steroid production and is used for the treatment of acute crystal-induced arthritis in postoperative patients and those who cannot take oral medications. It is not the mainstay therapy for acute gout.


Answer C: Nitrates are typically used in the treatment of angina. They are not used for treating gout.


Answer D: Allopurinol is used for the treatment of chronic gout and had little role in acute therapy.




Bottom Line: NSAIDs such as indomethacin are the mainstay therapy for acute gouty attacks. Colchicine is an alternative therapy but is associated with more side effects.

For more information, see:
"Clinical manifestations and diagnosis of gout" on UpToDate. http://www.uptodate.com/contents/clinical-manifestations-and-diagnosis-of-gout?source=search_result&search=Gout&selectedTitle=2~134.

Patient Presentation: Sensory Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: History & Physical Examination
Body System: Hepatobiliary System
Discipline: Surgery
A 32-year-old female presents to the emergency department with abdominal pain. History reveals the pain is primarily in the right upper quadrant and radiates to the epigastrum and right subscapular region. She states she has a decreased appetite, nausea, vomiting, and chills. Vital signs reveal a blood pressure of 92/56 mmHg, a heart rate of 104/min, and a temperature of 101.4°F. Physical exam reveals an ill-appearing female with scleral icterus and tenderness in the right upper quadrant. There is paravertebral hypertonicity present from T7-9.  n style="Font-Family:Verdana; ">Laboratory analysis shows an elevated white blood cell count, elevated AST and ALT, and an elevated direct bilirubin.


Question 1 of 2 in this set
The most likely diagnosis is

1. acute cholangitis
2. acute cholecystitis
3. acute pancreatitis
4. cholelithiasis
5. hepatoma

The correct answer is: A
The most likely diagnosis is acute cholangitis, also referred to as ascending cholangitis. This is most often characterized by Charcot's triad of clinical features including fever, RUQ pain and jaundice. The addition of hypotension and altered mental status would be consistent with Reynold's pentad which may be seen in some patients with worsening acute cholangitis. The most common cause of acute cholangitis is a stone in the common bile duct, also known as choledocholithiasis. Laboratory analysis will reveal signs of inflammation and infection (elevated white blood cells) as well as biliary obstruction (elevated ALT, AST, and direct bilirubin).

Viscerosomatic reflexes may result in tissue changes in the thoracic paravertebral musculature corresponding to the sympathetic innervation of the involved tissue.  The gallbladder will cause changes from T8-10, the liver T7-9, so since cholangitis affects the gallbladder, bile duct, and liver, you would expect to see viscerosomatic reflexes somewhere between T7-10.

Answer B: Cholecystitis is inflammation of the gallbladder that most commonly results from a gallstone in the cystic duct (cholelithiasis). Because the common bile duct is not obstructed (choledocholithiasis), the patient is usually not jaundiced and the laboratory analysis very rarely reveals signs of obstruction as seen in this case.


Answer C: Acute pancreatitis is inflammation of the pancreas that most commonly presents as severe, epigastric pain that radiates to the mid back. Most patients also have nausea, vomiting, and fever with no signs of jaundice as in this case. The most common causes include alcohol consumption and gallstones. Laboratory analysis would reveal elevated amylase and lipase with no signs of biliary obstruction as in this case.


Answer D: Cholelithiasis describes a gallstone in the cystic duct, not the common bile duct (choledocholithiasis). This may eventually result in cholecystitis, or inflammation of the gallbladder. A patient with cholelithiasis is usually not jaundiced and the laboratory analysis very rarely reveals signs of obstruction as seen in this case. Acute cholangitis is the most likely diagnosis in this case.


Answer E: Hepatoma, or hepatocellular carcinoma, often presents with right upper quadrant pain, weight loss, and an enlarged liver that is nodular, firm, and irregular to palpation.




Bottom Line: Acute ascending cholangitis commonly presents with Charcot's triad which describes the presence of fever, jaundice, and right upper quadrant pain.

For more information, see:
"Cholangitis in Emergency Medicine" on Medscape.

Patient Presentation: Sensory Pain
Physician Tasks/ Skills: Diagnostic Technologies
Body System: Hepatobiliary System
Discipline: Surgery
A 32-year-old female presents to the emergency department with abdominal pain. History reveals the pain is primarily in the right upper quadrant and radiates to the epigastrum and right subscapular region. She states she has a decreased appetite, nausea, vomiting, and chills. Vital signs reveal a blood pressure of 92/56 mmHg, a heart rate of 104/min, and a temperature of 101.4°F. Physical exam reveals an ill-appearing female with scleral icterus and tenderness in the right upper quadrant. There is paravertebral hypertonicity present from T7-9.  Laboratory analysis shows an elevated white blood cell count, elevated AST and ALT, and an elevated direct bilirubin.


Question 2 of 2 in this set
The most appropriate test to confirm the diagnosis is

1. endoscopic retrograde cholangiopancreatography
2. hepatobiliary iminodiacetic scan
3. laparoscopy
4. liver biopsy
5. ultrasound

The correct answer is: A
Endoscopic retrograde cholangiopancreatography (ERCP) is the best test to confirm the diagnosis of ascending cholangitis. Percutaneous transhepatic cholangiography is also acceptable but was not listed as a possible choice.

Answer B: HIDA scans are used to evaluate blockage of the cystic duct. A radioactive tracer is injected through any accessible vein and allowed to circulate to the liver, where it is excreted into the biliary system. It is used to diagnosis cholecystitis.


Answer C: Although the patient is in a great deal of pain, she is stable and immediate laparoscopy is not indicated when less invasive tests can be employed first.


Answer D: Liver biopsy is not done to confirm ascending cholangitis.


Answer E: Ultrasound may show biliary duct dilatation, but is not used for definitive confirmation of cholangitis.




Bottom Line: Endoscopic retrograde cholangiopancreatography (ERCP) is the most appropriate test for the confirmation of ascending cholangitis.

For more information, see:
"Cholangitis in Emergency Medicine" on Medscape.
